ERV/HRV Module — Mechanical Ventilation for Tight Buildings
Why this module exists
Any building that achieves below 3.0 ACH@50Pa on a blower door test requires mechanical ventilation by code (IECC 2018 R403.6). SIP construction, well-detailed ICF, and carefully air-sealed stud framing on this property are all capable of reaching that threshold. Natural infiltration in a tight building is not enough to maintain acceptable indoor air quality — the building needs a controlled fresh air supply and a controlled exhaust path. An ERV or HRV provides both while recovering most of the energy from the outgoing air. Without one, the owner either opens windows (defeating the insulation investment) or lives with elevated CO2, humidity, and odors.
This module must be completed before the HVAC trade package for any Living Quarters zone can be finalized. ERV/HRV Duct Records (Section 7.3a) require supply and exhaust port locations, which must be confirmed before wall close-in.
ERV vs HRV — Which one for Zone 4A Kansas
Both units transfer heat from the outgoing stale air to the incoming fresh air, recovering 70–85% of the energy that would otherwise be lost. The difference is moisture.
An ERV (Energy Recovery Ventilator) transfers both heat and moisture. In summer, it pulls humidity out of the incoming hot humid outdoor air before delivering it inside, reducing the cooling load. In winter, it retains some moisture from the outgoing interior air rather than sending it all outside, preventing the air from becoming excessively dry.
An HRV (Heat Recovery Ventilator) transfers heat only. Moisture passes through independently in each direction. In very cold dry climates this is preferable because it lets the building exhaust excess interior moisture in winter. In humid climates it means the incoming summer air arrives at full outdoor humidity.
For IECC Zone 4A Kansas: ERV is the correct choice. Kansas summers are hot and humid — outdoor dew points regularly reach 65–70°F from June through August. An HRV in a Kansas summer delivers that humid air directly into the conditioned space, adding cooling load and risking condensation on cold surfaces. The ERV’s moisture transfer membrane handles summer humidity passively. In Kansas winters the indoor humidity concern is mild compared to a Minnesota or Montana climate — the ERV’s small winter moisture retention is a benefit, not a liability.


App field: ERV/HRV type (ERV / HRV). Auto-recommended as ERV when zone type = Living Quarters and climate zone = 4A. Owner can override with a reason note.
Unit sizing — ASHRAE 62.2 calculation
ASHRAE 62.2-2022 sets the minimum continuous ventilation rate for residential spaces:
Minimum CFM = (7.5 × number of occupants) + (0.01 × conditioned floor area in sq ft). The occupant count for sizing purposes is the number of bedrooms plus one (not actual occupancy). This is the ASHRAE 62.2 default — it provides a conservative estimate without requiring occupancy data.


Worked example — update when floor plan is confirmed:
Assume 1 bedroom + 1 = 2 occupants (ASHRAE formula). Assume 600 sq ft conditioned floor area as a planning placeholder. Minimum CFM = (7.5 × 2) + (0.01 × 600) = 15 + 6 = 21 CFM continuous. In practice, select the next standard unit size above the minimum — most residential ERV units come in 60, 80, 100, 150, and 200 CFM capacities. A 60 CFM unit is adequate for this placeholder. If the living quarters expand to 1,200 sq ft with 2 bedrooms: (7.5 × 3) + (0.01 × 1,200) = 34.5 CFM minimum — still a 60 CFM unit.
App field: ERV minimum CFM (auto-calculated from ASHRAE 62.2 using zone floor area and bedroom count from zone record). Displayed alongside unit selection field so owner can confirm selected unit meets or exceeds the minimum.
Defrost capability — Kansas winters
ERV and HRV units can freeze in cold weather. When outdoor air drops below approximately 14°F, moisture in the exhaust stream can freeze on the heat exchange core, blocking airflow. Kansas design temperature is -4°F at the 99% heating condition (ASHRAE Handbook of Fundamentals, Kansas City). At that temperature, a unit without defrost capability will freeze and shut down — exactly when fresh air ventilation in a tight building matters most.
Defrost is required for this project. Specify a unit with an active defrost cycle. Most manufacturers offer defrost either as a standard feature or as an option at the time of purchase — confirm in the product spec sheet. Common defrost methods: motorized damper that temporarily recirculates interior air to warm the core (most common), electric element preheater on the incoming air stream, or frost-sensing controls that modulate airflow. Any of these is acceptable.


App field: Defrost capability (yes / no / unknown). Blocks Equipment Record from reaching Confirmed status until set to yes.
Ductwork layout — dedicated vs shared
The ERV/HRV serves two separate air streams simultaneously: incoming fresh air (supply) and outgoing stale air (exhaust). These streams must be kept physically separate through the entire duct run. They share only the heat exchange core inside the unit.
Dedicated duct system (preferred): the ERV has its own supply duct (fresh air to living spaces) and its own exhaust duct (stale air from bathrooms and kitchen). Completely independent of the HVAC distribution system. Benefits: no interaction with the HVAC system, easier commissioning, no short-circuit risk, cleaner air quality. This is the right choice for any new construction where the ERV is being designed in from the start.
Shared with HVAC distribution (not applicable for this project): only viable with a ducted forced-air system — which is already blocked for SIP and cathedral ceiling construction (Section 5.7.1). Since the primary HVAC strategy for this project is mini-split or radiant, there is no shared plenum available. Dedicated ducts are the only option.
App field: ERV duct type (Dedicated / Shared — Shared blocked when HVAC type = mini-split or radiant).
Supply and exhaust port placement
Port placement drives both air quality and conflict avoidance. Two rules govern placement:
* Short-circuit prevention: ERV supply ports must be at least 10 ft from any HVAC return air grille. If fresh air enters within 10 ft of the HVAC return, it gets pulled directly into the return without reaching occupants. This conflict check is enforced in the tool (Section 7.3a): placing an ERV supply port within 10 ft of an HVAC return fires a Tier 2 amber flag.
* Source and destination logic: supply fresh air to living areas — bedrooms and main living spaces. Exhaust stale air from bathrooms, kitchen, and utility areas. Do not supply fresh air into a bathroom or exhaust from a bedroom.
Recommended port locations for a barn living quarters layout:
* Fresh air supply: main living area, 7–8 ft height on an interior wall. If two or more living spaces, split the supply between them.
* Stale air exhaust: bathroom ceiling or high wall. If a separate kitchen zone, kitchen exhaust above the range area — not the same duct as the range hood. The range hood handles cooking grease and is always dedicated exterior exhaust; the ERV exhaust handles background humidity and odors only.
Port locations are wall close-in items. ERV/HRV Duct Records (Section 7.3a) require port location coordinates. These records cannot reach Confirmed status until port locations are placed on the floor plan layer, and the HVAC trade package cannot be finalized until all ERV/HRV Duct Records are Confirmed.
Exterior wall penetrations
Each ERV port exits through the exterior wall with a capped exterior grille. Two penetrations minimum (one supply, one exhaust) — they must be on different wall faces or at least 6 ft apart on the same wall face to prevent short-circuiting outdoor air between them.
Penetrations appear on the roof plan layer conflict check (Section 10.5) — if either falls inside the solar reservation zone boundary, a conflict flag fires.
Exterior grille spec: louvered or hooded cap with a back-draft damper, insect screen, and a gasket seal at the wall. Diameter matches duct diameter (4" or 6" round — 4" standard for units under 100 CFM, 6" for units 100 CFM and above). The sleeve through the wall is a framing rough-in item — installed during wall framing, before sheathing closes.
Duct sizing
* Units under 100 CFM: 4" round duct throughout. Pressure drop at 4" diameter is negligible for runs under 25 ft.
* Units 100–200 CFM: 6" round duct on the main trunk from the unit to the first branch. 4" branches to individual rooms.
* Duct material: rigid sheet metal (best), rigid plastic (acceptable), insulated flex duct (acceptable for short sections only — flex duct has high friction loss and should not be used for runs over 6 ft or around sharp bends).
* Insulate all ERV duct runs that pass through unconditioned space. Condensation forms on uninsulated cold duct carrying warm humid interior exhaust air in winter.
App field: ERV duct diameter (4" / 6" — auto-recommended from unit CFM rating). Duct Segment Records auto-populate from the ERV/HRV Duct Record when port locations are confirmed on the floor plan.
Filter maintenance
ERV and HRV units have filters on both the incoming and outgoing air streams. In Kansas, incoming air carries significant dust, pollen, and agricultural particulates — filter loading is faster than in urban environments.
Maintenance interval for this property: inspect filters every 3 months, replace or clean every 3–6 months depending on condition. During harvest season (late summer through fall) inspect monthly — field dust loading can clog filters in weeks. A clogged filter reduces airflow, defeats the ventilation purpose, and can overheat the heat exchange core on some units.
Most residential ERV units use standard 4" or 1" panel filters accessible through a front access panel — no tools required. Some units use washable foam filters. Confirm filter type and availability for the selected unit before purchasing.
App field: Filter maintenance reminder — a maintenance schedule entry is auto-created when an ERV Equipment Record reaches Confirmed status. Default interval: 90 days. Owner can adjust.
Regional sourcing
ERV and HRV units are HVAC equipment and source through HVAC distributors and contractors. They are not stocked at big box stores or farm supply stores. Use Claude Prompt 2 (regional sourcing, Section 13.4) with the confirmed unit spec (CFM rating, ERV type, defrost required, manufacturer preference if any) as input. Specify “ERV, residential, [CFM], defrost required, Kansas City metro area” as the category in the prompt input.
Lead time: residential ERV/HRV units are typically 1–3 weeks from a distributor. Order after HVAC equipment selection is locked, before framing inspection — the duct sleeves through walls are a framing rough-in item and must be installed before walls close.
App actions when ERV/HRV module is complete
* Equipment Record created for ERV unit at Concept status, moving to Estimated when unit spec is selected
* ERV/HRV Duct Records created for supply and exhaust runs (Section 7.3a) — initially unpopulated, become pre-wall-close-in blockers when Living Quarters zone reaches Roughed-in status
* Electrical circuit record created for ERV unit (most residential ERV units draw 0.5–1.5A at 120V — a shared 15A circuit is acceptable; dedicated circuit optional)
* Filter maintenance schedule entry created at 90-day default interval
* Short-circuit conflict check activates for supply port placement (10 ft from HVAC return)
* Solar reservation zone conflict check activates for exterior wall penetration coordinates
* Defrost capability field set to required — blocks Equipment Record from Confirmed until confirmed yes
* HVAC trade package ERV/HRV section populates when all Duct Records reach Confirmed status


Kitchen Coordination Module
What this module resolves
A kitchen zone has more pre-pour and rough-in dependencies than any other zone type except a bathroom. The cabinet layout determines outlet heights. The island location determines whether an AAV goes in the slab. The range fuel type determines the gas stub size and range hood duct diameter. All of these are wall-close-in or pre-pour decisions — they cannot be revisited after concrete is poured or drywall is hung.
This module must be completed before the Kitchen zone pre-pour document can be generated. It does not require a final cabinet selection — it requires enough layout information to lock rough-in coordinates.
Work triangle and cabinet layout coordination
The work triangle is the path between the sink, range, and refrigerator. It determines where the wet wall goes, where the gas stub goes, and where the dedicated circuits land. The NKBA (National Kitchen and Bath Association) guideline is a maximum 26 ft total perimeter for the three legs of the triangle. This is a planning reference, not a code requirement — but it reflects real ergonomics and drives rough-in placement.
Before any rough-in coordinates can be confirmed, the owner needs to decide:
* Which wall the sink goes on (drives wet wall location and island sink AAV if applicable)
* Where the range goes (drives gas stub-up location for propane range and range hood duct sleeve location)
* Where the refrigerator goes (drives 1/4" ice maker stub-out location and dedicated circuit stub height)
These do not need to be finalized cabinet dimensions. A rough floor plan sketch with approximate appliance positions is sufficient to lock rough-in coordinates. The app stores these as Fixture Record coordinates (sink, dishwasher) and Appliance BTU Record coordinates (range) — both are pre-pour or rough-in blockers that cannot be cleared without confirmed positions.
App field: Kitchen layout confirmed (yes / no). This field gates Kitchen Fixture Records from advancing past Roughed-in status.
Appliance clearance requirements
These minimum clearances are required for safe installation and code compliance and must be accounted for before cabinet dimensions are ordered.
Range clearances:
* 30" minimum from the cooking surface to any combustible material directly above (overhead cabinet or range hood). Non-combustible hood or over-range microwave: 24" minimum from cooking surface to bottom of unit — confirm in the specific hood spec sheet.
* 12" minimum from the edge of the range to any adjacent combustible sidewall or cabinet face.
* Gas range: shutoff valve must be accessible without moving the range. Standard location: behind the range at the floor or low on the adjacent wall. Stub-up with flexible connector.
Refrigerator clearances:
* 1" minimum on each side for air circulation; 1" minimum above the top of the unit.
* Counter-depth vs standard-depth: confirm before ordering cabinets. Counter-depth refrigerators are typically 24–25" deep; standard refrigerators are 30–35" deep and protrude past the cabinet face.
Dishwasher clearances:
* Standard dishwasher width: 24". The cabinet opening must be exactly 24".
* Air gap fitting: required above the sink deck or on the countertop adjacent to the sink (UPC requirement). Requires a hole in the countertop — finish item but drain routing must be planned at rough-in.


Island sink — venting detail
An island sink has no adjacent wall to run a vent stack through. Two solutions exist and the choice must be made before slab rough-in.
Option 1 — AAV (Air Admittance Valve) at island (recommended):
A one-way mechanical valve that opens to admit air when the drain creates negative pressure during drainage and closes when idle. Mounts inside the island cabinet, accessible for inspection and replacement. This is the standard professional solution for island sinks and is fully acceptable under Kansas plumbing code.
Pre-pour requirement: the island drain stub-up location must be confirmed and placed on the pre-pour drawing. The drain runs below the slab from the island location to the wet wall stack. The AAV itself is above-slab — no pre-pour item needed for the AAV, only for the drain.
Option 2 — Loop vent through floor:
A vent loop rises from the trap arm, loops above the flood rim of the sink, and returns down through the slab to connect to the main vent stack below. Requires two slab penetrations for the vent loop plus one for the drain. Not recommended when AAV is permitted.
PRE-POUR BLOCKER: island sink location must be confirmed on the floor plan before the pre-pour document can be generated for the Kitchen zone. If the distance from the island stub-up to the wet wall stack exceeds 12 ft, verify that 1/4" per foot drain slope is achievable within the slab assembly depth — if not, the drain may need to drop below standard slab assembly and the foundation must accommodate it.


App field: Island sink venting method (AAV / Loop vent). Selecting AAV adds the AAV to the Fixture Record and adds the island drain stub-up to the pre-pour coordination document.
Range hood — CFM selection and duct routing
The range hood is an exterior exhaust item only. Recirculating hoods are not permitted in a tight building (Section 5.7.1 — blocked for buildings targeting below 3.0 ACH@50Pa). Every range hood on this project exhausts to exterior.
CFM selection by range type:
* Electric range: 100 CFM minimum
* Propane range, residential BTU (under 60,000 BTU total): 100 CFM minimum
* Propane range, high-output (60,000–100,000 BTU total): 200 CFM minimum
* Professional/commercial-style range (over 100,000 BTU total): 1 CFM per 100 BTU input. A 150,000 BTU range requires 1,500 CFM — this path requires a commercial hood and makeup air system and must be flagged explicitly if the owner is considering a commercial range.
Makeup air: any range hood over 400 CFM in a tight building requires a makeup air system — a controlled fresh air supply that replaces the exhausted air, preventing the building from going negative pressure during cooking. Negative pressure in a tight building with fuel-burning appliances is a combustion air and back-draft risk (Section 7.3). If hood CFM selection lands above 400 CFM, the makeup air requirement activates as a Tier 2 amber flag.


Duct sizing: up to 400 CFM = 6" round; 400–700 CFM = 8" round; over 700 CFM = 10" round or equivalent rectangular.
Duct routing: the range hood duct sleeve through the wall or roof is a framing rough-in item — must be installed before sheathing closes. The sleeve exit point appears on the roof plan layer conflict check (Section 10.5) — if it falls inside the solar reservation zone, a conflict flag fires. Route the hood exhaust to a non-solar wall face where possible.
App field: Range hood CFM (entered when range is placed as an Appliance record). Duct diameter auto-calculated from CFM. Makeup air flag fires if CFM exceeds 400 and building blower door target is below 3.0 ACH@50Pa.
Electrical rough-in heights — cabinet coordination
Kitchen outlet heights are driven by the countertop height, not the standard 18" above finished floor used in other rooms. Standard heights for a 36" counter (34.5" base cabinet + 1.5" countertop):
* Countertop small appliance outlets (NEC 2020 210.52 — two 20A circuits minimum): 18" above the finished countertop surface = 54" above finished floor at standard counter height. Spaced so no point on the countertop is more than 24" from an outlet.
* Under-cabinet strip lighting or outlets: 6" above the finished countertop surface = 42" above finished floor. Box mounts on the wall before upper cabinets are hung.
* Refrigerator dedicated circuit: stub at 72" above finished floor — above the refrigerator to avoid the outlet being hidden behind the unit. Dedicated 20A 120V circuit.
* Microwave dedicated circuit: if over-range, stub at 72" above finished floor near range hood location. If countertop, treat as standard countertop outlet.
* Dishwasher dedicated circuit: stub under the sink in the sink cabinet, 18" above finished floor, accessible for the supply cord connection.
These heights are set at electrical rough-in before drywall. If non-standard counter height is planned (accessible 34", bar height 42"), adjust all heights proportionally before rough-in begins.
App field: Kitchen counter height (default 36", editable). All electrical rough-in height calculations auto-adjust from this single field. If counter height changes after electrical rough-in is marked complete, a cascade flag fires.
GFCI requirements — kitchen
NEC 2020 requires GFCI protection on all receptacles within 6 ft of a kitchen sink. In practice this means all countertop outlets in a kitchen of normal size should be GFCI-protected. The simplest approach: use a GFCI breaker for each small appliance circuit rather than individual GFCI outlets — this protects the entire circuit with one device at the panel.
GFCI is not required on the refrigerator dedicated circuit (typically more than 6 ft from the sink and a GFCI nuisance-trip could cost a refrigerator full of food). For the dishwasher dedicated circuit, check with the Miami County building inspector — NEC 2020 has an exception but many inspectors recommend GFCI regardless.
Pre-pour items from Kitchen zone
The following kitchen items must appear on the pre-pour coordination document:
* Sink drain stub-up: drain centerline coordinates, pipe size (1.5" minimum for kitchen sink), slope toward wet wall stack confirmed at 1/4" per foot minimum
* Island sink drain stub-up (if island sink planned): separate stub-up at island location, slope to wet wall confirmed
* Gas stub-up at range location: if propane range, 1/2" black iron or CSST stub-up at range position — the below-slab gas sleeve is a pre-pour item
* Dishwasher drain routing (if exits through slab — uncommon but possible in some layouts)
* In-slab conduit if any kitchen circuits cross open slab
Pre-pour blocker: Kitchen Fixture Records (sink, dishwasher) and the range Appliance BTU Record must all be at Roughed-in status with confirmed floor plan coordinates before the pre-pour document can be generated for the Kitchen zone.
App actions when Kitchen Coordination module is complete
* Kitchen layout confirmed field set — gates Fixture Records past Roughed-in
* Sink Fixture Record: drain size locked, wet wall assignment confirmed, AAV or stack vent path selected
* Island sink Fixture Record (if applicable): drain stub-up on pre-pour drawing, AAV above-slab item added to plumbing rough-in list
* Range Appliance BTU Record: gas stub-up location confirmed, pipe size calculated from BTU rating and run length
* Range hood Equipment Record: CFM confirmed, duct diameter set, duct sleeve added to framing rough-in list, makeup air flag evaluated
* All kitchen small appliance circuits added to electrical circuit records at confirmed heights
* Dishwasher and refrigerator dedicated circuit stubs confirmed
* GFCI breaker recommendation added to panel schedule for small appliance circuits
* Kitchen module status: Complete


6.3 Item Specification System — Three-Tier Library
Every physical item in the project — every electrical device, fixture, appliance, HVAC unit, plumbing fitting, structural connector, and material — moves through three specification tiers. The tier is stored on the item record and drives what appears in the takeoff, what Claude prompts fire, and what budget status is possible. No item can skip a tier; each tier must be completed before advancing to the next.
Tier 1 — Generic Placeholder
The owner knows they need an item but has not selected a product. Enough information is captured to drive all rough-in, pre-pour, and cascade decisions. Rough-in dimensions use category defaults. Budget line item is at Concept status. Takeoff line shows the generic description.
Tier 1 fields required: item category (from the taxonomy below); installation zone; rough-in requirements derived from category defaults (e.g. toilet default: 12” rough-in, 3” drain, 1/2” cold supply); electrical requirements derived from category (e.g. dishwasher default: 20A 120V dedicated circuit, GFCI); Layer 3 dependency flag (fires immediately for any item that requires a pre-pour or in-wall rough-in before walls close). All pre-pour documents can be generated from Tier 1 items using category defaults. A Tier 1 item in the pre-pour document is shown with an amber “Generic spec — confirm before pour” notation.
Tier 2 — Category Specification
The owner has selected the category spec: key dimensions, performance requirements, material preferences, and finish choices. Rough-in dimensions are locked to the specified values (overriding category defaults). Budget line item moves to Estimated status. Claude Prompt 1 (decision support) fires with the category spec as context, returning product recommendations and regional pricing. Supplier call script can be generated.
Tier 2 fields by item category — see taxonomy below. All Tier 2 fields are optional individually but the combination must be sufficient to generate a specific supplier call script. The tool flags if the Tier 2 spec is too vague to generate a useful call: “Add [field] to generate a supplier call script for this item.”
Tier 3 — Specific Product
The owner has selected a specific product: manufacturer, model number, confirmed dimensions, confirmed price or quote. All rough-in dimensions locked to product spec. Budget line item moves to Quoted or Committed status. No further cascade changes from this item’s rough-in dimensions — changing to a different model at Tier 3 triggers the Stage 3 or Stage 4 change protocol.
Tier 3 fields required: manufacturer; model number; confirmed rough-in dimensions (from product spec sheet — must match or supersede Tier 2 spec); price (Quoted or Committed); supplier; lead time; product spec sheet uploaded or linked (optional but strongly recommended — the spec sheet URL or PDF is stored on the item record and accessible from the pre-pour document).
Claude Prompt — Product Selection (fires at Tier 2)
When an item reaches Tier 2 status, the tool assembles a Claude prompt containing: zone type, Tier 2 spec fields, owner budget target (from the budget line item), Miami County Kansas region, and any stated owner preferences (owner-brand, local supplier preference). The prompt asks Claude to return 2—3 specific product recommendations with: manufacturer and model number, why it fits the spec, current regional price range, regional availability (suppliers within 60 miles of Paola KS), and lead time. The response is parsed into the item record’s recommendation field and presented to the owner as a decision prompt. Selecting a recommendation pre-fills all Tier 3 fields. The owner can reject recommendations and enter a different product manually.
Item Category Taxonomy and Tier 2 Fields
The following categories are defined. Each category has a set of Tier 2 specification fields specific to that category. Fields marked (R) are required for call script generation; fields marked (O) are optional.
ELECTRICAL DEVICES AND FIXTURES — Outlets: (R) amperage (15A / 20A); (R) GFCI required (yes/no — auto-set from zone type); (R) AFCI required (yes/no — auto-set from zone type); (O) tamper-resistant (TR) required (yes/no — required in all new residential per NEC 2020 406.12); (O) style (decorator / standard / industrial); (O) finish (white / ivory / almond / black / stainless); (O) weatherproof cover required (in-use cover / flip-lid / none). Switches: (R) type (single-pole / 3-way / 4-way / dimmer / timer / occupancy sensor); (R) amperage (15A / 20A); (O) style (decorator / standard); (O) finish. Fixtures — hardwired: (R) fixture type (recessed can / surface mount / pendant / exterior sconce / under-cabinet strip / explosion-proof); (R) lamp type (LED / fluorescent / incandescent — LED strongly recommended for all new construction); (R) wattage or lumen output target; (O) trim style and finish; (O) dimmable (yes/no). Panels and disconnects: (R) amperage; (R) number of spaces; (R) brand (required for interlock kit compatibility); (O) main breaker included (yes/no); (O) indoor/outdoor enclosure.
PLUMBING FIXTURES — Toilets: (R) rough-in dimension (10” / 12” / 14” — 12” is standard); (R) bowl shape (elongated / round); (O) height (standard / comfort/ADA at 17—19”); (O) flush type (gravity / pressure-assist); (O) GPF rating (1.28 / 1.6 — 1.28 is WaterSense); (O) finish (white / bone / biscuit); (O) seat included (yes/no). Sinks: (R) installation type (undermount / drop-in / farmhouse / wall-mount); (R) basin count (single / double); (R) dimensions (width — length); (R) drain location (center / offset / two-drain); (O) material (stainless steel / cast iron / composite / porcelain); (O) gauge for stainless (16 ga / 18 ga — 16 ga is heavier/better); (O) garbage disposal knockout required. Showers: (R) shower drain type (center / offset / linear); (R) shower size (width — depth); (O) shower base type (prefab / tile-ready / tile). Faucets: (R) hole configuration (1-hole / 3-hole / wall-mount); (R) finish; (O) handle type (single / two-handle); (O) pull-down sprayer (yes/no). Water heaters: (R) type (tank / tankless / hybrid heat pump); (R) fuel (electric / propane); (R) capacity (gallons for tank; GPM for tankless); (R) first-hour rating; (O) energy factor / UEF.
HVAC EQUIPMENT — Mini-splits: (R) capacity (BTU/hr cooling); (R) single-zone / multi-zone; (R) SEER rating minimum; (O) HSPF rating minimum (heating efficiency — important for Kansas winters); (O) low-ambient heating rating (minimum outdoor temperature for heating operation — Kansas design: -10”F — specify equipment rated to at least -13”F for reliability); (O) hyper-heat specification (yes/no — Mitsubishi Hyper Heat or equivalent for reliable operation below 0”F); (O) line set length maximum; (O) wall-mount / ceiling cassette / floor-mount head type. Boilers: (R) fuel (propane / electric); (R) output BTU/hr; (R) type (boiler only / combi boiler — combi provides both heat and domestic hot water from one unit); (O) AFUE rating minimum; (O) modulating vs single-stage. Unit heaters: (R) fuel (propane); (R) output BTU/hr; (R) mounting (ceiling / wall); (O) thermostat included. ERV/HRV: (R) CFM rating; (R) type (ERV / HRV — ERV recovers moisture, preferred for Zone 4A); (O) core efficiency; (O) defrost capability (important for Kansas winters — required below 14”F outdoor temperature).
APPLIANCES — Ranges: (R) fuel (propane / electric / dual-fuel); (R) width (30” / 36” / 48”); (R) burner count; (O) convection oven (yes/no); (O) BTU/hr rating for propane (drives gas pipe sizing); (O) finish. Range hoods: (R) CFM rating; (R) duct diameter (6” / 8” / 10”); (R) width; (R) mount type (wall / island / under-cabinet); (O) recirculating vs external exhaust (external exhaust ONLY for tight buildings per Section 7.3). Refrigerators: (R) width; (R) configuration (French door / side-by-side / top-freezer / bottom-freezer); (O) ice maker (yes/no — drives 1/4” water line stub-out in wall); (O) finish. Dishwashers: (R) width (24” standard); (R) energy star (yes/no); (O) finish. Washers and dryers: (R) fuel for dryer (electric / propane); (R) stacked or side-by-side; (R) connection type (top-load / front-load).
STRUCTURAL HARDWARE — Hurricane ties: (R) rafter size (2x6 / 2x8 / 2x10); (R) top plate width; (O) manufacturer (Simpson / MiTek / USP); specific model selected by the tool from manufacturer tables based on rafter size, plate width, and wind zone. Holdowns: (R) wall type; (R) required uplift value (from engineer spec); specific model selected from manufacturer tables. Post bases: (R) post size; (R) installation (standoff / direct-bearing); (O) wet location rated.
Item Specification in the Takeoff Export
The takeoff CSV and JSON include the item’s specification tier on every line item. Tier 1 items in the export are flagged with an amber status in the planning_status column: “Concept — generic spec”. Tier 2 items: “Estimated — category spec”. Tier 3 items: “Quoted” or “Committed” with the model number in the spec_notes column. The structured specification columns (spec_material, spec_size, spec_grade) are populated from the Tier 2 fields and overridden with Tier 3 product spec at Tier 3. A supplier receiving a Tier 1 takeoff line knows the quantity is reliable but the product is still being selected. A supplier receiving a Tier 3 line knows the exact product and can confirm price and availability directly.
HOMESTEAD PLANNER
Master Context & Specification Document
Version 4.0  |  Miami County, Kansas Farm Project  |  March 2026
PURPOSE: Paste this entire document into any new Claude conversation to instantly restore full project context. Version 4.0 adds the three-phase cost and sourcing pipeline: decision-support estimates (Prompt 1), regional supplier sourcing for Miami County Kansas (Prompt 2), and quote comparison with anomaly detection (Prompt 3). Includes budget line item status model (Concept → Estimated → Quoted → Committed → Actual), call script generator for non-builder users, regional supplier knowledge base, quote expiry tracking, and contingency budgeting.
1. Project Summary
The Homestead Planner is a web-based planning, construction guidance, and project management tool built for Anthony (developer) and his parents planning a farm property build in Miami County, Kansas. The tool has expanded significantly beyond initial scope and now serves these purposes simultaneously:
* A structured decision tool that guides non-builders through the right decisions in the right order, with cascading logic — one decision automatically adjusts what questions need to be answered next
* A construction companion — provides trade-specific knowledge, materials takeoffs, build sequences, and owner-builder guidance for every assembly type in the project
* A layout coordination platform — a layered spatial plan where placing an item triggers calculations across all relevant trades
* A pre-pour coordination document generator — the single most critical output, assembling everything every trade needs in the slab before concrete is placed
* A Claude AI prompt companion — generates structured, context-aware prompts for Claude.ai and parses structured responses back into the app
* A living project bible that starts vague and gets more precise over time
* A document generator producing builder introduction brief, full planning document, specification sheet, pre-pour coordination package, and trade-specific packages
* A timeline tracker capturing builder meeting notes, feedback, and milestone dates with a visual Gantt-style view
* A dashboard export source — milestone dates and project data export as JSON compatible with Anthony and Ruth&#x2019;s existing Flask dashboard at E:\anthony_dashboard


2. The People & Property
2.1 Users & Roles
User
	Role
	Access & Notes
	Anthony
	Owner / Developer
	Full access — create, edit, delete, invite users, generate documents, manage all projects
	Ruth
	Head Consultant
	Full access — same as Anthony
	Parents
	Primary users
	Full edit access to their property and projects. Non-technical — interface must be simple and jargon-free. All trade terms are tooltip-enabled (see Section 2.1 Universal Glossary Tooltip System below).
	Brothers
	Collaborators
	View and comment access, possibly edit. Exact scope TBD
	Builder
	Read-only guest
	Controlled read-only access to specific projects. Never sees budget ceiling, line-item costs, private notes, or internal estimate data. Receives generated PDF leave-behind only. See Builder View Filter Rules in Section 2.1 below.
	Architect
	Read-only guest
	Phase 2 feature — can view specific projects, possibly add comments
	

2.1a Universal Glossary Tooltip System
Every trade term, code reference, and construction concept used anywhere in the tool is tooltip-enabled. The same tooltip system applies to all users identically — there is no jargon tier for technical vs. non-technical users. Any user who does not know a term can hover over it (desktop) or tap it (mobile) to see a plain-language definition in a popup. This eliminates confusion at the source rather than trying to predict which users need help with which terms.
* Flagged terms: every trade term is underlined with a subtle dotted underline to signal it is tooltip-enabled. Examples: frost depth, AFCI, invert elevation, SHGC, PEX-A, CSST, vapor retarder, LVL, holdown, manifold, combi boiler, IECC Zone 4A, perc test, AAV.
* Tooltip content: plain-language definition (1–3 sentences), why it matters for this project, and a &#x201C;Learn more&#x201D; link to the relevant section of the document where it is explained in full context.
* Scope: tooltips apply in all views — layout plan labels, pre-pour document, document generator outputs, code compliance panel, budget line items, build sequence steps, and construction knowledge packages.
* Implementation: a central glossary JSON object maps term strings to definitions. Rendered terms are wrapped in a span with a data-term attribute. A single JavaScript tooltip handler covers the entire app. Adding a new term requires only one entry in the glossary object.
* Phase 1 deliverable: seed the glossary with all terms that appear in generated documents and the pre-pour coordination package. Expand to full app coverage in Phase 2.


2.1b Builder View Filter Rules
The builder read-only view and the generated builder PDF leave-behind apply these filters. Every field in the data model is classified as either Builder-visible or Owner-only. The filter runs automatically — there is no manual redaction step.
* Builder-visible (always shown): building dimensions, zone layout, surface construction types, R-values, foundation spec, all pre-pour coordination data, rough-in locations, window and door schedules, code compliance items, build sequence, material specifications, HVAC strategy, site plan (without budget data), timeline milestones.
* Owner-only (always hidden from builder): all budget data (Concept / Estimated / Quoted / Committed / Actual amounts), budget ceiling, contingency amount, any field tagged as Private Note, supplier quotes and call scripts, Claude prompt outputs, estimate vs. actual variance data, internal planning notes, open questions list.
* Private Note field: every record type (zone, surface, building, item, timeline entry) has a Private Note field. Content entered here is visible only to Owner and Head Consultant roles. It is never included in any generated document, builder view, or exported JSON. Shown with a lock icon in the owner view so the distinction is always visible.
* Ruth&#x2019;s workflow role: Ruth (Head Consultant) has identical access to Anthony. This means Ruth can review and approve the pre-pour document before it goes to trades, generate all document types, add private notes, and view all budget data. The dashboard export at E:\anthony_dashboard is shared between Anthony and Ruth. When decisions are locked, either Anthony or Ruth can lock them.


2.2 The Property

	Location
	Miami County, Kansas — south of Kansas City metro
	Project type

	Applicable codes

Tracked property-level field: Site Drainage Condition (Elevated / Flat / Low-lying / Unknown). Low-lying sites and sites with water table within 6 ft of slab elevation require perimeter drain tile with sump pit — this decision must be made before the pre-pour document is finalized. Generator rough-in is the foundation for the Backup Power Planning Module (Section 7.6) — existing infrastructure is entered as a property-level input field so the module can build on what is already in place rather than starting from scratch.
	

3. Data Model — Five Levels
The data model has four levels. Every decision is scoped to its correct level, and changes cascade up and down appropriately.


3.1 Level 1 — Property
A physical location. Holds shared site data: lot size, county, frost depth, utility locations, setback rules, soil notes, site drainage condition (Elevated / Flat / Low-lying / Unknown — drives subsurface drainage recommendation for each building), electrical topology decision, propane tank sizing, water tap strategy, septic long-term plan, site plan coordinates, electric service trench distance from road, existing infrastructure inventory (existing buildings, panels, rough-ins, generator connections), backup power strategy, renewable energy intent, and site orientation (true north arrow on the site plan — captured once, inherited by every building). Site orientation is the root of all solar calculations: south-facing roof faces, solar array zones, overhang sizing, HVAC solar gain, and window SHGC multipliers all derive from it. Every building placed on the property inherits the site orientation and uses it to determine which wall face is south, which roof plane is optimal for solar, and which windows carry the largest summer cooling load.


3.1b Level 1.5 — ScenarioA Scenario is a named project configuration that sits between the Property and its Buildings. It represents one complete answer to the question: "What should we build?" A Scenario can contain one building or several. Scenarios on the same Property share the site — the same land, the same septic field, the same propane tank, the same site drainage plan — but each Scenario has its own independent set of Buildings, systems, and budget.SCENARIO RECORD FIELDS:Name: owner-assigned label (e.g. "Option A — Barn + Workshop", "Option B — Barn Only"). Status: Draft (being developed, not yet ready to present) / Active (fully developed, candidate for build) / Presented to Builder (has been included in a Builder Brief or Options Brief handed to a builder) / Archived (not selected, retained for reference, read-only). Created date and last modified date. Scenario notes: a free-text field for owner assumptions specific to this option (e.g. "assumes owner-build framing", "requires hiring out electrical"). This is owner-only and never appears in any generated document.DEFAULT SCENARIO: when a user first creates a Property, the tool automatically creates a single Scenario called "Option A." If the user never adds a second Scenario, the Scenario layer is invisible — the tool behaves exactly as if Scenarios do not exist and the UI shows no Scenario chrome. The Scenario layer only surfaces in the UI when a second Scenario is created. This ensures the single-project case is not complicated by machinery the user does not need.WHAT SCENARIOS SHARE (Property level — unchanged by adding Scenarios):Site plan canvas base layer: lot boundary, true north arrow, site orientation. Site-level infrastructure: septic tank and drain field location, propane tank location, electric service entry point, site drainage plan, sump discharge outlets. Property data: soil notes, site drainage condition, frost depth, climate zone, applicable codes, permit authority. Placeholder Buildings: future structures that exist only for setback and utility routing purposes. Property-level budget items: costs that apply regardless of which Scenario is built (electric service trench from road, septic system, propane tank installation, site clearing and grading). These appear in a "Property Costs" section in the budget that is separate from any Scenario total.WHAT SCENARIOS OWN INDEPENDENTLY (Scenario level):All Buildings and their full hierarchy (Zones, Surfaces, Items). Budget line items for all Buildings in this Scenario. Electrical topology decision (main + sub-panel vs independent meters). Inter-building conduit plan (only exists when Scenario has more than one building). Propane load rollup and tank sizing recommendation. Septic fixture count and drain field sizing recommendation. Build sequence (Section 17) — applicable only after Scenario is promoted to Active Build. Claude Prompt outputs (Decision Support, Regional Sourcing, Quote Comparison). Builder Brief / Options Brief generated document.SCENARIO PROMOTION — SELECTING THE BUILD:When the owner decides which option to build, they promote one Scenario to Active Build status. Promotion actions: the promoted Scenario status changes to Active Build. All other Scenarios automatically move to Archived. Archived Scenarios are retained permanently in read-only mode — never deleted. The build sequence (Section 17) becomes executable. The budget for the promoted Scenario becomes the live project budget. A previously archived Scenario can be reinstated to Active status if the owner wants to revisit it — but only one Scenario can be Active Build at a time. Promotion requires an explicit confirmation dialog: "Promote [Scenario Name] to Active Build? All other Scenarios will be archived. This can be reversed." This prevents accidental promotion.3.2 Level 2 — Building
One physical structure. Belongs to exactly one Scenario (Section 3.1b). A Building cannot exist at the Property level without a Scenario parent — every Building is owned by a Scenario. Has a relationship type (Standalone / Lean-to / Addition / Existing), an enclosure type, a foundation type, an electrical feed source, a permit path, footprint dimensions (width × length in feet — entered at building creation, used for foundation cost, HVAC load starting point, and materials takeoff before the layout plan is complete), gross floor area (calculated from footprint — displayed read-only, drives budget Concept estimates), solar intent (No Solar / Grid-Tied / Battery Hybrid / Undecided — captured at building creation because it immediately cascades to roof orientation recommendation, conduit stub decision, and panel sizing), and subsurface drainage intent (None / Exterior drain tile only / Exterior drain tile + sump pit / Interior drain tile + sump pit — captured at building creation, cascades to pre-pour layer, plumbing layer, electrical circuit list, and budget line items). Sump pit location is a required field when subsurface drainage ≠ None — the pit is poured into the slab and must appear on the pre-pour drawing. Buildings inherit property-level site data (site orientation, site drainage condition, frost depth, climate zone) through their Scenario parent but can override inherited values at the Building level.
3.1a Placeholder Buildings — Future or Planned Structures
A Placeholder Building is a planned-but-not-yet-designed structure represented at the property level for site planning purposes only. It is not a full Building record — it has no zones, no surfaces, and no systems. It exists to protect space on the site plan so that underground utilities, septic drain fields, setback calculations, and propane tank placement account for its future presence.
Placeholder Building fields: approximate footprint (width × length — rough estimate only), approximate location on site plan (drag-to-place rectangle), label (e.g. &#x201C;Future House&#x201D;), and setback flag (tool enforces required clearances from septic field, propane tank, and inter-building conduit paths relative to this placeholder). Section 17 Step 1 requires the future house envelope to be identified before any underground utility or drain field placement — this is the data model object that satisfies that requirement. Section 7.4 recommends running a capped propane supply line stub toward the future house site during initial site work — the placeholder location determines where that stub terminates.
A Placeholder Building can be promoted to a full Building record at any time — at which point it inherits property-level data and the user begins adding zones and surfaces in the normal flow. Until promoted, it participates only in site-level calculations: setbacks, utility routing, and propane tank placement.


3.3 Level 3 — Zone / Section
A thermally or functionally distinct area within a building. Each zone has: a name, a zone type (enumerated — see taxonomy below), a heated/unheated flag, an enclosure type, a ceiling height, a floor assembly type, an assigned HVAC strategy, and floor area (width × length in feet — entered when the zone is created or derived from the layout plan once walls are placed). Floor area is required by the HVAC load calculator (Section 7.3), the radiant loop layout (Section 6.2 — loop count and lengths calculated from zone floor area), and the materials takeoff for under-slab XPS foam and vapor barrier. The heated flag is the single most consequential zone decision — it determines frost depth rules, slab assembly, insulation requirements, and which systems apply.ZONE TYPE TAXONOMY: the zone type field replaces the free-text "primary use" field. It is an enumerated dropdown. Selecting a zone type activates the correct knowledge package, cascade rules, pre-pour requirements, and trade package entries for that zone automatically. Zone type is the second decision surfaced after heated/unheated. The following zone types are defined:EQUIPMENT STORAGE / HAY STORAGE: unheated by default. Metal purlin construction typical. No plumbing. Weatherproof electrical only. Agricultural permit path likely. No HVAC calculation. Condensation watch at eave girt (Section 6.2 metal purlin).HEATED WORKSHOP: heated. Propane unit heater or mini-split depending on use pattern. GFCI throughout. Compressor circuit (240V/30A dedicated). Tool and equipment circuits. Floor drain recommended. May share building with unheated zones — dividing wall becomes thermal boundary (Section 3.4).LIVING QUARTERS: heated. Full IRC residential code requirements apply including: egress window required in every sleeping room (IRC R310 — min 5.7 sq ft net clear opening, 24" clear height, 20" clear width, max 44" sill height), smoke detector in every sleeping room and on every level, CO detector on every level, AFCI protection on all bedroom and living area circuits (NEC 2020 210.12), bathroom exhaust fan required in windowless bathrooms (IMC 2018 403), ERV/HRV mechanical ventilation required if building tightness is below 5 ACH@50Pa. Permit path: IRC residential, not agricultural. This is the zone type that triggers the full residential code path for the building it belongs to.KITCHEN: heated. Activates: range hood exhaust sleeve required (exterior exhaust only — no recirculating hoods in a tight building), two 20A small appliance circuits at countertop locations (NEC 2020 210.52), refrigerator dedicated circuit, microwave dedicated circuit, dishwasher dedicated 20A circuit + air gap fitting above sink drain, ice maker water line stub at refrigerator location if ice maker is planned, GFCI within 6 ft of all sink locations. Kitchen zone type may appear inside a Living Quarters zone (open plan) or as a standalone zone — the same electrical and plumbing requirements apply either way.BATHROOM: heated. Activates: toilet flange rough-in (12" from finished wall, set to finished floor height), shower drain with trap, sink drain stub-up, wet wall (2x6 minimum framing), vent stack or AAV, PEX supply sleeves, exhaust fan duct sleeve through wall or roof. Septic fixture count increments per fixture.MECHANICAL ROOM: heated. Houses panel, water heater, boiler, manifold, pressure reducing valve. Floor drain required. Minimum 3 ft clearance on all service sides of equipment. Location drives manifold position for radiant loop layout — mechanical room placement is a floor plan decision that locks before PEX layout.COLD STORAGE / ROOT CELLAR: below-grade room. Not a slab zone — a partial basement poured as part of the foundation before the main slab. Key fields activated: pit depth (minimum 8 ft below finished grade for Kansas ground temperature stabilization at ~55°F), hatch location (structural opening in barn slab above — pre-pour blocker, must appear on foundation layer before pre-pour document generates), hatch edge beam (reinforced opening perimeter). INVERTED INSULATION LOGIC: insulate the ceiling (barn slab above) to prevent barn heat from warming the storage space. Do NOT insulate the floor or walls — bare concrete against earth is the thermal mass and cooling source. This is the opposite of every other zone in the spec. The tool flags this explicitly when Cold Storage zone type is selected. VAPOR BARRIER EXCEPTION: no vapor barrier on cold storage floor — controlled moisture from the earth is intentional. Vapor barrier applies only to the main barn slab above. No HVAC. No heating. Temperature target: 32–50°F year-round from earth thermal mass. Access: hatch from barn floor above or exterior bulkhead — location is a pre-pour blocker. Lighting: GFCI protected. No plumbing required unless a floor drain is desired for cleaning (connects to main drain system, not septic).VEHICLE SERVICE PIT: below-grade room integrated into the slab. Structurally more complex than a cold storage room — vehicles drive over it, so the pit opening requires a reinforced edge beam cantilevering the slab. Key fields: pit length, pit width, pit depth (typically 4–5 ft for service access), grating/cover type (removable steel grating or hinged steel cover — specified at design time, opening sized accordingly). PRE-POUR BLOCKERS: pit structural opening with edge beam rebar detail must appear on foundation layer before pre-pour document generates. Oil/water separator rough-in must be on pre-pour drawing (petroleum-contaminated floor drain water cannot discharge to septic — separator is mandatory). Ventilation sleeve through pit wall must be on pre-pour drawing. CONFINED SPACE: a vehicle pit with a running engine is a confined space under OSHA definitions. CO accumulates below grade. Mechanical ventilation is required by IMC 2018: minimum 1.5 CFM per square foot of pit floor area. Ventilation duct sleeve goes through pit wall before concrete. ELECTRICAL: GFCI on every outlet in and around the pit. Weatherproof boxes. NEC 2020 Section 511 (automotive service areas) applies. DRAINAGE: floor drain at pit bottom with oil/water separator before connecting to main drain system. Pit drain cannot connect directly to septic. Sump in pit bottom if water table is a concern (clay soil — it is). STRUCTURAL: pit walls are cast-in-place concrete or CMU, poured before main slab. Rebar continuity between pit walls and main slab is an engineered detail. Pit is a Tier 1 pre-pour blocker — geometry must be decided before foundation is designed.OPEN FACE / EQUIPMENT BAY: unheated, no enclosure on one or more sides. No HVAC. Weatherproof electrical only. No plumbing. Agricultural permit path. Concrete apron typical. Enclosure type = open face in the zone record.GRAIN STORAGE: unheated. Fire separation from living zones and any ignition sources (IFC grain storage requirements). No ignition sources permitted — no propane appliances, no standard electrical fixtures (explosion-proof rated only in active grain handling areas). Separate from heated zones by fire-rated wall assembly if in the same building. Tool flags any ignition source placed in a Grain Storage zone.BASEMENT: below-grade habitable space beneath the main building slab or floor deck. Structurally distinct from a slab-on-grade building — the floor above is a structural deck (wood I-joist, LVL, or concrete), not a slab. Key fields activated: basement floor area, basement wall height, wall construction type (poured concrete / CMU / ICF — ICF is strongly recommended for a basement on Miami County clay soil: continuous insulation eliminates thermal bridging, mass dampens temperature swings, and the wall is inherently more water-resistant than bare poured concrete), waterproofing strategy (damp-proof only / membrane waterproofing with drainage board — membrane is required for any habitable basement on this property, damp-proofing alone is insufficient for Miami County clay soil with seasonal water table variation). STRUCTURAL FLOOR DECK: any zone above a basement uses a structural floor deck, not a slab. Deck type: wood I-joist (most common, fastest owner-build), engineered lumber (LVL or TJI), or poured concrete deck. Deck sizing is engineer-required: live load 40 psf for habitable space, 10 psf for storage. Bearing points at basement walls and any intermediate beam locations must be on the basement wall plan. WATERPROOFING AND DRAINAGE: exterior membrane waterproofing applied to the outside face of basement walls before backfill. Drainage board over membrane to protect it during backfill and direct water to footing drain. Footing drain tile connects to sump pit (Section 7.2 sump pump spec applies with increased priority — a basement sump is not optional on this site). Interior drainage channel at wall-floor joint as secondary measure if exterior waterproofing is breached. EGRESS: every habitable basement room requires at least one egress window (IRC R310): minimum 5.7 sq ft net clear opening, 24" clear height, 20" clear width, maximum 44" sill height from basement finished floor. Window well required (below-grade steel or concrete surround that brings grade away from window, minimum 9 sq ft clear area, drainage at bottom). Window well and egress window location are pre-pour blockers — must appear on foundation layer before foundation is formed. TORNADO SHELTER potential: a full poured concrete basement with ICF walls approaches FEMA 320 storm shelter performance. If tornado shelter use is intended, specify reinforced concrete lid (deck above) and inward-opening steel door — outward-opening doors fail in tornado winds. Shelter intent is a design-time decision, not a retrofit. MECHANICAL: basement is the natural home for all mechanical equipment (panel, water heater, boiler, manifold, ERV, pressure reducing valve) — locate Mechanical Room zone type in basement to keep equipment out of main living space. Sump pit locates in basement mechanical room. PRE-POUR BLOCKERS: egress window well location, waterproofing strategy (drives drainage board and footing drain placement), structural deck bearing point locations at basement wall top. All must appear on foundation layer before pre-pour document generates.NOTE: a single building can contain multiple zone types. A barn might contain Equipment Storage + Heated Workshop + Living Quarters + Cold Storage + Vehicle Pit all under one roof. The zone type taxonomy ensures every zone gets the right requirements regardless of what its neighbors are doing. The heated/unheated flag still applies to every zone independently — zone type refines it, it does not replace it.


3.4 Level 4 — Surface
A first-class object. Each wall face, each roof plane, and the floor slab of each zone is its own surface record. This is the most important addition in v2.0.


Surface Record Fields
Field
	Options / Notes
	Owner zone(s)
	Single zone = exterior surface. Two zones = shared/dividing wall between them
	Orientation
	N / S / E / W / Roof-pitch-direction / Floor — or a compass bearing (0–359°, true north = 0°, south = 180°). Cardinal shortcuts snap to the nearest 90° increment and are sufficient for walls. Roof planes and any surface where solar calculations apply should use an actual bearing. Bearing is measured as the direction the surface faces outward — a south-facing roof plane that slopes toward true south has a bearing of 180°. Off-axis bearings drive azimuth derate factors in the solar production estimate (Section 7.5) and correction factors in the overhang depth formula (Section 10.3). Measured in the field using iPhone Compass app — see Section 7.5 for measurement procedure.
	Construction type
	2x4 stud / 2x6 stud / SIP panel / ICF / Metal purlin / CMU / Wood frame / Existing-unknown
	Framing dimension
	2x4 / 2x6 / 2x8 / N-A (SIP and ICF lock this)
	Insulation method
	Batt / Spray foam / Rigid board / Combo / Locked (SIP/ICF determine R automatically)
	R-value
	Calculated or locked. IECC Zone 4A minimums: walls R-20, ceiling R-49, floor R-10
	Interior finish
	Drywall / OSB / T&G / Exposed / None
	Exterior finish
	LP siding (SmartSide or equivalent) / Fiber cement (Hardie or equivalent) / Metal panel / Brick veneer / Stucco (3-coat) / EIFS / Board and batten / Lap siding / Vinyl siding / T1-11 plywood / Exposed / None. Selecting an exterior finish activates the Wall Assembly System (Section 5.7) which auto-populates all required layers between the structural sheathing and the exterior finish: WRB type, drainage plane method, lath or furring (if required), and all associated takeoff lines. Incompatible combinations of exterior finish and construction type are blocked before selection with a plain-language explanation and a corrected alternative — see Section 5.7.1 Incompatibility Matrix. NEW FIELDS added to Surface Record for wall assembly: (1) WRB type (house wrap / building paper / self-adhered membrane / taped sheathing — Zip System / none — ICF and SIP); auto-recommended from construction type and finish, owner-overridable with reason note. (2) Vapor retarder class (Class I — poly/foil, impermeable / Class II — kraft-faced batts, semi-impermeable / Class III — latex paint, permeable / none required — mass walls such as ICF); auto-assigned from construction type and IECC Zone 4A rules — see Section 5.7 for assignment logic; incompatible vapor retarder selections flagged per Section 5.7.1. (3) Continuous insulation (ci) thickness (none / 1 in / 1.5 in / 2 in / 3 in / other); when ci > 0, HVAC load recalculates for updated whole-wall R-value and fastener length for cladding attachment updates in the framing takeoff. (4) Drainage plane method (integrated in WRB / rainscreen gap with furring strips / drainage mat product / none — masonry and ICF); required when ci foam is present behind siding, or when stucco is applied over OSB or SIP facing. (5) Air barrier method (taped sheathing / house wrap taped at all seams and penetrations / self-adhered membrane / spray foam at all penetrations / none); drives the blower door target confidence flag in the HVAC module — continuous air barrier is required to achieve below 3.0 ACH@50Pa.
	Roofing material
	Roof plane surfaces only. Standing seam metal / Exposed fastener metal panel / Asphalt shingles / Metal shingles / Undecided. Standing seam recommended when solar is possible — clamp attachment requires no roof penetrations. Drives pitch minimum, supplier sourcing, and solar attachment method. See Section 9.5 for pitch requirements by roofing type.
	Electrical routing
	Derived from construction type — overridable. SIP → conduit or service chase. Stud → Romex
	Openings
	List of windows and doors — each with size, type, U-factor, SHGC, overhang depth, orientation
	Thermal boundary
	Is this surface between a conditioned and unconditioned space? Drives insulation requirement
	Construction method
	Owner-build / Contractor / Hybrid-owner-prep / Undecided
	Planning status
	Concept — decision not yet made, no dimensions locked / Roughed-in — dimensions estimated, construction type selected, but not final / Confirmed — locked. This is the field the cascade engine watches. When a surface moves to Confirmed: (1) construction type is locked and drives electrical routing method, insulation R-value, and framing package in Section 9.1; (2) roofing material is locked and updates pitch minimum constraint and supplier lookup; (3) materials takeoff becomes calculable and budget line item moves from Concept to Estimated in Section 13.2; (4) Claude Prompt 2 (regional sourcing) becomes available for this surface. A zone is Confirmed only when all its surfaces are Confirmed. The property workspace summary view shows planning status for all surfaces and zones simultaneously. Locking a surface cannot be undone without a manual override — the tool warns before allowing status to revert.
	

KEY INSIGHT: A shared wall between Workshop (heated) and Equipment Bay (unheated) belongs to both zones. When the workshop side requires insulation because it is a thermal boundary, both zones' HVAC calculations update. The tool tracks which side is conditioned and which is not.


4. Building Layers Model
Every building is understood as a stack of layers. Decisions in lower layers constrain everything above. The tool surfaces layer dependencies as decisions are made.
Layer
	Name
	Contents & Notes
	1
	Shell
	Footprint, exterior walls, roof, exterior doors and windows. The physical envelope.
	2
	Structural
	Beams, posts, load-bearing walls, headers over openings, foundation type and depth.
	3
	Rough Systems
	Plumbing rough-in, electrical rough-in, HVAC rough-in, fireplace/chimney, radiant tubing in slab. All happen before walls close. Most expensive layer to fix after the fact. SIP EXCEPTION: for SIP construction, Layer 3 decisions must be made before Layer 1 — panels are manufactured with penetrations, electrical chase routing, outlet locations, and HVAC lineset sleeves already specified. The normal bottom-up layer sequence inverts for SIP: decide Layer 3 first, then order Layer 1 panels. See Section 5.2 SIP-Specific Pre-Pour Constraints for the full list of what must be resolved before panel manufacture.
	4
	Insulation & Vapor
	Under-slab XPS foam (R-10 min, R-15 for radiant), vapor barrier (6 mil poly), wall insulation (R-20 min Zone 4A), attic insulation (R-49 min), perimeter foundation insulation to frost depth. CATHEDRAL CEILING RULE: for any cathedral ceiling or SIP roof assembly, rigid foam above the roof deck must be at least 50% of the total assembly R-value — this is a code and physics requirement, not a preference. If the rigid foam ratio falls below 50%, the dew point lands inside the rafter cavity and condensation forms on the interior face of the sheathing. This causes rot, mold, and structural damage that is invisible until severe. Example: R-49 total assembly requires minimum R-25 rigid foam above deck. The remaining R-24 can be spray foam between rafters from interior. Verify the ratio at Layer 4 design before ordering roofing materials.
	5
	Finish Systems
	Fixtures, electrical panel trim, outlets, switches, HVAC registers, fireplace surround.
	6
	Appliances & Furnishings
	Everything owned and brought in. Library items. Placed last, most flexible layer for physical placement. LOAD PROFILE EXCEPTION: Layer 6 items with electrical loads, propane loads, or combustion air requirements must have their load profile declared at Layer 3 time — even if their exact location is not yet known. A welder (50A 240V dedicated circuit), EV charger (50A 240V), wood stove or propane fireplace (combustion air duct through wall), or large HVAC unit (dedicated circuit + lineset sleeve) all require Layer 3 rough-in work that cannot be added after walls close. The item library in the tool flags any appliance with a Layer 3 dependency and surfaces it in the pre-pour and rough-in checklists as soon as it is added — regardless of whether its final placement location is confirmed.
	

4.1 Layer Readiness — Decision Gates Between Layers
Each layer has a readiness gate — a set of decisions in the layer below that must be locked before the layer above can be finalized. The cascade engine (Section 9) tracks these gates and surfaces them as open blockers. A layer is not ready to build until its gate is clear.
Layer 1 (Shell) gate: footprint dimensions, zone layout, and roof shape confirmed. For SIP: all Layer 3 penetration decisions also confirmed before panel order. Layer 2 (Structural) gate: Layer 1 shell confirmed, post and beam locations derived from roof structure decision, ridge beam posts located in floor plan, holdown locations at shear wall corners identified. Layer 3 (Rough Systems) gate: Layer 2 structural confirmed, wet wall location locked (plumbing), SIP electrical routing method decided (chase vs conduit), HVAC lineset sleeve locations known, all Layer 6 load-bearing appliances declared. Layer 4 (Insulation & Vapor) gate: Layer 3 rough-in inspected and passed, cathedral ceiling rigid foam ratio verified at 50%+ of total R before ordering. Layer 5 (Finish Systems) gate: Layer 4 complete, rough systems inspection passed. Layer 6 (Appliances) gate: Layer 5 complete, utilities energized.
4.2 Pre-Layer Infrastructure — Property-Level Items Outside the Stack
Some infrastructure exists at the property level and does not belong to any building&#x2019;s layer stack. It must be installed during site work — before any building&#x2019;s Layer 1 begins — because the trench is open and equipment is on site. Installing it later requires reopening the trench at significant cost. These items are tracked in the property workspace, not in any building&#x2019;s layer view.
Pre-layer property infrastructure: inter-building electrical conduit (Schedule 40 PVC, install more than currently needed, cap and mark ends — Section 7.1), water service branch lines to future building locations, propane supply line stub toward future house site (capped — Section 7.4), septic line rough placement. All of these are triggered by the site plan (Section 17 Step 1) and installed at Section 17 Step 5. The tool surfaces them as a pre-layer checklist in the property workspace whenever a Placeholder Building (Section 3.1a) or a new Building record exists that has not yet started Layer 1.
4.3 Site Due Diligence Module
Site due diligence is the work that must be done before any building is placed on the site plan, before any permit is applied for, and before money is spent on design. It confirms that the land can support the intended project — legally, physically, and practically. Skipping or deferring site due diligence is one of the most expensive mistakes an owner-builder can make. A building placed in the wrong location relative to an easement, wetland boundary, or setback line must be moved or removed. A foundation designed for normal soil conditions that turns out to be on fill or organic soil requires expensive remediation.
The Site Due Diligence Module is a pre-design checklist. It should be completed before any zones, buildings, or systems are specified. Building placement on the site plan is available from the start (the owner can sketch ideas), but the site plan layer shows amber advisory flags on any building whose placement has not been confirmed against completed due diligence categories.


Module status tracking: each category has a status field (Not started / In progress / Complete / Not applicable). The property workspace shows a due diligence completion summary. Build Sequence Step 1 (Site plan finalized) is blocked from Complete status until the minimum required categories are confirmed.
Category 1 — Boundary Survey
A boundary survey establishes the exact legal boundaries of the property. Without confirmed property lines, setback calculations are guesses and building placement is unconfirmed.
Survey types:
* Boundary/property line survey: locates property corners and establishes the legal boundary. The minimum requirement for any building project. Produces a plat showing the property boundary, any recorded easements, and corner monuments.
* ALTA/NSPS land title survey: includes all improvements, easements, rights-of-way, encroachments, and title matters. Required by some lenders. Typically not needed for a self-funded rural owner-builder project unless the property has complex title history.
* Topographic survey: establishes ground elevation at a grid of points, producing contour lines. Strongly recommended when terrain is not flat or drainage concerns exist — the drainage design requirements in Sections 5.4 and 8.2 are much easier to address with a topo survey in hand.
Who does it: a licensed land surveyor. Check the county register of deeds for a recorded plat first — a recorded plat may reduce the scope of new survey work needed.
App fields and cascade: property boundary coordinates entered on site plan layer as the outer canvas boundary. Easement locations entered as exclusion zones — structures placed within an easement generate a Tier 1 red conflict flag. Topographic contours entered into the site drainage layer if topo survey completed — drives grade slope arrows and French drain recommendations (Section 5.4). Survey date, surveyor name, and survey PDF stored on the property record.
Budget line item: auto-created at Concept status at project creation. Typical range: boundary survey $800–$2,500; topographic survey adds $500–$2,000 depending on acreage.
Category 2 — Zoning Confirmation and Use Verification
Zoning determines what can be built and where. Even rural agricultural properties have zoning classifications that may restrict building type, size, setbacks, or use.
What to confirm with the county zoning office:
* Current zoning classification and permitted uses (confirm all intended buildings are permitted)
* Minimum setbacks from property lines, roads, and other structures
* Maximum structure height and minimum lot size for intended use
* Whether a variance or conditional use permit is required for any intended use
* Agricultural exemption status — many rural Kansas counties exempt agricultural buildings from standard permit requirements; confirm what qualifies
Flood zone determination: check the property’s flood zone designation at FEMA’s Map Service Center (msc.fema.gov). A property in a Special Flood Hazard Area (Zone A or AE) has restrictions on building placement and finished floor elevation requirements. In Kansas, many rural properties along creek drainages are in or near flood zones that are not obvious from casual site inspection.
App fields and cascade: zoning classification, permitted use confirmed, setback distances (auto-populate all setback boundary calculations on the site plan layer), agricultural exemption status (affects permit path per Section 12.3), flood zone designation. If SFHA: Tier 2 amber flag fires when any building is placed below base flood elevation. If variance required: Tier 1 red flag fires on the affected building.
Category 3 — Perc Test and Septic Feasibility
The perc test measures how quickly water drains through the soil and determines whether a conventional septic drain field is feasible. Without a passing perc test, no septic permit can be issued. Without a septic permit, no residential building permit can be issued for a structure with plumbing.
Perc test results and system implications:
* MPI 0–30 (minutes per inch): fast drainage. Conventional gravity-fed drain field. Standard system.
* MPI 31–60: moderate drainage. Conventional system with slightly larger drain field area.
* MPI 61–120: slow drainage. May require a pressure-dosed system or larger drain field.
* MPI 121+: very slow drainage. Alternative system required — mound system, aerobic treatment unit, or engineered alternative. Significantly more expensive.
* Test failure (no percolation): conventional septic not feasible. Evaluate municipal sewer connection or holding tank.
App fields and cascade: MPI value recorded. Drain field sizing calculation updates automatically (Section 5.3). If MPI > 120: Tier 1 amber flag fires advising alternative system evaluation. If test fails: Tier 1 red flag fires blocking septic permit path. Budget septic line item updates from generic Concept estimate to a range appropriate for the MPI result. Tester name, credentials, test location on site plan, and KDHE permit application status recorded.
Category 4 — Soil Bearing Capacity Test
The soil bearing test evaluates the load-carrying capacity of the soil for foundations — separate from the perc test, which evaluates drainage for septic. Strongly recommended for any building on expansive clay soil. Miami County clay has variable bearing capacity (Section 8.2, Section 19 open questions).
What it involves: a geotechnical firm performs 1–3 borings at proposed building locations to a depth below the planned footing depth. Borings recover soil samples tested for bearing capacity (psf), soil classification, and moisture content. The geotechnical report provides a recommended bearing capacity and may recommend fill removal, compaction, or alternative foundation systems.
App fields and cascade (Section 8.1a and Section 9.1 cascade):
* Bearing capacity ≥ 1,500 psf: standard spread footings proceed normally
* Bearing capacity 1,000–1,500 psf: footing sizing defaults apply with engineer confirmation flag
* Bearing capacity < 1,000 psf: screw piling evaluation flag fires (Section 8.1a Footing Type 6), footing takeoff lines replaced by pile contractor lines
Geotechnical firm name, bearing capacity result (psf), soil classification, and geotechnical report PDF stored on the property record. Budget line item: Concept status at project creation. Typical range: $800–$2,000 for 1–3 borings on a small rural site.
Category 5 — Utility Easements and Right-of-Way Confirmation
Utility easements are legal rights granted to utility companies to access a portion of the property for their infrastructure. Buildings cannot be placed within easement boundaries. Underground utilities may run through the property in locations not obvious from surface inspection.
What to research:
* Call 811 (national one-call system) before any digging — required by law, free. Identifies all buried utilities registered with the one-call system.
* County register of deeds: recorded easements appear on the deed or as separate recorded documents. The boundary survey should identify easements of record.
* Rural electric cooperative: location of overhead and underground power lines and easement corridor width (typically 20–50 ft for overhead lines).
* Rural water district: location of the district water main, service laterals, and easement width.
* State and county road right-of-way: in Kansas, county road right-of-way is typically 60–66 ft wide (30–33 ft from road centerline on each side). Setback requirements are measured from the right-of-way boundary, not from the road edge.
App fields and cascade: each easement entered as a polygon or line element on the site plan layer with type, width, and grantor. Buildings placed within any easement generate a Tier 1 red conflict flag. Underground utility locations entered on the site utilities layer (Section 7.7) to affect trench routing. 811 call completed field is a required field before any underground work is marked started in Build Sequence Step 5.
Category 6 — Environmental Constraints
Environmental constraints can restrict what can be built, where, and what permits are required beyond the standard building permit.
Wetlands: the US Army Corps of Engineers (USACE) regulates wetlands under Section 404 of the Clean Water Act. Filling, grading, or building on wetlands requires a USACE permit — a process that can take months and may be denied. Wetlands are not always obvious: they include seasonal wet areas, prairie potholes, and areas adjacent to streams or ponds that appear dry in summer. If any portion of the property has characteristics suggesting wetlands (hydric soils, wetland vegetation, seasonal standing water), a wetland delineation by a qualified consultant should be completed before the site plan is finalized.
Agricultural land preservation: some properties are enrolled in programs (Conservation Reserve Program, Purchase of Agricultural Conservation Easement) that restrict non-agricultural use. Check with the USDA Farm Service Agency for any enrolled acreage and program restrictions.
App fields and cascade: wetland boundaries entered as exclusion zones on the site plan layer. Buildings placed within a wetland boundary generate a Tier 1 red conflict flag. Agricultural preservation enrollment and restrictions recorded.
Category 7 — Access and Site Development
Access and site development covers the physical work required to make the site buildable.
* Road access: confirm legal road access exists and is adequate for construction traffic (minimum 14 ft wide, adequate turning radius, no low overhead obstructions). Concrete trucks, flatbed deliveries, and SIP panel cranes require reliable heavy vehicle access.
* Driveway culvert permit: in Kansas, installing a new driveway culvert on a county road requires a permit from the county road department. The county specifies culvert diameter and length. Budget as a soft cost if a new driveway or culvert is needed.
