Failure mode: moisture. Corrected alternative: 3/8” furring strips vertical at 16” OC over SIP joint tape, then vinyl siding over furring. Creates a drainage plane.
* LP siding or fiber cement siding directly against ICF foam face without furring — BLOCKED. Why: organic-faced siding in contact with ICF foam traps moisture between the siding and foam. Failure mode: moisture, siding deterioration. Corrected alternative: 1x3 furring strips screwed through foam into ICF concrete core (minimum 2” penetration into concrete), then siding over furring.
* Standard wire lath on ICF foam face for stucco — BLOCKED. Why: wire lath fasteners driven into foam alone have no holding power. Failure mode: structural. Corrected alternative: fiberglass mesh embedded in polymer base coat applied directly to ICF foam face — this is the correct ICF stucco system. Furring strips screwed into concrete as an alternative substrate for lath.
* Spray foam insulation (Class I, closed-cell) as the only insulation on the interior of a metal purlin building with no thermal break at the steel framing — WARNING (not blocked, but flagged). Why: the steel column and girt are still a thermal bridge from exterior to interior even if the cavities are filled with spray foam. In Kansas winter, the steel surface temperature drops below the dew point and condensation forms on the steel — especially at the eave girt connection (Section 6.2 metal purlin watch list). Corrected approach: closed-cell spray foam on the interior PLUS closed-cell foam on the column flanges and girt faces to interrupt the thermal bridge.
* EIFS (exterior insulation and finish system) on any wall without a drainage gap — BLOCKED. Why: barrier EIFS (no drainage) has a well-documented failure history in mixed-humid climates like Zone 4A. Any moisture that penetrates the finish coat is trapped against the sheathing with no drainage path. Failure mode: moisture, rot. Corrected alternative: drainable EIFS only — requires a drainage mat or textured back-surface foam board that creates a gap between the foam and the WRB.
* Any exterior finish over unprimed, unpainted, or unsealed T1-11 plywood — WARNING. Why: T1-11 used as both sheathing and siding must be primed on all six faces (including cuts and edges) before installation. Unprimed T1-11 absorbs moisture at the edges and delaminates. Failure mode: moisture, structural. The tool flags T1-11 selection with a mandatory primer reminder.
* Asphalt shingles on a SIP roof — BLOCKED. Why: asphalt shingles require nailing through the roofing and underlayment into the panel at 4”–6” OC across the entire roof surface — thousands of penetrations through the SIP panel facing. Each penetration is a potential moisture entry point into the panel core. SIP OSB facing has no practical ability to self-seal around driven fasteners the way solid lumber does. Over time, nail holes admit moisture into the EPS core, causing core saturation, loss of R-value, and OSB delamination. Failure mode: moisture, insulation degradation, structural. Corrected alternative: standing seam metal (concealed clips, zero panel penetrations — strongly recommended) or exposed-fastener metal panels (far fewer penetrations than shingles, easier to seal). Code basis: no IRC prohibition, but SIP manufacturer installation guides universally prohibit or strongly caution against asphalt shingles on SIP roofs — confirm with panel manufacturer warranty terms before specifying.
* Roof pitch below 3/12 with standing seam metal roofing — BLOCKED. Why: standing seam metal requires minimum 3/12 pitch for reliable water shedding at panel seams. Below 3/12, water backs up at seam caps during heavy Kansas rain events and infiltrates the panel joints. Failure mode: moisture. Exception: some low-slope standing seam profiles are rated to 1/12 but require a specific sealant bead at every seam — confirm the product being specified before allowing a pitch below 3/12. Corrected alternative: raise pitch to 3/12 minimum, or if low-slope is required for design reasons, use TPO or EPDM membrane roofing on a fully sheathed structural deck. Code basis: IRC R905.4 and metal roofing manufacturer installation guides.
* Forced-air ducted HVAC in a building with SIP roof or cathedral ceiling — BLOCKED. Why: SIP roofs and cathedral ceilings have no attic cavity. There is no space to route supply or return ducts above the ceiling plane. Attempting to run ducts on the interior face below the finished ceiling destroys the aesthetic and creates a thermal bypass through uninsulated duct surfaces inside the conditioned space. Return air path is equally impossible — there is no plenum. Failure mode: system cannot function as designed. Corrected alternative: mini-split heat pump (ductless, head unit mounts on wall or ceiling, outdoor unit on exterior) or in-floor radiant heat (for heating only — pair with mini-split for cooling). Both are fully compatible with SIP and cathedral ceiling construction. Code basis: IMC 2018 Section 601 duct installation requirements; this is a physical impossibility, not merely a code violation.
* Cathedral ceiling or SIP roof assembly where rigid foam above the deck is less than 50% of total assembly R-value — BLOCKED. Why: in a cathedral ceiling or SIP roof, the dew point location within the assembly is controlled by the ratio of exterior (cold-side) rigid foam R-value to total assembly R-value. When the rigid foam falls below 50% of total R, the dew point shifts inside the rafter or panel cavity, condensation forms on the interior face of the sheathing, and the result is rot, mold, and structural damage that is invisible until severe. Example: a target R-49 assembly requires a minimum R-25 rigid foam layer above the deck. The remaining R-24 may be spray foam between rafters from the interior. This ratio is a physics and code requirement — not a preference. Failure mode: moisture, structural rot (invisible until severe). Corrected alternative: increase exterior rigid foam thickness until the ratio is at or above 50%. Verify before ordering roofing materials. Code basis: IECC 2018 R806.5 (unvented attic assemblies), IRC R806.5.
* NM-B (Romex) wiring run inside a SIP wall panel or SIP roof panel — BLOCKED. Why: SIP panels are manufactured with a continuous EPS foam core between two OSB facings. There is no framing cavity to run Romex through. Drilling or cutting channels through the foam core weakens the structural diaphragm and voids the panel manufacturer’s structural warranty. NM-B is also prohibited in locations requiring conduit by NEC 2020. Failure mode: structural, code violation. Corrected alternative: SIP service chase (horizontal 2x3 strapping on the interior SIP face, Romex run in the gap behind drywall — see Section 6.2) or surface-mounted conduit on interior foam face where exposed finish is used. Code basis: NEC 2020 300.3, SIP manufacturer installation guides.
* Non-direct-vent (atmospherically vented) combustion appliance in a SIP, ICF, or any building with blower door target below 3.0 ACH@50Pa, without a dedicated exterior combustion air supply — BLOCKED (life safety). Why: tight buildings deplete oxygen around fuel-burning appliances and create negative pressure conditions that back-draft the flue, pulling combustion gases (including carbon monoxide) into the living space instead of exhausting them to exterior. This is a life safety issue, not a comfort issue. Failure mode: carbon monoxide poisoning. Corrected alternatives: (1) Preferred — specify direct-vent sealed-combustion appliances (the appliance draws its own combustion air through a dedicated sealed pipe from exterior; back-draft is physically impossible); (2) If existing appliance is atmospheric vent, install a dedicated exterior combustion air supply duct sized per IMC 2018: total BTU/hr of all fuel-burning appliances ÷ 2000 = minimum free area in square inches. Combustion air must come from exterior only — not from garage or crawlspace. Code basis: IMC 2018 Section 701, IRC M1703.
* Bathroom exhaust fan vented to an attic space rather than to exterior — BLOCKED. Why: moisture-laden exhaust air discharged into an attic causes condensation on the roof sheathing and framing, leading to mold, rot, and sheathing delamination. This is a well-documented failure in production housing and is explicitly prohibited by code. Failure mode: moisture, structural. Corrected alternative: duct exhaust fan through the wall or roof to exterior. Minimum 4” rigid or semi-rigid duct, insulated where it passes through unconditioned space to prevent condensation in the duct. Terminate with a dampered exterior cap. Code basis: IRC M1507.2, IMC 2018 Section 501.3.
* Overhead garage door or large agricultural door (8’+ wide) that is not wind-rated for 115 mph Vult — BLOCKED. Why: Miami County Kansas is in ASCE 7 Wind Exposure Category C with a 115 mph ultimate design wind speed (IRC 2018 Figure R301.2(4)). Large unrated doors are the single most common structural failure point in Kansas storms — when a door fails inward under wind pressure, interior pressurization can lift the roof off the walls. Standard residential and agricultural doors sold at farm supply stores are typically not wind-rated to this level. Failure mode: structural, catastrophic. Corrected alternative: specify wind-rated doors on the purchase order (product must carry a wind pressure rating in psf that meets the local requirement — confirm required psf with the structural engineer or Miami County building department). Wind-rated doors cost modestly more; the structural consequence of an unrated door in a Kansas storm is total loss. Code basis: IRC R301.2.1, ASCE 7.
* SIP roof overhang longer than 18” without engineering confirmation of panel edge and top plate connection capacity — WARNING. Why: Kansas design snow load is approximately 20 psf. A SIP roof panel with an overhang longer than 18” delivers the full cantilevered snow load at the overhang as a point load to the panel-to-top-plate connection — not as a distributed load across the panel. This connection is not engineered for large point loads in standard SIP specifications. The panel edge and top plate fastener pattern must be verified against the actual overhang depth and Kansas snow load by the structural engineer before the overhang is specified on the purchase order. Overhang depth is fixed at panel manufacture. Failure mode: structural. Code basis: IRC R802.4, ASCE 7 snow load provisions.
* S-trap on any plumbing fixture — BLOCKED. Why: the S-trap (a trap that discharges downward immediately after the trap seal) loses its water seal when fast-draining fixtures create enough velocity to siphon the trap dry. A dry trap has no seal and allows sewer gases — including hydrogen sulfide and methane — to enter the living space. Failure mode: health/safety, code violation. S-traps have been prohibited by the UPC for decades. Corrected alternative: P-trap with a properly vented drain line. Every fixture must have a P-trap and a vent — vent stack through roof or air admittance valve (AAV) per UPC. Code basis: UPC Section 1002.1.
* Plumbing vent stack roof penetration placed inside the solar array reservation zone — BLOCKED. Why: vent stack penetrations require flashing and create a permanent obstruction that prevents solar panel installation in that area. The solar reservation zone (Section 5.1, Solar layer) is a no-penetration zone — any penetration placed inside it during rough-in permanently compromises the solar array footprint. This is a pre-pour and rough-in decision, not a finish decision. Failure mode: solar array footprint reduced or eliminated. Corrected alternative: route the vent stack to a roof penetration location outside the solar reservation zone. On a SIP or cathedral ceiling roof, the vent path must be planned before panel order. The tool’s two-way linking between the vent path record and the roof plan layer flags this conflict automatically when a penetration coordinate is placed inside the reservation zone boundary.
5.7.2 Assembly Wizard — Guided Selection for Novice Path
The assembly wizard replaces raw dropdowns with a guided question sequence for any user who has not used the advanced Surface Record editor before. It is the default path for the Parents user role and is optionally available to all users. Advanced users can bypass the wizard and use the Surface Record fields directly.
Wizard question sequence for exterior wall assembly: Step 1 — “What do you want the outside of this wall to look like?” Shown as a photo grid: painted smooth (stucco/EIFS), horizontal lap siding, vertical board and batten, metal panel, brick look, wood panel. Each option maps to a finish family. Step 2 — “What is this wall made of?” Shown based on the construction type already selected for the surface: if stud framing, shows stud options; if SIP, shows SIP-compatible finishes only (wizard automatically hides incompatible finishes before they can be selected). Step 3 — “Is this wall on a heated space?” Yes/No. If yes, wizard adds insulation and vapor retarder layer selection. Step 4 — Wall assembly preview: a cross-section diagram of the selected assembly from exterior to interior. Each layer is labeled with its name, its purpose in one sentence, and a link to the how-to video for that layer (Section 5.7.3). Owner sees exactly what they are committing to before confirming. Step 5 — Confirmation: “This is your wall assembly. The takeoff for all materials in this assembly has been calculated. Confirm?” Two buttons: Confirm (locks the assembly and generates takeoff) or Change (returns to Step 1).
CROSS-SECTION DIAGRAM: the wall assembly cross-section in Step 4 is a live SVG rendering generated from the assembly library data, not a static image. Each layer is drawn to approximate scale with its thickness labeled. Clicking any layer opens its tooltip (Section 2.1a glossary system) with a plain-language definition and the how-to video link. The diagram updates in real time if the owner changes any selection in Steps 1—3. This is the single most important novice guidance feature — seeing the wall you are about to build before committing prevents the most common assembly mistakes.
5.7.3 How-To Video Library
The video library is a curated JSON object mapping assembly types, decision points, and individual trade operations to vetted external video resources. It uses the same architecture as the glossary tooltip system (Section 2.1a) — a central data object, rendered at the point of need. Adding a new video requires one entry in the JSON. Videos appear as a “Watch how-to video” link at every relevant decision point: in the assembly wizard cross-section diagram, in the construction knowledge package for each surface (Section 6.1), in the build sequence steps (Section 17), and in every pre-pour checklist item where installation technique matters.
VIDEO RECORD SCHEMA: each video entry in the library JSON contains: videoId (unique string key); title (descriptive title of the operation, e.g. “3-Coat Stucco Application on Wood Frame”); channel (YouTube channel or organization name, e.g. “Fine Homebuilding”); url (full YouTube or video URL — used first; if unavailable, falls back to search); searchQuery (3—6 word search string that will find an equivalent video if the original URL goes dead, e.g. “3 coat stucco wood frame installation”); searchPlatform (YouTube / Google — where to run the fallback search); relevantAssemblies (array of assembly IDs this video applies to); relevantSteps (array of build sequence step numbers this video supports); skillLevel (Beginner / Intermediate / Advanced — used to filter when the Parents role is active); durationMinutes (approximate runtime — shown to user so they can decide whether to watch now or save for later); notes (any project-specific caveat about this video, e.g. “Shows 2-coat system — this project uses 3-coat; skip to 4:30 for the lath installation technique which is identical”).
FALLBACK BEHAVIOR: when the tool renders a video link, it first checks whether the URL is reachable (Phase 2, with API access) or simply displays it as a clickable link (Phase 1). If the URL returns a 404 or is manually flagged as dead by the owner, the tool automatically displays the fallback: a search button labeled “Find replacement video” that opens “[searchPlatform] search: [searchQuery]” in a new tab. The search query is specific enough to find an equivalent instructional video within the first 3 results in almost all cases. The owner can paste a new URL into the video record to replace a dead link — the update persists in localStorage and syncs to Supabase in Phase 2.
Seeded Video Library — Wall Assembly and Key Trade Operations
* 3-coat stucco on wood frame: Fine Homebuilding, “How to Install 3-Coat Stucco”; search fallback: “3 coat stucco wood frame installation lath scratch brown finish”; skill: Intermediate; notes: confirm building paper is Grade D 2-layer for stucco use.
* House wrap installation and taping: Building Science Corporation or Pro Tool Reviews, “How to Install House Wrap Correctly”; search: “house wrap installation overlap lap sequence taping penetrations”; skill: Beginner; notes: pay attention to upper-over-lower lap sequence — this is the most common installation error.
* LP SmartSide / fiber cement lap siding installation: LP Building Products official channel or Fine Homebuilding; search: “LP SmartSide lap siding installation starter strip corner trim”; skill: Beginner.
* Board and batten siding installation: This Old House or Matt Risinger; search: “board and batten vertical siding installation spacing flashing”; skill: Beginner.
* SIP panel stucco — drainage mat and base coat method: Premier SIPs or Insulspan installation guide; search: “SIP panel stucco drainage mat fiberglass mesh base coat”; skill: Intermediate; notes: wire lath is NOT used on SIP — confirm the video shows the fiberglass mesh base coat method.
* ICF stucco finish — polymer base coat and mesh on foam: Nudura or Fox Blocks official channel; search: “ICF stucco finish polymer base coat fiberglass mesh foam”; skill: Intermediate.
* Rainscreen assembly — furring strips over rigid foam: Matt Risinger Build Show; search: “rainscreen furring strip rigid foam continuous insulation siding attachment”; skill: Intermediate.
* Metal wall panel installation on post-frame: Metallic Building Company or AgriBuildings; search: “metal wall panel installation post frame building girt trim”; skill: Beginner.
* Vapor barrier installation in slab (under-slab): Concrete Network or Fine Homebuilding; search: “under slab vapor barrier installation seam lap tape penetration seal”; skill: Beginner.
* PEX radiant loop layout and stapling: Uponor official channel or Fine Homebuilding; search: “PEX radiant floor loop layout staple manifold port assignment”; skill: Intermediate.
* ProPEX expansion fitting tool use: Uponor official channel; search: “Uponor ProPEX expansion fitting tool PEX-A installation”; skill: Beginner; notes: this is the default connection method for PEX-A in this spec.
* Conduit bending — EMT hand bender: Mike Holt or ElectricianU; search: “EMT conduit hand bender 90 degree offset stub up”; skill: Intermediate.
* Residential electrical panel wiring basics: Mike Holt Enterprises or ElectricianU; search: “residential panel wiring circuit breaker installation NEC”; skill: Intermediate; notes: owner may perform rough-in work under permit in Kansas — panel connections require licensed electrician.
* ICF wall stacking and pour — complete process: Nudura or BuildBlock official channel; search: “ICF insulated concrete form stacking rebar pour bracing owner builder”; skill: Intermediate; notes: this is the highest-stakes day of any ICF project — watch before the pour, not after.
* SIP panel erection — setting panels and ridge beam: Premier SIPs or Insulspan; search: “SIP panel erection wall roof ridge beam installation owner builder”; skill: Intermediate.
* Rebar placement and tying for slab on grade: Concrete Network or Fine Homebuilding; search: “slab rebar placement chairs tie wire inspection”; skill: Beginner.
* Footing and foundation form setup: This Old House or Fine Homebuilding; search: “concrete footing form setup snap tie stake level”; skill: Beginner.
Video Library — Phase Implementation
Phase 1: the video library JSON is seeded with the entries above. Links render as simple anchor tags opening in a new browser tab. No URL reachability check in Phase 1 — owner clicks and sees what comes up. Dead links are manually flagged by the owner and the fallback search button appears in place of the dead link. Phase 2: URL reachability is checked periodically (weekly, in the background using a lightweight HEAD request) and dead links are automatically flagged with the fallback search displayed. New videos can be added to the library by Anthony or Ruth via the admin panel without a code deploy — the video library JSON is stored in Supabase alongside the project data, not hardcoded in the app.
5.8 Data Provenance System
Every number in the Homestead Planner has an origin. Some are entered manually. Some are calculated from geometry. Some are derived through the cascade engine. Some come from encoded reference tables (NEC, NFPA, UPC, ASHRAE, IECC). Some come from Claude prompts. Some come from supplier quotes. The provenance system makes the origin of every number visible at the point where the owner encounters it — not buried in a log. A novice seeing a wire gauge or HVAC load can immediately understand what produced that number and how confident to be in it.
5.8.1 Provenance Source Types
Every calculated or retrieved value carries one of six source type labels, stored on the value record and rendered as a badge wherever the value appears.
* MANUAL — grey badge “Manual entry”: owner typed or selected this value directly. If overriding a calculated value: badge shows “Manual override — was [previous value] from [previous source]. Reason: [owner note].”
* GEOMETRY — green badge “From drawing”: calculated directly from dimensions drawn on the canvas. Highest confidence. Examples: wall length, roof area, zone floor area, radiant loop length, conduit run LF, drain pipe segment length.
* CALCULATED — blue badge “Calculated” with formula source cited: derived from geometry plus encoded rules or tables. Examples: stud count (wall length / stud spacing), wire gauge (NEC 310.15 from amperage and run length), drain pipe size (UPC Table 702.1 from fixture units), HVAC load (Manual J from surface R-values and window specs), concrete yardage (slab area x thickness + waste), conduit fill % (NEC Table C from conductor cross-sections), solar production (Section 7.5 formula), overhang projection (Section 10.11 formula).
* CASCADE — orange badge “Cascaded from [triggering field]”: set automatically by the cascade engine. Triggering decision identified in source note. Examples: XPS foam grade (from radiant = yes), electrical routing method (from wall type = SIP), AFCI/GFCI designation (from zone type), vapor retarder class (from construction type + climate zone).
* CLAUDE — purple badge “Claude Prompt [N]” with prompt date and confidence qualifier: ±25—35% for Prompt 1, ±10—15% for Prompt 2, ±3—5% for Prompt 3. A “Re-run prompt” button in the tooltip fires the same prompt with updated inputs. These are planning-grade estimates, not verified facts — the badge makes this explicit.
* EXTERNAL TABLE — teal badge “Ref: [table] [section]”: looked up from an encoded reference table. Examples: wire ampacity (“Ref: NEC 2020 Table 310.16”), conduit fill (“Ref: NEC 2020 Table C.1”), drain sizing (“Ref: UPC Table 702.1”), gas pipe sizing (“Ref: NFPA 54 Table 402.4”), ventilation rate (“Ref: ASHRAE 62.2-2022”), R-value minimums (“Ref: IECC 2018 Table R402.1.2”). Miami County local amendment noted where applicable.
* QUOTED — gold badge “Quoted: [supplier] [date]”: a real price entered from a supplier call or written quote. Quote expiry shown alongside — amber at 30 days, red at 60 days per Section 13.8. Most reliable financial data in the system.
5.8.2 Where Provenance Is Displayed
* PROPERTY PANEL (Trade tab): every calculated field shows its provenance badge inline, immediately to the right of the value. Compact — icon + short label. Tapping opens the source tooltip (Section 5.8.3). This is the primary provenance display location.
* TAKEOFF PANEL (live sidebar): each takeoff line shows its quantityConfidence badge (GeometryDerived = green, Estimated = amber). Tapping an Estimated badge shows the formula and what additional information would upgrade it to GeometryDerived.
* BUDGET LINE ITEMS: source badge in the status column alongside Concept/Estimated/Quoted/Committed/Actual. Claude Prompt badge shows prompt date. Quoted badge shows supplier and date. No number in the budget is sourceless.
* DIAGRAM VIEWS (panel face, one-line, riser, gas flow, radiant balance): each labeled value shows a compact provenance dot (colored by source type). Tapping opens the source tooltip. Keeps diagrams readable while making provenance accessible.
* GENERATED DOCUMENTS (Builder Brief, pre-pour, trade packages): no source badges — documents are clean outputs for builders and contractors. Any value carrying Estimated or Claude source in the app is marked with an asterisk (*) in the document. Footer on any page with an asterisked value: “* Planning-grade estimate. Confirm before ordering.”
5.8.3 Source Tooltip Content
Tapping any badge opens a small non-blocking source tooltip showing the full calculation chain or source reference.
* GEOMETRY: “Measured from your drawing. To change it, edit the element on the [layer] layer.” Shows canvas element ID.
* CALCULATED: full chain. Format: “[Value] = [formula] = [result]. Inputs: [field 1] = [value] (from [source]), [field 2] = [value] (from [source]). Reference: [citation].” Example — wire gauge: “12 AWG. Ampacity required: 20A. Run: 67 LF. Voltage drop at 12 AWG over 67 LF at 20A: 2.8% (NEC max 3%). Amperage: Manual entry. Run length: Geometry from floor plan. Ref: NEC 2020 Table 310.16.”
* CASCADE: “Set when [triggering field] was changed to [value] on [date]. Was: [old value]. To change: modify the triggering decision or enter a manual override with reason note.”
* CLAUDE: “From Claude Prompt [N] on [date]. Confidence: ±[X]%. Prompt inputs: [summary]. To update: re-run the prompt with current data.” Re-run button in tooltip.
* EXTERNAL TABLE: “Looked up from [table], [edition], [section]. Inputs: [input 1] = [value], [input 2] = [value]. Result: [value]. Note: table encoded as of [app version date]. Miami County amendments not auto-incorporated — confirm with Miami County Building Department.”
* QUOTED: “Real quote from [supplier], received [date], valid until [expiry]. Quantity: [qty] [unit] of [description]. To update: enter new quote from regional sourcing panel.”
5.8.4 Source Chain for Complex Calculations
Multi-step calculated values show a full expandable chain. Owner can drill into any input to see how it was derived.
* HVAC LOAD: Load = sum of surface heat losses and gains. Each surface in the chain shows: area (Geometry), R-value assembly (Cascade from construction type or Manual override), window U-factor and SHGC (Manual entry, Section 6.3 Tier spec), wall bearing (Geometry from surface record), overhang depth (Calculated per Section 10.11 or Manual). Climate inputs: design temperature (Ref: ASHRAE Handbook of Fundamentals, Kansas City MO, 99% heating —4°F, 1% cooling 98°F), latitude 38.5°N (Manual in property record).
* SOLAR PRODUCTION: Estimated kWh/day = array wattage ÷ 1000 × peak sun hours × azimuth derate × pitch derate × system derate. Peak sun hours: Ref NREL PVWatts Miami County KS 4.5 hr/day. Azimuth and pitch derates: Calculated from Section 7.5 tables using Geometry roof bearing. System derate 0.80: Ref NREL PVWatts composite. Confidence: ±10—15% — verify with solar installer before system sizing.
* CONCRETE YARDAGE: (slab area × thickness + footing volume) × 1.10. Slab area: Geometry. Thickness: Manual (4““) or Cascade (4.5““ when radiant = yes). Footing volume: Calculated from Geometry perimeter, width, depth. Waste factor 10%: Ref Section 5.5.2. Note: re-quote concrete the day before the pour.
* GAS PIPE SIZE: looked up from NFPA 54 Table 402.4 using BTU demand at segment and run length from regulator. BTU demand: sum of all Appliance BTU Records downstream — each rating is Manual entry or Claude Prompt 1. Run length: Geometry from drawn segment. Badge: “Ref: NFPA 54 Table 402.4, LP gas, [run] ft, [BTU] BTU/hr = [pipe size].”
* PACK ORDER QUANTITY: ceiling(adjusted qty / sold-as unit). Net qty: Geometry or Calculated. Waste factor: Ref Section 5.5.2. Sold-as unit: Ref Section 5.5.8. Tooltip: “Net: [X] LF. Waste +10% = [Y] LF. Sold as 10-ft sticks. Order: [N] sticks ([N×10] LF bought, [offcut] LF remnant).”
5.8.5 Encoded Reference Tables and Their Sources
The following tables are encoded in the application. Editions are fixed at the app version date and shown in Settings under Reference Data Versions.
* NEC 2020 Table 310.16 — wire ampacity. NEC 310.15(C)(1) — conduit fill derating. NEC 2020 Tables C.1—C.12 — conduit fill by conductor count, gauge, conduit type.
* NFPA 54 Table 402.4 — LP gas pipe sizing, black iron. NFPA 54 Table 402.4(4) — CSST sizing. NFPA 58 — LP tank setbacks and regulator rules.
* UPC Table 702.1 — drain fixture units by fixture type. UPC Table 703.2 — drain pipe sizing by fixture units.
* ASHRAE 62.2-2022 — ventilation: 7.5 CFM/person + 0.01 CFM/SF. ASHRAE Handbook of Fundamentals — Kansas City design temps: —4°F heating 99%, 98°F cooling 1%.
* IECC 2018 Table R402.1.2 — insulation minimums, Zone 4A. IMC 2018 Section 607.1 — mixing valve requirement at 140°F. IRC 2018 R310 — egress window minimums.
* NREL PVWatts — Miami County KS: 4.5 hr/day peak sun hours, 0.80 composite system derate.
* Section 5.5.2 waste factors — encoded defaults editable per project in Settings.
5.8.6 Provenance in Exports
* TAKEOFF CSV: quantityConfidence (GeometryDerived / Estimated), sourceElementType, and product_tier columns carry provenance. Estimated lines auto-marked with asterisk in description column.
* TAKEOFF JSON: each item carries sourceType, sourceReference (table citation or formula string), and calculationChain (array of input field references). External tools can trace any value back to its drawing geometry or manual entry.
* BUDGET CSV/JSON: each line carries source (Manual / Claude1 / Claude2 / Claude3 / Quoted / Actual), promptDate, quoteSupplier, quoteDate, confidencePct. Lets a lender see which estimates are Claude-grade (±25—35%) vs quote-confirmed (±3—5%).
* GENERATED DOCUMENTS: asterisk on planning-grade values. Footer on any asterisked page: “* Planning-grade estimate. Confirm before ordering. Source: [plain-language description].”
5.8.7 Provenance for the Parents User Role
The Parents role sees full provenance badges in plain language. Instead of “Ref: NEC 2020 Table C.1”: “From the electrical code rule book” with a plain-language tooltip. Instead of “Cascade from zone type = Kitchen”: “Added automatically because this is a kitchen.” The underlying data is identical — only the label text changes. Technical users (Anthony, Ruth, Builder) see full citations. Plain-language labels are one entry in the glossary JSON (Section 2.1a).
5.6 Visual Design Grammar — Per Trade LayerEvery trade layer has its own visual language: a symbol palette for drop-and-place items, a line style and color convention for runs and routes, automatic diagram views that generate from placed symbols, and two-way linking between the floor plan and all schedule or diagram views. This section is the authoritative visual spec for every layer. UI implementation in Tier 3 (Fabric.js canvas) must conform to this grammar. Tier 1 data model fields must capture every attribute this grammar requires — so that when the canvas is built, no field definitions need to be retrofitted.5.6.1 UI LAYOUT PRINCIPLESThe UI has four persistent regions when a layer is active:(1) CANVAS: the main floor plan or site plan, occupying center and right. Active layer elements rendered at full opacity. Inactive layers rendered at 20% opacity as reference context. Grid always visible at active layer zoom level.(2) SYMBOL PALETTE: left sidebar. Shows only the symbols valid for the active layer. Drag from palette to canvas to place. Palette collapses to icons when canvas width is constrained.(3) PROPERTY PANEL: right sidebar. Opens when any symbol or element is selected. Two tabs: Spatial (position, dimensions, orientation) and Trade (circuit number, pipe size, BTU demand, etc.). All trade-specific fields live in the Trade tab. Changes to Trade fields trigger cascade preview (Section 5.5.3) before applying.(4) DIAGRAM VIEW: bottom panel, collapsible. Shows the auto-generated diagram for the active trade (one-line diagram for electrical, riser diagram for plumbing, flow diagram for gas, balance chart for radiant). Updates live as symbols are placed or edited. Clicking any element in the diagram highlights the corresponding symbol on the canvas, and vice versa. This two-way linking is mandatory — it is the primary navigation tool when a floor plan has many items on one layer.LAYER SWITCHER: a tab bar above the canvas shows all active layers with color-coded tabs matching each trade’s color. Inactive layers can be toggled as reference underlays without switching the active layer. Pre-pour composite view shows all trade layers simultaneously with per-trade color coding.5.6.2 ELECTRICAL LAYER — Visual GrammarCOLOR: yellow. All electrical elements render in yellow on the canvas. Circuit colors cycle through 8 distinguishable hues when circuit coloring is toggled on — each circuit gets a unique color, items on the same circuit share it. AFCI circuits shown with a solid fill, GFCI circuits with a diagonal-hatch fill, dual-function breakers with both.SYMBOL PALETTE — ELECTRICAL:Outlets: duplex receptacle (standard symbol — circle with two horizontal lines), GFCI receptacle (circle with G), 240V receptacle (circle with two diagonal lines), weatherproof receptacle (circle with WP), floor outlet (circle with F), dedicated outlet (circle with D).Switches: single-pole (S), 3-way (S3), 4-way (S4), dimmer (SD), timer (ST), occupancy sensor (SO).Fixtures: recessed can (circle with crosshairs), surface mount (filled circle), pendant (circle with dot), exterior wall sconce (half-circle on wall line), under-cabinet strip (rectangle), explosion-proof (circle with X — for pit and grain storage zones).Distribution: panel (square with P), sub-panel (square with SP), critical loads sub-panel (square with CL), disconnect (square with D), transfer switch (rectangle with T), generator inlet (rectangle with G), solar inverter (rectangle with INV), battery bank (rectangle with BAT), EV charger (rectangle with EV), interlock kit (small bracket symbol at panel).Safety: smoke detector (circle with S, dashed), CO detector (circle with CO, dashed), hardwired connection shown as dashed line to circuit.LINE CONVENTIONS — ELECTRICAL:Home-run line: dashed arc from last device on circuit to panel, labeled with circuit number at both ends. Arc curves away from walls to stay readable on dense plans. Number of slash marks on home-run indicates wire count (standard: 2 slashes = 2-wire, 3 slashes = 3-wire).Circuit wire: thin solid line connecting devices on the same circuit, rendered in circuit color when circuit coloring is active.Conduit run: thick solid line with conduit size labeled (e.g. "3/4" EMT").Underground/in-slab conduit: thick dashed line with depth labeled.PANEL FACE VIEW (required UI element):The panel face is a scaled visual representation of the actual breaker panel. It renders in the Diagram View panel (bottom of screen) and also as a standalone view accessible from the electrical layer toolbar. Layout: breaker slots numbered left (odd) and right (even), top to bottom, alternating — matching real panel wiring convention. Each slot shows: circuit number, description (truncated to 14 chars), amperage, breaker type icon (standard / AFCI / GFCI / dual-function / 2-pole). 2-pole breakers span two slots with a tie bar symbol. Empty slots shown as gray. Generator interlock slot shown with a bracket icon spanning the main breaker and the reserved slot. Panel face is color-coded by circuit type: standard = white, AFCI = blue, GFCI = green, dual-function = teal, 240V 2-pole = orange. Total load bar at panel bottom: filled bar showing used vs. available panel capacity. Turns amber at 80% (NEC continuous load limit), red at 100%.TWO-WAY LINKING — ELECTRICAL:Click any outlet/switch/fixture on the floor plan: its circuit highlights in the panel face, all other items on that circuit highlight on the floor plan, circuit run length and volt-drop calculation shown in the property panel.Click any breaker in the panel face: all devices on that circuit highlight on the floor plan, home-run path animates.Click the panel symbol on the floor plan: panel face view opens full-screen.ONE-LINE DIAGRAM (auto-generated, required UI element):The one-line diagram renders in the Diagram View and shows the complete electrical system topology from utility to loads. It draws itself from decisions already made — the user never draws the one-line manually. Elements appear in the diagram only when the corresponding decision is made:Always present: [Utility Meter] → [Main Disconnect] → [Main Panel].Solar = Grid-Tied adds: [Array] → [Inverter] → [Main Panel], bidirectional arrow at meter indicating net metering.Solar = Battery Hybrid adds: [Array] → [Inverter] → [Battery Bank] → [Critical Loads Sub-Panel], with [Main Panel] → [Critical Loads Sub-Panel] as backup feed.Generator added: [Generator] → [Interlock] → [Main Panel] or [Generator] → [Transfer Switch] → [Critical Loads Sub-Panel].Sub-panels added: [Main Panel] → [Sub-Panel A] (with feeder size labeled), → [Sub-Panel B], etc.Critical Loads Sub-Panel present: expands to show the circuits inside it (sump pump, refrigerator, HVAC, etc.) as a nested list.Wind turbine added: [Turbine] → [Charge Controller] → [Battery Bank] or [Grid Tie].Each connection in the one-line diagram is labeled with wire size and conduit size. Clicking any element in the one-line diagram opens its property panel and highlights the corresponding symbol on the floor plan.5.6.3 PLUMBING LAYER — Visual GrammarCOLOR: blue. Supply lines in blue, drain lines in dark blue, vent lines in light blue dashed.SYMBOL PALETTE — PLUMBING:Fixtures: toilet (standard plan symbol), sink (rectangle with circle drain), shower (square with corner drain), bathtub (rectangle with sloped floor), floor drain (circle with X), hose bib (circle with H on wall), washing machine box (rectangle with W), dishwasher (rectangle with DW), water heater (circle with WH), pressure reducing valve (PRV triangle symbol), oil/water separator (rectangle with OWS), sump pit (circle with SP), cleanout (circle with CO), air admittance valve (AAV, small symbol at vent end).Supply: shutoff valve (butterfly symbol), union (two lines with gap), tee, elbow, reducer.LINE CONVENTIONS — PLUMBING:Cold supply: thin solid blue line.Hot supply: thin solid red line (rendered on plumbing layer only).Drain: medium solid dark blue line, slope arrow every 5 ft, invert elevation labeled at every change of direction and at floor drain connection.Vent: thin dashed blue line, rises vertically to vent stack symbol.Wet wall: thick blue dashed rectangle around the wall containing the main stack — automatically placed when wet wall location is confirmed.RISER DIAGRAM (auto-generated):The plumbing riser diagram renders in the Diagram View. It shows the vertical relationship of all fixtures in a schematic elevation view: each floor level shown as a horizontal band, fixtures shown as symbols at their floor level, drain lines dropping to the main stack, vent lines rising above the roof line. Branch drain sizes labeled (1.5" for sink/shower, 3" for toilet). Vent stack size labeled (3" minimum for toilet branch). Isometric view toggle: the riser can switch between schematic and isometric view for a more spatial representation of the piping. Clicking any fixture in the riser highlights it on the floor plan.PIPE SIZING: drain and supply line sizes auto-calculated from fixture unit count per run segment and labeled on every segment in the riser diagram. Undersized segments shown in red.5.6.4 HVAC LAYER — Visual GrammarCOLOR: orange. All HVAC elements in orange.SYMBOL PALETTE — HVAC:Mini-split indoor head (rectangle with airflow arrows), outdoor unit (rectangle with fan symbol, placed on exterior wall or site plan), lineset path (double line from head to outdoor unit), radiant manifold (rectangle with loop connections), boiler (circle with B), combi boiler (circle with CB), ERV unit (rectangle with ERV, in mechanical room), HRV unit (rectangle with HRV), exhaust fan (circle with EF, on wall or ceiling), range hood (rectangle with RH, above range location), combustion air inlet (arrow through wall symbol), duct chase (rectangle, dashed outline), supply register (rectangle with lines), return air grille (rectangle with X pattern).LINE CONVENTIONS — HVAC:Lineset: double orange line, labeled with lineset diameter and length.ERV supply/exhaust duct: single orange line with direction arrow, labeled with CFM.Radiant PEX loops: orange serpentine lines within zone boundary, each loop a slightly different orange shade for visual separation. Loop length labeled at manifold end. Balance indicator shown as colored tick on each loop: green = within 10% of average, yellow = 10-20% off, red = 20%+ off.Duct runs: single orange line with width × height label (e.g. "10x6").HVAC LOAD OVERLAY:When HVAC layer is active, each zone polygon shows its calculated HVAC load as an overlay: heating load (BTU/hr) and cooling load (BTU/hr) displayed inside the zone boundary. Color: green if load is within equipment capacity, amber if near limit, red if equipment is undersized. This overlay updates live when any surface R-value, window SHGC, or zone dimension changes. Clicking the load overlay opens the full Manual J calculation breakdown for that zone.RADIANT BALANCE CHART (auto-generated, Diagram View):Bar chart showing loop lengths for all radiant loops in the building. Target line at average loop length. Bars within 10% of target = green, 10-20% off = amber, 20%+ = red. Manifold position on floor plan is adjustable — dragging the manifold symbol recalculates all loop lengths and updates the balance chart live. User finds the manifold position that turns all bars green before locking.5.6.5 GAS / PROPANE LAYER — Visual GrammarCOLOR: red. All gas elements in red.SYMBOL PALETTE — GAS:Propane tank (oval with P, on site plan), first-stage regulator (small rectangle at tank), second-stage regulator (small rectangle at building entry), shutoff valve (butterfly symbol, red), union (two lines with gap, red), stub-up (circle with arrow up, at each appliance location), sleeve through foundation (dashed rectangle at wall crossing), CSST flexible section (wavy red line).LINE CONVENTIONS — GAS:Main supply line: thick red solid line from tank to building entry.Branch lines: medium red solid line to each appliance stub.Each segment labeled with: pipe size (1/2", 3/4", 1") and BTU demand for that run.Pressure drop shown at each regulator as a label: "First stage: 10 PSI → 2 PSI. Second stage: 2 PSI → 11 WC".GAS FLOW DIAGRAM (auto-generated, Diagram View):Schematic tree diagram showing the propane system from tank to each appliance. Reads left to right: tank at left, appliances at right. Each branch labeled with BTU demand and pipe size. Total demand shown at tank. Pipe sizing validation: any segment where BTU demand exceeds capacity for that pipe size and run length shown in red with the required pipe size labeled. Tank sizing check at bottom: current total BTU demand vs. tank evaporation capacity at -20°F (Kansas design cold). If demand exceeds cold-weather evaporation rate, flag: "Tank undersized for peak demand at design temperature — consider 2 x 500 gallon or 1000 gallon." Clicking any appliance in the flow diagram highlights it on the floor plan.5.6.6 STRUCTURAL LAYER — Visual GrammarCOLOR: gray. Structural elements in dark gray, heavier line weight than other layers to convey structural solidity.SYMBOL PALETTE — STRUCTURAL:Post (filled black circle, sized to post dimensions), beam (heavy line, labeled with LVL/Glulam size), load-bearing wall indicator (double heavy line with hatching), header over opening (thick line across opening, labeled with LVL size), holdown (small X at shear wall corner), ridge beam (dashed heavy line on roof plan), hip or valley rafter (dashed line), column base plate (square symbol at footing).NOTE: the structural layer is largely auto-generated from construction type decisions, not drawn by the user. Post locations derive from roof structure and span tables. Header sizes derive from opening widths and load path. The user can override any auto-placed structural element but the tool flags the override as requiring engineer confirmation if the auto-placed element was structurally derived. Structural elements override with reason note required, same as locked element override (Section 5.5.4).STRUCTURAL SCHEDULE (Diagram View):Table listing every engineered element in the building: each header (location, span, load, required LVL size), each post (location, load, required size), each holdown (location, required model, uplift value). Items that require engineer stamp shown with a flag icon. Clicking any row highlights the element on the floor plan.5.6.7 RADIANT LAYER — Visual GrammarCovered under HVAC layer (Section 5.6.4 — radiant PEX loops, manifold symbol, balance chart). The radiant layer activates as a sub-layer of HVAC when any zone has radiant = yes. It can be viewed independently by selecting "Radiant" from the HVAC layer split.5.6.8 PRE-POUR COMPOSITE LAYER — Visual GrammarCOLOR: per-trade color coding on a white background. Plumbing = blue, electrical = yellow, gas = red, radiant = orange, structural = gray, sump/drain tile = dark blue dashed.The pre-pour composite is not an editable layer — it is a read-only coordination view generated from all active trade layers. No symbols can be placed or moved in this view. Errors must be corrected on the originating trade layer.TRADE FILTER BUTTONS: a row of toggle buttons above the canvas allows individual trades to be shown/hidden on the composite view. Default: all trades shown. Useful for: "show me only plumbing and electrical together" to check for conflicts between those two trades.CONFLICT HIGHLIGHTING: any two elements from different trades whose physical paths cross or occupy the same location are highlighted in magenta on the composite view. Clicking the conflict opens a dialog identifying both elements and suggesting resolution (e.g. "Electrical conduit at 18" depth conflicts with drain line at 12" depth at grid coordinate J-14. Adjust conduit depth or reroute.").CHECKLIST VIEW: the pre-pour composite layer has a companion checklist in the property panel listing every item from every trade that must be installed before concrete. Items are grouped by installation sequence (not by trade). Each item has a checkbox for field confirmation. Unchecked items block Ready-to-Pour status. The checklist is printable as a single-page field document.5.6.9 SITE PLAN LAYERS — Visual GrammarSite plan layers (septic, drainage, utilities, propane, solar) follow the same color conventions as their building-level counterparts but at site scale (1" = 20 ft default). Building footprints shown as gray filled rectangles. Setback boundaries shown as dashed colored lines (orange = propane setback, blue = septic setback, red = property line setback). Underground runs shown as thick dashed lines with depth labels. Conflict flags shown as red X symbols at the conflict location, with a flag panel listing all active conflicts sorted by severity.5.6.10 VISUAL DESIGN IMPLEMENTATION NOTES FOR TIER 3All visual grammar in this section is implemented in Tier 3 using Fabric.js. Tier 1 and Tier 2 collect the data fields that feed this visual layer — no visual canvas in those tiers. The visual grammar spec exists now so that Tier 1 field definitions capture every attribute the canvas will need. KEY IMPLEMENTATION REQUIREMENTS:(1) Every symbol on every layer must store its canvas coordinates as part of the element record — not as a separate visual-only property. Coordinates are data, not decoration.(2) Every auto-generated diagram (one-line, riser, flow, balance chart) must update in real time when any source data changes. Diagrams are not exports — they are live views.(3) Two-way linking (click floor plan → highlight diagram, click diagram → highlight floor plan) is mandatory for every trade layer. It is the primary navigation tool and cannot be deferred.(4) The panel face visual is a required UI element, not a nice-to-have. It must be visible alongside the floor plan on the electrical layer. Panel slot count and amperage must reflect the actual panel specification entered in the electrical module.(5) Mobile display: on small screens, the Symbol Palette collapses to a bottom drawer, the Diagram View collapses to a tab, and the canvas goes full screen. Two-way linking is touch-compatible. Placing symbols on mobile is via tap-to-select from palette, then tap-to-place on canvas — no drag required.6. Construction Knowledge System
Every surface record carries a construction knowledge package that is context-assembled from the decisions already made — not generic advice. The knowledge is specific to this assembly type in this zone with these adjacent decisions. GENERATION GATE: knowledge packages are generated only after the surface record reaches Roughed-in status or higher (Section 3.4 Planning Status field). Before that point, the tool shows a placeholder: &#x201C;Lock construction type to generate this surface&#x2019;s knowledge package.&#x201D; A knowledge package generated before construction type is locked would be generic rather than context-specific — which is worse than no package at all because it gives false confidence. The gate ensures every package is specific to the actual decisions made.


6.1 Three-Part Knowledge Package Per Surface
* Materials Takeoff — quantities calculated from surface dimensions and assembly choices. Formatted as a printable list for a supplier. For wall surfaces: includes all assembly layers from the Wall Assembly Library (Section 5.7) — WRB, lath or furring, siding, vapor retarder — not just the structural shell. HOW-TO VIDEOS: each knowledge package includes curated video links (Section 5.7.3) for the primary operations in this assembly. Videos appear alongside the build sequence card. If a URL is unavailable the search fallback opens the equivalent content. Skill level filtered to user role.
* Build Sequence Card — steps in order reflecting your ceiling height, electrical method choice, window types, interior finish, and applicable code requirements. Steps that do not apply to the configuration are suppressed. Kansas wind zone and frost depth steps are surfaced where relevant.
* Watch List — the non-obvious things that bite owner-builders on this specific assembly in this configuration. Things a good contractor knows from experience that never appear in manufacturer guides.


6.2 Assembly Knowledge — Key Items by Type
SIP Walls
* Buy: Panels (specify R-value, thickness, facing type), splines + structural adhesive, top and bottom plates, seam tape, foam sealant for all joints, window bucks lumber
* Build: Layout → set corner panels first → spline each joint with adhesive and mechanical fasteners → install top plate → electrical chase strapping before drywall
* Watch: Panel sequence is permanent once set. Thermal bridging occurs at splines — use adhesive generously. Electrical service chase (2x3 horizontal strapping on interior SIP face, Romex in gap behind drywall) is the cleanest owner-build electrical solution. Outlets on exterior SIP walls go on the chase, not through the panel. CHASE HEIGHT IS A ONE-TIME DECISION: the horizontal strapping runs at a fixed height and every outlet on that wall must be at that height. Decide all outlet heights on every SIP wall before the chase is strapped — standard outlet height is 16" but workbench outlets are typically 44–48", and a garage-style wall with a future workbench needs the chase at workbench height, not floor-outlet height. A second chase at a different height is possible but requires a second strapping run and must be planned before drywall. This decision cannot be revisited after drywall is hung.
* Electrical routing on SIP walls: If interior finish is drywall — use service chase. If interior finish is exposed OSB — conduit on surface is the only option. This is decided before panel order.


SIP Roof
* Buy: Roof SIP panels (R-38 minimum for Zone 4A), engineered ridge beam (LVL required — sized by engineer for Kansas wind and snow load), eave blocking, roofing underlayment, roofing material (standing seam metal recommended — see Watch list; or exposed fastener metal panel or shingles)
* Build: Ridge beam first → panels from eave to ridge → no attic cavity → all electrical on underside in conduit → vapor barrier critical on warm side (interior face)
* Watch: No attic space means no attic for HVAC equipment, no blown insulation, no attic electrical runs. Condensation at cold face if vapor barrier is wrong side. Ridge beam sizing is frequently underestimated — engineer required for Kansas wind. EAVE SNOW LOAD: Kansas design snow load is ~20 psf, but SIP roof panels with long overhangs accumulate snow at the eave where the load is cantilevered — the panel-to-top-plate connection carries the full overhang snow load as a point load, not a distributed load. Overhangs longer than 18" on a SIP roof require engineering confirmation that the panel edge and top plate connection can carry the cantilevered snow load. Do not spec a generous 24"+ overhang on a SIP roof without running this by the engineer. Mini-split or in-floor radiant are the only practical HVAC options with SIP roof. Roofing material choice: standing seam metal is strongly recommended for SIP roofs — concealed clips handle thermal expansion across Kansas temperature swings (–10°F to 105°F) without backing out fasteners through the panel. If solar is possible now or in future, standing seam is the correct choice — solar clamps attach to seams with zero roof penetrations, preserving the SIP panel integrity. Exposed fastener panels on a SIP roof create hundreds of potential penetration points that must each be maintained. Minimum pitch for standing seam: 1/12 (some profiles) but 3/12 preferred. Standing seam costs 2–3x exposed fastener panels but delivers 40–70 year lifespan vs 20–30 years.


Standard Stud Framing (2x6 for heated zones)
* Buy: 2x6 lumber at 16" OC, top and bottom plates, headers per opening schedule, OSB sheathing, house wrap (Tyvek or equivalent), batt insulation R-21, drywall, exterior siding, roofing material (standing seam metal / exposed fastener metal panel / asphalt shingles — standing seam recommended if solar is possible)
* Build: Plates → studs 16" OC → headers over openings → sheathing → house wrap → rough electrical + plumbing → insulation → drywall → siding
* Watch: House wrap laps must go upper over lower — always. Thermal bridging at every stud reduces effective R from R-21 batt to approximately R-17 whole-wall. Air seal all penetrations before insulating. Wet wall must be 2x6 minimum to clear 3" or 4" drain stack. Roofing material decision: stud framing with a trussed roof (flat ceiling chord + attic) is compatible with all three roofing types. Standing seam is the strongest long-term choice and the correct choice if solar is possible. Asphalt shingles are the cheapest upfront but have the shortest lifespan and are incompatible with no-penetration solar mounting.


Metal Purlin (equipment bay, unheated)
* Buy: Steel columns and girts, metal purlins (wall and roof), roofing panels (standing seam or exposed fastener — see Watch), closure strips, sealant, trim and flashing kit, anchor bolts per engineered pattern
* Build: Anchor bolts in concrete → columns → girts → purlins → roofing panels eave to ridge → wall panels bottom to top → trim last. All fasteners pre-drilled.
* Watch: Kansas wind — anchor bolt pattern must be engineered, not guessed. Closure strips at eave and ridge prevent pest entry — do not skip. Metal building with no thermal break = severe condensation on interior surfaces in Kansas winter. WORST CONDENSATION POINT: the girt-to-column connection at the eave is the single most common condensation failure location in metal buildings. The steel column and eave girt form a continuous thermal bridge from exterior to interior — in Kansas winter this connection drops below the dew point and produces steady drips directly onto stored equipment, vehicles, or hay below. Address this specifically: insulate the column flange on the interior face with closed-cell spray foam, and install a drip edge or condensation gutter at the eave girt before the interior is put into service. A general &#x201C;condensation happens&#x201D; warning is not enough — this specific connection is where it concentrates. If any conditioned space is adjacent, insulate the dividing wall. Roofing panel choice: exposed fastener panels are standard for agricultural metal buildings and are owner-installable. Standing seam costs more and requires skilled installation but is the correct choice if solar panels are planned — clip attachment leaves no penetrations in the roof. For a pure equipment storage bay with no solar intent, exposed fastener is the economical and practical choice. For a barn with living quarters or solar intent, standing seam on the roof section over those zones is worth the premium.


Radiant Slab Assembly
* Buy: XPS foam R-10 minimum (R-15 for radiant), 6 mil polyethylene vapor barrier, PEX-A tubing (not PEX-B — PEX-A is field-repairable), staples or clips, wire mesh or fiber-reinforced concrete, rebar at perimeter, manifold with zone valves
* Build: Compacted granular fill 4-6" → XPS foam → vapor barrier (seams lapped 12" and taped) → PEX loops stapled at 12" OC → pressure test → inspection → pour. Loops max 300 ft. Manifold location fixed before loop layout.
* Watch: Pressure test is mandatory and inspected. Keep system pressurized during pour — assign someone to watch gauge. PEX-A is the correct choice: if a loop is damaged during pour, it can be repaired. PEX-B with crimp fittings in a slab is a permanent problem. Loop lengths must be balanced — long and short loops in the same zone cause uneven heating. MANIFOLD PLACEMENT IS A FLOOR PLAN DECISION: the manifold location must be chosen based on zone geometry, not mechanical room convenience. Place the manifold at the geometric center of the zones it serves so that all loops are approximately equal length — every loop must reach the manifold and return within the 300 ft maximum. If the manifold is placed in a corner of a large or L-shaped zone, the loops to the far corners will be near 300 ft while loops near the manifold will be under 100 ft. Balancing valves can compensate partially but cannot fix a fundamentally unbalanced layout. The tool calculates loop lengths from manifold location and zone footprint — the manifold position is adjustable on the floor plan until all loops show balanced length. Lock manifold location before laying out PEX loops.


Cathedral Ceiling
* Buy: Engineered ridge beam (LVL or Glulam, engineer-sized for span and Kansas wind/snow load), rafters sized for insulation depth (deeper = more R-value space), rigid foam above deck (minimum 50% of total assembly R-value — this is non-negotiable), spray foam between rafters from interior, vapor retarder on warm side, tongue-and-groove decking or drywall as interior finish, roofing underlayment, roofing material (standing seam metal strongly recommended — concealed clips handle thermal movement across Kansas temperature swings without fastener failures; or exposed fastener metal panel or shingles)
* Build: Ridge beam set first (on posts bearing on footings) → rafters from ridge to top plate on each side → structural deck → rigid foam above deck → roofing underlayment → roofing → spray foam between rafters from interior → T&G or drywall as interior finish. No separate ceiling layer — the underside of the roof assembly IS the ceiling.
* Watch: No attic — HVAC must be mini-split or radiant only, no exceptions. Forced air has no distribution path. Rigid foam above deck must be at least 50% of total assembly R-value or condensation forms inside the rafter cavity — this is a physics and code requirement, not a preference. Ridge beam sizing is frequently underestimated — engineer required for any span over 20 ft in Kansas wind zone. All electrical must be run on the underside in conduit before interior finish — no attic means no rerouting later. Vapor drive goes from warm interior toward cold exterior — rigid foam on the cold (exterior) side is what intercepts it. Getting the foam-to-total-R ratio wrong is expensive to fix after finish is applied.
ICF (Insulated Concrete Forms)
ICF (Insulated Concrete Forms) — full owner-builder knowledge package.
Buy: ICF forms — specify brand and core thickness. Common brands: Nudura, Logix, Fox Blocks, Quad-Lock. Core thickness options: 4" core (R-22 total, residential only), 6" core (R-22 to R-25 total, standard for most applications), 8" core (R-28+ total, high wind or heavy load). For Kansas wind zone and IECC Zone 4A, 6" core is the standard choice. Forms are interlocking plastic foam blocks — order by linear foot of wall at the specified height, plus 10% waste. Rebar — vertical and horizontal schedule from structural engineer (do not guess rebar sizing in a Kansas wind zone ICF wall — engineer required). Typically #4 rebar at 24" OC vertical, #4 horizontal at every other course minimum. Concrete — ready-mix, 3000 psi minimum, specify &#x201C;ICF mix&#x201D; to batch plant: 4-5 inch slump, 3/8" max aggregate (standard 3/4" aggregate jams in ICF cavities). Window and door bucks — pressure-treated 2x lumber framed to exact rough opening dimensions, built before wall stacking begins. Ledger straps or anchor bolts for bond beam — specified by engineer, set before final pour. Tie wire and rebar chairs to hold rebar at correct position in cavity. Form bracing system — rent or buy vertical steel braces with turnbuckles, one brace per 4-6 linear feet of wall, required before pour. Waterproofing membrane for exterior below-grade surfaces (Tremco, Henry, or equivalent). Wood top plate material — pressure-treated 2x lumber bolted to bond beam after cure. Spray foam sealant for any gaps at corners and form joints before pour. Plastic ICF screws (not wood screws — use manufacturer-specified fasteners for attaching interior finishes to ICF foam face).
Build: Step 1 — Footing: pour conventional footing with keyway groove pressed into top while wet (keyway locks first ICF course to footing). Mark anchor bolt and rebar dowel locations on footing before pour. Rebar dowels must project up from footing into ICF cavity at correct spacing — these are set in wet footing concrete, not drilled in later. Step 2 — Prep: build all window and door bucks from pressure-treated lumber to exact rough opening dimensions before stacking any forms. Bucks must be braced square — concrete pressure will try to rack them. Step 3 — Stack forms: start at corners, work toward center of each wall. Interlock each course like brickwork (stagger joints). Thread rebar through cavities as you stack — do not try to push rebar through after walls are full height. Tie horizontal rebar at every course or per schedule. Set window and door bucks in place as you reach their height. Step 4 — Bond beam prep: at the top course, set anchor bolts or embedded ledger straps per engineer&#x2019;s plan. These are the only connection point between the ICF wall and the roof structure — their locations are permanent. Verify against roof framing plan before proceeding. Step 5 — Bracing: install wall braces before any concrete is placed. Brace every 4-6 linear feet, plumb walls, adjust turnbuckles. This is not optional — unbraced ICF walls have blown out during pours. Step 6 — Pour: order concrete as ICF mix. Pour in lifts of no more than 4 ft of wall height per lift — allow 30-60 minutes between lifts for pressure to equalize. Use a pencil vibrator inserted through the webs — do not vibrate excessively or move vibrator horizontally. Walk the entire wall during and after each lift looking for bulging. Step 7 — Cure: keep concrete moist, do not strip bracing for minimum 24 hours (48 hours preferred in cold weather). Step 8 — Below grade: apply waterproofing membrane to all exterior ICF surfaces below finish grade before backfill. Step 9 — Top plate: bolt pressure-treated top plate to bond beam anchor bolts. This is the nailing surface for roof framing. Step 10 — Electrical and mechanical: all conduit, boxes, and sleeves are surface-mounted on the interior foam face or strapped in a service chase. Use ICF-specific low-voltage boxes that screw into the foam. Cut foam carefully with a hot knife or oscillating tool — do not use a circular saw (foam dust is flammable). Step 11 — Interior finish: drywall screws directly into the plastic webs embedded in the ICF forms (webs are the structural connectors between the two foam faces — they appear as vertical plastic strips on the interior foam surface). Locate webs before hanging drywall — typically at 6" or 8" spacing depending on brand. Step 12 — Exterior finish: ICF exterior foam face accepts stucco, brick veneer, or fiber cement siding. Fiber cement over furring strips is the simplest owner-install option.
Watch: BOND BEAM ANCHOR TIMING IS THE SINGLE MOST CRITICAL STEP — anchor bolts and embedded roof straps must be set in the top course before the concrete is poured. There is no recovery after cure. Verify anchor locations against the roof framing plan before the pour truck arrives, not after. Missing or misplaced anchors mean epoxy-set replacements drilled into cured concrete — structurally inferior and expensive. REBAR DOWELS FROM FOOTING — vertical rebar must project from the footing up into the ICF cavity. These are set in wet footing concrete. Drilling them in later is possible but requires a rotary hammer, correct epoxy, and is significantly weaker. Set them right during the footing pour. BLOWOUT PREVENTION — ICF blowouts happen at corners, around window bucks, and at the base of the wall where hydrostatic pressure is highest. Never skip wall bracing. Pour in lifts. If a form starts bulging during a lift, stop the pour immediately and brace before continuing. A blowout mid-pour is a catastrophic mess that may require removing and rebuilding the entire wall section. WINDOW AND DOOR BUCKS — bucks must be built square and braced square. Concrete pressure will deflect unsupported bucks and make windows impossible to install square. Cross-brace every buck diagonally before the pour. ELECTRICAL ROUTING — all wiring is surface-mounted on the interior foam face or in a service chase. Running wire through the concrete core is impossible after pour. Plan every outlet, switch, panel feed, and low-voltage location before stacking forms — conduit sleeves through the wall must be placed during stacking. CONCRETE MIX MATTERS — standard 3/4" aggregate concrete will bridge in the ICF cavity and leave voids. Always specify 3/8" aggregate ICF mix. Voids in the concrete wall are invisible from outside and create structural weak points. INSECT BARRIER — foam is attractive to carpenter ants and termites in Kansas. Apply a borate treatment to all exposed foam surfaces and install a physical termite barrier at the base of the wall where ICF meets the footing. Do not skip this — insect damage to ICF foam is a long-term structural issue. ICF IS OWNER-BUILDABLE with careful preparation — the stacking and rebar work is straightforward. The pour day is the high-risk event. Have an experienced concrete contractor or ICF-knowledgeable person present for the first pour. The physics of concrete pressure in a form system are unforgiving, and the consequences of a blowout are severe.


Basement
Basement — full owner-builder knowledge package. Miami County clay soil considerations apply to every item below.
Buy: Excavation (machine hire or earthwork contractor — not owner-buildable at full basement depth on clay). Footing forms and lumber (or hire forming contractor). Footing rebar (#4 bars, keyway form strip, vertical dowels). Ready-mix concrete — three separate orders: footing pour, wall pour, basement slab pour — quote all three separately and confirm mix specs for each (footing and slab: standard 3,000 psi minimum; wall pour if ICF: 4”–5” slump, 3/8” max aggregate “ICF mix”). Basement wall system — ICF forms (Nudura, Fox Blocks, Logix, or Quad-Lock; 6” core standard for Kansas wind and Zone 4A), OR poured concrete wall forms (rental or purchase), OR CMU block and mortar. Waterproofing membrane (Tremco Tuff-N-Dri, Henry Blueskin, or equivalent full-coverage membrane — NOT damp-proofing, which is insufficient on this site). Drainage board (Delta-Drain or equivalent dimple mat — applied over membrane before backfill to protect membrane and direct water to footing drain). Footing drain tile (4” perforated pipe, filter fabric sock, washed gravel). Sump pit liner (18”–24” diameter, 30”–36” deep). Sump pump (primary 1/2 HP cast iron + battery backup — mandatory on this site, not optional). Egress window(s) (one per habitable basement room — min 5.7 sq ft net clear opening; source from window manufacturer with net clear area certified on product label — do not calculate from rough opening). Egress window well(s) (steel or concrete, min 9 sq ft clear area, drainage gravel at bottom). Structural floor deck materials (wood I-joist or LVL — engineer-sized; or poured concrete deck with rebar — significantly more expensive and complex). Backfill — granular fill (crushed stone or clean gravel) for the first 24” against all basement walls; do NOT use excavated clay as backfill against waterproofed walls. Engineer stamp — required for footing sizing, wall reinforcing schedule, floor deck sizing, and anchor bolt pattern — budget as a soft cost line item at Concept status (Section 13.10).
Build: Step 1 — Layout and excavation: mark basement footprint from the approved plan. Excavation must extend 2’–3’ beyond the footing perimeter on all sides to allow working room for forming, waterproofing, and drainage board. Minimum depth: basement floor elevation + 10” footing depth + 4” gravel bed under slab + 6” working clearance. On Miami County clay, stop excavation at undisturbed soil — do not over-excavate and backfill with clay. Step 2 — Footing: place 4” gravel bed at footing base, set footing forms, place 2 horizontal #4 bars, set vertical rebar dowels at 24” OC projecting 24” above footing top, set anchor bolts per engineer drawing. Pour footing. Press keyway form strip into top of wet concrete immediately and remove after initial set. Cure minimum 24 hours before proceeding. Step 3 — Basement walls (ICF path): stack ICF forms from footing keyway up, interlocking courses. Thread vertical rebar through cavities as you stack. Set window and door bucks at correct height. Install wall bracing (one brace per 4–6 LF) before any concrete. Pour in 4’ lifts with 30–60 minute rest between lifts. Walk entire wall looking for bulge during each lift. Cure 48 hours before removing bracing. Step 4 — Exterior waterproofing: apply membrane to all exterior wall faces below finish grade before any backfill. Lap all seams per manufacturer instructions. Apply drainage board over membrane with dimples facing wall. Step 5 — Footing drain tile: place 4” perforated pipe with filter fabric sock in washed gravel at footing perimeter, sloped minimum 1/8” per foot to daylight outlet or sump pit connection. Cover with gravel before backfill. Step 6 — Egress window wells: install window well surround at each egress window location before backfill. Place minimum 6” gravel drainage layer at bottom of each well. Step 7 — Backfill: granular fill in 6” compacted lifts. No jumping jack compactor within 3’ of wall. Transition to native fill after 24” from wall face. Establish positive grade (6” drop in first 10’). Step 8 — Basement slab: set sump pit liner in gravel bed (top 2” above finished slab surface). Install interior drain tile channel at wall-floor joint if specified. Place 4” gravel base, vapor barrier (seal to wall face), XPS foam if heated, rebar, pour slab. Step 9 — Structural floor deck: install I-joist or LVL deck over basement walls at top plate. Engineer-sized. Install bridging and blocking per engineer drawing. Step 10 — Egress windows: install in pre-formed bucks. Confirm net clear opening meets IRC R310 from manufacturer’s certified data sheet before ordering.
Watch: WATERPROOFING IS NON-NEGOTIABLE ON THIS SITE: Miami County clay has a seasonal water table and poor drainage. Damp-proofing is not waterproofing — it does not resist hydrostatic pressure. A basement on this site with only damp-proofing will eventually leak. Full-coverage membrane waterproofing with drainage board is required. Excavating around a finished basement to retrofit waterproofing costs $15,000–$40,000. Do it right before backfill. THREE SEPARATE CONCRETE POURS, THREE SEPARATE INSPECTIONS: footing inspection confirms depth, width, rebar, and dowel projection. Wall inspection confirms rebar schedule, bracing adequacy, and window buck sizes before wall pour. Slab inspection confirms sump pit location, drain tile placement, vapor barrier continuity, and under-slab system completion. Miami County building department requires all three hold points for a basement permit — do not combine or skip any. EGRESS WINDOW NET CLEAR: net clear is the unobstructed opening with the window fully open — smaller than the rough opening or frame size. Always confirm net clear from the manufacturer’s certified data sheet. Ordering a non-compliant window because you measured the rough opening is a common, expensive mistake. Casement windows typically achieve better net clear than double-hung for the same rough opening. SUMP IS NOT OPTIONAL: Miami County clay + seasonal water table + below-grade habitable space = mandatory sump. Primary pump + battery backup is the correct spec. The battery backup activates when power fails AND when primary pump is overwhelmed — both happen simultaneously in Kansas storms. TORNADO SHELTER OPPORTUNITY: reinforced concrete lid, ICF walls with extra rebar, and inward-opening steel door must be specified before the walls are poured — not a retrofit. Outward-opening doors fail in tornado winds. VIDEOS seeded in video library: (1) “Basement waterproofing membrane application before backfill” — search: “basement waterproofing membrane drainage board application clay soil”; skill: Intermediate. (2) “Egress window and window well installation” — search: “basement egress window well installation IRC R310 net clear opening”; skill: Beginner. (3) “Basement sump pit and interior drain tile installation” — search: “basement sump pit liner interior drain tile perimeter gravel installation”; skill: Beginner. (4) “I-joist floor deck installation over basement walls” — search: “I-joist floor deck basement wall bearing installation bridging blocking”; skill: Intermediate. See Section 8.1a for basement wall footing and ICF basement wall videos.
6.4 Window Specification Module
Every window placed on a wall surface affects four systems simultaneously: the thermal envelope (U-factor drives heat loss), the solar strategy (SHGC determines solar radiation admitted), the structural framing (rough opening drives header sizing), and code compliance (IECC Zone 4A U-factor and SHGC minimums). Windows are typically 15–20% of a building’s heat loss in a well-insulated building. Getting SHGC right by orientation often produces more benefit than upgrading to triple-pane universally.
U-factor Explained
U-factor measures how much heat the window assembly transfers per hour per square foot per degree Fahrenheit of temperature difference. Lower U-factor = less heat transfer = better performance. U-factor = 1 ÷ R-value: U-0.30 = R-3.3, U-0.20 (triple-pane) = R-5. Compare to a well-insulated wall at R-20 — even the best windows are the weakest thermal link in the envelope.
Always specify whole-unit U-factor (labeled on the NFRC label) — not the center-of-glass U-factor that some manufacturers use in marketing. The frame is 20–30% of the window area and a thermally poor frame degrades the whole-unit performance significantly.


IECC Zone 4A code minimum: U-factor ≤ 0.30 for the whole window unit. This is the floor, not the target.
SHGC Explained
Solar Heat Gain Coefficient measures what fraction of solar radiation passes through the window as heat (range 0–1). In Zone 4A Kansas, SHGC is a strategic choice by orientation:
* South-facing (150°–210°): SHGC 0.35–0.40 with correctly sized overhang (Section 10.11) admits useful winter solar gain while the overhang blocks summer sun. Net energy benefit. SHGC 0.25 without overhang is safer but sacrifices passive solar opportunity.
* West-facing (240°–300°): SHGC 0.20–0.25. Afternoon summer sun hits west windows at a low angle no practical overhang can block. Low SHGC is the primary lever for reducing west-facing cooling load.
* East-facing (60°–120°): SHGC 0.20–0.25. Morning summer sun adds to cooling load though less severely than west.
* North-facing (300°–60°): specify lowest available SHGC — no useful direct solar gain in any season.
IECC Zone 4A code maximum: SHGC ≤ 0.25. Exception: south-facing windows with completed overhang analysis (Section 10.11) may use SHGC up to 0.40.
App behavior: when a window is placed on a wall surface, the tool reads the wall orientation from the Surface Record bearing field and auto-recommends SHGC by orientation. Owner can override with a reason note.
Double-Pane vs Triple-Pane — When the Upgrade Pays
Double-pane (IGU): two panes with a gas-filled gap. Standard for modern residential construction. With low-e and argon, typically achieves U-0.27–0.30, meeting Zone 4A minimum. The baseline.
Triple-pane: three panes, two gas-filled gaps. Typically U-0.17–0.22. 30–60% cost premium over comparable double-pane. Heavier — structural implications for large operable sash.
Worth the triple-pane premium in Zone 4A:
* Very large windows on north or west faces where heat loss is significant
* Windows directly above or adjacent to primary seating or sleeping areas — triple-pane eliminates cold radiant discomfort near windows in winter, a significant comfort improvement
* Buildings targeting very high energy performance (below 2.0 ACH@50Pa, above code insulation minimums throughout)
Not worth the triple-pane premium:
* South-facing windows with correctly sized overhangs — solar gain benefit already offsets thermal loss; marginal improvement of triple-pane on a high-SHGC south window is small
* Small windows (under 6 sq ft) where the total heat loss contribution is minor regardless of U-factor
* Unheated or intermittently heated spaces
Zone 4A specific consideration: triple-pane’s primary benefit is reduced winter heat loss. Its summer cooling benefit is minimal — SHGC is determined by the glass coating, not pane count.
