In Kansas where both heating and cooling matter, correct SHGC selection by orientation and properly sized overhangs often produce better whole-year performance than upgrading all windows to triple-pane.


Gas Fill Options
* Argon: standard. Inert, non-toxic, inexpensive. Improves U-factor ~10–15% vs air. Default for any window above code minimum. Specify explicitly — some budget windows default to air fill.
* Krypton: better than argon (~25–30% improvement over air), performs better in thin gaps. Used in triple-pane where gaps are narrower. Achieves U-0.17 vs argon’s U-0.20 at the same glass spec. Significantly more expensive — rarely justified in double-pane.
* Air fill: not recommended for any conditioned space. App advisory fires: “Air-filled IGU — specify argon for better thermal performance in any conditioned space.”


Low-E Coating Types
Low-emissivity coatings reduce radiant heat transfer across the gas gap. For Zone 4A mixed-humid climate:
* South-facing passive solar windows: high-solar-gain low-e (SHGC 0.35–0.40) — admits solar radiation while reflecting interior radiant heat back into the room in winter.
* All other orientations: low-solar-gain low-e (SHGC 0.20–0.25) — reflects solar radiation away in summer, reflects interior radiant heat back in winter.
In practice: select by the SHGC shown on the NFRC label rather than specifying coating position. The app recommendation by orientation handles this automatically.
Frame Material Comparison
* Vinyl (PVC): lowest cost, good thermal performance, no painting required. Standard for most applications. Multi-chamber construction and reinforced corners matter in Kansas’s wide temperature range (-4°F to 105°F). Recommended for most applications on this project.
* Fiberglass: similar thermal performance to vinyl, better dimensional stability (very low thermal expansion — important for Kansas temperature swings), can be painted. Premium choice for high-performance or large operable windows where sash alignment over decades matters.
* Aluminum with thermal break: can meet code but thermally inferior to vinyl or fiberglass. Not recommended for heated living spaces. Acceptable for unheated or agricultural buildings.
* Aluminum without thermal break: BLOCKED for any conditioned zone. Flag: “Aluminum frames without thermal break do not meet Zone 4A U-factor requirements for conditioned spaces.”
* Wood / Clad wood: excellent thermal performance, high maintenance in Kansas climate. Appropriate for owners who prefer the aesthetic. Clad wood (aluminum or fiberglass exterior) reduces exterior maintenance.


Condensation Resistance
Condensation on the interior surface of a window in winter indicates the window surface temperature is below the interior dew point — a sign of poor window performance or high interior humidity. In a tight house with an ERV at 35–45% RH, U-0.25 or lower windows rarely condense at Kansas winter temperatures.
Condensation Resistance (CR) factor: NFRC rates windows on a scale of 1–100 (higher = better). CR 50+ is appropriate for Zone 4A residential. Informational only — not a code minimum. App field: CR factor (optional, from NFRC label). Displayed in window property panel as a comfort indicator.
Window Orientation Strategy — Where to Invest
Invest in better windows (lower U-factor, correct SHGC):
* South-facing windows in living areas (largest solar gain opportunity)
* Any window over 20 sq ft (disproportionate impact on heat loss)
* Windows in sleeping areas (occupants stationary and sensitive to radiant cold at night)
* West-facing windows (highest summer cooling liability — SHGC matters most)
Economize without significant penalty:
* Small windows under 6 sq ft
* Windows in spaces occupied only intermittently
* Windows high on walls where occupants are not in the radiant field
App behavior: the window property panel shows a “thermal impact” indicator — that window’s percentage contribution to the zone’s total heat loss. Top 25% heat-loss windows are flagged: “High-impact window — consider upgrading to lower U-factor.”
Item Specification Tiers for Windows (Section 6.3 Framework)
* Tier 1 — Generic placeholder: rough opening at category defaults, U-0.30 / SHGC-0.25 defaults, header auto-calculated from opening width. Budget at Concept status.
* Tier 2 — Category specification: window type, operation, rough opening dimensions, U-factor target, SHGC target by orientation, frame material, glazing type, gas fill. Budget moves to Estimated. Claude Prompt 1 returns 2–3 product recommendations with manufacturer, model, U-factor, SHGC, price range, and regional availability.
* Tier 3 — Specific product: manufacturer, model, confirmed dimensions, confirmed U-factor and SHGC from NFRC label, confirmed price, supplier, lead time. All rough-in dimensions locked. Budget moves to Quoted or Committed. For SIP construction: Tier 3 window dimensions lock the buck dimensions — changes after this require new bucks.


Takeoff Lines for Windows
* Window unit (EA — by type, size, U-factor, SHGC, frame material, glazing)
* Window buck lumber (LF — SIP construction only; pressure-treated 2× stock, auto-calculated from rough opening perimeter × buck depth)
* Window flashing kit (EA: 1 per window — self-adhered membrane at all four sides of rough opening; required for all wall types)
* Window sill pan flashing (LF — sloped sill pan at bottom of rough opening to drain any water past the exterior casing; required for all wall types)
* Window trim — exterior casing (LF — auto-calculated from rough opening perimeter)
* Expansion foam sealant (tubes — gap between frame and rough opening; 1 tube per 4 windows typical)
All lines added at Tier 2. Move to Quoted or Committed when window unit price is confirmed.
Lead time flag: windows require 3–8 weeks for custom or non-stock sizes. For SIP construction: lead time flag fires when any window is at Tier 2 or higher — “SIP panel order requires confirmed window dimensions — confirm all window sizes before placing panel order.” For non-SIP: order 3–4 weeks before framing inspection.
Skylights
A skylight is a roof-mounted glazed opening. It admits daylight from above, which distributes more evenly through a space than side-window light. It also introduces a roof penetration — the highest-risk location for water infiltration — and a significant thermal liability in Kansas’s climate.
Thermal performance of skylights vs vertical windows:
A skylight at the same U-factor as a vertical window performs worse thermally for two reasons. First, heat rises — the interior air immediately below a skylight is the warmest air in the room, so the temperature differential driving heat loss is higher than through a vertical wall window. Second, horizontal or near-horizontal glazing admits significantly more solar radiation in summer. The summer sun angle in Kansas (70°+ above horizon at solar noon) hits a horizontal skylight nearly perpendicularly — maximum solar heat gain — while a south-facing vertical window at that same angle is largely shaded by its frame and overhang. A skylight with SHGC-0.25 admits more total solar heat in Kansas summer than a vertical south window with SHGC-0.40.
Net result for Zone 4A Kansas: specify skylights with SHGC 0.20 or lower and U-factor 0.25 or lower — better than the code minimums for vertical windows — to partially offset their inherent thermal disadvantage. Even with these specifications, a skylight in a heated living space is a net thermal liability compared to an equivalent area of well-insulated roof. The correct justification for a skylight is daylighting quality and psychological benefit, not energy performance.


Roof compatibility — incompatibility flags:
* SIP roof panels — BLOCKED for field-cut skylights: cutting through SIP panels compromises the structural diaphragm, creates thermal bridges at cut edges, and voids the panel manufacturer’s structural warranty. Skylights in SIP roofs must be specified at panel manufacture as factory-cut openings with reinforced spline perimeters. The tool blocks skylight placement on SIP roof surfaces unless the “Ordered with panel” checkbox is checked on the skylight record. Flag text: “Skylights in SIP roofs must be specified at panel manufacture — field-cutting a SIP panel compromises the structural diaphragm and panel warranty.”
* Solar reservation zones — BLOCKED: a skylight placed within the solar array reservation zone eliminates solar panel capacity in that area (Section 10.5). Roof plan layer conflict check fires immediately. Resolution: relocate skylight outside the reservation zone, or reduce array size.
* Standing seam metal roofing — caution: requires a manufacturer-specific curb adapter that integrates with the seam profile. Standard residential skylight curb will not seal correctly. Tier 2 amber flag: “Standing seam roofing requires a manufacturer-specific skylight curb adapter — confirm curb compatibility with the roofing system before ordering.”
* Exposed fastener metal panels — not recommended: flashing at a skylight curb is difficult to achieve reliably with lapped metal seams. Tier 2 amber advisory fires.
* Cathedral ceiling — special requirement: no attic space for a light well. Skylight well sides must be insulated to the same standard as the roof assembly. Uninsulated skylight well sides create a thermal bridge. Tier 2 amber flag fires: “Cathedral ceiling — skylight well sides must be insulated. Include insulated skylight well in framing package.”
Skylight types:
* Fixed skylight: no operable sash, lowest cost, lowest leak risk. Provides daylighting only. Correct choice for any skylight in a tight building where ventilation is handled by the ERV/HRV. Recommended for Kansas living quarters.
* Venting skylight: operable sash for ventilation. Higher cost and leak risk. In a tight building with an ERV, ventilation benefit is largely redundant. In Kansas where summer temperatures regularly exceed 95°F, opening a skylight for cooling is counterproductive for most of the cooling season. Not recommended for Kansas living quarters unless owner has a specific reason.
* Tubular daylighting device (TDD): 10"–14" diameter reflective tube from a small roof dome to a ceiling diffuser. Much smaller roof penetration than a standard skylight, significantly lower leak and thermal risk, can turn corners. Excellent for bringing daylight into interior bathrooms, hallways, or closets without a full skylight installation. Rated by solar light transmittance (SLT) rather than U-factor/SHGC.
App fields for skylights: skylight type (Fixed / Venting / TDD), rough opening dimensions, U-factor and SHGC (from NFRC label, required for conditioned zone), roof surface reference (drives compatibility checks), SIP panel order flag (required for SIP roofs), solar reservation zone conflict check (auto).
Takeoff lines for skylights:
* Skylight unit (EA — by type, size, U-factor, SHGC)
* Skylight curb (EA: 1 per skylight — pre-built or site-built; standing seam requires manufacturer-specific curb adapter)
* Skylight flashing kit (EA: 1 per skylight — manufacturer-supplied for the specific roofing type)
* Skylight well framing (LF of 2× lumber — sides of well from roof to ceiling; non-cathedral ceiling construction only)
* Skylight well insulation (SF of rigid foam — required for cathedral ceiling applications)
* Skylight well finish (SF — interior finish on well sides)


7. Trade Knowledge — All Trades Including Licensed
The tool provides full knowledge for all trades including licensed trades (electrical, plumbing, HVAC, gas). The owner intends to do work themselves and have licensed professionals check and final the work. Knowledge is provided for owner-prep work, coordination, inspection readiness, and the watch items that prevent costly mistakes.


7.1 Electrical — NEC 2020
Owner-Buildable Work
* Conduit sleeves under slab — Schedule 40 PVC, sweep elbows (not standard elbows — wire must pull through), sand fill during pour to prevent crushing. Set before the pour, electrician pulls wire after cure.
* Inter-building conduit in trench — install during site work while equipment is on site. Always install more conduits than currently needed. Cap and mark ends. Electrician pulls wire when ready.
* Drilling framing penetrations, pulling wire through pre-drilled studs before electrician arrives
* Installing electrical boxes at correct heights before rough-in inspection
* SIP service chase installation — horizontal strapping on interior SIP face


Electrical Calculations
* Panel sizing uses NEC demand factors — not a simple sum of all appliances. 100A typical for small living quarters. 200A if welding equipment, EV charger, large HVAC, or solar with battery.
* Solar: grid-tied requires bidirectional meter and interconnect agreement with rural electric co-op. Battery hybrid requires critical loads sub-panel and transfer switch or gateway device. No solar = standard panel only.
* AFCI protection required in all living spaces (NEC 2020 210.12). LIVING QUARTERS zone type activates AFCI on all bedroom and living area circuits automatically. KITCHEN zone type activates AFCI on all kitchen circuits except small appliance circuits (small appliance circuits require GFCI, not AFCI per NEC 2020 210.12 exception).
* GFCI required: bathrooms, kitchen, garage, exterior, any open-face zone with weatherproof fixtures. VEHICLE SERVICE PIT zone type: GFCI on every outlet in and around the pit — NEC 2020 Section 511 (automotive service areas). All outlets within 10 ft of pit opening are treated as wet locations. KITCHEN zone type: GFCI within 6 ft of all sink locations, all countertop circuits. COLD STORAGE zone type: GFCI on all lighting and outlets.
* Dedicated circuits: refrigerator, microwave, dishwasher, washer, water heater, HVAC, radiant pump, EV charger, welder, sump pump (20A GFCI required per NEC 2020 210.8 — sump pump circuit must be on a GFCI breaker, not just a GFCI outlet. If a battery backup sump pump is installed, its battery charger requires its own dedicated circuit. Include sump pump in the critical loads sub-panel if generator backup is planned — power outages in Kansas are storm-caused, and storms are exactly when the sump runs hardest). ZONE-TYPE-ACTIVATED DEDICATED CIRCUITS: Kitchen zone type adds: two 20A small appliance circuits at countertops (NEC 2020 210.52 — mandatory, not optional), dishwasher 20A dedicated, refrigerator dedicated, microwave dedicated. Vehicle Service Pit zone type adds: pit lighting circuit (explosion-proof rated fixtures in pit), ventilation fan circuit. Living Quarters zone type adds: smoke detector circuit (hardwired per IRC R314), CO detector circuit (hardwired per IRC R315 if fuel-burning appliances present). These circuits add to the panel load calculation automatically when the zone type is selected.
* 240V circuits: welder (50A), EV charger (50A), electric water heater (30A), mini-split (20-30A per unit)
* CSST gas bonding: if CSST flexible gas tubing is used, it must be bonded to the electrical grounding system — coordination point between gas and electrical trades


Electrical — SIP Interaction
* SIP walls: service chase (horizontal blocking on interior face, Romex behind drywall) — no conduit needed if chase is used
* SIP walls with exposed interior: conduit on surface — plan conduit routing before panels are set
* SIP roof: all electrical on underside in conduit — no attic runs possible


Electrical Visual Layer — See Section 5.6.2The complete visual design grammar for the electrical layer is specified in Section 5.6.2. Key requirements that affect electrical data model fields:PANEL FACE: every panel record must store: total amperage, number of slots, panel brand and model (required for interlock kit compatibility), per-slot assignment (circuit number, description, amperage, breaker type). The panel face visual renders from these fields. Panel amperage and slot count cannot be left at default — they are required fields before the electrical layer is considered complete.ONE-LINE DIAGRAM: every electrical system decision (solar intent, battery hybrid, generator connection, sub-panels, critical loads sub-panel) writes a node into the one-line diagram data structure. The diagram auto-generates from these nodes. The data structure must be maintained in the electrical record — not derived on-the-fly at render time — so that it can be exported with the builder brief and trade packages.CIRCUIT RECORDS: each circuit must store: circuit number, panel slot, description, amperage, breaker type (standard/AFCI/GFCI/dual-function/2-pole), wire gauge (auto-calculated from amperage and run length), list of device IDs on the circuit, home-run length. These fields feed the panel face, the floor plan coloring, and the volt-drop calculation.Generator Interlock — Panel Integration
When generator connection intent is set to Yes in the backup power module (Section 7.6), the electrical knowledge package expands to include the interlock specification. A manual interlock kit is the code-legal owner-installable alternative to a full transfer switch — it mechanically prevents the main breaker and generator backfeed breaker from being on simultaneously, which is the NEC 2020 requirement for generator interconnection. It costs $50–150 vs $800–2000 for an automatic transfer switch and is the correct choice for a manual-start portable or standby generator.
Interlock requirements:
The interlock kit is panel brand and model specific — Siemens, Square D, Eaton, and Leviton each have their own kit that fits only their panel. The panel brand decision must be made and the specific panel model confirmed before the interlock is ordered. A generator backfeed breaker is installed in a specific slot in the panel (typically bottom right or as specified by the interlock kit instructions) — this slot must be reserved when the panel is first wired. If the panel is fully loaded before the interlock slot is reserved, adding generator capability requires either a panel replacement or a separate critical loads sub-panel. Critical loads sub-panel is the better long-term design: a small secondary panel fed from the main, containing only the circuits that must run during an outage (well pump, refrigerator, one lighting circuit, one HVAC circuit). The generator feeds the sub-panel through the interlock, not the main panel — a smaller generator can power the critical loads sub-panel where it could not power the full main panel load.
Claude Prompt — Generator Integration (fires when generator connection intent set to Yes):
When the owner sets generator connection intent to Yes, the tool collects the following generator profile before generating the Claude prompt: generator fuel type (propane / gasoline / diesel / natural gas), running watts, surge watts, start type (manual portable / manual standby / automatic standby), panel brand and model, critical loads selected from circuit list (checkboxes), whether a critical loads sub-panel is planned, and propane tank size if fuel type is propane. The tool then fires a Claude prompt containing this profile plus the full electrical load summary from Section 7.1, and requests: (1) interlock kit part number for the confirmed panel brand/model, (2) minimum generator size to run the selected critical loads simultaneously, (3) propane consumption estimate per hour at that load if fuel type is propane, (4) whether the existing propane tank size is adequate for a 72-hour outage at that consumption rate, and (5) any NEC 2020 compliance items specific to this configuration. The Claude prompt output is saved to the building record and surfaced in the electrical knowledge package alongside the standard circuit list and panel schedule.
7.1a Wire Type Taxonomy, Conduit Type Taxonomy, and Conduit Fill
The electrical takeoff requires three distinct specifications per run: the wire type, the conduit type (or none for direct burial and Romex), and whether multiple conductors share a conduit. These three choices interact — the wire type must be rated for the conduit type and burial condition, and the conduit size must be checked for fill compliance when multiple conductors share it. Every Circuit Record stores all three fields. The takeoff engine derives the correct wire footage and conduit footage from them.
Wire Type Taxonomy
* NM-B (Romex) — non-metallic sheathed cable. Used in dry, interior, protected locations: inside conditioned stud wall bays, above accessible ceilings, in attics. Available in 14/2, 14/3, 12/2, 12/3, 10/2, 10/3, 8/3 (gauge/conductor count, always includes a ground). Sold in rolls: 25, 50, 100, 250 ft by gauge. Cannot be used outdoors, in wet locations, in concrete, or underground. Cannot be pulled through conduit except for short protective sections (EMT sleeve at panel entry, for example). The tool flags any NM-B run assigned to a conduit segment that is buried, outdoor-rated, or in-slab.
* THWN-2 — thermoplastic heat- and moisture-resistant wire, rated 90”C wet. The standard conductor for conduit runs — indoor conduit, outdoor conduit, underground conduit, in-slab conduit. Pulled as individual conductors (one hot, one neutral, one ground per circuit) through a shared conduit. Multiple circuits can share a conduit subject to fill limits and derating rules. Available in all gauges 14 AWG through 4/0 AWG and larger. Sold by the foot off a spool at electrical supply houses, or in 500-ft and 1000-ft spools. The tool records individual conductor footage per circuit and calculates total conductor footage per conduit run for fill checking.
* XHHW-2 — cross-linked polyethylene insulation, 90”C wet, higher temperature rating than THWN-2. Preferred for service entrance runs, solar inverter wiring, any run near a heat source (boiler room, attic in summer), and long feeder runs where the higher temperature rating reduces derating losses. Same sold-as format as THWN-2. Interchangeable with THWN-2 for fill calculations — same outside diameter by gauge.
* USE-2 / RHW-2 — underground service entrance cable. Required for solar panel wiring exposed on the roof (from panel to combiner box or junction box), and for any outdoor exposed run that will be UV-exposed without conduit. Rated for direct burial, wet locations, and UV exposure. If the solar array uses exposed wiring on the roof surface, USE-2 is the correct wire type — THWN-2 and NM-B are not UV-rated. Sold by the foot or in spools.
* UF-B (underground feeder) — direct burial cable without conduit. Gray sheath, rated for direct burial and wet locations. Used for branch circuits where running conduit is impractical (e.g. a simple 120V circuit to a detached outbuilding, well pump circuit, yard lighting). Available in 14/2, 12/2, 10/2, 10/3. Sold in rolls: 25, 50, 100, 250 ft by gauge. NEC minimum burial depth: 24” for 120/240V branch circuits without conduit. Cannot be spliced underground — must be a single continuous run from the source to the load, or run through a listed underground junction box. The tool flags any UF-B run with a mid-run splice point that is not at a junction box.
* SE cable (service entrance) — service entrance from utility meter to main panel. Typically installed by the utility or a licensed electrician. Included in the takeoff for completeness but flagged as “Licensed electrician required for service entrance” and excluded from the owner-buildable work list.
* Low-voltage wire — thermostat wire (18/5 or 18/8 for multi-zone HVAC), doorbell wire, data cable (Cat6), speaker wire. No ampacity or conduit fill calculation required. Tracked as footage by wire type. Sold in spools: 50, 100, 250 ft.
Wire Type Selection Logic — Auto-Assignment Rules
The tool auto-assigns wire type from the circuit’s conduit type and installation method. The owner can override any auto-assignment with a reason note. Auto-assignment rules in priority order:
* Installation method = Direct Burial, no conduit — auto-assign UF-B. Flag if gauge is not available in UF-B (only 14, 12, 10 AWG available). Flag if a splice point exists that is not at a junction box.
* Installation method = In conduit, conduit type = Schedule 40 or 80 PVC underground or in-slab — auto-assign THWN-2. PVC is not UV-rated so all THWN-2 must be fully enclosed in conduit with no exposed sections.
* Installation method = In conduit, conduit type = EMT / IMC / RMC — auto-assign THWN-2. If run is near a heat source (boiler room, attic above 104”F summer temperature) — recommend upgrade to XHHW-2 and flag for owner confirmation.
* Installation method = Solar array wiring, outdoor exposed — auto-assign USE-2. If wiring is enclosed in conduit — auto-assign THWN-2 or XHHW-2.
* Installation method = Free air, interior, dry location — auto-assign NM-B. If the run passes through any conduit segment (even for physical protection) — flag: “NM-B in conduit — confirm this conduit is for physical protection only (short section) and not a wet or buried location.”
Conduit Type Taxonomy
* EMT (Electrical Metallic Tubing) — thin-wall steel conduit. Standard for all indoor exposed runs: shop walls, mechanical rooms, above drop ceilings, in open framing before drywall. Available in 1/2” through 4” trade size. Sold in 10-ft sticks. Connected with compression fittings (no threads). Not rated for direct burial, outdoor exposure without galvanizing, or concrete encasement. Lightest and cheapest metal conduit — easiest to field-bend with a hand bender.
* IMC (Intermediate Metal Conduit) — heavier wall than EMT, threaded or compression fittings. Rated for wet locations and outdoor exposed runs. Used for exterior wall runs, rooftop conduit (solar conduit from roof penetration to exterior wall), and any outdoor exposed section. Same fill tables as RMC. Can be direct buried with appropriate fittings — less common than PVC for burial because of cost. Sold in 10-ft sticks.
* RMC (Rigid Metal Conduit) — heaviest steel conduit, threaded fittings. Required for service entrance conduit (from meter to main panel) and any run subject to physical damage: under driveways, in exposed industrial locations, at equipment knock-outs where mechanical protection is critical. Direct burial rated. Sold in 10-ft sticks.
* Schedule 40 PVC conduit — standard gray plastic conduit for underground burial and in-slab sweeps. The correct choice for all inter-building underground runs (Section 7.1), all in-slab conduit stubs, and any run that needs to be buried cost-effectively. Not UV-rated — cannot be used for outdoor exposed runs unless painted or enclosed. Glued fittings. Sold in 10-ft sticks. Minimum burial depth: 18” for residential branch circuits in conduit (NEC 300.5), 24” under driveways.
* Schedule 80 PVC conduit — thicker wall than Sch 40, same outside diameter (same fill tables). Used under driveways, in locations with mechanical damage risk, or when specified by engineer. Heavier and more expensive than Sch 40 but same burial and gluing method. Required under concrete slabs subject to vehicle loading.
* FMC (Flexible Metal Conduit) — spiral-wound metal conduit for the final connection to equipment: last 6”—24” to a panel, motor, or hard-wired device that vibrates or requires alignment flexibility. Not a run conduit. Always terminates into a junction box or equipment enclosure at both ends. Sold in 25-ft and 100-ft coils. The tool flags any FMC segment longer than 36” — longer runs require a rigid conduit section.
* LFMC (Liquid-Tight Flexible Metal Conduit) — same application as FMC but with a plastic jacket for wet-location use. Required for final connection to outdoor HVAC units, outdoor generators, any wet-location equipment connection. Sold in 25-ft and 100-ft coils.
* ENT (Electrical Non-metallic Tubing) — corrugated blue flexible plastic conduit (“smurf tube”). Used inside walls before drywall for in-wall branch circuit rough-in (alternative to NM-B where conduit is preferred but rigid bending is impractical), and encased in concrete slabs and footings. Not rated for outdoor exposure. Sold in 100-ft and 200-ft coils. The tool offers ENT as an option when installation method = in-wall, zone type = living quarters or workshop (where some owners prefer conduit in walls for future rewire flexibility).
* No conduit — direct burial of UF-B cable, or NM-B in dry interior stud bays. Selected as the conduit type field value “None — Direct Burial” or “None — Free Air”. Wire type auto-assignment fires immediately when this is selected.
Conduit Type Selection Logic — Auto-Assignment Rules
The tool auto-assigns a recommended conduit type based on the run’s location and environment. The owner can select any conduit type; the auto-assignment is a starting point. Conflicts between selected conduit type and installation environment are flagged as Tier 2 amber.
* Location = underground, not under concrete slab or driveway — recommend Schedule 40 PVC.
* Location = underground, under driveway or concrete slab subject to vehicle loading — recommend Schedule 80 PVC.
* Location = in-slab sweep (pre-pour, horizontal or sweep elbow) — require Schedule 40 or 80 PVC. EMT in concrete is not permitted without special approval. Flag if EMT is selected for an in-slab segment.
* Location = indoor exposed (shop wall, mechanical room, open ceiling) — recommend EMT.
* Location = outdoor exposed — recommend IMC or RMC. Flag if EMT is selected for an outdoor exposed segment.
* Location = service entrance (meter to panel) — require RMC or IMC. Flag if Sch 40 PVC or EMT is selected.
* Location = final equipment connection (to panel knockout, motor, HVAC unit) — recommend FMC (dry) or LFMC (wet/outdoor). Flag if rigid conduit is selected for a segment under 36”.
* Location = in-wall, interior — conduit not required (NM-B is the default). If owner selects conduit for in-wall: recommend ENT.
Conduit Fill Calculation — NEC 2020 Table C
Every conduit segment that carries more than one conductor is subject to NEC 2020 conduit fill limits. The tool calculates fill percentage automatically when conductors are assigned to a conduit run, and flags over-fill before the segment is confirmed.
How fill is calculated: each conductor assigned to a conduit run contributes its cross-sectional area (based on wire type and gauge per NEC Table 5). The sum of all conductor cross-sectional areas divided by the conduit’s usable interior area (per NEC Table 4 for the conduit type and trade size) = fill percentage. NEC 2020 rule: maximum 40% fill for 3 or more conductors; 31% for 2 conductors; 53% for 1 conductor.
Fill limits by common configurations (EMT, for reference): 1/2” EMT — maximum 9 conductors of 14 AWG THWN-2, or 7 of 12 AWG, or 4 of 10 AWG. 3/4” EMT — 16 of 14 AWG, 12 of 12 AWG, 7 of 10 AWG. 1” EMT — 26 of 14 AWG, 19 of 12 AWG, 12 of 10 AWG. Schedule 40 PVC has slightly different interior areas — the tool uses the correct table per conduit type.
Conductor count rules: grounds count toward fill. Neutrals count toward fill. A standard 20A circuit (hot + neutral + ground) = 3 conductors. Three 20A circuits sharing a conduit = 9 conductors. A 240V 2-pole circuit (two hots + ground, no neutral) = 3 conductors. A multi-wire branch circuit (two hots + shared neutral + ground) = 4 conductors.
Derating: when 4—6 current-carrying conductors share a conduit, the ampacity of each must be derated to 80% of its rated value (NEC 310.15(C)(1)). When 7—9 conductors share a conduit, derate to 70%. The tool applies derating automatically and flags if derating reduces a conductor’s ampacity below the breaker size assigned to that circuit.
Conduit fill UI: the conduit segment property panel shows a live fill bar: used area vs available area, current fill percentage, count of conductors assigned, and a list of assigned conductors by circuit. When fill exceeds 40%, the bar turns red and a Tier 1 flag fires: “Conduit overfull — [X]% fill. Upgrade conduit to [next size] or move [N] conductors to a separate conduit.” The tool suggests the minimum conduit size that would clear the flag given the current conductor count.
INTER-BUILDING CONDUIT NOTE: the recommendation in Section 7.1 to install more conduits than currently needed is specifically about fill headroom. The conduit fill calculation makes this concrete: if you are installing 3 circuits today (9 conductors at 40% fill in 3/4” EMT) and anticipate 2 more circuits in the future, install a second 3/4” EMT conduit now. The fill calculation panel in the site utilities layer shows current fill plus a “planned future circuits” field that lets the owner see whether the current conduit size will accommodate the planned expansion.
7.2 Plumbing — UPC
Owner-Buildable Rough-In Prep
* Drain line layout and installation before slab pour — ABS or PVC, 1/4" per foot slope (no more, no less), torpedo level for checking slope
* Toilet flange: center 12" from finished wall face, set to finished floor height. If tile is planned, account for tile thickness when setting flange height — this is a common mistake.
* Shower drain with trap
* Every fixture needs a trap. Every trap needs venting — vent stack through roof or air admittance valve (Kansas code allows AAV in most cases)
* PEX supply sleeves through slab — install before pour, leave capped


Wet Wall Requirements
* The wet wall carrying the main drain stack must be 2x6 minimum framing — 3" or 4" stack needs the extra depth
* Wet wall location must be decided before framing — it is a structural framing decision
* All fixtures should share a stack whenever possible — distance from fixture to stack is a direct cost driver at 1/4" per foot slope


Sump Pump & Drain Tile SystemSUBSURFACE DRAINAGE DECISION: the decision to add a sump pump and drain tile must be made before the pre-pour document is finalized — all drain tile and the sump pit liner go in before the slab is poured. This is not a finish item. The decision is driven by: site drainage condition (Section 2), water table depth, building use (finished living space is higher stakes than open barn storage), and foundation type. On Miami County clay soil, any finished floor below grade or any heated slab in a low-lying location warrants serious evaluation.Owner-Buildable Rough-In Prep:Set sump pit liner in granular fill layer — before foam, vapor barrier, or rebar. Liner must be plumb and at correct depth so the top of the liner sits 2" above the finished slab surface after the pour. Set the discharge sleeve through the foundation wall at the correct elevation before the footing or stem wall pour on that side. Install interior perimeter drain tile (4" perforated pipe, filter fabric sock, washed gravel bed) if interior drain tile system is selected. Run to sump pit, slope minimum 1/8" per foot. Check all slopes with a torpedo level before foam goes down.Sump Pump Selection:1/3 HP adequate for most residential applications. 1/2 HP for high-water-table or large footprint buildings. Cast iron preferred over thermoplastic for longevity in continuous-duty applications. PRIMARY + BATTERY BACKUP recommended for this property: Kansas storms cause both flooding and power outages simultaneously. A battery backup sump pump in the same pit activates when power fails and when the primary pump is overwhelmed. The primary pump is AC-powered on its own dedicated GFCI circuit. The battery backup pump uses a 12V battery kept charged by a trickle charger on a second dedicated circuit.Discharge Requirements:Discharge pipe exits through the foundation wall sleeve set during foundation work. Check valve required on the discharge line inside the pit — prevents water from flowing back in when the pump shuts off. Discharge must terminate minimum 10 ft from the foundation on a slope that carries water away. Do not discharge toward the septic drain field. In Kansas winters, the discharge terminus must be designed to prevent freeze-back in the pipe — use a freeze-resistant discharge fitting or ensure the outlet slopes continuously to drain when the pump is off.Code Reference: IRC R405 — Foundation Drainage. Drain tile at footing perimeter, sloped to outlet, filter fabric required. Coordinate with building inspector — Miami County may require this as a mandatory element for heated slabs on clay soil.Greywater System — Kansas Regulatory Flag
Greywater Module — Miami County Kansas
What greywater is and why it matters for this project
Greywater is wastewater from sinks, showers, and laundry — everything except toilet and kitchen sink drain water (which is blackwater). On a rural property with a septic system, every gallon of greywater diverted from the septic reduces the load on the drain field, extending its service life and reducing the risk of saturation on Miami County clay soil. The benefit is real. Whether it is achievable depends entirely on county approval.
The call — what to ask Miami County Health Department
Call Miami County Environmental Health: (913) 294-4340.
Script: “I’m planning a new agricultural building with living quarters in Miami County. I’m wondering whether Kansas allows greywater reuse systems — specifically diverting sink and shower drain water to a separate collection tank for toilet flushing or subsurface irrigation. Does Miami County permit this, and if so, what approvals are required?”
Record the name of the person you spoke with, the date, and their exact answer. This becomes a project record. Enter the result in the Greywater Status field in the app — Approved, Denied, or Conditional.
Regulatory context
Kansas does not have a statewide greywater reuse code. The Kansas Department of Health and Environment (KDHE) defers greywater regulation to county health departments. Most Kansas counties default to requiring all wastewater — including greywater — to route to the permitted septic system. Miami County’s position is unknown until the call is made. Both Approved and Denied outcomes are acceptable answers that fully resolve the module. Do not design the slab rough-in around greywater until the call produces a confirmed answer — the separation happens at the slab and cannot be retrofitted.
BRANCH A — DENIED
If Miami County does not permit greywater reuse, all sink, shower, and laundry drain water routes to the main drain stack and on to the septic system. No changes to the standard plumbing spec.
App actions on Denied:
* Greywater Status field set to Denied
* Any greywater separation line items are removed from the pre-pour coordination document and the plumbing trade package
* Plumbing rough-in spec shows all fixture drains connecting to the main stack — no separation tee, no collection tank stub, no irrigation line
* Septic fixture count uses full fixture unit tally (no greywater offset)
* A permanent note appears on the plumbing trade package: “Greywater reuse denied by Miami County Health Dept — [date] — [contact name]. All fixture drains route to main stack and septic.”
* The Greywater module status changes to Complete — Denied
* No further action required on this module
BRANCH B — APPROVED
If Miami County approves greywater reuse, the following spec applies. Do not proceed with any of this until Approved status is confirmed and any required county permit or approval letter is in hand.
Qualifying fixtures (greywater sources):
Bathroom sinks, showers, and bathtubs. Laundry machine drain — check with county, as some approvals exclude laundry due to detergent content.
Disqualifying fixtures (blackwater — always to septic):
Toilets, kitchen sink, dishwasher, any drain that may carry food waste or fecal matter.
Slab rough-in — what goes in before the pour:
The greywater separation happens at the drain stub level inside the slab. Each qualifying fixture has its drain directed to a greywater branch line rather than the main blackwater stack. This requires a separate below-slab branch that runs parallel to the main drain system and exits the slab at a different point.
Pre-pour items added to the coordination document:
* Greywater branch line — ABS or PVC, minimum 2" diameter, sloped 1/4" per foot toward the collection tank exit point
* Greywater branch exit sleeve through the foundation wall or slab edge — located at the collection tank position
* Blackwater stub-outs unchanged — toilets and kitchen drain remain on the main stack as normal
* Each qualifying fixture rough-in must have a tee at the trap arm level routing to the greywater branch rather than the main stack — these tee locations are pre-pour items and must appear on the coordination drawing
Collection tank:
* Minimum 20-gallon polyethylene tank, up to 50 gallons depending on fixture count and intended reuse volume
* Located adjacent to the building exterior or in a below-grade vault — tank location set on the site plan layer before slab is poured (the exit sleeve from the slab must point toward the tank location)
* Tank must have a lid, overflow line back to the main septic line (required — if the tank fills, overflow must go to septic, not to ground), and an access port for inspection and pumping
* Overflow line is a separate sleeve through the foundation — must be on the pre-pour drawing
Reuse options (confirm which are approved in the county’s specific approval):
* Toilet flushing: supply line from tank (filtered and pumped) to toilet cisterns. Requires a dedicated supply loop — not connected to the potable water supply under any circumstances. Cross-connection prevention is a code requirement (UPC Section 603).
* Subsurface drip irrigation: below-grade emitters only — no surface spray, no vegetable gardens, no areas where people walk. Requires a separate distribution line from the tank.
Filtration:
Minimum: a debris screen or filter on the tank inlet to catch hair and solids. More robust: a small inline filter before the reuse pump. County may specify filtration requirements in the approval — confirm before selecting components.
Takeoff lines added (Approved branch only):
* Greywater branch pipe: ABS or PVC 2" (LF — geometry derived from floor plan branch routing)
* Separation tees at each qualifying fixture trap arm (count — one per qualifying fixture)
* Greywater exit sleeve through foundation (count: 1 for branch line + 1 for overflow)
* Collection tank (EA — specify volume in gallons)
* Tank inlet screen/filter (EA: 1)
* Overflow line — ABS or PVC 2" from tank to septic connection point (LF)
* Reuse pump if toilet flushing (EA: 1 — 12V or 120V submersible, confirm with county whether pump is required)
* Reuse supply line from tank to toilet cisterns if toilet flushing (LF — PVC or PE, 1/2" minimum)
* Budget line items for all of the above added at Concept status on Approved confirmation, moving to Estimated after Claude Prompt 1 runs for this assembly
App actions on Approved:
* Greywater Status field set to Approved
* Separation tees added to Fixture Records for all qualifying fixtures
* Greywater branch line added to Pipe Segment Records (separate from main drain records)
* Exit sleeve and overflow sleeve added to pre-pour coordination document as pre-pour blockers (must be located before pre-pour document can be generated)
* Collection tank placed on site plan layer (user drags to location — required before exit sleeve orientation can be confirmed)
* All takeoff lines above added to the plumbing takeoff
* Plumbing trade package greywater section populates
* A note appears on the plumbing trade package: “Greywater reuse approved by Miami County Health Dept — [date] — [contact name]. Approval documentation filed in project records.”
* The Greywater module status changes to Complete — Approved
Septic load offset (Approved branch only):
The fixture unit count used for septic sizing is reduced by the greywater fixtures diverted. Typical offset: bathroom sink = 2 FU, shower = 2 FU, bathtub = 3 FU, laundry = 3 FU. The app recalculates the building FU total on Approved status and updates the septic capacity check accordingly. The county may require that the septic system still be sized for full load regardless of greywater diversion — confirm in the approval.
A note on sequencing
The greywater call should happen before the floor plan is finalized — not because the plumbing layout changes dramatically, but because the pre-pour document needs to know which branch the project is on before any drain rough-in coordinates are locked. Making the call early eliminates a class of rework entirely.
ZONE-TYPE-SPECIFIC PLUMBING REQUIREMENTS:VEHICLE SERVICE PIT: oil/water separator on pit floor drain is mandatory — petroleum-contaminated water cannot discharge to septic system under Kansas environmental regulations. Separator tank sized for pit floor area. Separator location on pre-pour drawing. Periodic pumping/disposal of accumulated oil is owner maintenance responsibility. Pit sump if water table is a concern (clay soil on this property — it is).KITCHEN: dishwasher requires dedicated 20A circuit AND an air gap fitting mounted above the sink — the air gap prevents drain backflow into the dishwasher (UPC requirement). Ice maker water line stub at refrigerator location — 1/4" compression fitting stub-out, capped, in wall behind refrigerator position. Install at rough-in even if no ice maker is planned immediately — retrofitting later requires opening the wall. Kitchen sink drain: if island sink is planned, AAV (air admittance valve) is the practical venting solution — running a vent stack through the center of the floor and up through the island is possible but complex.COLD STORAGE / ROOT CELLAR: no vapor barrier on cold storage floor — this is the opposite of every other zone and must be explicitly noted on pre-pour drawing and vapor barrier installer instructions. Moisture from earth is intentional — it is the humidity source that keeps produce from desiccating. Sealing the floor defeats the purpose. Optional floor drain connects to main drain system (not septic). No supply plumbing required.Pressure Reducing Valve (PRV) — Mandatory Item:
The water source is a metered rural district supply to the existing barn. Rural water district supply pressure in Kansas typically runs 80–100 psi — above the 80 psi maximum for residential supply lines per UPC. High supply pressure causes: water hammer throughout the system, shortened fixture and valve lifespan, elevated water heater pressure relief valve cycling, and dripping faucets. A pressure reducing valve (PRV) installed at the service entry point where the supply line enters the building reduces this to 60–75 psi. A PRV with integral gauge costs $40–80 and is a one-time installation at rough-in. It is a mandatory line item in the plumbing materials list for every building on this property that receives district water supply, not an optional upgrade. Install on the supply side before the first branch tee. Set to 65 psi. Install a union on both sides of the PRV so it can be replaced without cutting pipe.
7.2a Plumbing Data Records
The visual grammar in Section 5.6.3 defines the riser diagram, pipe sizing labels, and two-way linking for the plumbing layer. Those visuals auto-generate from the following data records. Every field listed below must be captured in the data model so the riser diagram, pre-pour coordination document, trade package, and plumbing takeoff can all derive from a single source of truth with no double entry.
Fixture Record
One record per plumbing fixture. Fields: fixture type (toilet / sink / shower / bathtub / floor drain / hose bib / washing machine / dishwasher / water heater / ice maker stub); zone ID (links to zone record); floor level; rough-in dimensions (centerline to finished wall for toilet: 12" standard; drain centerline coordinates on floor plan); fixture unit count (UPC Table 702.1 — toilet = 4 FU, sink = 2 FU, shower = 2 FU, bathtub = 3 FU, floor drain = 2 FU, dishwasher = 2 FU, washing machine = 3 FU); supply pipe size (cold and hot: 1/2" standard, 3/4" for water heater and washing machine); drain pipe size (auto-calculated from fixture unit count per UPC sizing table: sink/shower = 1.5", toilet = 3" minimum); trap type (P-trap standard; S-trap not permitted; floor drain = running trap); vent method (stack vent / AAV / island AAV); associated electrical circuit ID (dishwasher, washing machine, water heater — links to electrical circuit record); planning status (Concept / Roughed-in / Confirmed). Fixture unit count from this record rolls up to the building total which drives septic system sizing (Section 5.3) and drain field sizing. The fixture unit tally is shown as a live running total in the plumbing layer sidebar.
Pipe Segment Record
One record per pipe run between two connection points (fixture to stack, stack to cleanout, cleanout to building exit). Fields: segment ID; upstream connection (fixture record ID or fitting location); downstream connection (fixture record ID, stack junction, or building exit point); pipe material (ABS / PVC / copper / PEX); pipe size (auto-calculated from fixture units served per UPC Table 703.2, overridable); flow direction; length (derived from floor plan geometry — no double entry); invert elevation at upstream end (entered or calculated from slope and downstream invert); invert elevation at downstream end (entered at building exit point, drives all upstream invert calculations); slope (calculated from invert elevations and length — flagged red if below 1/4" per foot for pipes 3" and smaller, or below 1/8" per foot for 4" and larger per UPC); fixture units served (sum of all fixtures upstream of this segment); planning status. Invert elevations for all pre-pour segments appear automatically on the pre-pour coordination document. The riser diagram (Section 5.6.3) renders from pipe segment records — pipe sizes are labeled on every segment and undersized segments shown in red.
Vent Path Record
One record per vent run. Fields: vent type (stack vent through roof / AAV / island AAV / circuit vent); connected fixture record IDs; vent pipe size (3" minimum for toilet branch per UPC); vent stack height above roof deck (12" minimum per Section 12.4 for Kansas snow country); roof penetration coordinates (links to roof plan layer penetration marker); vent segment length from fixture trap arm to stack connection or AAV location; planning status. Cathedral ceiling vent routing constraint: when a bathroom zone is assigned to a building with cathedral ceiling construction, the vent path record must specify an exit route other than through the roof panel interior. The tool flags this as a Tier 2 amber item if no vent path is resolved when cathedral ceiling is confirmed. The vent stack roof penetration coordinates feed directly into the roof plan conflict detection (Section 10.5) — if the penetration lands inside the solar reservation zone, a red conflict flag fires immediately.
Plumbing Claude Prompt — Septic Capacity Trigger
This prompt fires automatically when a fixture is added that pushes the building's total fixture unit count past the septic system's design capacity. The trigger condition: new fixture's FU count + existing building FU total > septic system design capacity (entered in the septic record). When triggered, the tool assembles a prompt containing: total fixture unit count before and after addition, septic system type and design capacity, Miami County perc test result (if recorded), number of bedrooms (Kansas uses bedroom count for residential septic sizing), and the specific fixture type added. The prompt asks Claude to: (1) confirm whether the current system design is still adequate, (2) identify the path to upsizing (larger tank, additional drain field run, or separate system), and (3) flag any Kansas KDHE permit implications of the capacity change. The prompt is presented as a Tier 2 amber queue item — it does not block adding the fixture, but it blocks the plumbing trade package from advancing to final status until the septic capacity question is resolved.
7.3 HVAC — IMC 2018 / IECC Zone 4A
System Selection by Zone Type
Zone type
	Recommended HVAC
	Notes
	Heated living quarters, radiant slab
	Radiant primary + mini-split for cooling and backup
	Radiant handles heat efficiently. Mini-split handles cooling. Do not oversize mini-split — radiant carries the load.
	Heated living quarters, no radiant
	Mini-split (heat pump mode for heat, cooling mode for AC)
	Single unit handles both. Size for cooling load in Kansas summer — heating is less demanding with good insulation.
	Heated workshop, no living
	Propane unit heater or mini-split depending on use pattern
	Unit heater is cheaper. Mini-split is more comfortable for sustained work sessions.
	Unheated / open-face zone
	None — suppress from all calculations
	Open face eliminates heating viability. Weatherproof electrical only.
	SIP construction, any heated zone
	Mini-split only or radiant only — no forced air
	SIP roof eliminates attic. SIP walls eliminate duct chases. Forced air has no viable distribution path.
	Cathedral ceiling, any heated zone
	Mini-split or radiant only
	Same constraint as SIP roof — no attic, no duct distribution.
	

HVAC Routing — What Goes in the Wall
* Mini-split: lineset (refrigerant + electrical) runs from outdoor unit through wall sleeve to indoor head unit. Sleeve must be installed before wall close-in. Sleeve is typically 3" diameter, slightly pitched toward exterior for condensate drainage.
* Condensate drain from indoor head — routes to floor drain, exterior, or drywell
* Forced air (if used — only in stud-framed zones with attic): duct chases in wall framing must be coordinated with structural framing before framing begins. Return air path must be planned — return air needs a path back to the air handler.
* Combustion air: any propane combustion appliance (boiler, fireplace, unit heater) in a well-sealed building may need a dedicated outside combustion air supply. Tight buildings create negative pressure that can back-draft appliances. Combustion air duct goes through wall before close-in.
* Exhaust fans: bathroom exhaust fan required in windowless bathrooms (IMC 2018 Section 403). Duct through wall or roof before close-in.


HVAC Load Calculation Inputs — Per Zone
The HVAC load calculation for each zone iterates across all surface records belonging to that zone. Inputs:
* Each exterior surface: area minus openings, R-value from surface record, orientation
* Each window: U-factor, SHGC (solar heat gain coefficient), orientation, overhang depth
* Zone ceiling height — affects volume and stratification behavior
* Solar gain: calculated from the actual surface bearing (0–359°) stored in the Surface Record orientation field — not snapped to the nearest cardinal direction. Bearing-based solar gain multipliers for Kansas latitude 38.5°N: true south (180°) = 1.0x baseline; SSE/SSW (150°–210°) = 0.95x; SE/SW (120°–150° or 210°–240°) = 0.80x winter gain, 0.90x summer load; E/W (60°–120° or 240°–300°) = 0.40x winter gain, 1.10x summer afternoon load (west) or 1.10x summer morning load (east); north (300°–60°) = 0.10x negligible. West-facing windows are the highest summer cooling liability — afternoon sun angle in Kansas summer drives significant load. Tool runs both winter heating and summer cooling scenarios separately, applying the appropriate bearing multiplier to each window&#x2019;s SHGC in each scenario.
* Fireplace offset: approximately 4,000 BTU heating load reduction but requires combustion air calculation
* Ceiling type: flat ceiling with attic allows R-49 blown insulation. Cathedral ceiling limits insulation to rafter depth plus rigid above deck — typically lower effective R. Stratification: heated air rises, so 14 ft ceiling with radiant floor works better than 14 ft ceiling with forced air registers at ceiling height.
* Adjacent zone thermal relationship: workshop heated, equipment bay unheated, dividing wall insulated — both zones account for the shared wall in their load calculations
Combustion Air — Tight Building Requirements:
Any fuel-burning appliance (wood stove, propane heater, propane fireplace, gas range, combustion generator) installed in a building tighter than 0.35 ACH (air changes per hour) requires a dedicated exterior combustion air supply. SIP buildings, well-insulated stud buildings with sealed penetrations, and any building that passes a blower door test below 3 ACH@50Pa are tight enough to deplete oxygen around a fuel-burning appliance. The failure mode is carbon monoxide poisoning — the appliance back-drafts when the building goes negative pressure, pulling flue gases into the living space instead of exhausting them. This is a life safety issue, not a comfort issue. Combustion air calculation per IMC 2018: total BTU/hr of all fuel-burning appliances in the space divided by 2000 = minimum combustion air opening in square inches. Example: a 40,000 BTU propane heater requires a 20 square inch combustion air opening (roughly a 4"x5" duct from exterior). Combustion air supply must be from exterior — not from an adjacent garage or crawl space. Direct-vent sealed combustion appliances (the appliance draws its own combustion air through a dedicated sealed pipe directly from exterior) are exempt from this calculation — they are the preferred option for any tight building. The tool flags any fuel-burning appliance added to a zone with construction type SIP, ICF, or any zone where blower door target is below 3 ACH@50Pa, and requires combustion air method selection: direct-vent sealed combustion (preferred) or dedicated combustion air duct (sized by calculation).
Mixing Valve Decision — Radiant Systems with Domestic Hot Water
The mixing valve is required whenever a radiant heating system shares a boiler or combi boiler with domestic hot water, and any fixture that a person can contact (shower, bathtub, lavatory faucet) is served by that hot water. IMC 2018 Section 607.1 and IRC P2723 require a thermostatic mixing valve at the domestic hot water distribution point when the water heater or boiler storage temperature exceeds 120°F. This is not optional — it is a code requirement with a life safety rationale: water at 140°F causes a third-degree burn in 5 seconds. Radiant systems run most efficiently at 90–140°F supply temperature. A system without a mixing valve either delivers dangerously hot water to fixtures or runs the radiant at sub-optimal temperature. The mixing valve solves both problems simultaneously.
TIER 1 RED BLOCKER: the mixing valve decision fires as a Tier 1 red blocker when all three of the following are true for any building: (1) radiant heat = yes for any zone; (2) the building has a boiler or combi boiler that also provides domestic hot water; (3) at least one shower, bathtub, or lavatory sink exists in the building. If all three are true and no mixing valve decision is recorded, the HVAC module cannot advance to Complete status and the plumbing trade package cannot be finalized. Displayed in the Next Decision Queue at Priority 1.75.


Three resolution paths:
Path 1 — Thermostatic mixing valve (TMV) at the domestic hot water distribution point:
A dedicated valve installed where the hot water supply leaves the boiler toward the domestic fixtures. Blends hot boiler water with cold supply water to maintain a set delivery temperature regardless of boiler temperature fluctuations. Set point: 110–120°F for residential applications. Standard solution for combi boiler systems. Above-slab plumbing rough-in item — mounts in the mechanical room near the boiler, not a pre-pour item. App action: TMV added to Fixture Record list, mechanical room rough-in list updated, budget line item added at Concept status.
Path 2 — Combi boiler with integrated mixing valve confirmed:
Some combi boiler models include an integral mixing valve or modulating domestic hot water heat exchanger that maintains safe delivery temperature without a separate valve. If the owner selects a combi boiler model with this feature confirmed in the product spec sheet, Path 2 satisfies the requirement. App action: boiler Equipment Record requires an “integrated mixing confirmed” checkbox that must be checked before the blocker clears. This checkbox blocks the Equipment Record from advancing to Confirmed until the product spec sheet is uploaded or the feature is verified by model number.
Path 3 — Radiant-only boiler, domestic hot water from a separate dedicated water heater:
If the radiant boiler serves only the radiant loops and domestic hot water is provided by a separate dedicated water heater (electric, propane tank, or tankless), the mixing valve requirement does not apply to the boiler. The separate water heater is set to 120°F maximum delivery temperature at its own thermostat. App action: a note is added to the HVAC and plumbing records: “Radiant boiler serves radiant only — domestic hot water from separate water heater. Mixing valve not required at boiler. Confirm water heater thermostat set to 120°F maximum.” The blocker clears.
App field: Mixing valve decision (TMV installed / Combi with integral mixing confirmed / Radiant only, separate DHW / Not decided). Tier 1 red blocker until one of the first three options is selected and confirmed.
Equipment Model Selection Path — From BTU to Confirmed Spec
The Manual J simplified calculation produces a BTU/hr heating load and BTU/hr cooling load per zone. This is the minimum required capacity — not the equipment size to order. The following steps convert a BTU number to a confirmed make/model.
Step 1 — Standard size selection:
Mini-split standard sizes: 6,000 / 9,000 / 12,000 / 15,000 / 18,000 / 24,000 / 30,000 / 36,000 BTU/hr nominal cooling capacity. The tool selects the smallest standard size that meets or exceeds the larger of the heating load and cooling load for the zone.
Oversizing rule: do not select more than 1.5× the calculated cooling load. Oversized mini-splits short-cycle — they reach setpoint quickly, shut off, allow humidity to rebuild, and restart — never running long enough to dehumidify properly. This is a real comfort problem in humid summer climates. The tool flags any mini-split selection more than 1.5× the cooling load with a Tier 2 amber warning.


Step 2 — Cold-climate rating check:
For any zone where the mini-split is the primary or only heat source, the unit must be rated to operate at the property design heating temperature. Standard mini-splits lose significant capacity below 0°F and may shut down entirely below -13°F. Cold-climate (hyper-heat) units from Mitsubishi, Daikin, Fujitsu, and others maintain rated capacity down to -13°F or lower. The tool flags any mini-split selected for a heating-primary role where the manufacturer’s rated minimum outdoor operating temperature for heating is above property.design_heating_temp_f.
Step 3 — Spec sheet upload and field locking:
Confirming an Equipment Record from Roughed-in to Confirmed requires: manufacturer and model number entered, rated heating capacity at design temperature confirmed, rated cooling capacity confirmed, electrical circuit spec confirmed (auto-populates the linked circuit record), and lineset diameter confirmed (auto-populates the Lineset Record). Spec sheet upload is optional but strongly recommended — the spec sheet URL or PDF is stored on the Equipment Record and appears in the HVAC trade package. If no spec sheet is uploaded, the Equipment Record shows an amber advisory: “No spec sheet on file — verify field installation matches specified unit.”
Step 4 — Multi-zone outdoor unit coordination:
If one outdoor unit serves multiple indoor heads (multi-zone mini-split), the outdoor unit capacity must equal or exceed the sum of all indoor head capacities at design conditions. A 0.8 diversity factor may be applied if zones have offset peak demand (bedrooms cooled at night, living area during day). The diversity factor is shown transparently with a note: “Diversity factor of 0.8 applied — assumes zones do not reach peak demand simultaneously. Verify with HVAC contractor.”
ERV Integration with HVAC Load Calculation
The ERV introduces a fresh air load that the Manual J simplified calculation does not account for by default. In heating season, incoming fresh air at the outdoor design temperature must be heated to interior setpoint; in cooling season, incoming fresh air at outdoor design temperature must be cooled. The ERV recovers 70–85% of that energy, but the remaining 15–30% is a genuine addition to the HVAC load.
ERV fresh air load calculation:
* Heating season additional load (BTU/hr) = ERV CFM × 1.08 × (interior setpoint °F − outdoor design temp °F) × (1 − ERV sensible efficiency). Example: 60 CFM ERV, indoor 70°F, outdoor design -4°F, ERV sensible efficiency 75%: 60 × 1.08 × 74 × 0.25 = 1,198 BTU/hr added heating load.
* Cooling season additional load (BTU/hr) = ERV CFM × 1.6 × (outdoor design temp °F − interior setpoint °F) × (1 − ERV sensible efficiency). The 1.6 multiplier approximates combined sensible + latent load for humid summer conditions.
App behavior: when an ERV Equipment Record is added to a building, the HVAC load calculation for affected zones automatically adds the ERV fresh air load to both heating and cooling totals. ERV efficiency values (sensible and latent efficiency %) are taken from the Equipment Record. If not yet entered, the tool uses conservative defaults (sensible 70%, latent 60%) and flags them with a provenance badge: “Estimated — enter from spec sheet for accurate load calculation.”
App fields added to ERV Equipment Record: sensible efficiency % (default 70%); latent efficiency % (default 60%); rated CFM at 0.1" static pressure (the standard rating condition). All three are required fields for accurate load calculation.
Ventilation Strategy Selection
The ventilation strategy decision determines whether the building uses an ERV, HRV, or exhaust-only ventilation. This decision is driven by climate zone and blower door target.
ERV — required when:
* Building blower door target is below 3.0 ACH@50Pa (IECC R403.6 mandatory mechanical ventilation threshold), AND
* Climate zone is 4A through 7, AND
* The building has any Living Quarters zone type.
ERV is the correct default for any tight residential building in Zone 4A. See Section 16 ERV/HRV module for full selection rationale.
HRV — acceptable when:
* Climate zone is 6, 7, or 8 (cold to very cold, low summer humidity), OR
* The building is a basement-only application where winter moisture removal is the priority.
HRV is not recommended for Zone 4A.
Exhaust-only — acceptable only when:
* Building blower door test result is confirmed above 3.0 ACH@50Pa (natural infiltration provides adequate fresh air), OR
* The zone is a workshop, storage, or agricultural zone with no sleeping areas and no continuous occupancy.
Exhaust-only is never acceptable for a Living Quarters zone targeting below 3.0 ACH@50Pa. If selected, a Tier 1 red blocker fires: “Exhaust-only ventilation does not meet IECC R403.6 for buildings below 3.0 ACH@50Pa with living quarters. Select ERV or HRV.”


App field: Ventilation strategy (ERV / HRV / Exhaust-only / Not decided). Auto-recommended from property.climate_zone and building blower door target.
Radiant System Accessories — Boiler and Distribution Package
Every hydronic radiant system requires an accessory package beyond the boiler and PEX loops. These are mechanical room rough-in items that must be installed before the system is commissioned.
Expansion tank:
Every closed hydronic system requires an expansion tank to absorb the volume increase when water heats from fill temperature (~60°F) to operating temperature (~120°F for radiant). Without it, pressure builds until the pressure relief valve opens, dumping water on the mechanical room floor. Sizing: 1 gallon of expansion tank capacity per 20 gallons of system water volume (approximate — size from manufacturer sizing chart using system volume and operating pressure). Mounted on the boiler return line. App item: added to Equipment Record list when radiant = yes.
Pressure relief valve (PRV):
Required by code on every boiler (ASME Boiler and Pressure Vessel Code, IMC 2018). Factory-installed on most boilers — confirm it is present and correctly sized for the boiler BTU output before installation. The PRV discharge pipe must route to a floor drain or terminate near the floor (not at eye level — scalding discharge hazard). Discharge location is a mechanical room rough-in item.
Fill valve and backflow preventer:
The fill valve allows the system to be refilled with fresh water when pressure drops. The backflow preventer (required by UPC) prevents system water from siphoning back into the potable supply. These are typically combined as a “fill valve assembly.” Mounted on the cold water supply line feeding the boiler. App item: added to plumbing rough-in list when radiant = yes.
Air separator and automatic air vent:
Air in a hydronic system causes gurgling, uneven heat distribution, and pump cavitation. An air separator (magnetic or centrifugal) installed on the boiler supply removes air from the circulating water. An automatic air vent at the high point of the system allows trapped air to escape. Both installed during mechanical rough-in, before the system is filled and pressurized.
Circulator pump(s):
One pump per heating zone minimum. For a single-zone radiant slab, one pump circulates water from the boiler through the manifold and loops. For multi-zone systems, zone valves or multiple pumps provide independent zone control. The tool generates a placeholder “residential circulator 1/25 HP” at Concept status and flags for confirmation when manifold port assignments are complete and total loop footage is known.
Primary/secondary loop configuration (combi boilers only):
A combi boiler serving both radiant and domestic hot water uses a primary loop (boiler to/from a hydraulic separator or closely-spaced tees) and secondary loops (radiant and DHW circuits, each with their own circulator). This prevents the radiant circulator from fighting the DHW flow. The hydraulic separator or closely-spaced tees is a mechanical room rough-in item. App field: primary/secondary loop configuration (yes / no / not applicable — auto-set to yes when combi boiler is selected).
App actions when radiant system accessories are triggered (radiant = yes):
* Expansion tank added to Equipment Record list (Concept status)
* Fill valve assembly added to plumbing rough-in list
* Air separator and automatic air vent added to Equipment Record list
* Circulator pump(s) added to Equipment Record list (count from zone count)
* PRV discharge routing added to mechanical room rough-in checklist
* Hydraulic separator added if combi boiler selected (Concept status)
* All accessories added as budget line items at Concept status, moving to Estimated when boiler Equipment Record reaches Roughed-in status


HVAC Module Complete When
The HVAC module is complete when all of the following are true across all buildings in the active Scenario:
* Ventilation strategy selected (ERV / HRV / Exhaust-only) and ERV/HRV Equipment Record created if applicable
* Mixing valve decision resolved (one of three paths confirmed) — required for any radiant + domestic hot water combination
* Manual J load calculation run for all heated/cooled zones — all zones show a BTU/hr result
* HVAC system type locked per zone (radiant / mini-split / unit heater / forced air) — matches zone type and construction type constraints
* Equipment Records at Roughed-in status or higher for all HVAC units — make/model may still be Undecided but zone assignment and capacity target must be confirmed
* All Lineset Records created with wall sleeve locations confirmed — sleeve locations block wall close-in if not placed before framing inspection
* All Radiant Loop Records created with manifold port assignments — required before pre-pour document can be generated
* All ERV/HRV Duct Records created with supply and exhaust port locations confirmed — required before wall close-in
* All Condensate Drain Records created with route type selected
* Radiant system accessories added to Equipment Record list (expansion tank, fill valve, air separator, circulator pumps)
* ERV fresh air load incorporated into HVAC load calculations (auto-calculated when ERV Equipment Record exists)
* HVAC trade package can be generated without red blockers


7.3a HVAC Data Records
The visual grammar in Section 5.6.4 defines the HVAC load overlay, radiant balance chart, and two-way linking. Those visuals auto-generate from the following data records. All HVAC equipment, lineset, radiant loop, ERV/HRV duct, and condensate records must be stored at the field level so the balance chart, trade package, pre-pour document, and load calculation all derive from a single source of truth.
Equipment Record
One record per HVAC unit (mini-split, boiler, combi boiler, unit heater, ERV, HRV). Fields: equipment type (mini-split indoor head / mini-split outdoor unit / boiler / combi boiler / propane unit heater / ERV / HRV / exhaust fan / range hood); make and model (or Undecided — a required field that blocks trade package finalization but not design work); heating capacity BTU/hr input and output; cooling capacity BTU/hr (mini-split only); SEER rating (mini-split only); AFUE or HSPF rating (boiler and heat pump); refrigerant type (mini-split: R-410A or R-32 — R-32 is emerging but verify contractor availability in Kansas metro); electrical circuit ID (links to electrical circuit record — every HVAC unit with a compressor or heating element requires a dedicated circuit); service clearances required (front / sides / top — derived from manufacturer spec, shown as a clearance zone on the floor plan when the unit is placed); zone IDs served (which zones this unit conditions — drives the load overlay); planning status (Concept / Roughed-in / Confirmed). When equipment status reaches Confirmed, the electrical circuit record for this unit is automatically locked to the equipment's electrical spec — amperage and circuit type cannot be changed without unlocking both records simultaneously.
Lineset Record
One record per refrigerant lineset (one per mini-split outdoor/indoor pair). Fields: associated indoor head record ID; associated outdoor unit record ID; lineset diameter (liquid line and suction line — derived from equipment capacity, overridable); total lineset length (derived from routing path on floor plan — no double entry); routing path (wall sleeve location coordinates, exterior routing coordinates to outdoor unit pad); wall sleeve location (coordinates on floor plan, links to roof plan or wall penetration marker); sleeve diameter (3" standard, pitched toward exterior for condensate drainage); maximum lineset length for this equipment model (manufacturer spec — flagged if routing path exceeds it); elevation change between indoor and outdoor units (affects refrigerant charge — flagged if change exceeds 25 ft without manufacturer confirmation); planning status. Lineset length feeds the materials takeoff automatically. The roof plan layer checks the sleeve exit point against the solar reservation zone (Section 10.5).
Radiant Loop Record
One record per PEX loop. Fields: loop number; manifold port assignment (which port on the manifold this loop connects to — port numbering matches the physical manifold left-to-right); zone ID served; loop length (derived from floor plan layout — no double entry, updated live as loop path is drawn); supply and return header coordinates at manifold; PEX tubing diameter (1/2" standard for residential loops, 3/4" for long loops or high-flow zones); spacing (12" OC standard); balance status (auto-calculated: green if within 10% of average loop length for this manifold, amber if 10-20% off, red if 20%+ off — drives the balance chart in Section 5.6.4); planning status. The manifold port assignment is the critical field: without it, the balance chart cannot render and the pre-pour drawing cannot label which loop feeds which zone. Manifold port assignment must be completed before the pre-pour document can be generated for any building with radiant heat. The tool enforces this as a pre-pour blocker: if any loop record has an unassigned manifold port, the pre-pour document generation button shows a red blocker: "Radiant loop manifold port assignments incomplete — all loops must be assigned to a port before pre-pour document can be generated."
ERV/HRV Duct Record
One record per ERV or HRV duct run (supply and exhaust are separate records). Fields: unit record ID (links to equipment record); duct function (fresh air supply / stale air exhaust / recirculation); duct diameter; duct length (derived from routing path on floor plan); CFM rating for this duct run (derived from unit total CFM and zone distribution); supply or exhaust port location coordinates (must not be within 10 ft of HVAC return air grille — short-circuiting fresh air with HVAC return defeats the ERV/HRV function; the tool flags this conflict when the port is placed); exterior wall penetration coordinates (links to wall penetration record and roof/wall conflict detection); planning status. Short-circuit conflict check: when an ERV/HRV supply port is placed within 10 ft of any HVAC return air grille on the same zone, the tool fires a Tier 2 amber flag: "ERV supply port too close to HVAC return — fresh air will recirculate without reaching occupants. Minimum 10 ft separation or relocate port."
Condensate Drain Record
One record per mini-split indoor head condensate drain route. Fields: equipment record ID; drain route type (to floor drain / to exterior wall / to drywell / to condensate pump); drain route path length (derived from floor plan geometry); destination record ID (floor drain fixture record, or exterior wall penetration coordinates); trap required flag (condensate drains serving horizontal runs longer than 5 ft require a trap to prevent air lock — auto-flagged); planning status. Condensate drain destination coordinates feed into the plumbing layer conflict check — if the condensate route crosses a radiant loop zone boundary at a point where it would need to penetrate the vapor barrier, a Tier 1 amber flag fires requiring the condensate sleeve to appear on the pre-pour drawing.
HVAC Claude Prompt — Equipment Sizing Confirmation
This prompt fires when Manual J output for a zone produces a result within 15% of the next standard equipment size up or down — the borderline condition where oversizing and undersizing are both plausible errors. Trigger condition: calculated zone load within 15% of a standard equipment capacity step (e.g., calculated load is 18,000 BTU and standard sizes are 12,000 and 18,000 — 18,000 is exactly at the boundary). When triggered, the tool assembles a prompt containing: zone dimensions and envelope spec, calculated heating and cooling loads, adjacent zone thermal relationship, construction type, HVAC system type selected, and the specific equipment capacity in question. The prompt asks Claude to: (1) confirm or challenge the sizing, (2) identify the dominant load component (envelope loss, infiltration, solar gain, or internal gains), (3) advise on whether the radiant system (if present) meaningfully offsets the sizing decision, and (4) flag any Kansas climate behavior that affects the sizing (summer afternoon west-facing load, winter design temperature at -10°F). Presented as a Tier 2 amber queue item.
Mixing Valve Decision — Radiant Systems with Domestic Hot Water
TIER 1 RED BLOCKER: the mixing valve decision must be resolved before the HVAC module can reach Complete status and before the plumbing trade package can be finalized. It cannot be deferred past framing inspection — the mixing valve installs in the mechanical room during above-slab plumbing rough-in.


The mixing valve is required whenever a radiant heating system shares a boiler or combi boiler with domestic hot water, and any fixture a person can contact (shower, bathtub, lavatory faucet) is served by that hot water.
