IMC 2018 Section 607.1 and IRC P2723 require a thermostatic mixing valve at the domestic hot water distribution point when the water heater or boiler operates above 120°F. Radiant systems run most efficiently at 90–140°F. Without a mixing valve, the system either scalds occupants or runs the radiant at sub-optimal temperature.
Blocker fires when all three are true: (1) radiant heat = yes for any zone; (2) building has a boiler or combi boiler that also provides domestic hot water; (3) at least one shower, bathtub, or lavatory sink exists in the building.
Path 1 — Thermostatic mixing valve (TMV) at the domestic hot water distribution point:
A dedicated valve installed where the hot water supply leaves the boiler toward domestic fixtures. Blends hot boiler water with cold supply water to maintain set delivery temperature (110–120°F). Above-slab plumbing rough-in item — not a pre-pour item. Mounts in mechanical room near the boiler. App action: TMV added to Fixture Record list, mechanical room rough-in list updated, budget line item at Concept status. Blocker clears.
Path 2 — Combi boiler with integrated mixing confirmed:
Some combi boiler models include an integral mixing valve or modulating domestic hot water heat exchanger. If the owner selects a model with this feature confirmed in the product spec sheet, Path 2 satisfies the requirement. App action: boiler Equipment Record requires “integrated mixing confirmed” checkbox — blocks Equipment Record from reaching Confirmed until spec sheet is uploaded or feature is verified by model number. Blocker clears when checkbox is set.
Path 3 — Radiant-only boiler, separate domestic hot water source:
If the radiant boiler serves only the radiant loops and domestic hot water is provided by a separate dedicated water heater, the mixing valve requirement does not apply to the boiler. App action: note added to HVAC and plumbing records confirming separation. Blocker clears.
App field: Mixing valve decision (TMV installed / Combi with integral mixing confirmed / Radiant only, separate DHW / Not decided). Displayed in Next Decision Queue at Priority 1.75 when radiant + domestic hot water + shower/tub/sink all exist in the same building.
Equipment Model Selection Path — From BTU to Confirmed Spec
The Manual J simplified calculation produces a BTU/hr heating load and cooling load per zone. This is the minimum required capacity — not the equipment size to order. The following steps convert a BTU number to a confirmed make/model.
Step 1 — Standard size selection:
Mini-split standard sizes: 6,000 / 9,000 / 12,000 / 15,000 / 18,000 / 24,000 / 30,000 / 36,000 BTU/hr nominal cooling capacity. The tool selects the smallest standard size that meets or exceeds the larger of the heating load and cooling load for the zone.
Oversizing rule: do not select more than 1.5× the calculated cooling load for mini-splits. Oversized mini-splits short-cycle — they reach setpoint quickly, shut off, allow humidity to rebuild, and restart — never running long enough to dehumidify properly. This is a common and real comfort problem in humid summer climates. The tool fires a Tier 2 amber warning if any mini-split selection exceeds 1.5× the calculated cooling load.


Step 2 — Cold-climate rating check:
For any zone where the mini-split is the primary or sole heat source, the unit must be rated to operate at the property design heating temperature. Most standard mini-splits lose significant capacity below 0°F and may shut down below -13°F. Cold-climate (hyper-heat) mini-splits maintain rated capacity down to -13°F or lower. App flag: if a mini-split is selected for a heating-primary role where the manufacturer’s rated minimum outdoor operating temperature is above the property design heating temperature, a Tier 2 amber flag fires.
Step 3 — Spec sheet and field locking:
Confirming an Equipment Record (Roughed-in to Confirmed) requires: manufacturer and model number entered; rated heating and cooling capacity confirmed from spec sheet; electrical circuit spec confirmed (auto-populates circuit record); lineset diameter confirmed (auto-populates Lineset Record). Spec sheet upload is optional but strongly recommended — stored on the Equipment Record and included in the HVAC trade package.
Step 4 — Multi-zone mini-split coordination:
If one outdoor unit serves multiple indoor heads, the outdoor unit capacity must equal or exceed the sum of all indoor head capacities at simultaneous design conditions. The tool calculates a diversity factor: simultaneous demand = full combined load; offset peak demand = 0.8 diversity factor applied. Displayed transparently with a note for HVAC contractor verification.
ERV Integration with HVAC Load Calculation
The ERV introduces a fresh air load not accounted for by default in the Manual J simplified calculation. Incoming fresh air in winter must be heated; in summer it must be cooled. The ERV recovers 70–85% of that energy, but the remaining 15–30% is a genuine addition to the HVAC load.
ERV fresh air load formulas:
* Heating season additional load (BTU/hr) = ERV CFM × 1.08 × (interior setpoint − outdoor design temp °F) × (1 − ERV sensible efficiency). Example at design conditions: 60 CFM ERV, indoor 70°F, outdoor -4°F, 75% efficiency: 60 × 1.08 × 74 × 0.25 = 1,198 BTU/hr added heating load.
* Cooling season additional load (BTU/hr) = ERV CFM × 1.6 × (outdoor design temp − interior setpoint °F) × (1 − ERV sensible efficiency). The 1.6 multiplier approximates combined sensible + latent load for humid summer conditions.
App behavior: when an ERV Equipment Record is added to a zone, the HVAC load calculation automatically adds the ERV fresh air load to both heating and cooling totals. The load overlay (Section 5.6.4) updates accordingly.
App fields added to ERV Equipment Record: sensible efficiency % (default 70%); latent efficiency % (default 60%); rated CFM at 0.1” static pressure. All shown with provenance badge “Estimated — enter from spec sheet for accurate load calculation” until confirmed.
Ventilation Strategy Selection
The ventilation strategy decision is required before the HVAC trade package can be finalized.
ERV — required when:
Building blower door target is below 3.0 ACH@50Pa (IECC R403.6 mandatory mechanical ventilation threshold), AND climate zone is 4A through 7, AND the building has any living quarters zone type. ERV is the correct default for any tight residential building in Zone 4A.
HRV — acceptable when:
Climate zone is 6, 7, or 8 (cold to very cold, low summer humidity), OR the building is a basement-only application where winter moisture removal is the priority. Not recommended for Zone 4A.
Exhaust-only — acceptable only when:
Building blower door test result is confirmed above 3.0 ACH@50Pa, OR the zone is a workshop, storage, or agricultural zone with no sleeping areas and no continuous occupancy.
If Exhaust-only is selected for a Living Quarters zone with blower door target below 3.0 ACH@50Pa, a Tier 1 red blocker fires: “Exhaust-only ventilation does not meet IECC R403.6 for buildings below 3.0 ACH@50Pa with living quarters. Select ERV or HRV.”


App field: Ventilation strategy (ERV / HRV / Exhaust-only / Not decided). Auto-recommended from climate zone and blower door target. Blocks HVAC trade package finalization until set.
Radiant System Accessories — Boiler and Distribution Package
Every hydronic radiant system requires the following accessories to function safely and reliably. All are auto-added to the Equipment Record list when radiant = yes for any zone, at Concept status.
Expansion tank:
Absorbs volume increase when system water heats from fill temperature (~60°F) to operating temperature (~120°F). Without it, pressure builds until the pressure relief valve opens. Sizing: ~1 gallon per 20 gallons of system water volume. Mounted on the boiler return line.
Pressure relief valve (PRV):
Required by code on every boiler (ASME BPVC, IMC 2018). Factory-installed on most boilers — confirm size is correct for the boiler BTU output. PRV discharge pipe must route to a floor drain or terminate within 6” of the floor — not at eye level (scalding discharge hazard). Discharge routing is a mechanical room rough-in item.
Fill valve and backflow preventer:
Allows system to be refilled when pressure drops. Backflow preventer required by UPC to prevent system water from siphoning back into the potable supply. Typically a combined unit. Mounted on the cold water supply line feeding the boiler. Added to plumbing rough-in list.
Air separator and automatic air vent:
Air separator (magnetic or centrifugal) on the boiler supply removes circulating air. Automatic air vent at the high point of the system allows trapped air to escape. Installed during mechanical rough-in before system is filled and pressurized.
Circulator pump(s):
One pump per heating zone minimum. Pump sizing is derived from total loop footage and pressure drop. The tool generates a placeholder “residential circulator 1/25 HP” at Concept status and flags for confirmation when manifold port assignments are complete and total loop footage is known.
Primary/secondary loop configuration (combi boilers only):
A combi boiler uses a primary loop (boiler to/from a hydraulic separator or closely-spaced tees) and secondary loops (radiant distribution and DHW circuits, each with their own circulator). This prevents the radiant circulator from fighting the domestic hot water flow. App field: primary/secondary loop configuration (yes / no / not applicable — auto-set to yes when combi boiler selected).
App actions: expansion tank, air separator, automatic air vent, circulator pump(s), hydraulic separator (if combi boiler) all added to Equipment Record list at Concept status. Fill valve assembly added to plumbing rough-in list. PRV discharge routing added to mechanical room rough-in checklist. All accessories move to Estimated when boiler Equipment Record reaches Roughed-in status.
HVAC Module Complete When
The HVAC module is complete when all of the following are true across all buildings in the active Scenario:
* Ventilation strategy selected (ERV / HRV / Exhaust-only) and ERV/HRV Equipment Record created if applicable
* Mixing valve decision resolved (one of three paths confirmed) — required for any radiant + domestic hot water combination with shower, tub, or sink
* Manual J load calculation run for all heated/cooled zones — all zones show BTU/hr result for both heating and cooling
* ERV fresh air load added to HVAC load calculations (auto-calculated when ERV Equipment Record exists and efficiency fields are confirmed)
* HVAC system type locked per zone — must match zone type and construction type constraints from Section 7.3 system selection matrix
* Equipment Records at Roughed-in status or higher for all HVAC units — must reach Confirmed before HVAC trade package is finalized
* Cold-climate rating confirmed for any mini-split serving a heating-primary role
* Oversizing check passed for all mini-splits (no unit exceeds 1.5× calculated cooling load without owner override)
* All Lineset Records created with wall sleeve locations confirmed on floor plan — sleeve locations are wall close-in items
* All Radiant Loop Records created with manifold port assignments — required before pre-pour document can be generated
* All ERV/HRV Duct Records created with supply and exhaust port locations confirmed — required before wall close-in; short-circuit conflict check passed
* All Condensate Drain Records created with route type selected and destination confirmed
* Radiant system accessories added to Equipment Record list (expansion tank, fill valve, air separator, circulator pumps, hydraulic separator if combi boiler)
* Mixing valve TMV (Path 1) or integral mixing confirmation (Path 2) added to mechanical room rough-in list if applicable
* HVAC trade package generation unlocked — all above conditions met


7.4 Gas / Propane System
System Design Principles
* Propane is the thermal backbone — no natural gas available. Design as a complete system, not appliance by appliance.
* Two-stage regulation: one regulator at the tank, one at the building entry. Stable pressure over long line runs.
* CSST (yellow flexible tubing) is fast to install and code-approved in Kansas but requires bonding to electrical ground. Black iron pipe is traditional, requires more fittings, no bonding required.
* Size pipe for BTU demand per run: larger loads and longer runs need larger pipe. Tool calculates this from the appliance library — each propane appliance has its BTU rating, the gas line sizing tables are encoded.


What Goes in the Wall — Gas
* Black iron pipe or CSST from meter/regulator to each appliance stub-out
* Fireplace supply line — route through wall to firebox location before framing close-in
* Range supply — stub-up at range location
* Dryer supply — stub-up at dryer location
* Boiler/unit heater supply — to mechanical room or heater location
* Each stub-out gets a shutoff valve — accessible, not buried in wall
* Generator stub — if generator is planned, run line now. Capped if not used immediately.


Tank Sizing — This Property
Tank size
	Typical use
	Notes
	500 gallon
	Single home, moderate heating
	Minimum for barn living quarters with heating. Fill 1-2 times per year.
	1000 gallon
	Large home or high demand
	Recommended minimum for barn + future house combined load. Fill once per year or less.
	2 x 500 gallon
	Redundancy and flexibility
	Two tanks connected — one fills while other runs. Good for farm continuity.
	RECOMMENDATION: 1000 gallon minimum, sized for barn living quarters plus future house combined load. Place tank with correct setback from BOTH barn AND future house site. 1000 gallon: 25 ft minimum from buildings per NFPA 58.
* Run capped propane supply line stub toward future house site during initial site work — trench is already open, cost is marginal
* Size first-stage regulator at tank for combined barn plus future house peak demand
Two-Stage Regulator Configuration:
Propane systems serving multiple appliances over a line run longer than 30 ft use a two-stage regulator configuration. Understanding the two configurations drives pipe sizing and inspection requirements. Configuration 1 — Two-stage separated: first-stage regulator at the tank steps pressure from tank pressure (~100–200 psi) down to an intermediate 10 psi. The line from tank to building runs at 10 psi intermediate pressure. Second-stage regulator at the building entry steps from 10 psi down to the 11" water column (0.4 psi) working pressure for appliances. This is the correct configuration for any run longer than 30 ft or when serving multiple buildings. The 10 psi intermediate line requires black iron pipe or copper tubing rated for LP service — CSST (flexible corrugated stainless) is acceptable for the interior low-pressure side only. Configuration 2 — Integral two-stage at tank: a single regulator at the tank performs both pressure reduction steps, delivering low working pressure (11" WC) directly. The entire line from tank to building runs at working pressure. This is only suitable for short runs (under 30 ft) serving a single building — pressure drop over long runs at working pressure causes appliance starvation. For this property with the tank at the barn and a future house supply line planned, Configuration 1 with first-stage at tank and second-stage at each building entry is the correct design. The tool flags integral two-stage configuration as a warning if any building is more than 30 ft from the tank or if more than one building is served.
7.4a Gas / Propane Data Records
The visual grammar in Section 5.6.5 defines the gas flow diagram, pipe segment labels, and pressure drop display. Those visuals auto-generate from the following data records. All appliance BTU, pipe segment, and tank records must be stored at the field level so the flow diagram, pre-pour document, trade package, BTU pipe sizing, and tank sizing check all derive from a single source of truth.
Appliance BTU Record
One record per propane appliance. Fields: appliance type (boiler / combi boiler / unit heater / propane fireplace / range / dryer / generator / water heater / other); equipment record ID if appliance is also an HVAC unit (links to HVAC equipment record for coordination); BTU/hr input rating (from manufacturer spec or appliance library default — required field, no default allowed for heaters over 10,000 BTU/hr); dedicated shutoff valve location (coordinates on floor plan, required field — every appliance gets an accessible shutoff valve before it can be placed in Confirmed status); pipe size to stub-up (auto-calculated from BTU demand and run length using NFPA 54 / NFPA 58 sizing tables encoded in the tool, overridable with a reason note); stub-up location coordinates (links to pre-pour drawing if stub is in-slab, links to wall penetration record if through-wall); pressure at stub (11" WC working pressure for all interior appliance connections — the second-stage regulator at the building entry is responsible for this; tool flags any appliance stub not on the low-pressure downstream side of a second-stage regulator); CSST flag (yes / no — if CSST flexible tubing is used anywhere between the second-stage regulator and this appliance stub, the bonding coordination field activates — see below); planning status. BTU ratings from all appliance records in a Scenario roll up to the Scenario-level peak BTU demand, which drives the tank sizing check and the first-stage regulator sizing.
Pipe Segment Record — Gas
One record per pipe run between two fittings, regulators, or appliance stubs. Fields: segment ID; upstream connection (tank / first-stage regulator / second-stage regulator / tee / appliance stub — record ID of the upstream element); downstream connection (same options — record ID of the downstream element); pipe material (black iron / CSST / copper — copper not permitted for LP gas in Kansas, tool blocks copper selection for LP service); pipe size (auto-calculated from BTU demand at this segment and total run length from tank to appliance using NFPA 54 Table 402.4 for black iron and NFPA 54 Table 402.4(4) for CSST, encoded in the tool); BTU demand at this segment (sum of all appliance BTU ratings downstream of this segment — auto-calculated from appliance BTU records); run length (derived from floor plan geometry — no double entry); pressure drop at this segment (calculated — flagged if cumulative pressure drop from regulator to appliance exceeds 0.5" WC for low-pressure systems); planning status. Undersized segments shown in red on the gas flow diagram. The flow diagram (Section 5.6.5) renders from pipe segment records — BTU demand and pipe size are labeled on every segment.
CSST Bonding Coordination Record
One record per building where CSST is used. This record exists specifically to create a cross-trade coordination item that appears in both the gas trade package and the electrical trade package. Fields: building ID; CSST used (yes / no — activates this record); bonding conductor size (10 AWG copper minimum per NFPA 54 and manufacturer requirements — auto-populated, overridable with engineer confirmation); bonding connection point on gas system (at the CSST fitting closest to the electrical panel, or at the equipment bonding lug if the appliance has one — location coordinates); bonding connection point on electrical system (to the grounding electrode conductor or equipment grounding bus in the main panel — links to electrical panel record and panel slot assignment); bonding conductor routing path (wall or surface mount — routing coordinates on floor plan); inspection status (required by gas inspector and electrical inspector independently — both must sign off). The tool adds a coordination flag visible in both the gas and electrical trade packages: "CSST bonding required — gas and electrical inspectors must both verify bonding connection. See CSST Bonding Record [ID]." This flag cannot be cleared from either trade package independently — it clears only when both inspector sign-offs are recorded.
Tank Evaporation Capacity Record
One record per propane tank on the property. Fields: tank size in gallons; tank type (above-ground / underground); tank model (or Undecided — required for evaporation capacity check, blocks the capacity warning calculation until entered); propane supplier (Ferrellgas / AmeriGas / rural co-op / other); fill schedule (monthly / bi-monthly / annual / on-call); minimum reserve level in gallons (typically 20% of tank capacity — below this level, liquid propane may not cover the withdrawal tube in cold weather, causing vapor supply issues); cold-weather evaporation rate at design temperature (calculated: standard LP evaporation rate at -20°F Kansas design cold for the specified tank size — 500 gallon tank: approximately 109,000 BTU/hr at -20°F; 1000 gallon tank: approximately 183,000 BTU/hr — encoded in the tool by tank size, overridable with supplier data); peak BTU demand (auto-populated from the sum of all appliance BTU records in all buildings served by this tank); capacity check result (auto-calculated: if peak BTU demand exceeds cold-weather evaporation rate, flag fires: "Tank undersized for peak demand at design temperature — current demand [X BTU/hr] exceeds [tank model] evaporation rate at -20°F [Y BTU/hr]. Consider 2 x 500 gallon or 1000 gallon tank."); 72-hour outage check (auto-calculated when generator fuel type = Propane: heating BTU demand + generator BTU demand x 72 hours / BTU per gallon of LP (91,500) = gallons consumed; if this exceeds tank capacity at minimum reserve, a Tier 2 amber flag fires: "Tank may be depleted in under 72 hours at combined heating plus generator load during a winter outage."); planning status.
Gas/Propane Claude Prompt — System Sizing Trigger
This prompt fires when any of three conditions are met: (1) a new appliance is added that pushes peak BTU demand above the tank evaporation capacity at design temperature; (2) a new appliance is added that changes the pipe sizing on the main supply run from the tank; or (3) generator fuel type is set to Propane and the 72-hour outage check fails. When triggered, the tool assembles a prompt containing: all appliance BTU ratings and their locations, current tank size and model, peak BTU demand total, cold-weather evaporation rate, 72-hour outage calculation (if applicable), and the specific appliance that triggered the condition. The prompt asks Claude to: (1) recommend tank size and configuration (single vs two-tank) for this load profile; (2) calculate pipe size for the main supply run at the new peak demand; (3) estimate first-stage regulator size required; (4) if generator is propane, confirm whether the tank can sustain 72 hours of combined winter heating plus generator operation and recommend minimum tank size if not. Presented as a Tier 2 amber queue item when triggered by appliance addition, Tier 1 red when triggered by the 72-hour outage check failing with generator fuel type = Propane.
7.5 Renewable Energy System — Solar, Wind, or Both
This section covers all renewable electricity generation options for the property. Solar and wind are treated as complementary generation sources that feed the same storage, backup, and distribution system. The battery, generator, inverter, critical loads sub-panel, and utility interconnect logic is identical regardless of which generation sources are active. The tool supports three generation source configurations: Solar only, Wind only, or Solar + Wind combined. Each can be paired with any of the three system configurations below (no generation / grid-tied / battery hybrid).
Generation Source Selection:
* Solar only: roof or ground mount. Production peaks midday, zero at night. Miami County: ~4.5–5 peak sun hours/day average, latitude 38.5°N. Best roof orientation: south-facing at 28–35 degrees pitch. ROOFING NOTE: standing seam metal roofing is strongly recommended for any roof that will carry solar panels. Solar clamps attach to the seams with zero penetrations — the roof warranty and panel integrity are preserved. Exposed fastener panels and shingles require roof penetrations for each mount. Roofing material decision (Section 3.4 Surface Record) should be made before solar layout is finalized.
* Wind only: always tower or ground mount — never roof mount. Production often strongest in evenings, overnight, winter, and cloudy days — complementary to solar profile. Miami County average wind speed ~12–14 mph (viable range for small wind). Kansas is one of the strongest small wind states in the US. Zoning setback required: tower height x 1.1 minimum from property lines and structures. Miami County zoning must be confirmed before specifying.
* Solar + Wind combined: most consistent generation profile. Solar covers daytime/summer peak. Wind covers evenings, overnight, and winter. Together they reduce battery depth-of-discharge and generator runtime. The shared battery bank, inverter, and distribution system handles both inputs simultaneously. This is the recommended configuration for a farm property with backup power goals and open land for tower placement.


Configuration 1: No Renewable Generation
* Standard grid-tied electrical panel only
* No extra conduit or special equipment needed
* Simplest permit path
* Still stub out conduit for future solar inverter connection — costs almost nothing now


Configuration 2: Grid-Tied Renewable Generation
* Bidirectional meter required — requires interconnect agreement with rural electric co-op (Ferrellgas territory — confirm provider)
* Inverter location must be on layout plan — conduit from array to inverter to panel
* Array sizing: Miami County ~4.5-5 peak sun hours/day. Daily load (kWh) ÷ peak sun hours ÷ system efficiency = panel wattage needed
* String inverter vs micro-inverters vs power optimizers: micro-inverters or optimizers recommended if any shading occurs. String inverter is cheapest for unshaded arrays.
* Net metering application filed with utility before system is energized


Configuration 3: Renewable Generation + Battery Hybrid
* Critical loads sub-panel required — separates loads that get backup power from loads that do not
* Battery bank location on layout plan — significant weight and ventilation requirements
* Transfer switch or gateway device (Enphase, SolarEdge, Tesla Powerwall, etc.) — model determines installation requirements
* Generator backup at existing barn generator rough-in is the foundation for this configuration
* Battery chemistry: lithium (lighter, longer cycle life, higher cost) vs lead acid (heavy, shorter life, lower cost)


Solar — Roof Decisions
* New barn roof should be designed for optimal solar from the start: south-facing, 20-35 degree pitch for Kansas latitude
* Existing barn roof: suboptimal but workable — estimated 80-90% of optimal production
* Roof structural assessment: panel weight load (typically 3-4 lb/sq ft) must be confirmed against roof framing capacity
* Ground mount option: alternative if roof orientation is poor. Requires engineered footings for Kansas wind on ground arrays.


Solar + Propane: Complementary Systems
Solar is poor at heating — too many panels needed for resistance heat. Propane is very good at heating — dense energy delivering BTUs efficiently. Solar handles electrical loads. Propane handles thermal loads. This is the standard design for rural Kansas properties.
Solar Orientation & Pitch Assessment — Measuring Real Roofs
Solar production estimates in the tool are only accurate if the actual roof azimuth (compass direction the roof faces) and actual roof pitch are entered. The default assumption of south-facing at 30 degrees does not apply to the existing barn or any building where orientation has not been confirmed. Measure before estimating.
How to Measure Roof Azimuth and Pitch with an iPhone
Measuring roof azimuth (compass bearing):
1. Open the Compass app on iPhone (built-in, no download needed). 2. Stand at the base of the roof slope you want to measure — face directly away from the building, perpendicular to the ridge, looking in the direction the roof slopes downward. This is the direction the roof faces. 3. Hold the phone flat (screen up) and read the compass bearing shown. That number is the roof azimuth. 4. True south = 180°. Record the exact number — do not round to the nearest cardinal. 5. Repeat for each roof face if the building has multiple slopes (gable has two, shed has one). Enter each face&#x2019;s bearing into its Surface Record in the tool.
Note: iPhone compass reads magnetic north by default. For solar calculations, true north is needed. In Miami County Kansas, magnetic declination is approximately 3° east — subtract 3° from the compass reading to get true bearing. This small correction matters more for east- or west-facing roofs than for south-facing roofs.
Measuring roof pitch:
1. Open the Measure app on iPhone (built-in). Tap the Level tab at the bottom. 2. Climb onto the roof or use a straight board held flat against the roof surface from the ground. 3. Place the phone flat against the roof surface (or against the board). The level reads the angle of inclination in degrees. 4. Convert to pitch notation: degrees to pitch conversion: 4° = 1/12, 9° = 2/12, 14° = 3/12, 18° = 4/12, 23° = 5/12, 27° = 6/12, 30° = 7/12, 34° = 8/12. For solar purposes, recording the degree value directly is more precise than the x/12 pitch notation. 5. If roof access is not safe, measure from inside the attic: hold phone against the underside of a rafter and add 90° to the reading (or use the Measure app&#x2019;s protractor mode directly against the rafter face).
Existing barn — measurement workflow:
The existing barn has an unknown azimuth and pitch. Before the solar module can produce a reliable production estimate for the barn, take these measurements on site: (1) Stand at the south eave of the barn facing away from the building — record compass bearing. (2) Measure pitch of the south-facing roof face using the Measure app level. (3) Note any shading obstructions (trees, adjacent structures) visible from the roof surface looking south. Enter all three into the existing barn&#x2019;s Surface Record. The tool will then calculate the derate-adjusted production estimate and compare it to a new optimally-oriented barn roof to help decide whether barn solar or new-barn solar is the stronger investment.
Azimuth Derate Table — Annual Production Loss vs True South
Applied to the 4.5–5 peak sun hours/day baseline when calculating array production. Multiply baseline peak sun hours by the derate factor for the measured azimuth before sizing the array.
Roof azimuth range
	Production derate
	Notes
	165°–195° (true south ±15°)
	1.00 — no derate
	Optimal zone. Full 4.5–5 peak sun hours applies.
	150°–165° or 195°–210° (SSE/SSW, ±15–30°)
	0.97 — ~3% loss
	Still excellent. Practically no impact on system sizing.
	120°–150° or 210°–240° (SE/SW, ±30–60°)
	0.85–0.92 — ~8–15% loss
	Workable. Add ~10–18% more panels to hit same output. Existing barn likely falls in this range.
	90°–120° or 240°–270° (ESE/WSW, ±60–90°)
	0.70–0.85 — ~15–30% loss
	Marginal for roof mount. Consider ground mount instead. Needs significantly larger array for same output.
	0°–90° or 270°–360° (facing north half)
	0.40–0.65 — not viable
	Do not install solar on north-facing roof. Ground mount on south-facing open ground is the correct solution.
	Pitch derate (applied in addition to azimuth derate): optimal pitch for Kansas 38.5°N is 28–35 degrees. A flat roof (0°) loses ~15% vs optimal. A very steep roof (45°+) loses ~8% vs optimal. Within the 15–40 degree range, pitch derate is minor (less than 5%) — azimuth is the more important variable. For a pitched roof at the correct azimuth, pitch correction is rarely worth adjusting array tilt angle unless the roof is very flat or very steep.


Wind Turbine — Site Decisions
* Tower placement: must be on open ground away from buildings and trees that create turbulence. Turbulence reduces output and increases wear on the turbine. Rule of thumb: turbine hub height should be at least 30 ft above any obstacle within 300 ft. Tower appears on the site plan layer as a placed object with setback radius shown.
* Zoning setback: Miami County zoning must be confirmed before specifying tower height. Typical small wind setback is tower height x 1.1 from property lines and structures. Tool flags a conflict if tower placement violates the calculated setback radius.
* Tower footing: engineered concrete footing required for Kansas wind loads. Tower base appears on the site plan and the foundation layer. If within trench distance of buildings, underground cable run to inverter goes in the site work trench alongside electrical conduit and water line (Section 17 Step 5).
* Turbine sizing: rated output (kW) selected to match load goals. Small residential wind turbines: 1–10 kW typical range. Sized by the tool using the critical loads list and desired backup autonomy hours. Oversizing is common and acceptable — excess generation charges battery or exports to grid.
* Maintenance: wind turbines have moving parts and require periodic inspection — typically annually. Factor into long-term operating cost vs. solar (no moving parts). Budget line item includes a maintenance reserve.
Solar Production Estimate Calculator
The solar module produces a production estimate whenever solar intent is Grid-Tied or Battery Hybrid and a roof surface azimuth and pitch have been measured and entered. The estimate is a daily average kWh output used for array sizing and battery sizing decisions. It is not a billing-grade calculation — it is a planning-grade estimate accurate to ±10-15%.
Calculation formula:
Estimated daily kWh = Array wattage (W) ÷ 1000 × Peak sun hours × Azimuth derate × Pitch derate × System derate. Each factor defined: Array wattage = number of panels × panel rated wattage (STC). Peak sun hours = 4.5 hours baseline for Miami County Kansas annual average (use 3.8 for winter planning, 5.5 for summer planning). Azimuth derate = factor from the Azimuth Derate Table in Section 7.5 based on measured roof bearing. Pitch derate = 1.00 for 25–35° pitch, 0.97 for 15–25° or 35–45°, 0.92 for less than 15° or greater than 45°. System derate = 0.80 composite factor accounting for: inverter efficiency (0.96), wiring losses (0.98), soiling/dust (0.95), temperature derating (0.95), mismatch (0.98), availability (0.99). Multiply all five together. Example: 10 panels at 400W each = 4000W array. 4000 ÷ 1000 × 4.5 peak hours × 0.97 azimuth (SSW roof) × 1.00 pitch (30°) × 0.80 system derate = 13.9 kWh/day estimated output.
Existing barn application:
When barn azimuth and pitch are measured and entered (see Section 7.5 iPhone measurement procedure), the tool runs the calculation for the barn roof and displays the estimated daily output alongside the estimated output for a new optimally-oriented roof at the same array size. The comparison shows the production penalty of the barn&#x2019;s actual orientation vs ideal — expressed as kWh/day difference and as annual kWh difference. This gives a concrete number to weigh against the cost difference between barn-mount solar and a new ground-mount or new-structure-mount array at optimal orientation.
Array sizing from target output:
The tool can also work the calculation in reverse: target daily kWh ÷ (peak sun hours × azimuth derate × pitch derate × system derate) = required array kW. Divide by panel wattage to get panel count. This is the array sizing path — enter a daily kWh target derived from the electrical load summary (Section 7.1) and the tool returns minimum panel count for the measured roof. If the required panel count exceeds the available roof area, the tool flags the shortfall and suggests ground mount for the remainder.
7.6 Backup Power Planning Module
The Backup Power Planning Module is a property-level guided decision flow. It works for any property — whether starting from scratch or building on existing infrastructure. The module takes existing infrastructure as an input and builds on what is already in place. It guides the user from a load inventory through to a complete backup power specification, cascading all decisions into the electrical, solar/wind, and build sequence layers.
Step 1 — Existing Infrastructure Inventory
The first screen of the module asks the user to record what already exists on the property. This is entered once at property setup and lives in the Backup Power record at the property level. It is not required to start the module — fields can be left Unknown and updated later. Unknown fields generate amber placeholders in the output rather than blocking progress.
Backup Power Infrastructure Record fields:
* Existing panel per building: size in amps (100 / 150 / 200 / other / none), location, feed type (utility direct / fed from another building / unknown)
* Existing generator: yes / no / unknown. If yes: fuel type (propane / gasoline / diesel / natural gas), running watts, surge watts, model number (optional — used to look up interlock kit compatibility in Section 7.1), connection type (cord and inlet only / hardwired to transfer switch / hardwired to interlock)
* Existing generator rough-in conduit: yes / no / unknown. If yes: conduit size (3/4" / 1" / 1.5" / 2" / other), location on site plan (placed as a symbol on the electrical layer), wire already pulled (yes / no / unknown)
* Existing transfer switch: yes / no / unknown. If yes: type (manual interlock / manual transfer switch / automatic transfer switch / whole-house generator disconnect), location, panel it is connected to
* Existing critical loads sub-panel: yes / no / unknown. If yes: size, location, which circuits are on it
How the tool uses existing infrastructure:
* Existing conduit of adequate size = generator connection cost reduced. The conduit adequacy check (Step 4) compares the existing conduit size against the wire size required for the recommended generator. If adequate: budget line item for conduit is removed; only wire pull and termination remain. If inadequate: budget line item for new conduit added with a note explaining why.
* Existing generator = Steps 3 and 4 pre-populate from the recorded specs. User confirms rather than selects.
* Existing transfer switch = Step 3 config recommendation adjusts. A manual interlock already installed steers away from Config B (ATS) unless the user explicitly wants to upgrade.
* No existing infrastructure = module starts from scratch with all fields at default. Normal path for a new property with no prior development.


Step 2 — Load Inventory and Critical Loads List
The module pulls the electrical load list automatically from the circuit schedule across all buildings. Every circuit record in the electrical layer appears in the load inventory. The user flags each load as Critical or Non-critical.
How the load list is built:
* Source: every Circuit Record in the electrical layer (Section 5.6.2) across all buildings on the property. Circuits not yet placed appear as “Planned — not yet on electrical layer” and can be added manually.
* Display: grouped by building, then by zone within each building. Each entry shows: circuit description, amperage, voltage, estimated running watts (auto-calculated from amperage × voltage × 0.8 power factor for resistive loads; user-adjustable), surge watts (auto-populated from load type defaults — see surge multiplier table below), Critical / Non-critical toggle.
* Pre-flagged as Critical by default: sump pump circuit, smoke and CO detector circuit, well pump circuit (if present), any circuit tagged as life-safety in the circuit record.
* Pre-flagged as Non-critical by default: EV charger, welder, large power tools, secondary HVAC zones, decorative lighting.
* All others: user decides. The module prompts with a plain-language question for any circuit that is ambiguous: “[Circuit description] — does this need to run during a power outage?”
Surge watts multipliers by load type (encoded in the tool):
* Motor loads (well pump, sump pump, HVAC compressor, refrigerator compressor): surge = 3× running watts. Well pump is typically the largest single surge load on a rural property.
* Resistive loads (electric resistance heat, water heater element, toaster, hair dryer): surge = 1× running watts (no surge).
* Electronic loads (computers, phone chargers, LED lighting, inverters): surge = 1.2× running watts.
* Fluorescent and some LED fixtures with magnetic ballasts: surge = 1.5× running watts.
* All multipliers are editable per circuit in the load inventory. If the user knows the actual locked-rotor amperage (LRA) from a motor nameplate, they can enter actual surge watts directly.
Critical loads summary calculated automatically:
* Total critical running watts: sum of running watts for all Critical circuits
* Peak surge demand: total critical running watts + (largest single motor surge — largest motor running watts). Only the largest motor surge is added, not all motors simultaneously — motors do not all start at the same instant.
* Critical load kWh per day: sum of (circuit running watts × estimated daily hours) for all Critical circuits. Daily hours are set per circuit from defaults (refrigerator = 8 hr equivalent, lighting = 4 hr, heat circulator = 6 hr, sump pump = 0.5 hr) and are user-adjustable. This number drives battery sizing in Step 5.


Step 3 — Configuration Selection and Recommendation Logic
The module presents all three configurations and recommends one. The recommendation is advisory — the user can select any configuration regardless of the recommendation. The recommendation logic is transparent: the user can see exactly why a configuration was recommended.
Config A — Generator + Manual Transfer Switch
Manual switchover at the panel when outage occurs. No automatic start. One generator can back up multiple buildings if sub-panel topology is used.
Recommended when all of the following are true: (1) someone is reliably on-site or nearby during outages — manual start and switchover is practical; (2) outage frequency at the property location is low (tool uses county-level utility reliability data from EIA if available, otherwise prompts user to estimate); (3) critical loads do not include any life-critical medical equipment; (4) no renewable generation is planned or existing.
Cost range: $500–$2,000 for manual interlock kit plus generator (if generator not already owned). Interlock kit alone: $50–$150, panel brand and model specific.
Config B — Generator + Automatic Transfer Switch (ATS)
Generator starts automatically and transfer switch moves load to generator within seconds of grid failure. No manual intervention required.
Recommended when any of the following are true: (1) primary occupants are elderly, mobility-limited, or unlikely to manage manual switchover in a storm; (2) property is a vacation or seasonal property — no one reliably on-site; (3) critical loads include a sump pump in a flood-prone location (outages and flooding occur simultaneously; manual start may be too late); (4) standby generator (not portable) is being specified.
Requires: critical loads sub-panel (separates backed-up circuits from grid-only circuits so a reasonably-sized generator can handle the critical list without powering the whole property). ATS connects between utility feed and critical loads sub-panel, not the main panel.
Cost range: $2,000–$5,000 for ATS plus critical loads sub-panel plus installation. Standby generator (propane or natural gas, automatic start): $3,000–$15,000 installed depending on size.
Config C — Renewable Generation + Battery + Generator
Battery handles short outages and overnight. Solar and/or wind charges battery continuously. Generator activates only when battery reaches a low state-of-charge threshold or outage extends beyond battery capacity. Grid-tied solar must be paired with battery to remain operational during an outage — grid-tied-only solar shuts off by code when grid is lost.
Recommended when any of the following are true: (1) solar or wind generation is already planned (battery is required to use that generation during outages); (2) autonomy goal is 24+ hours without generator runtime (battery covers overnight, generator handles extended events); (3) generator noise or fuel consumption is a concern — battery reduces generator runtime significantly; (4) future EV charging with overnight charging from solar is planned.
Requires: critical loads sub-panel, battery bank, inverter/charger, and generator. Most capable but highest upfront cost and complexity.
Cost range: $8,000–$30,000+ depending on battery capacity, inverter type, and whether solar is already planned.
RECOMMENDATION DISPLAY: the tool shows the recommended configuration with a plain-language explanation of why it was recommended, listing the specific conditions from the user’s answers that triggered the recommendation. Example: “Config B recommended because: primary occupants flagged as non-technical, sump pump is in critical loads list, property has a standby generator rough-in already in place.” The user can override to any configuration — selecting a non-recommended config prompts: “You selected Config [X]. The recommendation was Config [Y] because [reasons]. Confirm?” with a brief explanation of the trade-off.


Step 4 — Generator Sizing
Generator is sized to the critical loads list, not the total property load. Sizing to total load produces an oversized, expensive, inefficient generator that runs at low load factor most of the time.
Sizing calculation:
* Step 4a — Running load: sum all critical circuit running watts. This is the steady-state demand the generator must sustain.
* Step 4b — Surge demand: add the largest single motor starting surge to the running load total. Only one motor starts at a time in normal operation. Example: running load = 4,200W, well pump surge = 2,400W (800W running × 3). Total surge demand = 4,200 + 2,400 = 6,600W. Generator must handle this peak without stalling.
* Step 4c — Add 20% headroom: 6,600W × 1.20 = 7,920W. Round up to next standard generator size. Standard portable sizes: 5kW, 7.5kW, 10kW, 12kW. Standard standby sizes: 10kW, 14kW, 20kW, 22kW, 26kW.
* Step 4d — Altitude and temperature derating: generators lose approximately 3.5% capacity per 1,000 ft of elevation above sea level, and approximately 1% per 10°F above 77°F. For most properties at low to moderate elevation with standard summer temperatures this derating is negligible — tool flags if elevation exceeds 2,000 ft or design cooling temperature exceeds 100°F.
Output: the tool displays a recommended generator size range (minimum kW from Step 4c, comfortable kW at 1.5× running load for sustained operation). Both numbers shown so the user can choose based on budget and future load growth.
Existing rough-in conduit adequacy check:
If an existing generator rough-in conduit is recorded in Step 1, the tool checks whether it is large enough for the wire required to feed the recommended generator size.
* Wire size required: derived from generator output amps (generator kW × 1000 ÷ 240V = amps) using NEC Table 310.16. Example: 10kW generator = 41.7A → 8 AWG THWN-2 minimum → requires 3/4" conduit minimum for a 3-conductor run.
* Conduit size required: from NEC conduit fill tables (Section 7.1a) for the required wire gauge and conductor count.
* Result: if existing conduit ≥ required size: green flag “Existing conduit is adequate for the recommended generator — wire pull only required.” If existing conduit < required size: amber flag “Existing conduit is [size] — [required size] required for [kW] generator. New conduit required or reduce generator size.”
Generator fuel type decision (links to Section 7.4 propane system):
* Propane: no fuel degradation, uses existing tank infrastructure if propane is available, quieter than gasoline, lower maintenance than diesel. Requires propane system to be sized for the additional generator load. A 10kW propane generator consumes approximately 1.5 gal/hr at full load.
* Gasoline: lowest upfront cost for portable units, widely available, but degrades in 3–6 months (stabilizer required for storage), cannot be piped as a fixed standby unit.
* Diesel: best fuel stability (18–24 months), highest upfront cost, most durable for extended high-hour operation, requires separate fuel tank.
* Natural gas: requires utility natural gas service — available only where property.fuel_options includes natural_gas. Never runs out of fuel. Lowest operating cost for standby applications.
When generator fuel type = Propane: the propane load calculation in Section 7.4 automatically adds the generator running load (kW × 3,412 BTU/kWh ÷ generator efficiency 0.25 = BTU/hr input) to the total system peak demand. This may upsize the tank or first-stage regulator. The 72-hour outage check in Section 7.4a Tank Evaporation Capacity Record also re-runs.
Step 5 — Battery Sizing (Config C only)
Battery is sized to cover critical loads for a target autonomy period without generator runtime. The user selects the autonomy target; the tool calculates required battery bank kWh.
Sizing formula:
Required battery bank kWh = (critical load kWh/day × autonomy hours ÷ 24) ÷ usable depth of discharge. Usable DoD: 80% for lithium (LiFePO4), 50% for lead acid. Example: critical load = 8 kWh/day, autonomy target = 12 hours, lithium battery: (8 × 12 ÷ 24) ÷ 0.80 = 4 ÷ 0.80 = 5.0 kWh usable capacity required.


Battery chemistry comparison:
* Lithium iron phosphate (LiFePO4) — recommended: 80% usable DoD, 3,000–6,000 cycle life, ~20 lb/kWh, operates reliably down to -4°F (Kansas design heating temperature) with built-in battery management system heating on quality units. No off-gassing, no maintenance. Higher upfront cost, lower lifetime cost.
* Lead acid (AGM or flooded) — acceptable: 50% usable DoD, 500–1,200 cycle life, ~60 lb/kWh. Flooded lead acid requires ventilation (hydrogen off-gassing during charging — cannot be installed in an enclosed space without a dedicated vent). AGM is sealed and safe for enclosed installation. Cold temperature performance degrades significantly below 32°F — capacity drops 20–50% at 0°F. Lower upfront cost, higher lifetime cost due to shorter cycle life.
Cold weather note for this property: Kansas design heating temperature is -4°F. Any battery bank located in an unheated space (unheated garage, exterior enclosure) must be specified with a cold-weather rating at or below -4°F. Lithium battery banks without active heating circuitry can refuse to charge below 32°F even if they will discharge. Confirm the battery management system (BMS) includes low-temperature charge protection and heating for any battery located in an unheated space.


Battery bank location requirements:
* Weight: lithium ~20 lb/kWh, lead acid ~60 lb/kWh. A 10 kWh lithium bank = ~200 lb. A 10 kWh lead acid bank = ~600 lb. Floor structural capacity must be confirmed for large banks — residential floor framing is typically rated for 40 psf live load; a 600 lb bank concentrated on a small footprint may exceed this. Mechanical room on a slab (no floor structure) has no weight concern.
* Ventilation: lithium (LiFePO4) requires no special ventilation under normal operation. Flooded lead acid requires dedicated exhaust ventilation (hydrogen off-gassing rate: 0.017 cubic ft/hr per 100Ah at float charge — a 400Ah bank produces ~0.07 CFM; IMC requires minimum 1 ACH in battery rooms). AGM requires no ventilation but benefits from temperature-stable location.
* Temperature: ideally 50–77°F year-round. Mechanical room in the conditioned living quarters zone is the best location. If battery is in an unconditioned space, document the cold weather performance specification confirmed for the selected chemistry.
* App field: Battery bank location (coordinates on floor plan — required field; pre-pour blocker if located in a zone with a slab pour pending, because conduit runs from battery to inverter may need to go through the slab).


Step 6 — Electrical Topology Interaction
The backup power configuration selected in Step 3 directly determines the electrical topology for the property. This is the highest-impact cascade from the module — topology determines inter-building conduit sizing, panel placement, and the entire electrical distribution architecture.
Topology Option 1 — Sub-panel topology (one utility meter, one main panel, sub-panels in other buildings):
* One main panel (typically in the first or primary building). All other buildings fed from this main panel via inter-building feeder conduit.
* One generator and one transfer switch backs up all buildings simultaneously — the transfer switch is at the main panel, and backed-up power flows through the existing inter-building feeders.
* Simpler and lower cost than independent meters. One utility account, one interconnect agreement (if solar is planned).
* Limitation: if buildings are ever sold separately, the sub-panel building loses power service independent of the main building. Flag this in the decision record.
* Inter-building conduit sizing for this topology: feeder wire from main panel to sub-panel is sized for the sub-panel’s total load (not just critical loads). Typically 60A–100A feeder for a barn or workshop sub-panel. Wire size and conduit size calculated from feeder amps using NEC Table 310.16 and conduit fill tables.
Topology Option 2 — Independent meter topology (separate utility meter per building):
* Each building has its own utility connection, its own meter, its own main panel, and its own utility account.
* Each building requires its own backup power arrangement, OR a more complex inter-building transfer scheme must be designed (a dedicated backed-up feeder between buildings with its own transfer path — not standard and requires engineering).
* Higher cost and complexity. More flexible for future independent use or sale of buildings.
* Inter-building conduit sizing for this topology: conduit still recommended between buildings for low-voltage (data, phone, security, EV control) and for future flexibility — but no high-voltage power feeder required. Minimum 1" Schedule 40 PVC with pull string, capped.
Inter-building conduit sizing by config:
* Config A + sub-panel topology: feeder conduit sized for sub-panel load. Minimum 1" for feeders up to 60A (6 AWG THWN-2, 3 conductors + ground). 1.25" for 60–100A feeders (4 AWG or 3 AWG).
* Config B + sub-panel topology: same as Config A. ATS is at the main panel; backed-up power flows through existing feeder conduit. No additional conduit required if feeder is already sized for sub-panel load.
* Config C + sub-panel topology: battery bank and inverter location drives an additional conduit requirement. If battery is in one building and the critical loads sub-panel is in another, a dedicated backed-up power conduit is required between them — sized for the inverter output amps. Inverter output is typically 120/240V AC at the inverter’s rated output (e.g. 3,000W inverter = 12.5A at 240V → 12 AWG → 3/4" conduit minimum). This conduit runs separately from the main feeder conduit.
* All topologies: always install additional 1" conduits beyond what is currently needed during the site work trench pass. Cap and mark ends. Cost of conduit is trivial; cost of reopening the trench is not.
TOPOLOGY DECISION GATES CONDUIT SIZING: the inter-building conduit specification cannot be finalized until the topology decision is made. This is why Section 19 lists electrical topology as HIGH priority before site work. The conduit goes in the trench during Build Sequence Step 5 — before any foundation work begins. The topology decision must be made before Step 5, not after.


Backup Power Record — complete field list
Property-level fields:
* Existing panel per building (fields per building record — see Step 1)
* Existing generator: yes / no / unknown; fuel type; running watts; surge watts; model number; connection type
* Existing generator rough-in conduit: yes / no / unknown; conduit size; location (coordinates); wire pulled
* Existing transfer switch: yes / no / unknown; type; location; connected panel
* Electrical topology decision: Sub-panel / Independent meter / Not decided (HIGH priority — blocks inter-building conduit spec)
* Backup power configuration: Config A / Config B / Config C / Not decided
* Generator fuel type: Propane / Gasoline / Diesel / Natural gas / Not decided
* Generator size selected: kW (auto-calculated minimum + recommended; owner selects final)
* Generator location: coordinates on site plan (required for Config B standby; optional for Config A portable)
* Critical loads sub-panel: required (yes — Config B and C) / not required (Config A) / location (coordinates on floor plan)
* Battery bank capacity: kWh (Config C only — auto-calculated from Step 5 formula; owner confirms)
* Battery chemistry: LiFePO4 / AGM / Flooded lead acid (Config C only)
* Battery bank location: coordinates on floor plan (Config C only — pre-pour blocker if in slab zone)
* Autonomy target: 8 / 12 / 24 / 48 hours (Config C only)
* Module status: Not started / In progress / Complete


Takeoff lines generated
* Generator inlet box (EA: 1 — Config A and B; mounted at main panel or critical loads sub-panel)
* Manual interlock kit (EA: 1 — Config A; panel brand and model specific — sourced via Claude Prompt 2 with panel model as input)
* Automatic transfer switch (EA: 1 — Config B; sized to critical loads sub-panel amperage)
* Critical loads sub-panel (EA: 1 — Config B and C; sized from critical loads circuit count + 20% spare slots)
* Inter-building feeder conduit (LF — sub-panel topology; derived from site utilities layer geometry)
* Inter-building feeder wire (LF per conductor — derived from feeder amps and conduit run length)
* Generator (EA: 1 — Concept status until fuel type and size are locked; moves to Quoted when generator quote is entered)
* Battery bank (EA: 1 — Config C; at confirmed kWh capacity; Concept until chemistry and capacity locked)
* Inverter/charger (EA: 1 — Config C; sized to battery bank and critical loads)
* Battery conduit to inverter (LF — Config C; derived from battery location to inverter location on floor plan)
* Additional inter-building backed-up power conduit (LF — Config C with battery in different building from critical loads sub-panel)
All lines added at Concept status when configuration is selected. Move to Estimated when generator size, battery capacity, and topology are locked.
Cascade from Backup Power configuration selected
* Critical loads sub-panel added to electrical layer (Config B and C) — placement required before electrical trade package can be finalized
* Generator inlet box added to electrical layer (all configs) — placement required at main panel or critical loads sub-panel
* Inter-building conduit spec updates to required feeder size (sub-panel topology) — updates site utilities layer conduit record
* Generator location added to site plan (Config B standby only — standby generators are fixed; portable generators are not shown on site plan)
* Generator rough-in added to build sequence Step 11 (rough systems) if no existing rough-in, or flagged as “existing rough-in — verify adequacy” if existing rough-in was recorded in Step 1
* Battery bank location added to floor plan (Config C) — pre-pour blocker if in a zone with a pending slab pour
* Propane load calculation re-runs if generator fuel type = Propane (Section 7.4a Tank Evaporation Capacity Record re-evaluates)
* Renewable energy configuration confirmed (Config C requires solar or wind generation to be specified — fires Tier 2 amber if Config C selected and no generation source is set in Section 7.5)
* All budget line items above added at Concept status — move to Estimated when configuration is locked


Module complete when
Electrical topology decision recorded (sub-panel or independent meter). Backup power configuration selected (A, B, or C). Generator fuel type selected. Generator size confirmed (minimum kW from calculation accepted or overridden with reason). Critical loads list reviewed and all circuits flagged Critical or Non-critical. Critical loads sub-panel placed on electrical layer (Config B and C). Generator location on site plan (Config B standby). Battery capacity and chemistry confirmed (Config C). Battery bank location on floor plan (Config C). Inter-building conduit spec updated to reflect topology and config. All takeoff lines at Estimated status or higher.
Generator Fuel Type — Linked Decision with Propane System (Section 7.4)
Generator fuel type is a decision that propagates into the propane system design and must be made before the propane load calculation is finalized. When generator fuel type is set to Propane, the Section 7.4 propane load calculation adds the generator running load to the total system peak demand, which may require upsizing the tank or the first-stage regulator. When generator fuel type is set to Gasoline or Diesel, the propane system is unaffected but the site plan gains a fuel storage location and the build sequence gains a fuel rotation maintenance item.
The tool flags a propane tank sizing warning if the current tank size would be depleted in less than 72 hours at combined heating plus generator load during a winter outage. The 72-hour check uses the Tank Evaporation Capacity Record formula in Section 7.4a and re-runs automatically when generator fuel type is set to Propane and generator size is confirmed.
7.7 Site Utilities Layer — All Below-Grade and Exterior Runs
Individual trade sections (7.1 electrical, 7.2 plumbing, 7.4 propane) each describe their own below-grade and exterior runs. But there is no unified view of where all of these runs cross the site simultaneously. The Site Utilities layer provides that view — a single spatial layer showing every below-grade and exterior utility run across the property, regardless of trade. This layer is distinct from the building-level layers in Section 5 because the runs cross the site, not just a single building footprint. It is a property-level layer that activates as soon as any inter-building or site-crossing utility run exists.
What the Site Utilities layer shows:
Each run is color-coded by trade and labeled with pipe/conduit size, material, depth, and status (existing / planned / future stub). Electrical conduit runs — all Schedule 40 PVC inter-building conduit runs, service entry from road, conduit from panel to any outbuilding, generator feed conduit. Shown in yellow with conduit size and depth label. Water supply runs — supply line from district meter at barn to any building, future stubs to planned building locations. Shown in blue with pipe size and material (PEX, copper, HDPE). Sewer and septic runs — sewer line from each building to septic tank, slope arrows, cleanout locations. Shown in brown. Propane supply runs — underground LP line from tank to each building, pipe size and material (black iron or CSST), depth. Shown in orange. Drainage runs — French drain lines, downspout discharge pipes, any underground drainage swales. Shown in green. Each run entry in the layer links back to the trade section that specifies it — clicking an electrical conduit run opens the Section 7.1 conduit spec for that run. Depths are shown as a property for each run and flagged if any run is shallower than the frost depth (24" for Miami County).
Conflict flags generated automatically:
Any two runs crossing within 12" vertical separation — flag with both run identities and a note to verify separation at crossing point. Utility run crossing the septic drain field footprint — drain field setback from Section 5.3 applies to all utility trenches. Utility run shallower than 24" frost depth (electrical conduit in conduit is exempt per NEC but water lines are not). Propane line within 10" of electrical conduit in same trench — NEC and NFPA 58 require separation or physical barrier. Any run crossing a planned future building footprint (Placeholder Building) — flag for rerouting or sleeving before the building foundation is poured. Water line within 10 ft of sewer line at any crossing point. The Site Utilities layer is the primary coordination tool for the site work phase — it must be complete and conflict-free before any trenching begins, because rerouting runs after the trench is backfilled is one of the most expensive corrections on a rural property build.
8. Foundation Planning
8.1 Foundation Decision Tree
The heated/unheated flag on each zone is the root of the foundation decision tree. ZONE TYPE PRE-POUR BLOCKERS: two zone types require below-grade rooms that are poured as part of the foundation before the main slab — these are Tier 1 decisions that must be made before foundation design begins:VEHICLE SERVICE PIT (zone type = Vehicle Service Pit): a below-grade room integrated into the main slab. Pit walls are cast-in-place concrete or CMU, poured in a separate pour before the main slab. Rebar continuity between pit walls and main slab is an engineered detail — not a standard slab connection. The pit opening at slab level requires a reinforced edge beam: the main slab cantilevers over the pit opening and vehicles drive over it, so the edge beam carries significant live load. Edge beam design is engineer-required. FOUNDATION SEQUENCE for a slab with a vehicle pit: (1) excavate pit footprint to pit depth, (2) form and pour pit walls and floor, (3) cure pit structure, (4) install oil/water separator rough-in and ventilation sleeve through pit wall, (5) form main slab with pit opening, (6) pour main slab with edge beam reinforcement at pit perimeter. The pit is a Tier 1 blocker — if the owner is considering a vehicle pit, this decision must be made before any foundation design work begins. Adding a pit after the slab is poured means breaking and replacing a significant section of the slab with new engineering — it is not a retrofit.COLD STORAGE / ROOT CELLAR (zone type = Cold Storage / Root Cellar): a partial basement poured as part of the foundation. Minimum depth 8 ft below finished grade for Kansas ground temperature stabilization (~55°F annual). Cold storage room walls and floor are poured before the main barn slab. The barn slab forms the ceiling of the cold storage room. FOUNDATION SEQUENCE for a slab with a cold storage room: (1) excavate cold storage footprint to 8+ ft depth, (2) form and pour cold storage walls and floor, (3) cure cold storage structure, (4) install hatch rough-opening framing in the barn slab forms above, (5) pour main barn slab — the cold storage ceiling. Hatch location is a Tier 1 blocker — must be decided before slab is formed. Insulate the underside of the barn slab (cold storage ceiling) with minimum R-20 rigid foam — this is applied after the barn slab cures, before the cold storage room is put into service. Do NOT insulate the cold storage floor or walls — see Section 3.3 zone type notes.TOOL ENFORCEMENT: if a building contains a Vehicle Service Pit or Cold Storage zone type and the below-grade room geometry is not placed on the foundation layer, the tool blocks pre-pour document generation with a Tier 1 blocker message: "Below-grade room geometry not placed — [zone name] pit/cold storage must appear on foundation layer before pre-pour document can be generated."
Heated Zone Foundation
* Frost depth applies: 24" minimum Miami County
* Foundation must extend below frost depth — footing at 24"+, stem wall to grade or monolithic thickened-edge
* Perimeter insulation: XPS R-10 on exterior face of foundation wall from grade to frost depth — prevents frost heave AND thermal bridging at slab edge
* SUBSURFACE DRAINAGE DECISION POINT: before specifying the slab assembly, confirm site drainage condition. If site drainage condition = Low-lying or water table is within 6 ft of the slab bottom: exterior perimeter drain tile is required at the footing base (IRC R405). This is installed during foundation excavation (Build Sequence Step 6), not during under-slab rough-in. If interior collection is also needed: interior perimeter drain tile at the footing perimeter routes to a sump pit set in the granular fill before the slab assembly is placed. The sump pit liner must be located and sized before the pre-pour document is generated — it is a pre-pour blocker if subsurface drainage ≠ None. With radiant: slab assembly bottom-to-top = compacted granular fill 4-6" → XPS foam R-15 min (two layers, staggered joints) → 6 mil poly vapor barrier → rebar + PEX loops → concrete 4.5-5". XPS FOAM JOINT TREATMENT: R-15 requires two layers of 2" XPS (R-10 each layer). The two layers must be installed with joints staggered — no joint in the top layer directly above a joint in the bottom layer. All joints in the top layer taped with XPS-compatible tape. Unstaggered or untaped joints create a thermal short-circuit path between the heated slab and the ground, undermining the radiant system efficiency. Single-layer R-10 foam with untaped joints is acceptable for unheated slabs only — never for radiant.
* Without radiant: slab assembly = compacted granular fill 4-6" → XPS foam R-10 min → 6 mil poly vapor barrier → wire mesh or fiber → concrete 4"
* Anchor bolts: 6" OC minimum at sill plates, engineered holdowns at shear wall corners for Kansas wind. ENGINEERING REQUIRED: anchor bolt pattern and holdown locations must be derived from a licensed engineer&#x2019;s stamped drawing for any building in a Kansas high wind zone — this is a permit requirement, not a suggestion. The engineer specifies bolt diameter (typically 1/2" or 5/8"), embedment depth, spacing, and holdown hardware model at each shear wall corner. The stamped anchor bolt drawing is a mandatory pre-pour deliverable — the inspector will check bolt locations against this drawing before approving the pour. The tool flags anchor bolt layout as an unresolved blocker in the pre-pour checklist until the engineered drawing is uploaded and marked received.


Unheated Zone Foundation
* No frost depth requirement for the slab — but posts still need to be below frost depth for wind uplift anchoring
* Gravel pad: 6-8" compacted crushed stone. No foam required. Vapor barrier recommended (clay soil).
* Bare concrete slab: 4" minimum. 6 mil poly vapor barrier recommended. No foam required. Can add vapor barrier without foam for moisture control on clay soil.
* Posts in ground: below frost depth 24" minimum — wind uplift requires it even for open unheated structures


Basement Foundation
A basement foundation is structurally distinct from every other foundation type in this document. It is not a slab-on-grade — it is a below-grade habitable room with walls that resist both vertical load and lateral soil pressure from the earth outside. The Priority 0 below-grade room decision (Section 9.3) must fire before any other foundation design work begins — adding a basement after the main slab is poured requires demolishing the entire slab. See Section 6.2 Basement knowledge package for the full Buy / Build / Watch specification.
Footing: Footing Type 5 — Basement Wall Footing (Section 8.1a). Auto-generated from basement perimeter at basement floor elevation. Engineer-required sizing. Default 20” wide × 10” deep at basement floor elevation. Keyway groove and rebar dowels projecting 24” into wall cavity. Three separate pour events: footing, walls, basement slab — each with its own inspection hold point.
Wall type: ICF strongly recommended for Miami County clay (Section 9.1 cascade row — Basement wall type selected). Poured concrete with interior furring and insulation is acceptable. CMU requires surface air barrier treatment plus interior insulation.
Waterproofing: Full-coverage membrane required (damp-proofing alone BLOCKED per Section 5.7.1). Drainage board over membrane. Footing drain tile at footing perimeter sloped to sump pit. Interior drainage channel at wall-floor joint as secondary measure.
Foundation sequence: (1) Excavate to full depth + 2’–3’ working room. (2) Form and pour basement wall footings — footing inspection hold. (3) Stack ICF walls or set wall forms — wall inspection hold before pour. (4) Pour basement walls. Cure 48 hours. (5) Waterproofing membrane + drainage board on all below-grade exterior faces. (6) Footing drain tile at footing perimeter. (7) Egress window wells before backfill. (8) Backfill in compacted granular lifts. (9) Sump pit liner, interior drain tile channel, gravel base, vapor barrier, XPS foam, rebar — basement slab inspection hold. (10) Pour basement slab. (11) Structural floor deck over basement walls. (12) Proceed with main building shell above the deck. The main Shell layer is blocked from Confirmed status until steps 1–11 are complete.
Hybrid Foundation — Mixed Zones
* Workshop (heated, radiant) and Equipment Bay (unheated) in same building: each zone gets the foundation appropriate to its type
* Transition joint between heated and unheated slab is an engineered detail — differential settlement risk. Step joint with compressible filler. Do not pour as one monolithic slab with two different assemblies below.
* Lean-to posts are always set to frost depth for wind anchoring, regardless of whether the lean-to is heated


8.1a Footing Type Taxonomy, Auto-Generation Rules, and Foundation Support Systems
Footings are the interface between the building structure and the soil. Every load-bearing element — every perimeter wall, every interior post, every column — must transfer its load to soil that can carry it without settling. On Miami County clay soil, footing design is not a formality: clay has low and variable bearing capacity, expands when wet, and contracts when dry. Getting footing type, size, and depth wrong on this site means differential settlement, slab cracking, or structural failure. This section defines every footing type used in the tool, the conditions that trigger each one, whether the tool generates it automatically or requires user placement, how each type appears on the foundation layer canvas and rebar map, and how each type flows into the materials takeoff and budget.
Footing Type 1 — Continuous Perimeter Footing
When used: Under all load-bearing perimeter walls of any heated zone building, and any building with a stem wall foundation. The footing runs continuously beneath the entire perimeter wall. It is the most common footing type for slab-on-grade buildings with a stem wall.
Auto-generated or user-placed: AUTO-GENERATED. When the user draws the perimeter wall outline in Draw mode on the foundation layer (Section 5.5.6 Step 1), the continuous perimeter footing appears automatically beneath it as a shaded band. Default dimensions populate immediately from the heated/unheated zone type: heated zone default = 16” wide × 12” deep (below frost depth); unheated zone default = 12” wide × 8” deep. User confirms or overrides width and depth in the property panel. Engineer-required flag fires automatically for any wall carrying a ridge beam point load or any wall in a Kansas 115 mph wind zone (which is every wall on this property).
Dimensions for Miami County clay: Minimum 16” wide × 8” deep for lightly loaded walls. More commonly 16”–20” wide × 10”–12” deep for full building perimeter on clay. Width is determined by dividing the total wall load (linear feet of wall × tributary roof and floor load per LF) by the allowable soil bearing pressure. Miami County undisturbed clay bearing capacity is approximately 1,000–1,500 psf — lower than many tables assume. When in doubt, engineer specifies. Depth: bottom of footing must be at or below 24” frost depth — always. If the footing is wider than the stem wall, it projects equally on both sides (centered under wall). Rebar: minimum 2 horizontal #4 bars continuous, lapped 20” at corners and splices. Vertical dowels project up from footing into stem wall at 24” OC.
Takeoff lines generated: Footing concrete volume (separate line from slab — LF of footing × width × depth × 1.10 waste); horizontal rebar footage (2 bars × perimeter LF + lap allowance); vertical dowel count (perimeter LF ÷ 24” OC); form board LF (both sides of footing × footing depth — if stem wall is formed separately); excavation cubic yards (trench width × depth × perimeter LF ÷ 27). Footing concrete volume moves from Concept to Estimated when footing geometry is Confirmed on the foundation layer — before that it shows at Concept status only.
How-to video: Seeded in video library — “Continuous perimeter footing forming and pour”; channel: Concrete Network or Fine Homebuilding; search fallback: “perimeter footing form stem wall rebar pour frost depth”; skill: Beginner; notes: confirm footing bottom is at 24” depth before ordering concrete — inspector checks this at footing inspection.
Footing Type 2 — Monolithic Thickened-Edge Slab
When used: When the perimeter foundation type is set to monolithic (slab and perimeter footing poured in a single continuous pour, no separate stem wall). Common for lightly loaded unheated buildings, agricultural slabs, and equipment bays. The slab thickens at its perimeter and at any interior post location — the thickened zone acts as the footing. NOT recommended as the primary foundation system for heated zones with radiant on Miami County clay — the frost depth requirement (24”) combined with the slab-level XPS foam and vapor barrier makes a separate perimeter footing and stem wall the cleaner assembly. Monolithic is acceptable for unheated or lightly loaded heated zones without radiant where the site drainage is confirmed good.
Auto-generated or user-placed: AUTO-GENERATED when foundation type field is set to Monolithic. The perimeter thickening appears automatically at the building footprint edge. When a structural post is placed anywhere on the floor plan, a thickened zone appears automatically at that post location (see Footing Type 3 below). The user confirms geometry and dimensions in the property panel.
Dimensions: Perimeter thickening: minimum 12” wide × 12” deep at the slab edge, extending to frost depth (24” total depth from grade to bottom of thickening for heated zones). Interior post thickening: minimum 24” square × 12” deep thickening centered under the post base plate. Rebar: perimeter thickening requires 2 horizontal #4 bars; interior post thickening requires a rebar mat (2 bars each direction, 6” from edges) with rebar turning from slab into thickened zone at all edges — the turning detail is the critical element that ties the thickened zone to the slab so they act monolithically.
Conflict detection: When a thickened slab zone is auto-generated at a post location, the tool immediately checks for conflicts with under-slab systems at that location: PEX radiant loops, drain lines, conduit runs, and the vapor barrier penetration checklist. If a PEX loop passes through the thickened zone footprint, a Tier 2 amber flag fires: “PEX loop conflicts with post thickening at [location] — reroute loop or upgrade to isolated column footing.” If a drain line passes through the thickened zone, a Tier 1 red flag fires: “Drain line conflicts with post thickening at [location] — drain cannot be rerouted after pour. Resolve before pre-pour document is generated.” Conflict resolution: (1) reroute the conflicting system around the thickened zone (preferred), or (2) upgrade the thickened slab to an isolated column footing (Footing Type 3) which is deeper and narrower, potentially clearing the conflict.
Takeoff lines generated: Incremental concrete volume for each thickened zone is added to the slab pour line item (not a separate line — monolithic pour is one event): thickening volume = zone footprint area × additional depth below slab thickness × 1.10 waste.
