Rebar turning detail adds bar footage to the rebar takeoff. Excavation: perimeter thickening adds a trench volume at the building edge; interior post thickenings add small pad excavations. The takeoff panel labels thickening volumes as sub-items under the main slab line so the owner can see the breakdown while the pour quote is one combined yardage number.
How-to video: Seeded in video library — “Monolithic thickened-edge slab forming and rebar”; channel: Concrete Network or Fine Homebuilding; search fallback: “monolithic slab thickened edge rebar turning detail frost depth”; skill: Intermediate; notes: pay attention to the rebar turning detail at the thickened zone — the horizontal slab rebar must hook down into the thickened zone, not stop at the edge. A straight bar terminating at the edge creates a weak plane.
Footing Type 3 — Isolated Column Footing (Spread Pad)
When used: A separate poured concrete pad below a single structural post, independent of the slab. Used when: (1) the post load (from ridge beam span calculation) exceeds what a thickened slab can carry — tool calculates post load from ridge beam span and Kansas roof load and flags when it exceeds thickened slab capacity; (2) a drain line or PEX loop conflict at the post location cannot be resolved by rerouting; (3) the engineer specifies isolated footings for the building design. On Miami County clay, ridge beam posts carrying spans over 16’ typically require isolated footings — the load per post at that span exceeds what a standard 24” square thickened slab can deliver to clay soil within the bearing capacity limit.
Auto-generated or user-placed: AUTO-UPGRADED from thickened slab. When a structural post is placed, the tool first generates a thickened slab. It then calculates the post load from the ridge beam span and roof load data. If post load exceeds the thickened slab capacity for Miami County clay (approximately 3,600 lbs for a 24” × 24” pad at 1,500 psf bearing), the tool automatically upgrades to an isolated column footing and shows a Tier 2 amber note: “Post load at [location] exceeds thickened slab capacity — upgraded to isolated column footing. Engineer must size footing for confirmed post load.” User can also manually upgrade any thickened slab to isolated footing in the property panel.
Dimensions: Engineer-required for final sizing. Tool default: 24” × 24” × 12” deep for loads under 5,000 lbs; 30” × 30” × 12” for loads 5,000–8,000 lbs; 36” × 36” × 14” for loads over 8,000 lbs. All defaults shown with “ESTIMATED — engineer confirmation required” badge. Footing bottom must be at or below 24” frost depth — the footing is poured separately before the slab, with a post sleeve or anchor bolt projecting up through the slab. The post sleeve is a Schedule 40 PVC or steel sleeve set in the footing pour that allows the post to pass through the slab to bear on the footing — the post does NOT bear on the slab surface when an isolated footing is used. Rebar: two-way mat of #4 bars at 8” OC each direction, with 3” clear cover at bottom.
Takeoff lines generated: Isolated footing concrete volume is a SEPARATE line item from the slab (different pour event, different mix confirmation): volume = pad length × width × depth × 1.10 waste, summed across all isolated pad locations. Rebar mat footage per pad × pad count. Post sleeve count (one per isolated footing). Excavation: pit volume per pad × count. Engineer stamp line item added to soft costs (Section 13.10) as a Concept-status placeholder — moves to Quoted when engineer is engaged.
How-to video: Seeded in video library — “Isolated column footing: forming, rebar cage, and pour”; channel: Concrete Network or Build Show (Matt Risinger); search fallback: “isolated column footing pad forming rebar cage concrete pour post sleeve”; skill: Intermediate; notes: confirm footing bottom is below frost depth before ordering concrete. Post sleeve orientation must point toward the post — confirm against floor plan before pour.
Footing Type 4 — Grade Beam
When used: A continuous concrete beam connecting posts-in-ground, used when construction type = metal post-frame (pole barn) and a concrete slab or apron is poured between the posts. The posts go into the ground below frost depth and carry the building loads directly. The grade beam sits at grade level between the posts and supports the slab edge, preventing the slab from shifting laterally and providing a form edge for the pour. Not used with slab-on-grade with separate stem wall foundations.
Auto-generated or user-placed: USER-PLACED. When construction type = metal post-frame and floor type = concrete slab, the tool prompts: “Grade beam required between posts — draw grade beam path on foundation layer.” The user draws the grade beam path between post locations in Draw mode. The grade beam path is a design decision — typically it follows the perimeter between posts, but on an open-face bay it may only be on three sides. The tool cannot auto-locate it because the path depends on which sides are open and where the slab edge is intended to be.
Dimensions: Minimum 8” wide × 12” deep for agricultural buildings. Top of grade beam flush with or slightly above finished slab surface. Rebar: 2 horizontal #4 bars, continuous. Where grade beam meets a post, it wraps around the post with rebar laps. Engineer-required for Kansas wind zone for any building over 20’ wide or over 40’ long.
Takeoff lines generated: Grade beam concrete volume (LF drawn × 8” × 12” × 1.10 waste — separate line from slab, same pour event if poured monolithically with slab); horizontal rebar footage (2 bars × LF + laps at posts); form board LF (one side only — the other side is the existing soil or compacted gravel). If grade beam and slab are poured monolithically, their volumes are shown as sub-items under one pour event line.
How-to video: Seeded in video library — “Grade beam between posts — pole barn foundation concrete”; channel: Perkins Builder Brothers or AgriBuildings; search fallback: “pole barn grade beam concrete between posts forming rebar”; skill: Beginner; notes: confirm post spacing matches engineer drawing before forming — grade beam dimensions may vary at corner posts vs mid-span posts.
Footing Type 5 — Basement Wall Footing
When used: Under all basement perimeter walls. Structurally distinct from a standard perimeter footing: it is deeper (at the basement floor elevation, not just 24” below grade), wider (carrying full basement wall height plus any floor deck load above), and includes a keyway groove pressed into the top surface while the concrete is wet — the keyway mechanically locks the first course of basement wall (ICF form or poured concrete wall form) to the footing, preventing lateral sliding under soil pressure. For ICF basement walls: rebar dowels must project vertically from the footing up into the first ICF course cavity at the spacing specified by the engineer — these dowels are set in the wet footing concrete and cannot be drilled in afterward without a specialty epoxy anchor system.
Auto-generated or user-placed: AUTO-GENERATED from the basement perimeter when Basement zone type is confirmed (Section 9.1 cascade row). The footing appears at the basement floor elevation on the foundation layer, not at the main slab frost depth. The user confirms depth, width, and keyway notation. Engineer stamp required — basement wall footings carry significant lateral soil pressure loads in addition to vertical loads, and Miami County clay creates variable lateral pressure depending on soil saturation.
Dimensions: Engineer-required for final sizing. Tool default for 8’ basement wall on Miami County clay: 20” wide × 10” deep (at basement floor elevation). All shown with “ESTIMATED — engineer confirmation required” badge. Keyway: 2” wide × 1” deep groove pressed into top of footing while wet using a 2x4 ripped to keyway profile — removed after initial set. Rebar dowels: #4 at 24” OC projecting 24” above the footing top surface into the wall cavity (ICF) or wall form (poured concrete). These are set in the footing pour — not drilled later.
Takeoff lines generated: Basement wall footing concrete volume is its own separate budget line item — distinct from both the basement slab and the basement wall concrete (three separate pour events: footing first, walls second, floor slab third). Volume = perimeter LF × footing width × footing depth × 1.10 waste. Horizontal rebar footage (2 bars × perimeter LF + laps). Vertical dowel count (perimeter LF ÷ 24”). Keyway form strip LF (perimeter). Excavation is included in the main basement excavation line — not a separate footing trench.
How-to video: Seeded in video library — “Basement wall footing: keyway groove and ICF rebar dowels”; channel: Nudura or Fox Blocks official channel; search fallback: “ICF basement footing keyway groove rebar dowel projection”; skill: Intermediate; notes: the keyway and rebar dowels must be set BEFORE the footing concrete reaches initial set — have the 2x4 keyway form strip and all dowels staged and ready before the truck arrives.
Footing Type 6 — Screw Pilings (Helical Piers)
When used: Screw pilings (also called helical piers or helical piles) are steel shafts with helical bearing plates screwed into the soil by a hydraulic drive machine until they reach competent bearing material below the active clay zone. They bypass the problematic surface clay entirely. On this property they are triggered when any of the following conditions are confirmed: (1) soil test shows bearing capacity below 1,000 psf at footing depth — Miami County clay bearing capacity is highly variable; disturbed clay, fill areas, or high water table zones may not support spread footings; (2) the site has organic soil, soft fill, or a history of settlement (confirmed by visual inspection or perc test anomaly); (3) the building is sited on a slope where one side of the perimeter footing would be significantly deeper than the other, creating unequal bearing conditions; (4) the structural engineer specifies helical piers after reviewing soil conditions; (5) construction timing requires avoiding wet-season concrete pours — screw pilings install in any weather and require no concrete curing time, making them faster and more schedule-reliable on a clay site in a Kansas spring. Screw pilings are NOT owner-installable — they require a specialty contractor with a hydraulic torque motor and calibrated torque monitoring equipment. Installation is fast (a small building can be piled in a half-day) but the specialty contractor cost replaces what would have been footing concrete, rebar, forming, and excavation costs.
Auto-generated or user-placed: USER-INITIATED, ENGINEER-REQUIRED. The tool suggests screw pilings when a soil test result is entered with bearing capacity below 1,000 psf — a Tier 2 amber flag fires: “Soil bearing capacity below threshold — evaluate screw pilings as alternative to spread footings. Engineer required.” The user then selects screw pilings from the foundation support type field, which changes the foundation layer display from footing outlines to pile symbols. Pile layout (number, spacing, location) is entered from the engineer’s pile layout drawing — not auto-generated by the tool, because pile placement is an engineered design output, not a geometric calculation.
How screw pilings work: A steel shaft (typically 1.5”–3.5” diameter) with one or more helical plates welded to it is driven into the soil by a hydraulic torque motor mounted on a mini-excavator or skid steer. As the shaft rotates, the helical plates auger the pile downward without disturbing the surrounding soil significantly. Installation torque is monitored continuously — when torque reaches the value correlating to the required bearing capacity (per ASTM A1194), the pile is at the correct depth. Depth is confirmed by torque, not by a fixed target depth — this is important because bearing material depth varies across even a small site. A cap plate is welded to the top of each pile at grade; a sill plate or beam bears on the cap plates. No concrete is required for the piles themselves, though a grade beam or concrete cap beam may be poured at grade level to connect the pile caps and support the slab edge.
Effect on takeoff — complete replacement of footing lines: When screw pilings are selected, the following footing takeoff lines are REMOVED from the budget: footing concrete volume (zero — no spread footings), footing rebar, footing form board, footing excavation trench. The following lines are ADDED: screw pile count × installed depth (from engineer’s drawing, at Concept status until engineer is engaged); specialty pile contractor line item (labor + equipment — owner cannot perform this work, no $0 Owner option); engineered pile layout drawing (soft cost, Section 13.10); cap plate count (one per pile — typically welded by contractor, included in contractor quote). If a concrete cap beam is also specified: cap beam concrete volume (separate line), rebar, form board, and excavation for the cap beam trench. The slab concrete takeoff is unaffected — the slab pours over the granular fill and XPS foam regardless of what is below. Net cost effect on Miami County clay: screw pilings are often cost-competitive with conventional spread footings once excavation, forming, rebar, and concrete are fully priced — especially when wet spring conditions are factored in. Phase 1 estimate treats screw pilings as a placeholder at Concept status; Phase 2 (regional sourcing) surfaces screw pile contractors in the southeast Kansas / greater KC region.
Regional suppliers — Miami County and surrounding area: Screw piling is a specialty trade. General contractors in Paola and Louisburg typically subcontract this work. Search radius for this property: Kansas City metro (Olathe, Lenexa, Shawnee) — 40–60 miles. Search terms: “helical pier installation Kansas City,” “screw pile contractor southeast Kansas,” “helical pile foundation contractor Paola KS.” Typical contractors: Magnum Piering (KC metro), Atlas Resistance Piers regional dealers, and local foundation repair contractors who also do new construction piling. Get minimum 2 quotes — pricing varies significantly by shaft diameter, helix plate count, and site access for the drive machine. This entry is seeded in Section 13.7 Regional Supplier Knowledge.
How-to video: Seeded in video library — “Screw piling (helical pier) installation — what to expect”; channel: Magnum Piering or Atlas Pier; search fallback: “helical pier screw pile installation torque monitoring depth confirmation”; skill: Beginner (owner observation only — not owner-installable); notes: as owner, your job during pile installation is to observe torque readings being logged at each pile and confirm pile locations match the engineer’s layout drawing. Ask the contractor to show you the torque log at each pile — this is your record of confirmed bearing capacity and becomes part of the project file. Also seeded: “When to use helical piers vs spread footings”; channel: Foundation Supportworks or Build Show; search fallback: “helical pier vs spread footing clay soil cost comparison”; skill: Beginner; notes: watch this before the engineer visit to understand the trade-off conversation.
Foundation Support Type Decision Summary — Which Fires When
AUTO-GENERATED (no user placement required): Continuous perimeter footing — fires when perimeter wall is drawn on foundation layer. Monolithic thickened edge — fires when foundation type = monolithic. Thickened slab at post — fires when any structural post symbol is placed. Isolated column footing — auto-upgraded from thickened slab when post load exceeds threshold or conflict detected. Basement wall footing — fires when Basement zone type is confirmed.
USER-PLACED (tool prompts, user draws): Grade beam — prompted when construction type = metal post-frame + concrete floor; user draws path between posts.
USER-INITIATED, ENGINEER-REQUIRED: Screw pilings — suggested when soil bearing capacity < 1,000 psf or engineer flag; user confirms and enters pile contractor information. Pile layout entered from engineer’s drawing.
Pre-pour document deliverables for all footing types: Every footing type requires a confirmation field in the pre-pour document before Ready to Pour status can be set: continuous footing — depth confirmed at or below 24”, engineered anchor bolt drawing uploaded; thickened slab — post load calculation confirmed, conflict check passed; isolated footing — engineer footing size confirmed, post sleeve location confirmed; grade beam — path confirmed against post layout, rebar continuous confirmed; basement footing — keyway form strip staged, dowels staged, engineer drawing uploaded; screw pilings — engineer pile layout drawing uploaded, contractor confirmed, torque log field added to build sequence Step 6a.
8.2 Miami County Clay Soil — Applies to Every Foundation
* Vapor barrier under every slab — clay wicks moisture upward through concrete, causing mold, odor, and warped floors
* Replace native clay under slab with compacted crushed stone — clay expands when wet and will heave the slab. SCREW PILING FLAG: if a soil test reveals bearing capacity below 1,000 psf at the planned footing depth, or if the native clay at footing depth is wet, disturbed, or has a history of fill, the tool fires a Tier 2 amber flag recommending screw piling evaluation (Section 8.1a Footing Type 6). Spread footings on clay with insufficient bearing capacity will settle differentially — one corner settles more than another — causing slab cracking, door binding, and eventually structural distress. On this property, a soil test before foundation design is strongly recommended and is flagged as an open question in Section 19.
* Grade away from building on all sides — minimum 6" drop in first 10 ft
* French drain at perimeter — especially on any side with uphill grade. IMPORTANT DISTINCTION: a French drain intercepts and redirects surface runoff before it reaches the foundation — it is a surface drainage tool. Drain tile at the footing (Section 8.1 and IRC R405) relieves hydrostatic pressure that builds up against the foundation wall below grade — it is a subsurface drainage tool. On a low-lying Miami County clay site, both may be needed simultaneously: the French drain handles surface water, the drain tile handles the water table and soil saturation. They are not interchangeable
* Vapor barrier seams lapped 12" minimum and taped with compatible tape (Dow Weathermate, Tyvek tape, or equivalent — standard duct tape is not compatible and will delaminate). Do not leave gaps at wall perimeters — run barrier up the inside face of the perimeter foam and tape to the foundation wall. PENETRATION SEALING REQUIRED: every conduit stub, PEX sleeve, gas line sleeve, and floor drain that penetrates the vapor barrier must be individually sealed. Use a compatible foam-and-tape boot or closed-cell spray foam at each penetration — a vapor barrier with unsealed penetrations on Miami County clay soil is nearly as ineffective as no vapor barrier. The pre-pour checklist includes one line item per penetration requiring a sealed confirmation check before pour authorization. The rebar map (Section 8.3) marks every penetration location so the vapor barrier installer knows in advance where sealing is required.
8.3 Rebar Map — Pre-Pour Layer Output
The rebar map is a formal layer output generated from the foundation layer canvas alongside the pre-pour document. It exists because rebar placement is done in the field from memory, then buried permanently — without a map there is no way to verify what was installed, where lap splices occurred, or where rebar was omitted. The rebar map is the record of what is in the slab. It is also the document the inspector checks against before signing off on the pour.
What the rebar map shows:
Rebar grid lines with bar size labeled (#4, #5) and spacing labeled (12" OC, 16" OC). Direction of each bar run (longitudinal vs transverse). Lap splice locations — where two bars overlap, with lap length noted (minimum 40 bar diameters: 20" for #4, 25" for #5). Chair and support locations — rebar must be elevated to the correct position in the slab (1.5" clear from bottom for slab-on-grade, 3/4" for slabs with drainage below). Thickened edge zones with increased rebar density. Any zones where rebar is deliberately omitted (gravel apron, equipment wash pad) labeled explicitly so the absence is intentional and documented, not accidental. Penetration locations marked as open circles with trade label (E = electrical conduit, P = PEX sleeve, G = gas sleeve, D = drain, SP = sump pit liner). The sump pit liner is a large-diameter penetration in the granular fill — it must appear on the rebar map so rebar is not laid across the pit opening and so the vapor barrier installer knows to seal around the liner before foam placement — these are the same penetrations that require vapor barrier sealing, so the rebar map and vapor barrier checklist reference the same location identifiers. Dowel locations at any hybrid slab transition joint. Anchor bolt locations shown as filled squares with bolt size and embedment depth labeled — derived from the engineered anchor bolt drawing (Gap A).
The rebar map is generated from the foundation layer canvas — rebar grid lines are drawn in Draw mode using the Layer Interaction Model (Section 5.5.1). Bar size and spacing are set in the element property panel. The tool calculates total rebar footage from the drawn grid and adds it to the materials takeoff automatically. The rebar map is printed at 1/4"=1&#x2019; scale as part of the pre-pour document package and is filed with the permit drawings. After the pour, the owner marks the rebar map as &#x201C;As-built&#x201D; with any field deviations noted and it becomes a permanent property record.
8.4 Hybrid Slab Transition Joint — Physical Detail
When a heated and unheated zone share a building, the two slabs must be poured as physically independent slabs that can move without cracking each other. They will have different thermal expansion rates (the heated slab expands and contracts with radiant cycles), potentially different settlement rates (different load distributions), and different moisture content over time. A continuous slab across this boundary will crack — not might crack, will crack — at the thermal differential line.
Transition joint assembly from bottom to top:
1. Compressible foam isolation board (1/2" minimum, Dow Ethafoam or equivalent closed-cell PE foam) placed vertically at the full depth of the slab — this is the primary separation element. It must run the full width of the building at the zone boundary. 2. Bond breaker: the isolation board acts as a bond breaker between the two pours — do not allow concrete from one pour to contact the formed face of the other. 3. Dowels: smooth steel dowels (not deformed rebar) placed horizontally through the isolation board at mid-slab depth, spaced 18" OC. Dowels transfer vertical load between slabs while allowing horizontal movement. One end of each dowel is greased or sleeved to allow sliding — the greased end is on the unheated side so the heated slab can expand freely. 4. Surface sealant: after both slabs are cured, the top of the joint is routed 1/4" wide and 1/4" deep and filled with a polyurethane joint sealant (Sika or equivalent). This prevents water infiltration at the surface while remaining flexible. The transition joint location is shown on the foundation layer canvas (Section 5.1, Gap A) and the rebar map. The joint location is a pre-pour blocker — the tool will not generate the pre-pour document for a building with mixed heated and unheated zones until the transition joint is placed on the foundation layer canvas.
8.5 Backfill Compaction on Clay Soil
The Section 8.2 grading requirement (6" drop in first 10 ft) depends on the backfill staying where it is placed. On Miami County clay soil, improperly compacted backfill settles unevenly over 1–3 years and pulls grade away from the building — creating negative grade against the foundation. This is one of the most common causes of foundation moisture problems in the region. Proper backfill procedure eliminates the problem before it starts.
Backfill requirements for foundation walls on clay soil:
Do not use excavated clay as backfill against foundation walls. Clay expands when wet and contracts when dry — this cycles against the foundation wall and eventually causes lateral cracking. Use compactable granular fill (crushed stone, gravel, or clean sand) for the first 24" against the foundation wall on all sides. Place in 6" lifts and compact each lift with a plate compactor or hand tamper before placing the next. Do not use a jumping jack compactor within 3 ft of the foundation wall — the impact load can crack the wall before the concrete has fully cured. At 24" from the wall, transition to native soil or import fill is acceptable if compacted in lifts. The first 10 ft of grade away from the building is then established over the compacted fill with topsoil and seeded — the compacted base prevents the 6" grade drop from settling away. The build sequence card for the foundation layer includes backfill compaction as a named step with lift count tracked in the pre-pour and site work checklist.
8.6 Pre-Pour Document — Foundation Output
All foundation decisions described in Section 8 are finalized in the foundation layer canvas and output as a single pre-pour document before any concrete is ordered. The pre-pour document is the formal coordination output of the tool — it is not a summary of decisions made elsewhere, it is the direct output of the layer canvas. See Section 5.2 for the full pre-pour layer spec and sequence enforcement rules.
The pre-pour document package for a foundation includes: rebar map (Section 8.3) at 1/4"=1&#x2019; scale, engineered anchor bolt layout (stamped drawing uploaded and referenced), vapor barrier penetration checklist (one line per penetration with sealed confirmation field), PEX loop layout with manifold position and pressure test record, conduit stub locations with sweep directions, hybrid slab transition joint location if applicable, XPS foam layer diagram showing stagger pattern, concrete spec (mix design, slump, fiber or wire mesh, yardage), pour sequence for hybrid slabs (which zone pours first and cure time before second pour), and inspector sign-off field that unlocks &#x201C;Ready to Pour&#x201D; status. No concrete is ordered until Ready to Pour status is set. The tool blocks concrete quantity from appearing in the budget at Estimated status until the pre-pour document is complete — partial or assumed quantities are shown at Concept status only.
9. Decision Cascade Engine
The cascade relationships below are the calculation side of layer activation. When a layer activates (Section 5.1), the corresponding calculations in this section fire simultaneously. Every item placement has both a visual output (layer) and a numeric output (cascade).
The tool tracks which decisions have been made (locked nodes) and which are still open (variable nodes). When a node is locked, all downstream calculations that depend on it update automatically. The HVAC module shows: these 4 inputs are confirmed, these 3 are still open, here is your current load range.

9.2 Cross-Scope Cascades
Some decisions affect multiple buildings or the property level:
* Shared wall between zones — insulation decision updates HVAC load calculations for both zones simultaneously
* Any building's propane load — rolls up to property-level tank sizing
* All buildings' electrical loads — inform electrical topology decision (main + sub-panels vs independent meters)
* Septic: each bathroom fixture adds to the septic load calculation across all buildings on the property
* Inter-building conduit: electrical topology decision determines whether conduit runs are needed and how many
* Workshop fireplace + radiant + south solar gain — overheating risk calculation spans all active heat sources simultaneously


9.3 Next Decision Queue
The cascade engine surfaces the single most important open decision to the user at all times, based on what is currently locked. The queue is prioritized by a three-tier system — not first-in-first-out. SCENARIO SCOPING: the Next Decision Queue is always scoped to the currently selected Scenario. Switching Scenarios reloads the queue for that Scenario. When multiple Scenarios exist, the property workspace summary view shows each Scenario's Tier 1 / Tier 2 / Tier 3 counts simultaneously — "Scenario A: 2 blockers, 4 estimates pending | Scenario B: 0 blockers, 6 estimates pending" — so the owner can see at a glance which Scenario is further developed. The queue itself always shows one Scenario at a time. Decisions that block construction are always surfaced before decisions that only affect cost estimates, which are always surfaced before decisions with no immediate downstream consequence. Tier 1 (blocks construction): pre-pour blockers, permit dependencies, engineered drawing requirements, life safety items (combustion air method). These appear in red and cannot be bypassed. Tier 2 (blocks budget estimation): decisions that move a line item from Concept to Estimated status. These appear in amber. Tier 3 (planning decisions): finish material choices, supplier preferences, aesthetic decisions. These appear in the queue only when all Tier 1 and Tier 2 items are resolved. The queue always shows the user&#x2019;s current Tier 1 count, Tier 2 count, and Tier 3 count as a summary — so the owner always knows how many blocking issues remain before construction can proceed.
The Next Decision Queue directly fulfills Section 1&#x2019;s promise that &#x201C;one decision automatically adjusts what questions need to be answered next.&#x201D; The cascade engine updates calculations (Section 9.1 and 9.2); the Next Decision Queue updates the guided question flow for the user.
* Priority 1 — Zone heated/unheated: unblocks foundation type, slab assembly, HVAC strategy, insulation requirements, and permit path simultaneously. Always the first decision surfaced for any new zone.
* Priority 2 — Wall construction type: unblocks electrical routing method, insulation R-value, structural assumptions, roof structure options, and framing package.
* Priority 3 — Radiant floors yes/no: unblocks slab assembly spec, XPS foam grade, PEX loop layout, manifold location, and boiler requirement.
* Priority 4 — Ceiling type: unblocks HVAC system selection (cathedral forces mini-split or radiant only), ridge beam engineering requirement, and attic availability for HVAC and electrical.Priority 0 (pre-queue, fires before Priority 1) — Below-grade room decision: if a building is being created, the tool asks "Does this building contain a vehicle service pit, cold storage room, or basement?" before any other zone decisions. This is Priority 0 because the answer changes the foundation engineering requirements fundamentally — a pit, cold storage room, or basement must be in the foundation design from the beginning. Once the main slab is poured, none can be added without breaking concrete. This question appears as a mandatory prompt at building creation, not as an open item in the queue. If the answer is Yes: vehicle pit zone, cold storage zone, and/or basement zone are added to the building, and the relevant Tier 1 blockers (geometry placement on foundation layer, below-grade room pour sequence, egress window location for basement) immediately enter the queue. If the answer is No: the question is archived and does not re-appear. It can be revisited only with an explicit "Add below-grade room" action that includes a warning: "Adding a below-grade room requires redesigning the foundation. This is only practical before the slab is poured."Priority 1.5 (between zone type and wall construction) — Zone type selection: immediately after heated/unheated is confirmed, zone type is surfaced. Zone type unlocks the knowledge package, pre-pour requirements, and system activations for that zone. Cannot be left as default — the tool requires an explicit zone type selection before proceeding to wall construction type.
* Priority 1.75 (between zone type and wall construction, fires only when Basement zone type is selected) — Basement structural decisions: immediately after Basement zone type is confirmed, three decisions are surfaced in sequence before wall construction type is asked, because they determine the wall construction type options and the foundation engineering scope. (a) Basement wall type (ICF / poured concrete / CMU) — ICF auto-recommended for Miami County clay soil; selection locks insulation method, vapor retarder class, and interior finish path. (b) Waterproofing strategy (membrane required — damp-proof only blocked per Section 9.1 cascade row) — confirmation unlocks drainage board, footing drain tile, and backfill sequence in the pre-pour checklist. (c) Egress window well location — placed on foundation layer as a Tier 1 blocker before foundation can be formed; window well and egress window specs enforced (IRC R310: 5.7 sq ft net clear, 24” height, 20” width, max 44” sill). All three must be at Confirmed status before the foundation layer is unlocked for the basement zone.
* Priority 5 — Solar intent: unblocks roof orientation requirement, conduit stub spec, utility interconnect process, and array zone reservation on roof plan.
* Priority 6 — Wet wall location lock: unblocks drain pipe sizing (stack diameter determines wall framing requirement — 3” or 4” stack requires 2x6 minimum), Pipe Segment Records for all drains running to that stack, and vent stack roof penetration placement. Wet wall location drives the plumbing riser diagram layout. Once locked, moving the wet wall requires re-routing all upstream pipe segment records — cascade preview shows affected segment count before allowing the move. Cannot be left unresolved when any bathroom or kitchen zone is at Roughed-in status or higher.
* Priority 7 — HVAC equipment selection lock (make/model confirmed): unblocks Lineset Record finalization (diameter and maximum length are model-specific), electrical circuit amperage lock, Condensate Drain Record route finalization, and HVAC trade package finalization. Equipment lock also fires the BTU confirmation check: if confirmed equipment capacity differs from Manual J calculated load by more than 20%, a Tier 2 amber flag fires requiring an override note explaining the sizing decision. Cannot be deferred past framing inspection — lineset sleeve locations must be confirmed in the wall before close-in.
* Priority 8 — Gas pipe sizing lock (all Appliance BTU Records Confirmed): unblocks gas trade package finalization, CSST Bonding Coordination Record completion (if CSST is used anywhere in the building), Tank Evaporation Capacity Record final check, first-stage regulator sizing confirmation, and pre-pour gas sleeve placement. Gas pipe sizing cannot be locked until every appliance in the building has a Confirmed BTU Record — a Concept-status appliance holds the entire system at planning grade. The cascade engine surfaces the highest-BTU unconfirmed appliance as the next item to resolve when this priority is active.
PHASE 1 IMPLEMENTATION: Display the Next Decision card as a persistent banner at the top of the workspace. Show the single highest-priority open decision with a one-sentence explanation of why it matters and a direct link to the relevant input. When a node is locked, the card updates immediately to the next open decision. If all high-priority decisions are locked, the card shows a project completeness summary instead.


9. Structural Recommendations by Wall & Roof Construction
Wall construction type does not just constrain roof structure — it largely determines which roof structures are viable. These recommendations are specific to Kansas wind zone, Miami County, and owner-builder context.


9.1 Wall-to-Roof Structural Matrix
Wall type
	Recommended roof structure
	Avoid / caution
	Key structural note
	SIP panels
	Best: SIP roof panels + structural ridge beam (LVL/Glulam). Good: conventional rafters on SIP top plate + structural ridge beam. Simple (lean-to): shed roof — no ridge needed.
	Avoid: hip roof — triangle cuts at hips create high waste and complex eave connections. Caution: traditional ridge board with rafter pairs — outward thrust must be engineered at plate connection.
	Structural ridge beam eliminates outward thrust — cleanest load path with SIP walls. Ridge beam posts must be located in floor plan before anything else — they bear on foundation footings. Engineer required for Kansas wind uplift.
	2x6 stud framing
	Best: pre-engineered trusses 24" OC — fastest and most economical. Good: stick-framed rafters with ridge board or structural ridge beam.
	Caution: cathedral ceiling with stick rafters — vapor drive ratio must be correct (50%+ of total R above deck). Easy to get wrong and expensive to fix.
	Trusses include ceiling chord — flat ceiling plus attic space automatic. Open-web trusses allow HVAC equipment in attic. Kansas wind: hurricane ties required at every truss-to-plate connection — not optional.
	ICF (insulated concrete forms)
	Best: any conventional roof structure — ICF bond beam handles outward thrust extremely well. Hip roof is viable here — walls handle corner loads.
	Caution: SIP roof panels on ICF walls — eave connection to concrete bond beam is non-standard, needs specialty engineered detail.
	Anchor bolts set in bond beam (top ICF course) before pour. Rafter connection is to a wood plate bolted to the bond beam. ICF is the most tornado-resistant wall system — pairs well with a basement for Kansas storm shelter.
	Metal post-frame / pole barn
	System: roof structure is integral to the building frame — not a separate choice. Trusses or purlins span between main frames, main frames carry to posts, posts carry to slab.
	Caution: adding conditioned living space to a metal building after the fact — insulation and vapor barrier retrofit is difficult and expensive. Decide at design whether any zone will ever be conditioned.
	Most familiar system to Kansas agricultural contractors. Engineered by manufacturer for wind zone. Anchor bolt pattern comes from manufacturer drawings — do not substitute. Best choice for pure equipment storage.
	

9.2 Roof Shape Compatibility by Wall Type
Roof shape
	SIP walls
	Stud framing
	Metal post-frame
	Notes
	Gable — two planes, one ridge
	Best choice. Panels cut rectangular, minimal waste.
	Standard. Trusses or rafters.
	Standard. Comes as gable by default.
	Simplest geometry. Best for SIP. Best for solar — one clean south face.
	Shed / mono-pitch — one plane
	Simplest of all. No ridge needed. Wall heights differ — order panels accordingly.
	Standard. One bearing wall higher than other.
	Standard lean-to configuration.
	Natural choice for lean-to. Low wall attaches to host building high wall.
	Hip — four planes, no gable ends
	Avoid. Triangle cuts at hips = high waste, complex eave connections, specialty contractor required.
	Viable. More complex framing. Better aerodynamic wind profile.
	Possible but non-standard for agricultural buildings.
	Lower wind profile than gable — but SIP complexity makes it inadvisable for owner-builder.
	Gambrel / barn roof — two pitches per side
	Very difficult. Angle break is a complex SIP panel joint — specialty only.
	Traditional barn aesthetic. Maximizes loft volume above upper chord.
	Non-standard for metal buildings.
	Classic barn look. Only practical with stud framing for this project.
	Asymmetric — steeper south, shallower north
	Fully viable with SIP. South panels longer than north — specify at order.
	Fully viable.
	Can be specified at manufacturer design stage.
	Recommended when solar is a priority. South face at 30-35 degrees optimal for 38.5 N latitude. Ridge offset from center.
	

9.3 The Structural Ridge Beam Cascade
The ridge beam is the central structural decision for any pitched roof on SIP walls or any cathedral ceiling. It creates a four-step cascade that reaches from a wall choice all the way down into the concrete:
* Step 1 — Wall choice: SIP panels selected for workshop zone
* Step 2 — Roof structure: structural ridge beam required (no outward thrust on SIP walls)
* Step 3 — Ridge beam posts required: posts must be located in floor plan — they are load-bearing elements
* Step 4 — Foundation: post locations need thickened slab or individual column footings in the pre-pour plan
CASCADE RULE: The ridge beam post locations must be in the floor plan before the slab is designed. This is a four-step cascade from a wall choice to a concrete decision that is invisible unless the tool traces it explicitly.


9.4 Ridge Height and Wall Panel Height Calculation
With SIP walls, wall panel height is fixed at manufacture. Changing it after ordering is not possible. The ridge height must be calculated before ordering wall panels.
* Ridge height above floor = wall panel height + (half building width x pitch rise per foot)
* Example: 10 ft wall panels, 5/12 pitch, 40 ft wide building → half span = 20 ft → rise = 20 x (5/12) = 8.3 ft → ridge height = 18.3 ft above floor
* Interior ceiling height at eave = wall panel height (the low side). This is the minimum clearance in the space.
* Interior ceiling height at ridge = wall height + full rise. For cathedral ceiling, this is the maximum clearance.
* Ridge beam span and height above floor determines beam size: longer span + taller post = larger beam required. Tool generates approximate LVL size from span and load tables, flags when engineering is required (essentially always in Kansas wind zone for spans over 20 ft).
* Asymmetric roof: south face steeper (e.g. 6/12), north face shallower (e.g. 3/12). Ridge is offset from building center toward north wall. South panels are longer. North panels are shorter. Specify both pitches and ridge offset position at SIP order.


9.5 Roof Pitch — Constraints and Recommendations
Consideration
	Minimum / optimum
	Notes
	SIP panels with standing seam metal
	1/12 minimum (profile-dependent). 3/12 preferred.
	Concealed clips allow thermal movement — correct for Kansas temperature swings. No exposed fasteners penetrating panel. Solar clamp attachment requires no penetrations. Recommended choice when solar is possible.
	SIP panels with exposed fastener metal panel
	3/12 minimum. 4/12 preferred.
	Less than 3/12 risks water infiltration at panel joints. Exposed fasteners penetrate SIP facing — each is a potential long-term leak point. Kansas thermal cycling causes fasteners to back out over time. Not recommended for living quarters or solar roofs.
	Solar panel production — Kansas 38.5°N
	28-35 degrees optimal (roughly 6/12 to 8/12)
	Steeper than 35 degrees starts losing production. South-facing surface (180°) at latitude angle is theoretical optimum. Off-axis roofs: azimuth derate applies — a roof pitched correctly but facing SE (135°) or SW (225°) loses ~10–15% annual production vs. true south. A roof facing E (90°) or W (270°) loses ~25–30% regardless of pitch. The pitch optimum also shifts: for a SE/SW roof the optimal pitch increases slightly (add ~3–5 degrees to the south-facing optimum). Existing barn roof: measure actual azimuth and pitch with iPhone before assuming production estimate. See Section 7.5 for measurement procedure and derate table.
	Snow shedding
	4/12 or steeper sheds snow well
	Kansas design snow load ~20 psf. Steeper pitches self-clear. Shallower pitches accumulate.
	Wind profile
	Lower pitches present less wind surface area
	4/12 or less catches less wind. High pitches catch more but shed better than flat.
	Attic availability
	Any pitch with truss framing
	Flat ceiling chord + pitched rafter top chord = attic space at any pitch. Cathedral ceiling eliminates attic regardless of pitch.
	Overhang solar control — south face
	Overhang projection = window height ÷ 2 (approx for Kansas latitude)
	South window 4 ft tall → 2 ft overhang projection blocks summer sun, admits winter sun. This is a load-bearing calculation input, not a decorative choice.
	

9.6 Structural Elements Visible on the Building Plan
The structural layer of the floor plan and the structural layer of the roof plan together make the complete structural picture visible. What each view shows:
View
	Structural elements shown
	Floor plan — structural layer
	Load-bearing wall locations, post base plate locations (with footing callout), beam pocket locations, header sizes over all openings, holdown locations at shear wall corners, LVL beam runs, interior load-bearing walls vs non-load-bearing partitions
	Roof plan — structural layer
	Ridge beam line with elevation marked, ridge beam span and post locations (linked to floor plan post locations), rafter or truss spacing, hip or valley lines (if applicable), structural screws or hurricane tie callout at every rafter-to-plate connection
	Section view — structural layer
	Complete load path from ridge to foundation: ridge beam bearing on post, post bearing on footing, rafters/SIP panels bearing on top plate, top plate connection to SIP wall, SIP wall bearing on sill plate, sill plate anchored to foundation, footing at frost depth. Dimensions: wall height, ridge height, eave height, footing depth.
	9.6 Decision History Log
Every decision field in the tool maintains an append-only change log. The log records what changed, when, and how the change originated. For a multi-year project like this one, the decision log is a first-class feature — not an afterthought. Being able to see that a construction type was changed eight months ago, why, and what cascaded from it is the difference between confidently continuing a project and reconstructing it from memory.
Each log entry records:
Timestamp (date and time). Field changed (human-readable label, e.g. &#x201C;Workshop — Wall Construction Type&#x201D;). Old value. New value. Source — one of five: Manual (owner typed or selected the value directly), Claude Prompt 1 (decision-support output), Claude Prompt 2 (sourcing output), Claude Prompt 3 (quote comparison output), or Cascade (set automatically by the cascade engine). For calculated values the source is always Cascade and the reason note auto-populates with the calculation chain and reference table citation per Section 5.8. For values sourced from external reference tables (NEC, NFPA 54, UPC, ASHRAE, IECC) the source note identifies the specific table and section. The full provenance display specification is in Section 5.8. Reason note — optional free text for manual changes, auto-populated for cascade changes with the triggering decision identified (e.g. &#x201C;Set by cascade: Solar intent changed to Grid-Tied&#x201D;).
Log access and display:
Every field in the property panel and surface record shows a small clock icon if it has more than one log entry — indicating the value has changed at least once. Clicking the clock icon opens the full log for that field in a sidebar. The log is read-only — entries cannot be edited or deleted. The log is not visible in the Builder View (Section 2.1b) to keep the interface clean for non-technical users, but is always accessible in the Owner/Developer view. A property-level &#x201C;Recent Changes&#x201D; panel in the main workspace shows the last 10 log entries across all fields — useful for resuming work after a gap of weeks or months. This panel answers the question &#x201C;what did I last decide?&#x201D; without requiring the owner to remember where they left off.
Log export: the full decision history log exports as a JSON file matching the dashboard schema (Flask app at E:\anthony_dashboard) and as a printable chronological report. The printable report is useful for permit applications and inspector conversations — it demonstrates that decisions were made deliberately and in sequence, not improvised on site. Phase 2: the log feeds a project timeline view showing decision velocity over time — useful for identifying periods of stall and for estimating how long similar future decisions will take to resolve.
10. Roof Plan Layer
The roof plan is a top-down view of the roof surface. It is a separate layer from the floor plan but linked to all the same decisions. It is the view where structure, drainage, solar, and all roof penetrations are visible simultaneously — making conflicts visible that are invisible in any other view.


10.1 What the Roof Plan Layer Shows
Element
	Layer it belongs to
	What is shown
	What it drives
	Ridge line
	Structural
	Line at roof peak with elevation callout
	Ridge beam sizing, post locations, wall panel height confirmation
	Ridge beam and posts
	Structural
	Heavy line for beam, solid squares for posts
	Floor plan post locations, foundation footing locations — cascade to pre-pour plan
	Rafters or trusses
	Structural
	Dashed lines at 24" OC across both roof faces
	Penetration placement — vents and linesets must land between rafters, not on them
	Slope direction arrows
	Always on
	Arrows showing downhill direction on each roof face, with pitch callout (e.g. 5/12)
	Drainage design, solar panel orientation, snow accumulation zones
	Gutters
	Drainage
	Heavy line at eaves where gutters are planned
	Downspout locations, site drainage direction, foundation protection
	Downspouts
	Drainage

	Overhang extent

	Flue must not be inside solar array zone. Chimney cap above ridge by code minimum.
	Exhaust fan penetrations

	Bathroom fans, range hood duct exits. Must clear solar zone and land between rafters.
	Valley lines

	Water concentrates at valleys — valley flashing is critical. Lean-to intersection with main barn creates a valley.
	Conflict flags
