	Penetration inside solar zone, vent on a rafter, flue too close to solar panels, downspout discharging toward foundation
	

10.2 Roof Plan Activation — What Turns It On
* Shell layer: roof outline always visible as soon as building is created
* Structural layer: ridge line, posts, and rafter spacing appear when construction type is selected
* Drainage layer: gutters and downspouts appear when roof area exceeds a threshold or when living quarters zone exists in the building
* Solar layer: array zone appears when solar intent is not none — even if solar is only 'future' or 'maybe', the zone is reserved so no penetrations are placed in it
* Penetrations: each appears on roof plan as soon as its source is placed on the floor plan — plumbing vent when toilet or shower is placed, lineset when mini-split is placed, flue when fireplace is placed
* Conflict detection runs automatically whenever any two layers are both active — flags appear without user action


10.3 The Overhang — Roof Plan Detail That Feeds HVAC Sizing
The overhang depth on the south face is not a decorative choice. It is a calculated dimension that directly reduces summer cooling load and it lives on the roof plan.
* South-facing windows at 38.5°N latitude: summer sun is 70+ degrees above horizon, winter sun is 25-30 degrees above horizon
* A correctly sized overhang blocks high summer sun completely but admits low winter sun fully
* Calculation: overhang projection = window height x 0.5 (for true south-facing walls, bearing 180°, at Kansas latitude 38.5°N). Off-axis correction: for walls between 150°–210° (within 30° of true south) the 0.5 multiplier is still accurate. For walls between 120°–150° or 210°–240° (SSE/SSW, 30–60° off south) reduce the multiplier to 0.35 — the lower sun angle means less overhang projection is needed to block summer sun. For walls more than 60° off south, horizontal overhangs lose effectiveness — flag in the tool and recommend vertical fins or interior shading instead. The wall bearing (from Section 3.4 Surface Record orientation field) drives which multiplier is used.
* Example: 4 ft tall south window requires 2 ft overhang projection for full summer shade and full winter solar gain
* The roof plan shows this dimension on the south eave. The HVAC load calculation reads it as a shading factor on south window solar heat gain coefficient.
* East and west overhangs: less effective because sun angle is low in morning and afternoon. Vertical fins work better than horizontal overhangs on east and west faces — flag in the tool when east or west windows are large.
* This dimension also feeds the solar array zone boundary — the overhang extension is where the array cannot start. Array begins inboard of the overhang edge.


10.4 Conflict Detection — Why the Roof Plan Layer Matters
The most important function of the roof plan is detecting conflicts that are invisible in any other view. Automatic conflict flags are generated for:
* Penetration inside solar array zone — plumbing vent, lineset, exhaust fan, or flue located within the planned array footprint. Resolution: relocate the penetration or adjust the array boundary.
* Penetration on a rafter line — vent or lineset sleeve positioned at a rafter location. Resolution: shift penetration 6 inches to land between rafters.
* Flue too close to solar panels — code requires minimum clearance between flue exhaust and combustible materials including panel frames. Flag when flue is within 3 ft of array boundary.
* Downspout discharging toward foundation — drainage direction arrow points toward building rather than away. Flag when downspout location does not have a clear path away from the foundation perimeter.
* Valley under solar array — a lean-to or addition creates a valley at the intersection. Valley requires flashing and regular maintenance. Flag when array zone extends to within 12 inches of a valley line.
* Chimney cap below ridge — code requires flue termination minimum 2 ft above any roof surface within 10 ft horizontally. Flag when flue height is not confirmed to meet this clearance.
10.5 Roof Penetration Conflict Zones vs Solar Reservation
Every penetration placed on the roof plan layer — plumbing vent, exhaust fan, flue, HVAC lineset sleeve, skylight — is checked against the solar reservation zone boundary from the solar layer (Section 5.1). A penetration inside the solar reservation zone is a conflict. Solar panel installers require a minimum 18" clearance from any roof penetration to the nearest panel edge for fire code access (IRC R324). A penetration in the middle of a future array zone is not just an aesthetic problem — it either eliminates panels from that area or requires relocating the penetration through the finished roof at significant cost.
Conflict flags: penetration placed inside solar reservation zone — red flag with penetration identity and panel count impact (how many panels are lost if the penetration stays). Penetration within 18" of solar reservation zone boundary — amber flag (marginal clearance, may cause installer problems). Ridge vent or continuous ridge cap conflicting with ridge-to-array clearance — flag if solar intent is active or reserved (ridge vents and solar arrays share the ridge zone). The tool also checks in the opposite direction: if a penetration is placed first and the solar reservation zone is drawn later, the zone boundary automatically excludes the 18" clearance radius around each penetration. The owner sees the net usable array area after exclusions, not the gross roof area.
10.6 Gutters and Downspouts — Roof Plan Layer Elements
Gutters and downspouts are drawn on the roof plan layer as the link between roof drainage and site drainage. They are not a finish item — downspout discharge locations are a foundation protection decision that must be coordinated with the site drainage layer (Section 5.4) before grading begins. A downspout discharging against the foundation or toward the septic drain field is a drainage conflict that is easy to prevent and expensive to correct after landscaping is established.
Gutter elements on the roof plan layer:
Gutter run drawn as a line element along the eave with size property (4" or 6" K-style, 4" or 6" half-round). Downspout placed as a symbol at each gutter low point with discharge direction arrow. Downspout sizing: one 3"x4" downspout per 600 sq ft of roof area draining to it — the tool calculates drainage area from the roof surface geometry and flags undersized downspout count. Discharge direction arrow feeds directly into the site drainage layer — when the downspout symbol is placed, a corresponding discharge arrow appears on the site drainage layer automatically. The two layers stay in sync: moving a downspout on the roof plan moves its discharge arrow on the drainage layer. Conflict flags: discharge arrow pointing toward foundation within 6 ft, discharge arrow pointing toward septic drain field, gutter slope insufficient (minimum 1/16" per foot toward downspout — flag if gutter run exceeds 40 ft without a downspout at each end).
10.7 Rake Overhang — Decision and Structural Flag
The eave overhang (Section 10.3) shades south-facing windows and is sized by formula. The rake overhang — the overhang at the gable end, perpendicular to the ridge — has no solar shading function but has structural implications that the spec currently does not address. Rake overhang depth is a separate decision from eave overhang depth and must be set explicitly on the roof plan layer.
Rake overhang structural flags by construction type: Metal purlin building — rake overhang beyond 12" requires a fly rafter (outrigger) system extending from the last purlin. This is additional framing cost and must appear in the framing takeoff. SIP roof — rake overhang beyond 12" requires structural consideration at the panel edge. SIP panels are not designed to cantilever at the rake without a structural outlooker or rake beam — the panel manufacturer must confirm the detail. Stud framing — rake overhang up to 24" is standard with lookout framing from the last rafter, beyond 24" requires engineering. The tool flags any rake overhang depth that triggers a structural requirement for the selected construction type and adds a structural review item to the Tier 1 decision queue. Rake overhang also affects the total roof footprint used in solar available area calculation (Section 10.8) — rake overhangs are outside the usable array zone.
10.8 Roof Valley Design — Material Selection and Conflict Flags
A valley forms wherever two roof planes meet at an inside corner — L-shaped and T-shaped footprints always produce valleys. Valleys are the highest leak risk on any roof because water from two roof planes concentrates and accelerates through them. Valley design is a material and labor decision that affects the roofing takeoff, the framing sequence, and the maintenance schedule. The roof plan layer shows valley lines automatically when two intersecting roof planes are drawn, and requires a valley material selection before the roofing takeoff is calculated.
Valley material options and trade-offs:
Open valley with W-metal flashing: best drainage, most durable, visible from ground, requires a clear 3"–6" channel down the center. Standard for metal roofing in Kansas. Owner-installable. Takeoff adds valley flashing linear footage and sealant. Closed cut valley (shingles only): one shingle plane runs through, the other is cut. Not applicable to metal roofing. Woven valley (shingles only): both planes interweave. Not applicable to metal roofing. For this property with metal roofing as the primary recommendation, open valley with W-metal flashing is the default. The valley symbol on the roof plan layer shows the required clear channel width as a hatched zone — any penetration, solar panel, or other element placed in this zone triggers a conflict flag. Valley blocking: the framing layer requires solid blocking in the valley rafter bay before roofing — this appears as a pre-roofing checklist item. Snow and ice accumulate heavily in valleys in Kansas winters — valley flashing must extend a minimum of 18" on each side of the valley centerline and be sealed at all laps.
10.9 Snow Load Accumulation Zones
Kansas design ground snow load is approximately 20 psf (Section 9.5). The roof plan layer identifies zones where snow accumulates above the design load due to geometry — these are structural risk locations that must be flagged for the engineer and for the owner&#x2019;s maintenance awareness. The design snow load assumes uniform distribution across the roof surface. Geometry that concentrates snow produces point or line loads that can exceed the uniform design load significantly.
Accumulation zones flagged automatically on the roof plan layer: Valley zones — snow slides from both planes into the valley and piles. Flag at every valley with a note: &#x201C;Snow accumulation zone — confirm valley framing for 1.5x design load.&#x201D; Base-of-upper-roof zones — where a higher roof abuts a lower roof (common on L-shaped buildings), snow slides off the upper roof and lands on the lower roof at the wall intersection. This drift load can be 2–3x the design ground snow load. Flag at any step-down roof geometry with: &#x201C;Drift load zone — engineer review required for lower roof framing at this wall.&#x201D; Long eave zones — eave overhangs longer than 18" accumulate a cantilevered snow load at the panel-to-top-plate connection (same issue identified in Section 6.2 SIP Roof Watch List). Flag when eave overhang exceeds 18" on any roof: &#x201C;Eave snow load — confirm panel edge or rafter connection for cantilevered load.&#x201D; Ice dam risk zones — flag the first 3 ft of eave on any heated zone as an ice dam risk area in Kansas climate. Recommend minimum 6 ft of ice-and-water shield underlayment at eave (2 ft past the interior wall line) for any heated space below.
10.10 Roof Geometry as Solar Array Size Cap
The solar production estimate calculator (Section 7.5) works from array wattage as an input. But array wattage is constrained by the physical area available on the roof. The roof plan layer provides the geometry that caps the maximum array size — this connection must be explicit so the tool can flag when the required array exceeds the available roof area and recommend ground mount for the shortfall.
Available roof area calculation:
Start with gross roof surface area of the solar-facing plane (derived from roof plan geometry — width × rafter length on slope). Subtract: ridge clearance (18" minimum from ridge to top panel row per fire code), eave clearance (12" minimum from eave to bottom panel row), rake clearance (12" minimum from rake edge to side panel column), penetration exclusion zones (18" radius around each penetration), valley exclusion zone if applicable. Result is net usable array area in square feet. Divide by panel footprint (typically 18–22 sq ft per 400W panel) to get maximum panel count. Multiply by panel wattage to get maximum array wattage. If the array wattage required by the production estimate reverse-sizing calculation (Section 7.5) exceeds this maximum, the tool flags: &#x201C;Roof area insufficient for target output — [X] kW of additional capacity requires ground mount. Estimated ground mount area: [Y] sq ft.&#x201D; The ground mount area estimate uses the same panel footprint plus 1.5x row spacing factor for tilt-mount ground racks.
The roof plan layer displays available array area as a live number in the solar layer sidebar whenever solar intent is active or reserved. This number updates automatically when penetrations are added, when overhang depth changes, or when the roof surface dimensions are edited. It is the single number the owner needs to answer &#x201C;how much solar can this roof actually fit?&#x201D; before committing to a system size.
10.11 Overhang Decision Support — Full Geometry with Location, Wall, Pitch, and Window
The overhang is the most consequential passive solar decision on the building exterior. A correctly sized overhang blocks summer sun from entering south-facing windows while allowing winter sun in — reducing cooling load in summer and heating load in winter simultaneously, with no moving parts and no operating cost. An undersized overhang fails at summer shading. An oversized overhang blocks winter sun and increases heating load. The tool calculates the correct overhang depth for each window individually, based on the full six-input geometry described below, and presents the result as a decision support suggestion with a real-time seasonal performance preview.
Why latitude is the foundation of every overhang calculation:
Latitude determines the sun&#x2019;s altitude angle at solar noon on the two critical dates: summer solstice (longest day, highest sun) and winter solstice (shortest day, lowest sun). The overhang must block the summer solstice noon sun from the window glass while allowing the winter solstice noon sun to reach the window sill. These two constraints together define the correct overhang depth for any given window at any given latitude. The formula for noon sun altitude angle is: 90° minus (latitude minus solar declination), where solar declination is +23.45° at summer solstice and -23.45° at winter solstice. For this property at Miami County Kansas latitude 38.5°N: summer solstice noon sun angle = 90° - (38.5° - 23.45°) = 75.0°. Winter solstice noon sun angle = 90° - (38.5° + 23.45°) = 28.1°. The difference between these two angles — 46.9° — is what makes passive solar overhang design effective at this latitude. Kansas sits close to the theoretical optimum for fixed overhang performance in North America: the summer sun is high enough to block easily, and the winter sun is low enough to admit freely, with a single fixed overhang depth satisfying both constraints simultaneously. The property latitude is stored in Section 3.1 and feeds all overhang calculations automatically. If this tool is used for a property at a different latitude, every overhang suggestion updates to reflect the new sun angles — no manual adjustment required.
The six inputs and what each contributes:
Input 1 — Latitude (from property record, Section 3.1): drives summer and winter solstice noon sun angles. Fixed for this property at 38.5°N. Input 2 — Wall bearing (from surface record, Section 3.4): drives off-axis correction factor. True south (180°) uses full multiplier. SSE/SSW (150°–210°) within 30° of south uses 0.95x. SE/SW (120°–150° or 210°–240°) uses 0.65x — the lower afternoon or morning sun angle on these faces means the overhang needs less projection to achieve shading. Walls more than 60° off south are flagged: fixed horizontal overhangs are ineffective on east and west walls — the low morning and afternoon sun angles mean no practical fixed overhang can block summer sun without also blocking all useful daylight. Operable shading or interior blinds are recommended instead. Input 3 — Roof pitch (from roof plan layer): the overhang runs along the roof slope, not horizontally. The horizontal projection — which is what blocks the sun — is overhang length along slope multiplied by cosine of pitch angle. Pitch angle in degrees = arctan(rise/run): 3/12 pitch = 14.0°, cos = 0.97; 4/12 = 18.4°, cos = 0.949; 6/12 = 26.6°, cos = 0.894; 8/12 = 33.7°, cos = 0.832; 12/12 = 45.0°, cos = 0.707. A 24" overhang on a 6/12 roof delivers only 21.4" of horizontal projection. The tool works in horizontal projection throughout and converts back to along-slope length for the framing takeoff. Input 4 — Wall height (from zone record): the overhang attaches at the roof-wall intersection at the top of the wall. Taller walls place the overhang higher above the window, reducing its effective shading depth for a given horizontal projection. The wall above the window head acts as a secondary shading element — it is already blocking sun for the depth of wall between the window head height and the wall top. Input 5 — Window head height (new required field in window property panel): the height from finished floor to the top of the window rough opening. The distance from window head to overhang soffit is wall height minus window head height. This distance of wall above the window contributes to shading and reduces the overhang projection needed. Input 6 — Window sill height and window height (new required fields in window property panel): sill height is the distance from finished floor to the bottom of the window rough opening. Window height is head height minus sill height. Both are needed: window height drives the shading requirement (the overhang must shade the full window height in summer), and sill height drives the winter unobstructed check (winter sun must clear the sill and enter the room).
The geometry:
Define: H_proj = horizontal overhang projection = overhang length along slope x cos(pitch angle). Define: H_wall = wall depth above window head = wall height minus window head height. Define: effective shading depth = H_proj + H_wall. Define: window height = window head height minus window sill height. Full summer shading condition (goal: 100% of window shaded at summer solstice noon): tan(summer solstice sun angle) x effective shading depth is greater than or equal to window height. For Kansas: tan(75.0°) x effective shading depth is greater than or equal to window height, which means effective shading depth is greater than or equal to window height divided by 3.73. Winter unobstructed condition (goal: winter sun reaches window sill): tan(winter solstice sun angle) x effective shading depth is less than or equal to distance from overhang soffit to window sill. Distance from overhang soffit to window sill = wall height minus window sill height. For Kansas: tan(28.1°) x effective shading depth is less than or equal to (wall height minus window sill height), which means effective shading depth is less than or equal to (wall height minus window sill height) divided by 0.534. The tool solves for the minimum H_proj that satisfies the summer shading condition given the known H_wall, then checks that the winter unobstructed condition is also satisfied. If both conditions can be satisfied simultaneously — which is the normal case for south-facing windows at this latitude — the tool outputs the minimum overhang projection needed and converts it to overhang length along slope for the framing spec. If the two conditions cannot be simultaneously satisfied (rare at 38.5°N, more common at high latitudes), the tool reports the conflict and shows the performance percentages for any overhang depth the owner chooses.
Decision support flow — what the tool presents to the owner:
Step 1 — Window-driven suggestion: when a window is placed on a wall, the tool reads all six inputs from the stored property and zone records plus the window property panel fields. It calculates the suggested overhang length along slope and displays it in the window property panel as: &#x201C;Suggested overhang: [X]" along slope ([Y]" horizontal projection) for full summer shading.&#x201D; The owner does not need to know the formula. Step 2 — Seasonal performance preview: two live numbers shown alongside the overhang depth field. Summer shading: percentage of window height shaded at summer solstice noon at this latitude and wall bearing (goal 100%). Winter admission: percentage of window height reached by winter solstice noon sun at this latitude and wall bearing (goal 100%). Both numbers update in real time as the overhang depth slider is adjusted. Step 3 — Override and see consequences: the owner can set any overhang depth they choose. The seasonal performance numbers reflect the actual chosen depth, not the suggested depth. If the owner chooses a shallower overhang for aesthetic reasons, the summer shading percentage drops below 100% and the tool shows the unshaded fraction explicitly: &#x201C;Bottom [Z]" of window unshaded at summer solstice noon — this glass contributes to cooling load.&#x201D; The tool does not block overrides. Step 4 — Off-axis explanation: for walls between 120°–150° or 210°–240° the tool shows: &#x201C;This wall faces [bearing] — [X]° off true south. Overhang suggestion reduced to [Y]" horizontal projection using 0.65x off-axis factor. Summer sun approaches from a lower angle on this face.&#x201D; Step 5 — East/west window flag: for walls more than 60° off south the tool shows: &#x201C;Fixed horizontal overhangs are not effective on this wall bearing. The low morning or afternoon sun angle cannot be blocked by a practical fixed overhang without eliminating all useful daylight. Recommend operable exterior shading, deep window reveals, or interior blinds for summer cooling control on this window.&#x201D;
Window property panel additions required:
The current window property panel captures width and height only. Two additional required fields are needed to enable the full overhang geometry: Head height — distance from finished floor to top of window rough opening (required field, no default). Sill height — distance from finished floor to bottom of window rough opening (required field, no default). These fields are also used by the HVAC solar gain calculator (Section 7.3) which needs window position on the wall to calculate the solar heat gain contribution at different times of day. Head height and sill height are standard dimensions on any window schedule and are available from the window manufacturer&#x2019;s rough opening specification. The tool should prompt for these fields when a window is first placed and prevent the overhang suggestion from calculating until both are entered. A window with only width and height entered shows a placeholder in the overhang suggestion field: &#x201C;Enter head height and sill height to calculate overhang suggestion.&#x201D;
11. Section View
The section view is a vertical slice through the building showing how everything connects from the foundation to the ridge in one continuous picture. It is the view that makes the structural load path readable, confirms interior dimensions, and shows every assembly layer in its correct relationship to every other layer.


11.1 What the Section View Shows
Element
	What is visible in section
	Foundation to slab
	Footing at frost depth (24" Miami County), stem wall or monolithic edge, XPS foam on exterior foundation face, XPS foam under slab, vapor barrier, granular fill, rebar, slab thickness. For radiant: PEX loops shown in slab cross-section.
	Wall assembly
	Each layer from exterior to interior: exterior finish, sheathing or SIP facing, insulation or SIP core, framing (if stud), interior finish. R-value callout for each assembly. Thermal boundary walls show insulation between conditioned and unconditioned zones.
	Wall height
	Floor to top plate dimension. Critical for SIP ordering — this dimension is fixed at manufacture.
	Roof structure
	Rafter or SIP roof panel from top plate to ridge. Roof assembly layers: roofing material, underlayment, decking, rigid foam (if used), rafter/panel, interior finish or exposed structure. For SIP roof: the underside IS the ceiling — no separate ceiling layer. Roofing material shown on each roof plane: Standing Seam / Exposed Fastener Panel / Shingles. If solar is selected in Section 7.5, the roof plane flagged for solar array shows array zone hatching and a note confirming standing seam compatibility.
	Ridge height
	Wall height plus rise. Dimension from floor to underside of ridge beam shown explicitly. This is the clearance at the peak for a cathedral ceiling.
	Eave height
	Wall height at the low side (same as wall panel height for SIP). The minimum interior clearance in the space.
	Ridge beam
	Beam profile shown in section at peak. Post shown dropping from beam to floor to footing below slab. The full load path is visible in one view.
	Overhang
	Extension of roof panel or rafter tail beyond wall face. South overhang projection dimension labeled with note that it feeds HVAC solar gain calculation. If overhang decision support has been run (Section 10.11), the section view additionally shows two construction annotation lines for the south-facing wall: (1) Summer shading line — drawn at 75.0° from horizontal at the eave tip toward the wall, confirming the overhang blocks direct sun at the window head at summer solstice noon; (2) Winter unobstructed check line — drawn at 28.1° from the window sill upward, confirming the line clears the overhang tip so winter sun reaches the sill unobstructed. Both angles are pre-calculated for latitude 38.5°N and applied automatically. For walls off true south, the corrected sun angle is used per the azimuth derate logic in Section 10.11. These lines are annotation only — they do not appear in the permit set export and are suppressed when the section view is locked.
	Ceiling type
	Flat ceiling with attic: ceiling line shown horizontal with attic space above, insulation in attic floor. Cathedral ceiling: no ceiling line — roof underside is ceiling. Attic note: available for HVAC equipment and blown insulation (flat only).
	Mechanical room
	Shown in cross-section with mechanical equipment footprint, floor drain, panel clearance zone, boiler and manifold wall mounting height.
	Thermal zones
	Color band or hatch pattern showing heated vs unheated zones. Thermal boundary surfaces highlighted where they transition between zones. R-value callout shown at each thermal boundary surface visible in the cut — including interior walls separating conditioned from unconditioned zones, and the floor/ceiling of a conditioned zone over an unconditioned space (crawl, unheated storage, or attic). IECC Zone 4A minimum R-values are checked against each boundary surface assembly: walls R-20 cavity or R-13 + R-5 continuous, ceilings R-49, floors over unconditioned space R-30. If any boundary surface assembly falls below the applicable minimum, an amber flag is shown on that surface in the section view with the label &#x201C;Below Zone 4A minimum — review assembly.&#x201D;
	Window and door openings
	When the section cut passes through or adjacent to a window or door opening, the header beam is shown in section with a size callout (LVL or doubled lumber per wall type and span). Rough opening width and height are labeled. Header bearing condition shown — confirming the header bears on full-height king studs (stud wall) or on the SIP panel face (SIP wall). For SIP walls: buck framing shown within the panel opening — the pressure-treated wood frame that provides the nailing surface for window and door installation. Buck dimensions are fixed at panel manufacture and cannot be changed after order; the section view confirms the buck is correctly sized for the specified rough opening before the order is placed. For ICF walls: buck shown as pressure-treated frame set before the pour, with the bond beam visible above the opening.
	Snow load accumulation zones
	When roof geometry creates accumulation zones per Section 10.9, the section view shows each zone in profile with its load multiplier. Valley accumulation zones: shown at the low point of intersecting roof planes with a 1.5× ground snow load callout and hatch indicating the increased load area. Step-down drift zones: shown as a triangular drift profile above the lower roof surface at the step-down face, with drift height h_d labeled and an engineer-required red flag. The step-down height (the vertical distance between the two roof levels) is the primary driver of drift depth and is readable in section — this geometry is only visible in section view, not in the roof plan. Long eave accumulation zones (eave projection >18&#x201D;): hatched zone at the eave with 1.2× callout. Ice dam risk zone: hatched band at the first 3 ft of heated eave from the eave edge, with a note referencing the required ice and water shield underlayment from Section 10.9.
	

11.2 Section View Cuts — Which Cuts Are Generated
The tool generates section cuts automatically at meaningful locations based on what has been placed on the floor plan. The user can also request a cut at any location.
* Default north-south cut: through the center of the building, showing full roof profile, ridge beam, and wall assemblies on both faces. This is the primary structural section.
* Default east-west cut: through the widest span, showing floor-to-ridge height at maximum span. This is the beam sizing section.
* Bathroom cut: automatic when bathroom zone exists — shows wet wall with drain stack, floor drain, vent stack through roof, shower or tub in section.
* Mechanical room cut: automatic when mechanical room exists — shows boiler, manifold, radiant tubing in slab cross-section, floor drain, panel clearance.
* Lean-to cut: automatic for any lean-to — shows attachment to host wall, ledger connection, rafter from ledger to outer beam, outer beam on posts, posts to footing. The ledger connection is the most critical structural detail in any lean-to and must be visible in section.
* Thermal boundary cut: automatic at any shared wall between a heated and unheated zone — shows insulation in the dividing wall, highlighting the thermal boundary.


11.3 The Section View and the Ridge Beam Cascade
The section view is where the ridge beam cascade from Section 9.3 becomes visible as a single continuous picture. Reading the section view from bottom to top:
* Footing: below frost depth, sized for column load from beam above
* Post: from footing through slab (sleeve in slab, base plate on slab surface, or thickened slab integral footing), rising to beam height
* Ridge beam: spanning between posts at peak, shown in section profile with size callout
* Roof panels or rafters: sloping down from ridge beam to top plate on each side
* Top plate: at wall height, connecting roof structure to wall
* Wall assembly: from top plate to slab, showing all layers
* Slab: showing foam, vapor barrier, radiant PEX if present, and thickness
* All dimensions are shown: footing depth, wall height, ridge height, roof pitch, overhang projection. These are the numbers that go on the panel order, the lumber order, and the structural engineer's drawings.


11.4 Section View and the Pre-Pour Plan
The section view is the companion to the pre-pour coordination document. The pre-pour document shows what goes in the slab in top-down view. The section view shows the same items in cross-section — confirming depths, slopes, and relationships between items are correct before the pour.
* Drain pipe shown in section with invert elevation and slope direction confirmed
* Conduit sleeves shown at correct depth and angle
* PEX loop depth in slab confirmed (typically mid-slab, 2 inches above foam)
* Post sleeves or thickened slab footings shown at correct depth relative to frost line
* Gas line entry sleeve shown at correct depth below frost line with seal detail
* Vapor barrier continuity shown — confirming no gaps at wall perimeter, at post sleeves, or at any transition


11.5 Section View Output Format and Trade Visibility
The section view is a read-only 2D drawing generated from model data. It is not a freehand canvas. All geometry is derived from decisions already recorded in the model — wall height, roof pitch, foundation depth, zone assignments, roof penetrations, and trade rough-in data. The user cannot draw on the section view directly; they change inputs elsewhere and the section view updates automatically.
Output format
* Default drawing scale: 1/4&#x201D; = 1&#x2019;. User can switch to 1/8&#x201D; = 1&#x2019; for tall buildings or to show full site context.
* Title block on each generated cut: cut direction (e.g. &#x201C;Section A-A: North–South through centerline&#x201D;), building name, date generated, scale.
* Printable and PDF-exportable. Each cut exports as a separate page. All active cuts can be exported as a single multi-page PDF.
* Updates automatically when any upstream decision changes (wall height, roof pitch, foundation depth, trade assignments). A &#x201C;Section view out of date&#x201D; amber flag appears if a relevant decision has changed since the last export.
* Locks for permit submission once the foundation inspection is signed off (consistent with Section 5 layer locking logic). Locked cuts display a &#x201C;Permit set&#x201D; watermark and cannot be regenerated until the next permitted phase begins.
Trade visibility in section
The section view renders trade elements that fall within the cut plane. A cut that does not pass through the wet wall will not show plumbing — this is correct behavior. Trade elements only appear in the sections where the geometry intersects them. The three trade ownership zones carry through into the section view:
* Below-slab segment: owned by the pre-pour coordination document (Section 8.6). Shown in the section view in its correct depth and slope position as a cross-reference — drain pipe invert elevation, conduit sleeve angle, PEX loop depth. These are confirmation elements, not re-entry. The pre-pour document remains the authoritative record.
* Above-slab vertical segment (in-wall or in-chase): shown rising from slab top to top plate. Wall height is a surface record property — the section view reads it directly. For plumbing, the drain stack and vent stack are shown as single-line elements within the wall thickness. For HVAC, a chase element must be placed on the floor plan layer for the section view to render it in section (chase placement is a floor plan layer feature — see floor plan gap log). If no chase is placed, HVAC duct routing in the wall cavity is not shown.
* Above-ceiling segment and roof penetration: vent stack continuation above the ceiling line (attic space for flat ceiling, or continuing to ridge for cathedral) shown as a dashed element. Terminates at the roof penetration marker from the roof plan layer. For a flat ceiling with attic, the attic segment length is derived from ceiling height to roof deck — a calculable dimension from model properties.
Plumbing vent transition logic
When a bathroom or wet zone is present and the section cut passes through it, the section view shows the full plumbing vent path as three labeled segments in one continuous picture. The transition points between segments are derived from model properties — no user input beyond what is already recorded:
* Segment 1 — Below slab: from fixture rough-in depth to slab top. Length = slab thickness + granular fill depth + any drop required for drain slope. Owned by pre-pour document. Shown solid in section.
* Segment 2 — In-wall vertical: from slab top to top plate. Length = wall height (surface record property). Shown solid in section. Dimension callout labeled &#x201C;stack in wall.&#x201D; This is the segment that drives wet wall framing width and confirms that the wall type can accommodate the stack diameter (3&#x201D; or 4&#x201D; ABS/PVC in a 2x6 wet wall).
* Segment 3 — Above ceiling to roof: for flat ceiling with attic, length = attic height at vent stack location (roof deck elevation at that point minus ceiling height). For cathedral ceiling, the vent stack cannot run through the roof panel interior — it must exit through the wall below the eave or be routed to a plumbing wet wall that terminates below the roof plane. Cathedral ceiling vent routing constraint is flagged as a Tier 2 amber item in the decision queue when a bathroom zone is assigned a cathedral ceiling.
Section view as takeoff confirmation layer
The section view does not generate takeoff quantities directly. Takeoff quantities for vertical trade runs are calculated by the model from surface record properties (wall height, ceiling type, slab thickness, roof geometry) and assembled in the materials takeoff system. The section view acts as a visual confirmation that the calculated lengths are geometrically consistent — a user reading the section can see whether a pipe run length that appears in the takeoff makes sense given the drawn geometry. Discrepancies between the section view geometry and the takeoff numbers indicate a surface record property that has not been updated. The section view displays a labeled dimension for each trade segment it renders, matching the dimension used in the takeoff calculation, so the two can be compared at a glance.


11.6 Section View Library
The section view library is the management layer for all generated cuts. It gives each cut a name, tracks which cuts are active, allows the user to add cuts at arbitrary locations, and tags each cut with the trades it is relevant to. Trade tagging is the mechanism that the trade report generator (Section 15) consumes when assembling trade-specific report packages.
Named cuts and user-added cuts
* Each auto-generated cut (Section 11.2) receives a default name tied to its trigger: &#x201C;Structural N–S,&#x201D; &#x201C;Structural E–W,&#x201D; &#x201C;Bathroom,&#x201D; &#x201C;Mechanical Room,&#x201D; &#x201C;Lean-to,&#x201D; &#x201C;Thermal Boundary at [zone name].&#x201D; User can rename any cut at any time.
* User can add a cut at any location by drawing a cut line on the floor plan. The section view generates automatically from that line. User-added cuts receive a default name of &#x201C;Section A,&#x201D; &#x201C;Section B,&#x201D; etc., incrementing alphabetically. User can rename.
* Cuts can be reordered in the library by drag to set export sequence.
* Cuts can be hidden (suppressed from export) without being deleted. Hidden cuts remain in the library and update with the model but do not appear in any exported package. Useful for working cuts that are not permit-ready or are for internal reference only.
Trade tagging
Each cut carries a set of trade tags. Tags are assigned two ways: automatically by the model based on what elements fall within the cut plane, and manually by the user to handle cases the auto-logic does not cover.
Automatic tag logic
* Ridge beam, posts, full load path in cut plane → tags: Framing, Foundation
* Wet wall, drain stack, vent stack in cut plane → tags: Plumbing
* Mechanical room, boiler, manifold, radiant supply/return headers in cut plane → tags: HVAC, Plumbing, Electrical
* Chase element in cut plane (once chase is a floor plan feature) → tag: HVAC
* Conduit sleeves, panel clearance zone in cut plane → tag: Electrical
* Thermal boundary wall, assembly R-value callout in cut plane → tag: Insulation
* Lean-to ledger connection in cut plane → tag: Framing
* Pre-pour geometry visible in slab cross-section → tags: Foundation, Plumbing
Manual tag override
User can add or remove tags on any cut. Auto-assigned tags are shown with a lock icon indicating their source; manually added tags are shown without the lock. Removing an auto-assigned tag does not suppress the element from the drawing — it only removes the cut from that trade&#x2019;s report package. The intended use is for user-added cuts that capture a specific detail for a specific trade that the auto-logic would not detect.
Library UI
* List view showing all cuts: name, cut direction, auto or user origin, trade tags, hidden/visible status.
* Each cut row shows a preview thumbnail of the generated drawing.
* &#x201C;Export selected&#x201D; button: user checks one or more cuts and exports as a multi-page PDF in library order.
* Trade filter: user selects a trade from a dropdown and the library shows only cuts tagged to that trade. This is the preview of exactly what that trade will receive in their generated report package (Section 15, Gap H).


11.7 Section View Claude Prompt — Dimensional Problem Detection (Phase 2)
The section view Claude prompt fires automatically when the generated section geometry reveals a dimensional condition that warrants review. The prompt does not fire on every section view generation — only when one or more trigger conditions are met. This is a Phase 2 feature requiring full model data to be available to the prompt assembly layer.
Trigger conditions
* Interior eave height < 7&#x2019;-6&#x201D;: IRC minimum habitable space ceiling height is 7&#x2019;-0&#x201D; for at least 50% of floor area; 7&#x2019;-6&#x201D; is used as the trigger threshold to provide a warning buffer before the hard minimum is reached. Fires as a Tier 2 amber item — does not block construction but blocks budget status advancement until resolved.
* Attic height at ridge < 30&#x201D;: IRC R807.1 requires a minimum 30&#x201D; clear height at the attic access opening. If the attic height at the ridge is below 30&#x201D;, attic access is non-compliant and HVAC equipment cannot be located in the attic. Fires as a Tier 1 red item if HVAC equipment is assigned to the attic zone; Tier 2 amber otherwise.
* Cathedral ceiling ridge height < wall height + 24&#x201D;: A cathedral ceiling with less than 24&#x201D; of rise above the wall plate produces a nearly flat interior roofline that is both visually flat and structurally marginal for ridge beam sizing. Fires as a Tier 2 amber item prompting the user to review pitch selection.
* Step-down drift zone engineer flag: when a step-down roof geometry triggers a drift zone per Section 10.9 and no structural engineer is recorded against the project, fires as a Tier 1 red item. The section view is the first place this geometry becomes calculable — the drift triangle height h_d depends on the step-down height which is only visible in section.
Prompt behavior
When one or more triggers fire, the tool assembles a Claude prompt containing: the triggered condition with the measured value and the threshold it failed, the full section geometry values (wall height, roof pitch, ridge height, eave height, ceiling type, attic height if applicable), the building zone assignment, and any other upstream decisions relevant to resolving the condition. The prompt asks Claude to identify the root cause, explain the practical consequence, and present the available resolution paths with their downstream effects. The prompt is presented to the user as a decision queue item at the appropriate tier level with a &#x201C;Get decision support&#x201D; button — it does not fire automatically to the API without user action.


12. Systems & Code Knowledge
12.1 Foundation — Code Notes (extended from v1)
See Section 8 for complete foundation planning. Additional notes:
* Full basement under living quarters: rare but valuable in Kansas tornado country. Adds cost, adds protected space, suitable for all mechanical systems.
* Hybrid foundation option: storage on shallow slab, living quarters on frost-depth foundation. Transition detail must be engineered — differential settlement risk.
* FPSF (frost-protected shallow slab): requires perimeter XPS insulation — verify Miami County inspector familiarity before specifying.


12.2 Other Systems — Retained from v1
Sections covering Heated Floors, Fixtures, Fireplace, Windows & Doors, Electrical, Septic, and Solar from v1 are fully retained. The construction knowledge in Section 6 and trade knowledge in Section 7 of this document supersede and extend the v1 content for owner-builder purposes. The code compliance minimums for all these systems are consolidated in Section 12.4.


12.3 Permit and Inspection Sequence — Miami County, Kansas
Miami County enforces IRC 2018 with Kansas state amendments. Owner-builders must pull their own permit and are subject to the same inspection sequence as licensed contractors. Separate permits are required for building, electrical (NEC 2020), plumbing (UPC), and mechanical (IMC 2018). The app tracks inspection status and flags when a downstream activity is being planned without a required preceding inspection recorded.
Building permit inspection sequence
* Footing/foundation inspection: required before any concrete is poured. Inspector verifies footing depth (24&#x201D; frost depth), footing dimensions, rebar placement, anchor bolt layout per stamped drawing. App gate: concrete quantity blocked from Estimated status until this inspection is recorded as scheduled (consistent with Section 8.6 pre-pour document requirement).
* Framing and rough-in inspection: required after all framing, roof structure, rough electrical, rough plumbing, and rough mechanical are in place — before insulation is installed. Inspector verifies structural connections, header sizes, holdowns, anchor bolts, and rough-in locations. App gate: insulation budget lines blocked from Committed status until framing inspection is recorded as passed.
* Insulation inspection: required after insulation is installed and before any interior finish (drywall, T&G) covers it. Inspector verifies R-values, vapor retarder placement, and thermal boundary continuity against IECC Zone 4A minimums. App gate: interior finish budget lines blocked from Committed status until insulation inspection is recorded as passed.
* Final inspection: required before occupancy. Covers all completed systems. Certificate of Occupancy issued on pass. App gate: project status cannot advance to Occupied until final inspection is recorded as passed.
Separate permit inspection sequences
* Electrical (NEC 2020): rough-in inspection (before insulation covers wiring), service entrance inspection (before utility connection), final electrical inspection (before energizing panel). Licensed electrician required in Kansas for service entrance and panel work — owner-builder may perform branch circuit rough-in under permit in Kansas but verify with Miami County.
* Plumbing (UPC): underground rough-in inspection (before slab pour — coordinates with foundation inspection), above-slab rough-in inspection (before insulation), pressure test inspection, final plumbing inspection.
* Mechanical (IMC 2018): rough-in inspection (ductwork or radiant rough-in before insulation), appliance inspection (boiler, water heater, combustion appliances before startup), final mechanical inspection.
* Septic: Kansas Department of Health and Environment (KDHE) permit separate from building permit. Soil percolation test required before permit. Installation inspection before backfill. Miami County Environmental Health coordinates. App flag: septic permit status tracked as a separate permit record; plumbing budget lines for drain connections flagged Tier 1 red if septic permit is not recorded as issued.


12.4 Code Minimums Reference — IECC Zone 4A / IRC 2018 / NEC 2020 / IMC 2018
This table is the single source of truth for minimum values used in app flags throughout the spec. All amber and red threshold values that reference code minimums are derived from this table. Values apply to Miami County, Kansas unless a local amendment is noted. Verify with Miami County building department for any local amendments not listed here.
Thermal envelope — IECC Zone 4A minimums
* Walls above grade: R-20 cavity, or R-13 cavity + R-5 continuous exterior (ci). SIP walls: R-value locked to panel thickness — 6.5&#x201D; SIP = R-23, 8.25&#x201D; SIP = R-30 (both compliant). ICF walls: 6&#x201D; core = R-22 to R-25 (compliant).
* Ceilings / attic: R-49 minimum. Flat ceiling with attic: blown insulation to R-49 depth. Cathedral ceiling (SIP roof): rigid foam above deck must be at least 50% of total assembly R-value — R-25 minimum above deck for a R-49 target assembly.
* Floors over unconditioned space: R-30 minimum.
* Slab on grade (heated): R-10 continuous under slab full coverage, R-10 vertical on foundation exterior to frost depth (24&#x201D;). XPS R-15 target (two 2&#x201D; layers) used on this project — exceeds minimum.
* Windows: U-factor ≤ 0.30, SHGC ≤ 0.25 for Zone 4A. Exception: south-facing windows used for passive solar gain may use SHGC up to 0.40 if a dynamic overhang shading analysis (Section 10.11) is completed and documented.
* Doors: U-factor ≤ 0.32 for swinging doors, ≤ 0.29 for sliding glass doors.
* Air sealing: blower door test target ≤ 3.0 ACH@50Pa for Zone 4A. Below 3.0 ACH@50Pa triggers mandatory mechanical ventilation (ERV or HRV) and combustion air review per Section 7.3.
Structural — IRC 2018 / Kansas wind zone
* Frost depth: 24&#x201D; minimum for Miami County. All footings at or below 24&#x201D;. Post sleeves through slab must bear on footings at frost depth.
* Anchor bolts: 1/2&#x201D; diameter minimum, 7&#x201D; embedment, maximum 6&#x2019; OC spacing, within 12&#x201D; of each plate end. Stamped engineering drawing required before pour (Section 8.2).
* Headers over openings: engineer required for any opening over 8&#x2019; in a load-bearing wall, or any opening in a wall resisting lateral wind load. Minimum doubled 2x10 for openings 4–8&#x2019; in standard stud walls.
* Habitable space ceiling height: 7&#x2019;-0&#x201D; minimum over at least 50% of floor area (IRC R305.1). App warning threshold set at 7&#x2019;-6&#x201D; per Section 11.7.
* Attic access: 22&#x201D; x 30&#x201D; minimum opening, 30&#x201D; minimum clear height at the access point (IRC R807.1). App warning threshold per Section 11.7.
Plumbing — UPC minimums
* Drain slope: 1/4&#x201D; per foot minimum for pipes 3&#x201D; and smaller; 1/8&#x201D; per foot for 4&#x201D; and larger. Pre-pour drain lines must achieve this slope between the fixture rough-in and the point of exit through the slab or foundation.
* Vent stack height above roof: 6&#x201D; minimum above roof surface at the penetration point. In snow country (Kansas qualifies), increase to 12&#x201D; minimum to clear expected snow accumulation. App: roof penetration marker for vent stacks carries a 12&#x201D; minimum callout.
* Cleanout locations: required at the base of each stack, at each change of direction greater than 45°, and at maximum 100&#x2019; intervals on horizontal runs. Pre-pour cleanout locations must appear on the pre-pour coordination document.
* PRV (pressure reducing valve): required when supply pressure exceeds 80 psi. Rural water district supply pressure at this property is approximately 65 psi — PRV set to 65 psi per Section 7.2. Unions required on both sides for serviceability.
Electrical — NEC 2020 minimums
* Panel working clearance: 36&#x201D; deep x 30&#x201D; wide x 6&#x2019;-6&#x201D; tall in front of panel (NEC 110.26). Mechanical room layout must provide this clearance. App: panel clearance zone shown in mechanical room section cut (Section 11.1).
* AFCI protection: required on all 120V 15A and 20A branch circuits in dwelling areas (bedrooms, living rooms, hallways, kitchens) per NEC 210.12. Not required in unfinished shop/storage areas.
* GFCI protection: required in bathrooms, kitchens (within 6&#x2019; of sink), garages, outdoor receptacles, crawl spaces, unfinished basements, and within 6&#x2019; of any water source (NEC 210.8).
* Generator interlock: required where a generator is connected to building wiring. Manual interlock at main panel per Section 7.1. Transfer switch or interlock must prevent simultaneous utility and generator connection.
Mechanical — IMC 2018 minimums
* Combustion air: minimum 1 sq in free area per 1,000 BTU/hr of combined appliance input. Formula and direct-vent preference per Section 7.3. Unresolved combustion air method is a Tier 1 life safety blocker.
* Bathroom exhaust: 50 CFM minimum continuous or 100 CFM intermittent per IRC M1507. Duct must exhaust to exterior — not to attic.
* Whole-house ventilation: required when blower door result is below 3.0 ACH@50Pa (IECC R403.6). ERV or HRV preferred for Zone 4A — recovers energy from exhaust air.


12.5 Propane Code Requirements — NFPA 58
This property uses propane as primary fuel with no natural gas. NFPA 58 (LP-Gas Code) governs installation. The propane supplier typically handles tank installation and initial inspection, but the owner is responsible for the service line from tank to building and all interior piping. Key NFPA 58 requirements for this configuration:
Tank setbacks (1000-gallon above-ground tank)
* 10&#x2019; minimum from any building or structure (measured from the tank to the nearest wall).
* 10&#x2019; minimum from any opening in a building (windows, doors, vents).
* 10&#x2019; minimum from any ignition source (electrical panels, generators, HVAC equipment).
* 25&#x2019; minimum from property line. Verify Miami County local amendment — some jurisdictions require greater setback from property lines for tanks over 500 gallons.
* App: propane tank placed on the site utilities layer (Section 7.7) with a 10&#x2019; setback ring displayed automatically. Conflict flag fires if any building element, opening, or ignition source is placed within the ring.
Regulator and service line requirements
* Two-stage regulation required for this configuration: first-stage regulator at the tank (reduces tank pressure ~250 psi to 10 psi), second-stage regulator at the building entry (reduces 10 psi to 11&#x201D; water column for appliances). Full specification in Section 7.4.
* Excess flow valve: required on the tank service valve for tanks over 200 gallons. Installed by supplier at tank — confirm on delivery.
* Underground service line: must be rated for LP gas service, buried at minimum 12&#x201D; depth (18&#x201D; preferred under driveways), sleeved where it passes under the slab, sealed at the sleeve with approved sealant. Shown on site utilities layer and pre-pour coordination document.
* Leak test: pressure test required before covering any piping and before first gas-up. Inspector or licensed plumber must witness. Document test pressure and duration in project file.


12.6 Wind and Tornado Code Requirements — Miami County, Kansas
Miami County is in ASCE 7 Wind Exposure Category C (open terrain, typical for rural Kansas) and falls within a high-wind design zone. Design wind speed for this location is 115 mph Vult (ultimate design wind speed, ASCE 7-16 / IRC 2018 Figure R301.2(4)). All structural connections, holdowns, and roof-to-wall connections must be designed for this wind speed. The following requirements apply throughout the spec and are the basis for high-wind flags in the app.
Structural hardware minimums
* Roof-to-wall connections: hurricane ties or H-clips required at every rafter-to-top-plate connection. Simpson H2.5A or equivalent minimum for 115 mph Vult zone. Not optional — inspected at framing inspection.
* Wall-to-foundation holdowns: required at all shear wall locations and corners. Engineer specifies holdown type and anchor bolt size for this wind speed and wall height. Holdown locations shown on structural layer (Section 5) and rebar map (Section 8.3).
* SIP panel wind rating: SIP panels must be specified with wind uplift rating adequate for 115 mph Vult. Confirm with manufacturer at order — standard residential SIP panels are rated for this zone but specify explicitly on the purchase order.
* Garage and large overhead doors: must be rated for 115 mph wind load. Wind-rated doors are a specific product category — standard residential doors are not rated. Specify wind rating on purchase order. Unrated large doors in a high-wind zone are a frequent failure point in Kansas storms.
* Metal roofing: standing seam with concealed clips handles thermal movement and wind uplift better than exposed fastener at this wind speed. Exposed fastener panels are acceptable but require closer fastener spacing in high-wind zones — confirm with manufacturer for 115 mph Vult specification.
Tornado shelter consideration
Miami County is in an active tornado corridor. IRC 2018 does not require tornado shelters in single-family residential construction, but Kansas strongly recommends them for new rural construction. The following options are presented to the user as a decision prompt when the project type includes a living zone:
* Full basement: highest protection level, doubles as mechanical space and storage. Significant cost adder. Requires separate foundation decision.
* In-ground safe room: pre-cast concrete or steel unit, installed in the garage slab or adjacent to the building. FEMA P-320 compliant units available from regional suppliers. Most practical option for slab-on-grade construction.
* Above-ground safe room: steel-lined interior room, FEMA P-361 compliant. Lower protection than in-ground but can be integrated into the floor plan as a bathroom, closet, or utility room. Requires engineered anchor connections to slab.
App: when a living zone is present and no shelter decision is recorded, a Tier 3 grey queue item appears: &#x201C;Tornado shelter — no decision recorded. Miami County is in an active tornado corridor. Consider shelter option before finalizing floor plan.&#x201D; This is advisory only and does not block any budget or construction activity.


13. Cost Estimation & Material Sourcing
The tool operates in three distinct cost phases. Each phase uses a different Claude prompt and produces a different level of confidence. A budget line item moves through all three phases as the project progresses — the estimate never disappears, it becomes the benchmark against which quotes and actuals are measured.


13.1 Three-Phase Cost Pipeline
Phase
	Name
	Trigger
	Confidence
	Claude prompt
	1
	Decision support estimate
	User is considering a construction type or system — decision not yet locked
	±25-35%. Directional. Helps choose between options, not buy materials.
	Prompt 1 — Decision Support
	2
	Regional sourcing
	Decision is locked and surface record dimensions are set — takeoff is calculable
	±10-15%. Supplier names, distances, current regional price ranges.
	Prompt 2 — Regional Sourcing
	3
	Quote comparison
	User has called suppliers and entered real quotes into the app
	±3-5% (quote validity window). Recommend which quote to accept and why.
	Prompt 3 — Quote Comparison
	

13.1a Response Format & Parsing
Each Claude prompt instructs Claude to return structured output wrapped in labeled tags so the app can parse it reliably from a paste. The prompt text must explicitly instruct Claude to use these tags and nothing else — no preamble, no markdown, no prose outside the tags.
Prompt 1 tags (Decision Support):
* [RECOMMENDATION] Which option is recommended and why [/RECOMMENDATION]
* [COST_RANGE] Low estimate — High estimate — Confidence % [/COST_RANGE]
* [INCLUDES] What the estimate covers [/INCLUDES]
* [EXCLUDES] What the estimate does not cover [/EXCLUDES]
* [WATCH] Top 2–3 watch items for this configuration [/WATCH]
Prompt 2 tags (Regional Sourcing):
* [SUPPLIER category="material category"] Name | City | Distance | Phone/URL | Price range per unit | Lead time [/SUPPLIER] — one tag per supplier
* [LEAD_TIME_FLAG] Any material with lead time over 2 weeks, with action required [/LEAD_TIME_FLAG]
* [CALL_SCRIPT supplier="supplier name"] Full plain-language call script for this supplier [/CALL_SCRIPT] — one tag per supplier
Prompt 3 tags (Quote Comparison):
* [ANOMALY] Description of any quote more than 20% below or 30% above others, with diagnosis [/ANOMALY]
* [ACCEPT] Supplier name to accept and reason [/ACCEPT]
* [VARIANCE] Accepted quote vs Phase 1 estimate — dollar difference and cause [/VARIANCE]
PARSING BEHAVIOR: The app scans pasted text for these tags and populates the corresponding fields automatically. If a required tag is missing, the app flags that field as unparsed and stores the full paste as a note for manual review. If no tags are found at all, the full paste is stored as an unstructured note and the user is prompted to re-run the prompt with the tag instruction included. Phase 2 replaces paste-and-parse with direct Anthropic API integration, at which point these tags become the structured output schema passed directly to the API call.


13.2 Budget Line Item Status
Every cost line item in the budget has a status that tracks where it is in the pipeline. The status drives what the tool shows and what the next action is.
Status
	Meaning
	Confidence
	Next action
	Concept
	Decision not yet made. No dimensions locked.
	Order of magnitude only. Item library (Section 6.3): Tier 1 generic placeholder always corresponds to Concept status. A Tier 1 item cannot advance past Concept until a Tier 2 category spec is entered.
	Generate Claude Prompt 1 to support the decision
	Estimated
	Decision locked. Claude-generated estimate stored. No real quote yet.
	±25—35%. Item library (Section 6.3): Tier 2 category spec corresponds to Estimated status. Claude Prompt 1 fires at Tier 2, returning a product recommendation and regional cost range. A Tier 3 specific product is required before a line item can advance to Quoted.
	Generate Claude Prompt 2 to identify regional suppliers
	Quoted
	Real price received from a supplier. Quote expiry date tracked.
	±5% (before expiry)
	Accept the quote or enter a competing quote
	Committed
	Purchase order placed or contract signed. Price is locked.
	Exact (change orders only)
	Store PO/contract number and document in project file
	Actual
	Invoice received and paid.
	Exact
	Store receipt, update budget, note estimate variance
	

VARIANCE TRACKING: The tool preserves the original estimate even after a quote is received. Estimate vs actual variance is recorded for every line item. Over time this builds a calibration signal — if SIP quotes consistently come in 15% above Claude estimates, that offset is applied to future SIP estimates on the same project. Full calibration mechanics are specified in Section 13.11.


13.2a Labor Cost Model
Every trade in the budget has two line items: a materials line and a labor line. They are always shown together as a pair. Neither can be deleted independently. The status model from Section 13.2 applies to each line item separately — materials and labor can be at different statuses for the same trade.
Owner-performed labor
* When the owner plans to self-perform a trade, the labor line is set to $0 and tagged &#x201C;Owner.&#x201D; The $0 is intentional and correct — it is not a placeholder. Owner-performed labor does not appear in the project budget dollar total.
* Owner-performed labor is tracked separately as a time estimate in hours. The hours estimate is shown alongside the trade line item as a scheduling input, not a cost. This helps the owner decide when a trade is worth hiring out as the project grows in scope — comparing estimated hours against contractor quotes gives a meaningful &#x201C;what is my time worth&#x201D; calculation.
* Claude Prompt 1 (decision support) includes owner-builder intent in its inputs. When a trade is marked Owner-performed, the prompt is instructed to provide a time estimate for the work in owner-builder hours alongside the materials cost estimate.
Contracted labor
* When a trade will be performed by a contractor, the labor line starts at Concept status with a Claude-generated estimate range. It moves to Quoted when a subcontractor quote is entered. The call script system (Section 13.5) generates a subcontractor solicitation script alongside the material call scripts.
* Subcontractor quotes for labor are entered the same way as material quotes: supplier name (contractor name), unit (lump sum or per-SF), total, quote expiry, notes. The quote comparison prompt (Section 13.6) works identically for labor quotes.
Labor cascade flags
The cascade engine fires two labor-specific flags that prevent trades from falling silently through the budget:
* Unassigned labor flag (Tier 2 amber): fires when a trade&#x2019;s labor line is $0 and is not tagged Owner-performed. Message: &#x201C;Labor not accounted for — mark as Owner-performed or add a quote placeholder.&#x201D; Fires when the trade&#x2019;s materials line advances past Concept status.
* Licensed trade owner flag (Tier 2 amber): fires when a licensed trade (electrical, plumbing, gas, HVAC) is marked Owner-performed. Licensed trades require a licensed contractor to inspect and sign off the final, even when the owner performs the rough work in Kansas. The flag does not prevent Owner tagging — it adds a note: &#x201C;[Trade] is a licensed trade. Owner may perform rough work under permit, but a licensed [trade] contractor must inspect and final. Add a final inspection labor line.&#x201D;


13.3 Claude Prompt 1 — Decision Support with Estimate
Generated when the user is evaluating options and needs help choosing. The prompt carries enough context for Claude to give a meaningful recommendation and a regionally-adjusted cost range.
Prompt 1 inputs (auto-assembled by the app)
* Zone dimensions: width, length, ceiling height
* Construction type being evaluated (e.g. SIP panels vs 2x6 stud)
* Adjacent decisions already locked: foundation type, heated/unheated, HVAC strategy, solar intent
* Climate zone: IECC Zone 4A, Miami County Kansas, frost depth 24 inches
* Site orientation: true north bearing from Section 3.1 (degrees, 0–359°). Included in every Prompt 1 so that solar gain calculations, overhang depth recommendations, azimuth derate factors, and window placement advice are all corrected for the actual property orientation rather than assuming true south = building south. Magnetic declination for Miami County (3° east) is pre-applied before the value is passed to the prompt.
* Owner-builder intent: what the owner plans to self-perform vs contractor
* Specific question: 'Help me choose between SIP and stud framing for this heated workshop zone'


Prompt 1 outputs (structured for app capture)
* Recommendation with reasoning — which option fits this configuration and why
* Cost range for each option being compared — expressed as total and per-SF, with a ±% confidence qualifier
* What the estimate includes and excludes — labor, delivery, crane, engineering
* Key decision factors that would change the recommendation — ceiling height, solar intent, contractor availability
* Top 2-3 watch items for the recommended option in this specific configuration


Prompt 1 example output format
'For a 40x60 heated workshop in Miami County Kansas with a cathedral ceiling and solar intent, SIP panels are the stronger choice. Estimated cost for SIP walls and roof including panels, ridge beam, erection labor, and window bucks: $42,000-$56,000 (±25%, 2025 Kansas regional). Comparable stud framing: $28,000-$36,000 but adds HVAC complexity and loses the cathedral ceiling advantage for solar. This estimate excludes foundation, electrical, and mechanical. Main factors that would shift this: if you plan to erect panels yourself (reduces labor cost ~$8,000) or if SIP erectors are unavailable locally (adds mobilization cost).'


13.4 Claude Prompt 2 — Regional Sourcing
Generated after the decision is locked and the takeoff is calculated from surface record dimensions. The prompt asks Claude to identify specific regional suppliers and current price ranges for each material category.
Prompt 2 inputs (auto-assembled by the app)
* Complete materials takeoff: quantities, specs, dimensions for every item in the assembly
* Location: Miami County Kansas, zip code 66085 area, south of Kansas City metro
* Sourcing radius preference: primary within 30 miles, secondary within 60 miles, ship-to-site for specialty items
* Material categories in this takeoff (e.g. SIP panels, LVL ridge beam, metal roofing, anchor bolts)
* Owner preferences: local vs big box, prefer quotes vs just buying, lead time sensitivity


Prompt 2 outputs (structured for app capture)
* Per material category: supplier name, city, estimated distance from Paola KS, specialty, phone/website if known
* Current regional price range per unit — with date qualifier (prices change, treat as a starting point for the call)
* What specifically to ask for when calling — product name, grade, spec, quantity, and any supplier-specific terminology
* Lead time flags — anything with a long lead time (SIP panels 4-8 weeks, LVL engineered lumber 1-2 weeks, ready-mix same week) should be ordered early
* Delivery vs pickup notes — what makes sense to have delivered vs pick up yourself
* Generated call script — short structured script the user reads when calling each supplier


13.5 The Call Script Generator
For each supplier identified in Prompt 2, the app generates a short call script. This removes the intimidation barrier for non-builder users calling trade suppliers for the first time.
Call script structure
* Opening: 'Hi, I'm building a [building type] in [city], Miami County. I'm looking to get a quote on [specific material].'
* Specification read-out: exact specs from the takeoff — dimensions, grade, quantity, any code requirements
* Questions to ask: current price per unit, minimum order, lead time, delivery to [zip code], any quantity discounts
* What to record: price per unit, total for quantity, lead time, quote validity period, name of person quoted
* Follow-up: 'Can I get that in writing or via email?' — always get quotes in writing
NON-BUILDER FLAG: The call script is written in plain language, not trade language. It tells the user exactly what words to say and what to write down. This is one of the highest-value features for the parents as primary users.


13.6 Claude Prompt 3 — Quote Comparison and Recommendation
Generated after the user enters real quotes from supplier calls. The prompt asks Claude to compare quotes, flag anomalies, and recommend which to accept.
Prompt 3 inputs (auto-assembled by the app)
* Original estimate: Claude's Phase 1 estimate for this line item
* Takeoff quantities: what was asked for in each call
* Each quote entered: supplier name, unit price, total, lead time, quote expiry, any notes
* Any substitutions noted: if a supplier quoted a different spec, that is flagged


Prompt 3 outputs (structured for app capture)
* Anomaly flags: any quote more than 20% below others (may indicate wrong spec, quantity error, or bait-and-switch risk), any quote more than 30% above others (may indicate specialty supplier or delivery cost buried in price)
* Spec verification: did all suppliers quote on the same thing? Flag any substitutions.
* Recommendation: which quote to accept, and why — lowest price is not always the answer (lead time, delivery, supplier reliability all matter)
* Estimate variance: how far the accepted quote is from the Phase 1 estimate, and what drove the variance
* Budget update: line item moves from Estimated to Quoted, total project budget updates


13.7 Regional Supplier Knowledge — Miami County Kansas
Miami County sits approximately 40 miles south of Kansas City. The sourcing radius realistically covers the following areas and supplier types. This list is seeded from Claude's training knowledge and updated as real quotes are entered into the app.
