In-ground units require excavation — coordinate with slab pour or plan separately. Kansas SEMA may offer cost-share grants for residential storm shelters — check before purchasing.
	

13.8 Quote Expiry and Requoting
* Material prices change — lumber, steel, and foam are particularly volatile. A quote from 3 months ago may not be honored.
* The app tracks quote date and flags any quote older than 30 days as potentially stale. 60 days = expired, requote required before committing.
* Lumber and steel: recheck price within 2 weeks of purchase. Prices can move 10-20% in a month during volatile periods.
* SIP panels: manufacturer quotes are typically valid 30 days. Request an extension in writing if needed.
* Ready-mix concrete: quote the day before the pour. Price is per yard delivered and changes with fuel costs.
* Committed items (PO placed, deposit paid): price is locked regardless of market movement. Document the PO number.


13.9 Budget Summary View
The budget summary shows the project total at each confidence level simultaneously — so the owner always knows the range they are working within. When multiple Scenarios exist, the budget has three views:SCENARIO BUDGET VIEW (default): shows the budget for the currently selected Scenario only. Identical layout to the single-Scenario budget — Committed / Quoted / Estimated / Concept totals, confidence band, contingency line. All budget line items in this view belong to Buildings in this Scenario.PROPERTY COSTS VIEW: shows costs that apply regardless of which Scenario is built. These are property-level infrastructure items: electric service trench from road, septic system installation, propane tank installation, site clearing and grading, perc test, permits. These costs are entered once at the Property level and are not part of any Scenario budget. They appear in a separate budget section labeled "Property Costs — All Scenarios." This prevents double-counting when comparing Scenario totals and shows the owner which costs are fixed regardless of the build decision.SCENARIO COMPARISON PANEL (owner-only): a side-by-side view of all non-Archived Scenarios. Columns per Scenario: Scenario name and status, building count, total footprint (sq ft), construction type summary (e.g. "SIP walls / radiant heat"), key decisions summary, Estimated cost range (low–high with confidence band), Quoted total (if any quotes entered), number of Tier 1 blockers remaining. The Scenario Comparison panel is owner-only — budget numbers are never included in any Builder Brief or Options Brief. The builder sees the technical comparison; the owner sees the cost comparison.ACTIVE BUILD PROMOTION: when a Scenario is promoted to Active Build (Section 3.1b), its Scenario Budget View becomes the live project budget. Archived Scenario budgets are retained in read-only mode for reference. Property Costs remain unchanged — they were never part of any Scenario.
* Committed: the hard floor — money already spent or legally obligated. Cannot go down.
* Quoted: quotes accepted but not yet committed. Firm within the quote validity window.
* Estimated: locked decisions with Claude-generated estimates. ±25-35% confidence band shown explicitly.
* Concept: decisions not yet made. Order-of-magnitude placeholder only. Shown in gray, not included in the confident total.
* Total range displayed: Committed + Quoted + Estimated (low end) to Committed + Quoted + Estimated (high end). The range narrows as more items move from Estimated to Quoted.
* Contingency line: 15% of estimated total for a first-time owner-builder project in Kansas. Shown as a separate line, not buried in estimates. This is not optional — material price volatility and owner-builder learning curve both consume contingency.


13.10 Soft Costs
Soft costs are non-material, non-labor expenditures that are real money in any construction project. They are predictable, schedulable, and in some cases blocking — the structural engineer fee blocks the pour, the perc test blocks the septic permit. The app creates soft cost line items automatically at Concept status when specific decisions are made. Soft costs appear as a separate group in the budget summary view, labeled &#x201C;Soft Costs,&#x201D; and are included in the total project cost range.
* Building permit fee: auto-created when a building zone is added to the project. Miami County fee schedule is based on project valuation — typical range $500–$2,000 for a residential/agricultural build at this scale. Concept estimate: $800. Trigger: building zone created. Gate: none, but permit must be recorded as issued before any inspection can be scheduled.
* Electrical permit fee: auto-created when electrical trade is added. Typical range $150–$400. Concept estimate: $250.
* Plumbing permit fee: auto-created when plumbing trade is added. Typical range $150–$350. Concept estimate: $200.
* Mechanical permit fee: auto-created when HVAC or mechanical trade is added. Typical range $100–$250. Concept estimate: $150.
* Structural engineering fee: auto-created when any element requiring a stamped drawing is added to the project (anchor bolt layout per Section 8.2, ridge beam sizing, holdown schedule, any span over 20 ft). Typical range $800–$2,500 for a build at this scale. Concept estimate: $1,500. Cascade gate: concrete pour blocked from Estimated status until this fee line is at Quoted status (engineer engaged) per Section 8.2.
* Septic design and perc test: auto-created when a septic zone is placed. Perc test: $300–$600 (soil scientist or KDHE-approved tester). Septic system design: $500–$1,200. Both are required before the KDHE septic permit can be issued. Cascade gate: septic installation blocked from Committed status until KDHE permit is recorded.
* Property survey: auto-created at project creation. Required to confirm property lines before placing any structure within setback distance. Typical range $600–$1,500 for a rural parcel in Miami County. Concept estimate: $900. If the survey has already been completed, the user marks this line Actual and enters the amount paid.


13.11 Estimate Calibration
The calibration system learns from accepted quotes and applies offsets to future estimates in the same material category. It corrects for systematic bias in Claude&#x2019;s regional estimates — not random variance. Random variance (one quote came in high) does not trigger calibration; systematic variance (four quotes in a row came in above estimate) does.
* Minimum data points: 3 accepted quotes in a material category before any offset is applied. Fewer than 3 is treated as insufficient signal.
* Categories tracked: calibration is maintained per material category matching the Section 13.7 supplier table (lumber, SIP panels, engineered lumber, concrete, rebar, metal roofing, XPS foam, PEX/radiant, propane, electrical, windows/doors, ICF forms, structural hardware, spray foam, septic, safe room). Labor categories are tracked separately from material categories.
* Offset calculation: simple average of (accepted quote / Claude estimate) across all data points in the category, expressed as a percentage. Example: three SIP panel quotes came in at +8%, +12%, +14% above estimate — offset = +11.3%. Future SIP estimates are shown with this offset applied.
* Display: the offset is shown as a note on the Estimated line item, not silently applied. Example note: &#x201C;Historical offset +11% applied to SIP panel estimate based on 3 prior quotes.&#x201D; The user can override or clear the offset at any time.
* Phase 1 scope: calibration data is stored per project in localStorage. It does not persist across projects in Phase 1. The data structure is designed for Phase 2 upgrade — calibration records are stored as a named array keyed by material category so they can be migrated to Supabase without restructuring.
* Phase 2 upgrade path: in Supabase, calibration data aggregates across all projects for the same user, and optionally across all users in the same geographic region. Aggregated regional calibration would improve estimate accuracy for any new project in the Miami County area without requiring that project to accumulate its own data points first.


13.12 Budget Export
The budget can be exported in three formats from the budget summary view. All exports respect the viewer permission model from Section 2 — the builder read-only view excludes private notes and shows only committed and quoted totals, not Concept or Estimated amounts. The owner view exports full data.
* CSV export: one row per line item. Columns: trade/category, description, type (materials/labor/soft cost), status, concept estimate, Claude estimate, quote amount, committed amount, actual amount, variance (actual vs estimate), supplier/contractor name, quote expiry, notes. Suitable for import into Excel or Google Sheets for lender documentation or personal tracking.
* PDF export: formatted budget summary matching the Section 13.9 view. Includes project name, date generated, confidence bands for each status tier, contingency line, and total range. Suitable for sharing with a lender, family member, or contractor. The builder read-only PDF omits Concept and Estimated lines entirely — shows only Quoted, Committed, and Actual.
* JSON export: full project budget data including calibration records, quote history, and all line item history. Used for project backup and for Phase 2 Supabase import. JSON structure is the canonical data format for the budget module — the CSV and PDF exports are derived from it.

15. Document Generation
15.1 Document 1 — Builder Introduction Brief (Generate Early)
Generate weeks 2–3 of planning. Hand to every builder at first meetings. Target length: 1–2 pages, suitable for handing across a table. The pre-pour document is NOT part of this — that is an internal coordination document, not a builder brief.
Content (in order):
* Property overview: location (Miami County KS), site orientation (true north bearing), existing conditions (existing barn noted)
* Building list: each building with zone name, enclosure type (heated/unheated/conditioned), footprint dimensions, ceiling height, and primary use
* Construction approach summary: wall system (SIP / stud / ICF / post-frame), foundation type, roof shape and pitch, HVAC strategy
* Key decisions locked: list of decisions that are firm, so the builder knows what is not open for discussion
* Timeline milestones: target start date and any known constraints (permit, survey, perc test status)
* Open questions for builder input: decisions still open that the builder&#x2019;s feedback will inform (e.g. &#x201C;What is your experience with SIP erection in high-wind zones?&#x201D;)
Excludes: All budget data, private notes, supplier quotes, call scripts, Claude prompt outputs, and the open questions list (Section 19). Builder filter rules from Section 2.1b apply.
Generates when: at least one building zone exists with enclosure type, footprint dimensions, and wall system selected. Incomplete state: generate button active but document header notes which modules are incomplete; incomplete sections render with an amber &#x201C;[Not yet specified]&#x201D; placeholder rather than being omitted silently.


15.2 Document 2 — Full Planning Document (After Builder Feedback)
Generate after first-round builder meetings. Incorporates feedback. Intended audience: architect, designer, or engineer who could use this to start drawings. More complete than the builder brief; still excludes budget and private notes.
Content (in order):
* Everything in Document 1 (Section 15.1)
* Full surface record detail: per wall, roof plane, and slab — construction type, R-value assembly, openings schedule, electrical routing method, thermal boundary flag
* HVAC load summary: per-zone simplified Manual J output, equipment sizing recommendation, duct or ductless decision
* Solar analysis summary: configuration selected, array location and tilt, estimated annual production, panel count (if decided)
* Code compliance summary: all IECC Zone 4A and Miami County flags with green/yellow/red status and any mark-as-noted items
* Rough-in location summary: per trade, all wall and ceiling rough-in locations with zone references (not pre-pour slab items — those are Document 4)
* Builder meeting notes: feedback and quotes from builder meetings, organized by builder name and date
Excludes: All budget data, private notes, supplier quotes, call scripts, Claude prompt outputs. Builder filter rules from Section 2.1b apply.
Generates when: at least one builder meeting is recorded AND HVAC, Solar, and Greywater modules are each at a status of at least Concept-complete (decision made, even if not fully specified). Incomplete state: generate button is blocked with a message listing which modules are incomplete. Unlike Document 1, partial generation is not permitted — this document is intended for professional use and partial content would be misleading.


15.3 Document 3 — Specification Sheet (Pre-Construction)
Generate when decisions are firm and construction is imminent. Intended audience: the owner placing orders and the contractor executing the work. Every material, fixture, and system specified by brand, model, and grade.
Content (in order):
* Materials specification by trade: organized as Foundation, Framing, Roofing, Insulation, Electrical, Plumbing, HVAC, Gas, Solar. Each item: description, brand/model/grade (if decided), unit, quantity from takeoff, and supplier (Quoted or Committed status only).
* Fixture schedule: all plumbing fixtures, electrical fixtures, and mechanical equipment with model numbers
* Budget status summary (Quoted and Committed items only — no Concept or Estimated amounts): shows what is ordered, what is confirmed, and what still needs a PO
* Code compliance sign-off list: all items that require inspection or stamped drawing, with current status
Excludes: Concept and Estimated budget amounts, private notes, supplier call scripts, Claude prompt outputs, open questions.
Generates when: all surface records have a selected construction type AND at least 75% of budget line items are at Quoted or Committed status. Incomplete state: generate button active but a warning header lists the items still at Concept or Estimated status so the owner knows what is not yet locked.


15.4 Document 4 — Pre-Pour Coordination Package (NEW — Before Concrete)
The most critical document. Generated from the layout plan. One document per building, covering every trade&#x2019;s slab requirements on a single coordinate-accurate drawing. Sent to each trade for review before pour. Used as the inspection checklist. Includes: drain locations with invert elevations and slope arrows, conduit sizes and exit points, radiant loop layout with manifold location, anchor bolt pattern, gas sleeves, foam and vapor barrier zones, inter-building trench runs, and the pour sequence.
Additional required elements: (1) a per-trade signature block at the bottom of each trade&#x2019;s section — &#x201C;[Trade] has reviewed this document and confirmed all slab requirements. Signature: ___ Date: ___&#x201D; — this creates a paper record of trade review before the pour; (2) a Pour Hold section at the end listing every item that must be physically verified on-site before concrete is called — generated from the pre-pour checklist with checkboxes.
Generates when: the layout plan canvas has at least one building with all pre-pour elements placed (radiant loops, drain locations, conduit exits, anchor bolt pattern). Incomplete state: blocked — any missing pre-pour element triggers a red flag listing what is unplaced. This document cannot be generated in partial state.
Pre-pour items by trade are detailed in Section 5.2. This document is generated at Build Sequence Step 7 (Section 17).

Phase 2 addition: each package includes the relevant section cuts from the Section 11 section view library, auto-filtered by trade tag — so the Foundation package includes the pre-pour section cuts, the Framing package includes structural cuts, and so on.

16. Incomplete Modules — Return To These
The following modules are intentionally incomplete. They appear in the builder brief at minimum level. Full specification must be completed in dedicated planning conversations before Document 2 can be generated.BASEMENT ZONE TYPE KNOWLEDGE PACKAGE: Status: spec defined (Section 3.3 Basement zone type). Completion order: 1 — required before any Basement zone pre-pour document can be generated. Blocks: basement foundation engineering package, waterproofing trade package, structural floor deck spec, egress window well spec, Foundation & Concrete trade package for basement buildings. Content needed: poured concrete vs. CMU vs. ICF basement wall comparison for Miami County clay soil (ICF recommended — reasons: continuous insulation, no thermal bridging, integral waterproofing assistance, mass performance), exterior membrane waterproofing system spec (product selection, drainage board, dimple mat, connection to footing drain), window well options and sizing, egress window selection criteria (meet IRC R310, maximize light), structural floor deck options (wood I-joist vs. LVL vs. concrete deck), tornado shelter reinforcement options (poured concrete lid, inward-opening steel door, FEMA 320 checklist), interior drainage channel detail as secondary waterproofing backup, sump pit sizing for basement (larger than standard barn sump — basement collects more water from larger wall area).ERV/HRV MODULE: Status: spec defined (Section 7.3 Living Quarters zone type; ERV/HRV Duct Record defined in Section 7.3a). Completion order: 1 — required before any Living Quarters zone can be fully specified. Blocks: HVAC trade package for Living Quarters zones (ERV/HRV Duct Records must be complete before trade package can be finalized), Document 2 HVAC section, blower door test target, short-circuit conflict check (Section 7.3a) cannot fire until supply/exhaust port locations are entered. Content needed: ERV vs HRV selection logic for Zone 4A (ERV recovers both heat and moisture — preferred for Kansas humid summers; HRV recovers heat only — better for very cold dry climates), unit sizing calculation from occupant count and floor area (ASHRAE 62.2: 7.5 CFM/person + 0.01 CFM/sq ft floor area = minimum ventilation rate), ductwork layout requirements (dedicated ducts vs shared with HVAC distribution — dedicated preferred to prevent short-circuit), supply/exhaust port placement relative to HVAC supply and return (10 ft minimum separation per Section 7.3a), filter maintenance interval (ERV: every 3 months in Kansas dust season), regional suppliers for Miami County Kansas, maintenance schedule.KITCHEN COORDINATION MODULE: Status: spec defined (Section 3.3 Kitchen zone type, Section 7.1, 7.2, 7.3; Fixture Records for kitchen fixtures defined in Section 7.2a). Completion order: 2 — required before any Kitchen zone pre-pour document can be generated. Blocks: kitchen Fixture Records cannot reach Confirmed status without cabinet layout coordination (rough-in dimensions depend on cabinet placement), Pipe Segment Records for kitchen drains (island sink AAV routing requires island location to be locked), range hood Duct Record (CFM and duct size require hood selection), small appliance circuit layout in electrical circuit records (outlet heights depend on cabinet and countertop heights), plumbing trade package kitchen section. Content needed: work triangle coordination with cabinet layout (sink, range, refrigerator triangle — maximum 26 ft perimeter per NKBA), appliance clearance requirements (range: 30” min to combustibles above; refrigerator: 1” sides, 1” top), island sink Fixture Record venting detail (AAV at island — location must be confirmed before slab rough-in), range hood CFM selection by range type (gas range: 100 CFM minimum; electric range: 50 CFM minimum; professional range: 1 CFM per 100 BTU), duct sizing by CFM (6” round for up to 400 CFM; 8” round for 400–700 CFM), cabinet layout coordination with electrical rough-in heights (standard outlet at 18” above countertop; under-cabinet strip at 6” above countertop; refrigerator dedicated circuit stub at 72” height).VEHICLE SERVICE PIT KNOWLEDGE PACKAGE: Status: spec defined (Section 3.3, 5.1, 5.2, 7.1, 7.2, 7.3, 8.1, 9.1). Completion order: 3 — required before pit zone pre-pour document can be generated. Blocks: pit foundation engineering package, oil/water separator spec, confined space ventilation spec, Foundation & Concrete trade package for pit buildings. Content needed: pit dimension recommendations by vehicle type (car vs truck vs tractor), edge beam rebar detail (engineer-supplied but tool should flag what to ask for), oil/water separator sizing by pit floor area, grating/cover options and load ratings, CO detector placement in and around pit, OSHA confined space entry procedure note.COLD STORAGE KNOWLEDGE PACKAGE: Status: spec defined (Section 3.3, 5.1, 5.2, 7.2, 8.1, 9.1). Completion order: 4 — required before cold storage zone pre-pour document can be generated. Blocks: cold storage foundation engineering package, Foundation & Concrete trade package for cold storage buildings. Content needed: Kansas ground temperature profile by depth, optimal depth for target temperature range, shelf/rack layout recommendations, hatch type options (floor hatch vs exterior bulkhead, rated options), ceiling insulation detail (rigid foam on underside of barn slab above), ventilation for humidity control (passive vs active), produce storage guidelines by food type.SCENARIO COMPARISON MODULE: Status: spec complete (Section 3.1b, 9.2, 13.9, 15.1). Completion order: implement alongside Week 3 budget tracker and Week 4 document generator. Blocks: Options Brief (Document 1 multi-scenario mode), Scenario Comparison panel in budget, Scenario promotion to Active Build. The comparison module requires: Scenario switcher UI, per-Scenario budget rollup, Scenario Comparison panel (side-by-side configuration + cost summary, owner-only), Options Brief generation (two modes — separate PDFs or combined brief), and the Active Build promotion workflow with confirmation dialog.

Water Service Details Module — Miami County Kansas
What this module resolves
The existing barn has metered rural water district service. Every other building on the property needs water too — the new barn, the future house, livestock points, irrigation. How that water gets there is a one-time infrastructure decision made during Build Sequence Step 5, when the trench is open and equipment is on site. Getting this wrong, or leaving it unresolved until site work begins, means either an undersized supply line that has to be dug up and replaced, or a missed opportunity to stub toward the future house for a fraction of what it costs to trench later.
This module must be completed before Build Sequence Step 5. It directly determines pipe sizing, trench depth, tap location, and what gets stubbed toward future buildings — all of which are physically installed during the same site work pass as the electrical conduit, septic line, and propane stub.
Step 1 — Identify the rural water district
The district serving the existing barn is currently unknown. This is a one-call resolution.
Call Miami County Public Works or look at the existing barn’s water bill — the district name and account number will be on it. Rural water districts in Miami County include Miami County Rural Water District No. 1 and others — confirm which one serves this parcel.
Once identified, call the district and ask:
* What is the current static pressure at the meter serving the property? (Target: 40–80 psi at the meter. Above 80 psi requires a PRV at each building entry per Section 7.2.)
* What is the meter size currently installed at the barn? (Standard residential: 5/8" or 3/4".)
* What are the requirements and fees for adding a second tap to the same property, or for upsizing the existing meter?
* Is there a district main line running along the road frontage of this property, or does service come from a lateral? (Affects independent tap feasibility and cost.)
* What is the district’s policy on serving multiple buildings from one meter vs. requiring separate meters per building?
Record the district name, the name of the person you spoke with, the date, and the answers to each question. Enter all of this into the Water Service record in the app.
Step 2 — Choose a supply strategy
There are three viable strategies for this property. The right choice depends on the district’s policy, the distance between buildings, and the long-term plan for the property.
Strategy A — Branch from existing barn meter
One meter at the barn serves all buildings. A new supply line branches from the existing service line inside or just outside the barn and runs underground to each additional building. All water use rolls up to one meter and one bill.
Suitable when: buildings are close together (under 400 ft between the barn and the farthest point), the district allows multiple buildings on one meter, and the existing meter is large enough for the combined load (see Step 3).
Advantages: one account, one bill, lowest upfront infrastructure cost, no new tap fee, no new district connection fee.
Disadvantages: if the barn is ever sold separately from the rest of the property, the other buildings lose their water service. Any meter or service line problem affects all buildings simultaneously. The existing meter may need upsizing.
App field: Supply Strategy = Branch from existing meter. Unlocks: meter sizing check (Step 3), branch pipe sizing (Step 4), trench routing to each building on the site utilities layer.
Strategy B — Independent tap per building
Each building gets its own direct connection to the district main, its own meter, and its own account. Buildings are hydraulically independent of each other.
Suitable when: buildings are far apart, the future house is intended to be on a separate account from the farm, or the district requires separate meters per building.
Advantages: clean separation between farm and residential water use, each building can be sold independently, no single point of failure across all buildings.
Disadvantages: higher upfront cost (new tap fee per building, new meter per building, potentially new lateral from the district main per building). District may not have main line capacity or physical access at the desired tap location.
App field: Supply Strategy = Independent tap per building. Unlocks: per-building tap location on site plan, per-building PRV requirement, separate pipe sizing per lateral.
Strategy C — Branch from existing meter now, independent tap for future house
A hybrid approach. The new barn branches from the existing barn meter for now. The future house gets its own independent tap when it is built. Running a branch line toward the future house site now (capped stub, same trench pass as everything else) keeps the option open without committing to it.
App field: Supply Strategy = Branch now / Independent tap for house. Unlocks: branch pipe sizing for new barn, capped stub routing toward future house site on site utilities layer, future house tap flagged as a deferred line item at Concept status.
Step 3 — Meter sizing check (Strategy A and C only)
If branching from the existing meter, confirm the existing meter is large enough for the combined load of all buildings it will serve. Meter sizing is based on total fixture units across all buildings on the meter, using the fixture unit totals from the plumbing layer (Section 7.2a Fixture Records).
Reference values:
* 5/8" meter: adequate for up to approximately 30 fixture units total. Typical for a single residence or small barn with one bathroom.
* 3/4" meter: adequate for up to approximately 50 fixture units. Standard for a residence plus a small outbuilding.
* 1" meter: adequate for up to approximately 100 fixture units. Needed when combining a residence, a working barn with multiple hose bibs, and livestock watering points.
If the combined fixture unit count exceeds the existing meter’s capacity, the district must upsize the meter. This is a district-side change — they do it, they charge for it. Get the upsizing fee from the district in Step 1.
Step 4 — Pipe sizing
Supply pipe from the meter to each building is sized based on the fixture unit count at each building and the total run length from the meter. The tool calculates this automatically from the Fixture Records and the supply line routing drawn on the Site Utilities layer.
Rules encoded in the tool:
* 3/4" HDPE or PEX: adequate for most single-building runs up to 300 ft serving up to 20 fixture units.
* 1" HDPE or PEX: required when run length exceeds 300 ft, or fixture unit count at the served building exceeds 20, or multiple branch tees are on the same main run.
* 1-1/4" or larger: only if the district meter is 1" and the combined load is large — rare for this property at current scope.
Material selection:
* HDPE (high-density polyethylene): the standard for underground rural water service lines in Kansas. Flexible, freeze-resistant, available in long coils that minimize joints underground. Recommended.
* PEX (cross-linked polyethylene): acceptable underground with appropriate UV protection at exposed ends. Less common for long underground runs than HDPE but fully code-compliant.
* PVC Schedule 40: acceptable underground but rigid — requires fittings at every direction change, more joints underground than HDPE. Not recommended for runs longer than 100 ft.
* Copper: not recommended for underground burial in Kansas clay — corrosion risk over time.
Depth: minimum 18" below finished grade for frost protection in Miami County. Under driveways and vehicle traffic areas: minimum 24" with Schedule 40 PVC sleeve over the service line for physical protection.
Step 5 — PRV at each building entry
Regardless of supply strategy, a pressure reducing valve (PRV) is required at the service entry point of every building if supply pressure exceeds 80 psi (per Section 7.2 — PRV is already a mandatory item for this property given rural district pressures typically run 65–100 psi).
PRV location: inside the building at the point where the supply line enters, before the first branch tee. Set to 65 psi. Unions on both sides for serviceability.
IMPORTANT — Sleeve vs. PRV timing: The supply entry SLEEVE through the slab or foundation wall is a PRE-POUR item — it must appear on the pre-pour coordination document and be set before concrete is poured. The PRV itself is an ABOVE-SLAB rough-in item — it installs after the slab cures, when above-slab plumbing begins. These are two separate steps. The sleeve is concrete-critical; the PRV is not. Do not confuse them on the pre-pour checklist.


Step 6 — Stub toward future house site
Regardless of which strategy is chosen for the new barn, run a capped supply line stub toward the future house building envelope during the initial site work trench pass. The trench is open, the equipment is on site, and the marginal cost of extending a capped HDPE stub an extra 50–100 ft is negligible compared to reopening the trench later.
The stub terminates at or just inside the future house building envelope (per the Placeholder Building location on the site plan — Section 3.1a). Cap the end with a threaded cap and mark the location with a survey stake and a note in the project record. The stub is shown on the Site Utilities layer as a dashed line with “Future — capped” label.
What goes in the pre-pour document from this module
The following water service items must appear on the pre-pour coordination document before any slab is poured for a water-served building:
* Supply line entry sleeve through the foundation wall or slab edge — diameter, depth, and orientation toward the meter/source. Sleeve must be set before the footing pour on that wall or before the slab pour if coming up through the floor.
* Hose bib stub-ups at each planned exterior hose bib location — PEX sleeve through slab if the bib is floor-fed, or wall penetration sleeve if wall-fed.
* Any in-slab supply lines crossing open slab area (uncommon but required if the floor plan places fixtures far from the entry point and the supply must cross the slab to reach them).


App fields added by this module
Water Service record (property-level):
* Rural water district name and contact
* Static pressure at meter (psi) — measured or reported by district
* Existing meter size (5/8" / 3/4" / 1" / other)
* Meter upsizing required (yes / no / unknown)
* Supply strategy (Branch from existing / Independent tap per building / Branch now + independent tap for house)
* Future house stub planned (yes / no)
* Supply pipe material (HDPE / PEX / PVC)
Per-building water service fields (on each Building record):
* Supply pipe size (3/4" / 1" / 1-1/4")
* Run length from meter (derived from site utilities layer geometry — no double entry)
* PRV required (auto-set yes when district pressure confirmed above 80 psi)
* PRV set pressure (default 65 psi, editable)
* Supply entry sleeve location (coordinates on foundation layer — pre-pour blocker until placed)


Takeoff lines generated
* Underground supply pipe: HDPE or PEX by diameter (LF — geometry derived from site utilities layer, no double entry). Separate line per run segment (barn to new barn, barn to future house stub).
* Supply entry sleeve through foundation (count: 1 per building, with diameter)
* PRV assembly (count: 1 per building — includes PRV, two unions, pressure gauge)
* Hose bib assemblies (count: per placed hose bib symbol)
* Pipe insulation at above-grade exposed sections (LF — at building entry before going below grade or inside wall)
* Trenching (LF — shared with electrical conduit and propane trench from Section 7.7 — shown as combined trench line item, not duplicated per trade)
All lines added at Concept status when module is initiated. Move to Estimated after Claude Prompt 2 runs for site utilities.
Module complete when:
District identified and contact recorded. Static pressure confirmed. Meter size confirmed and upsizing decision made. Supply strategy selected (A, B, or C). Pipe size and material selected for each run. Future house stub decision recorded. Supply entry sleeve location placed on foundation layer for each water-served building (pre-pour blocker cleared). PRV spec confirmed per building. All takeoff lines generated and at Estimated status or higher.
Basement Zone Type Knowledge Package
Basement Wall Type Comparison
Three wall systems are viable for a basement on this property. The choice is made before any foundation work begins and is locked at the Basement wall type selected cascade row (Section 9.1). It cannot be changed after the walls are poured.
ICF (Insulated Concrete Forms) — recommended:
Continuous rigid foam insulation on both faces of the concrete core eliminates thermal bridging entirely. The foam also acts as a capillary break, reducing moisture migration through the wall. The concrete core provides significant thermal mass that dampens temperature swings. Owner-buildable stacking phase with contractor pour. On this property’s clay soil, ICF is the strongest long-term choice because the foam layer interrupts the slow but continuous moisture wicking that occurs through bare concrete walls in expansive clay.
Poured concrete with interior insulation:
Structurally sound, widely familiar to local contractors. Requires interior insulation applied after cure (rigid foam board or spray foam). No moisture capillary break built in — exterior membrane is the only moisture barrier. More susceptible to long-term moisture migration in clay soil than ICF. Lower upfront cost, higher lifetime cost if moisture remediation is ever needed.
CMU (Concrete Masonry Unit) block:
Acceptable but not preferred for a habitable basement on expansive clay soil. Mortar joints are the weakest link against hydrostatic pressure and moisture migration. Requires parge coat plus waterproofing membrane on all exterior faces plus interior insulation. Acceptable for non-habitable basement spaces (mechanical room, storm shelter only) where moisture tolerance is higher.
App field: Basement wall type (ICF / Poured concrete / CMU). Auto-recommended as ICF when property.soil_type = expansive_clay. Selection locks insulation method, vapor retarder class, interior finish path, and exterior waterproofing strategy per Section 9.1 cascade.
Exterior Membrane Waterproofing System
Damp-proofing alone is blocked for any habitable basement (Section 5.7.1). The required system is full-coverage membrane waterproofing with drainage board.
Membrane options (any acceptable — select based on contractor availability):
* Tremco Tuff-N-Dri: spray-applied asphalt emulsion membrane, standard for residential basements in Kansas, applied by specialty contractor with spray equipment
* Henry Blueskin: self-adhered sheet membrane, owner-applicable, stronger than spray-applied for puncture resistance
* Carlisle CCW-705 or equivalent rubberized asphalt sheet membrane: highest performance, most durable, most expensive
All membranes must cover the full below-grade exterior face from the footing to at least 6" above finish grade. Laps sealed per manufacturer instructions.
Drainage board: Delta-Drain, Platon, or equivalent dimple mat applied over the membrane before backfill. Dimples facing the wall, smooth face outward. Bottom edge must lap over the footing drain tile so water that runs down the drainage board enters the footing drain rather than pooling at the footing.
App field: Waterproofing system (Spray-applied membrane / Sheet membrane / Not selected). Damp-proof only is blocked and cannot be selected.
Window Well Options and Sizing
An egress window well is required wherever the egress window opening is below grade. IRC R310 requires at least one egress window per habitable basement room.
Steel window wells: galvanized corrugated steel semi-circle, standard sizes from 37"–60" wide and 16"–36" projection. Lowest cost, prone to rust at the bottom without drainage. Install 6" of washed gravel at the bottom of every steel well.
Precast concrete window wells: heavier, more durable, no rust concern. Requires equipment to place. Better long-term appearance and appropriate when the well will double as a tornado shelter egress point.
Minimum sizing for code compliance (IRC R310): minimum 9 sq ft clear floor area, minimum 36" wide × 36" projection. If well depth exceeds 44", a ladder or steps are required inside the well.
Drainage is mandatory: every window well must drain. Either connect to the perimeter drain tile system or provide a minimum 6" gravel drainage layer with positive slope away from the foundation. A well with no drainage fills with water in heavy rain and delivers it directly against the window and wall — the most common source of basement window leaks.


App field: Window well type (Steel / Precast concrete) and dimensions (width × projection). Window well location coordinates on the foundation layer are a Tier 1 pre-pour blocker — must be placed before foundation is formed.
Egress Window Selection Criteria
IRC R310 minimum clear opening: 5.7 sq ft net clear area, 24" minimum clear height, 20" minimum clear width, maximum 44" sill height above basement finished floor. Net clear is the unobstructed opening with the window fully open — always confirm from the manufacturer’s certified data sheet, not from the rough opening.
Window type comparison:
* Casement (recommended): swing outward on a side hinge. Best net clear opening for a given rough opening size — the entire sash swings clear. Easiest to meet the 5.7 sq ft requirement in a compact rough opening.
* Double-hung: only half the sash opens. Must be approximately twice as large as a casement for the same net clear area. Harder to meet code in a standard rough opening.
* Awning: hinge at top, swing outward at bottom. Acceptable but confirm screen is removable from inside for egress.
* Slider: only half the opening is usable for egress. Not recommended unless rough opening is oversized.
App field: Egress window type and confirmed net clear area (sq ft — entered from manufacturer data sheet). A window in a Basement Living Quarters zone without confirmed net clear ≥ 5.7 sq ft generates a Tier 1 red blocker.
Structural Floor Deck Options
Any zone above a basement uses a structural floor deck, not a slab. Three options:
Wood I-joist (TJI) — recommended:
Factory-manufactured engineered wood joist with OSB web and LVL flanges. Depths 9.5"–16", spans to 20+ ft. Lightest weight per linear foot. Open web allows mechanical, electrical, and plumbing to run through the depth without notching. Owner-installable with standard framing tools. Most common choice for owner-builder residential basements. Engineer sizing required.
LVL beams with dimensional lumber joists:
LVL beams carry the primary span, dimensional lumber joists (2×10 or 2×12) span between LVL beams. More familiar to general contractors. Heavier than TJI. Solid web — mechanical must go below or around joists, not through.
Poured concrete composite deck:
Reinforced concrete slab cast on formed steel deck. Highest structural performance, best fire rating, most sound isolation. Also highest cost, requires steel beam fabrication, not owner-buildable. Only appropriate for very high live loads or fire-resistive construction requirements.
App field: Structural floor deck type (Wood I-joist / LVL + dimensional / Concrete composite). Selection drives framing takeoff and bearing point locations at basement wall top — a pre-pour blocker for the basement wall pour. Engineer required for all three options.
Tornado Shelter Reinforcement Options
A full poured concrete basement with ICF walls approaches FEMA 320 performance without additional work. Three upgrade levels:
* Level 1 — No specific shelter upgrades: ICF basement provides substantial debris penetration resistance. Not FEMA 320 rated but significantly better than above-grade construction. No additional cost.
* Level 2 — FEMA 320 compliant in-residence shelter: reinforced concrete floor deck above (not wood I-joist), inward-opening steel shelter door, fully enclosed basement with no unrated openings. Cost adders: concrete deck instead of TJI, steel shelter door ($1,500–$3,500). Must be decided before walls are poured — door rough opening, deck bearing details, and wall reinforcement schedule all change.
* Level 3 — FEMA P-320 dedicated safe room within the basement: separately formed reinforced concrete room with its own rated door. Maximum protection, maximum cost, requires dedicated space.
DESIGN-TIME DECISION: tornado shelter specification must be confirmed before basement walls are poured. If FEMA 320 or higher is selected, structural floor deck type is forced to concrete composite and the basement door spec updates to steel shelter door. Not a retrofit.


App field: Tornado shelter intent (None / FEMA 320 compliant / FEMA P-320 dedicated room / Not decided).
Interior Drainage Channel — Secondary Waterproofing Backup
A perimeter interior drainage channel at the wall-floor joint routes water that seeps through the weakest point in any below-grade structure — the wall-floor joint — to the sump pit before it reaches the finished floor area.
Installation: a 4" wide × 4" deep channel formed along the perimeter of the basement slab at the base of the wall, during the basement slab pour. A 2" perforated pipe sits in the channel, gravel fills around it. Cannot be added after the slab is poured without cutting.
App action: when Basement zone type is confirmed and waterproofing strategy = membrane, the interior drainage channel is added to the pre-pour checklist as a recommended item (not a blocker). Owner can decline with a reason note. If declined, an amber advisory appears: “Interior drainage channel not included — exterior membrane is the only moisture management system.”
Sump Pit Sizing for Basement
A basement sump handles higher inflow than a standard barn slab sump and must be sized accordingly.
* Standard barn slab sump: 18"–24" diameter, 24"–36" deep, 1/3 HP pump
* Basement sump: 24"–36" diameter, 30"–48" deep. Larger diameter accommodates primary + battery backup pump side by side. 1/2 HP cast iron primary pump minimum. Primary + battery backup mandatory.
App field: Sump pit diameter (18"–24" standard / 24"–36" basement) — auto-set to 24"–36" when zone type = Basement. Pump sizing auto-updates to 1/2 HP minimum.
Vehicle Service Pit Knowledge Package
Pit Dimensions by Vehicle Type
Pit geometry must be decided before any foundation design work begins (Priority 0, Section 9.3). All three dimensions are required fields — the pre-pour document cannot be generated until pit geometry is confirmed.
* Standard car pit: 16"–18" wide × 20–24 ft long × 48"–54" deep. Full wheelbase coverage with working room at each end. Depth allows standing upright beneath the vehicle with clearance for hands above the work area.
* Truck and SUV pit: 20"–24" wide × 22–28 ft long × 54"–60" deep. Wider vehicles require greater pit width to access the full underside. Increased depth provides clearance for higher ground clearance vehicles.
* Agricultural equipment pit (tractor, combine): 24"–36" wide × 30–40 ft long × 60"–72" deep. Large equipment has significantly higher ground clearance. Drive-over pit with reinforced grating rated for equipment axle weight — specify axle load to engineer.
App fields: pit length (ft), pit width (ft), pit depth (ft), primary vehicle type. App auto-recommends dimension ranges from the table above.
Edge Beam Rebar Detail
The edge beam is the reinforced concrete perimeter around the pit opening that carries the main slab over the opening. Vehicles and equipment drive over this — it must be engineered, not guessed.
What to bring to the engineering meeting:
* Maximum expected vehicle weight (lbs gross vehicle weight — drives the live load specification)
* Maximum expected axle load (lbs per axle — the governing design load for grating and edge beam)
* Grating or cover type (removable steel grating vs hinged steel cover — each has different load distribution behavior)
* Pit width (drives the clear span of the edge beam — longer spans require deeper and more heavily reinforced beams)
App action: when Vehicle Service Pit zone type is selected, a Tier 2 amber flag fires: “Edge beam rebar detail is engineer-required. Bring pit dimensions and maximum vehicle weight to the engineering meeting. The edge beam cannot be sized from standard tables.” This flag remains amber until the engineered drawing is uploaded.


Oil/Water Separator Sizing
Petroleum-contaminated floor drain water cannot discharge to a septic system. An oil/water separator is mandatory between the pit floor drain and any discharge point.
Sizing by pit floor area:
* Pit floor area up to 150 sq ft, hose-down cleaning: 100-gallon separator adequate for light-duty residential use
* Pit floor area 150–500 sq ft or pressure washing planned: 500-gallon separator minimum
* Commercial or heavy agricultural use: consult manufacturer sizing guide using peak GPM flow rate
For a typical residential car or light truck pit, a 100–200 gallon precast concrete or polyethylene separator is the standard choice. Located outside the building, accessible for vacuum truck pumping. The drain line from the pit to the separator exits through the slab — pre-pour item.
App field: Separator size in gallons (auto-recommended from pit floor area). Separator location on site plan (pre-pour item — coordinates required before pre-pour document can be generated). Pumping schedule reminder added to maintenance schedule at 6-month default.
Grating and Cover Options and Load Ratings
The grating or cover must be rated for the maximum expected vehicle load.
Removable steel bar grating (most common):
* Welded steel bars in a grid pattern, set in a steel frame that drops into the edge beam recess
* Load ratings: 300 lbs/sq ft (pedestrian only) to 3,000+ lbs/sq ft (H-20 highway loading)
* For car pit: minimum 1,000 lbs/sq ft (H-5 loading). For truck pit: 1,500–2,000 lbs/sq ft minimum. For agricultural equipment: confirm rated axle load with supplier
* Panel size: each panel should weigh no more than 75–100 lbs for safe single-person handling
Hinged steel cover:
* Steel plate on hinges, opening to one side. Simpler than grating — no loose panels to store
* Requires the cover to be fully open to access any part of the pit. Less convenient than removable grating sections for partial access
App field: Grating/cover type (Removable grating / Hinged cover), load rating in lbs/sq ft (required field). The edge beam recess dimensions must be coordinated between the grating supplier and the structural engineer before the foundation is formed.
CO Detector Placement
Carbon monoxide accumulates in a vehicle pit — CO is slightly heavier than air and collects in low points. A running engine near an open pit can produce fatal CO levels within minutes.
Required CO detector locations:
* Inside the pit: one hardwired CO detector at mid-height of the pit wall. Not at the bottom (oil contamination); not at the top (CO stratifies toward the bottom). Must be explosion-proof rated (NEC Section 511 — automotive service area classified location).
* At the pit rim: one hardwired CO detector at floor level adjacent to the pit opening, within 6 ft of any point where a technician would lean over the pit while a vehicle is running
* In the occupied zone above: one CO detector per standard residential placement rules
All CO detectors in a Vehicle Service Pit zone must be hardwired, not battery-only.
App action: when Vehicle Service Pit zone type is selected, three CO detector Circuit Records are auto-created. All three are flagged as Critical loads in the Backup Power module. All three require GFCI-protected weatherproof outlets or hardwired connections.
OSHA Confined Space Entry Procedure
A vehicle pit with a running engine is a confined space under OSHA 29 CFR 1910.146 definitions: enclosed space, large enough to enter and work in, limited means of entry/exit, not designed for continuous occupancy, with potential hazardous atmosphere (CO and petroleum vapors).
Procedure before entering:
* Ensure no vehicles have run in the building for at least 30 minutes
* Verify CO detector reading is below 35 ppm (OSHA action level)
* Activate pit ventilation fan (required by IMC 2018 — 1.5 CFM per sq ft of pit floor area minimum) and run for 5 minutes before entry
* Tell someone you are going in and when you expect to come out
While in the pit: keep the ventilation fan running continuously. No running engines — vehicles being serviced must have engines off before anyone enters. A remote start or idling helper vehicle can produce fatal CO levels in a below-grade pit within minutes.
App action: when Vehicle Service Pit zone type is selected, the construction knowledge package for that zone includes a permanent Watch note: “This is a confined space. Follow confined space entry procedure before each pit entry. Maintain ventilation. No running engines.” This note cannot be dismissed.




Cold Storage Knowledge Package
Ground Temperature Profile by Depth
Ground temperature stabilizes at approximately 55°F year-round at depths of 8 ft or greater in Kansas. This is the mean annual air temperature for the Kansas City area — the thermal mass of the earth insulates from seasonal swings at sufficient depth.
Temperature by depth (approximate for Kansas City latitude):
* 2 ft: follows seasonal air temperature with 4–6 week lag. Range approximately 30°F–75°F
* 4 ft: 6–8 week lag. Range approximately 38°F–68°F
* 6 ft: nearly stable. Range approximately 45°F–62°F
* 8 ft: essentially stable year-round at 53°F–57°F
* 10 ft and deeper: stable at 55°F ± 2°F year-round
For cold storage targeting 32°F–50°F for produce: minimum 8 ft below finished grade. At 8 ft the space stays 53°F–57°F year-round without active refrigeration. If the owner wants reliably below 50°F in summer, 10 ft is more reliable — the marginal cost of the extra 2 ft of excavation is low relative to total basement cost.
App field: Cold storage target temperature range (32°F–40°F / 40°F–50°F / 50°F–60°F). Depth recommendation auto-calculated: 40°F–50°F = 8 ft minimum; 32°F–40°F = 10 ft minimum (passive only, 32°F not reliably achievable passively in Kansas); 50°F–60°F = 6 ft minimum.
Shelf and Rack Layout Recommendations
Cold storage is most efficient when designed for specific produce types before the room is built. Wall framing locations, hatch width, and ventilation path are affected by the intended use.
* General-purpose produce storage: wooden shelving on three walls, one wall clear for bulkier items. Shelf depth 18"–24", spacing 14"–18", minimum 3" clearance between shelf back and wall for air circulation.
* Root vegetable storage: high humidity preferred (90%+ RH). Open-slatted wooden or plastic crates on pallet rack systems allow air circulation on all sides. If floor is concrete, place crates on wooden pallets to keep produce off the cold floor and allow drainage.
* Apple and pear storage: ethylene gas emitters — accelerate ripening of other produce. Dedicate a separate compartment or plan for good ventilation turnover to dilute ethylene buildup.
* Wine storage: prefers 55°F–58°F and 60%–70% RH — Kansas ground temperature at 8 ft delivers exactly this range. Standard wine bottle dimensions: 3.375" diameter × 11.5" length, stored horizontally.
App field: Intended use (General produce / Root vegetables primarily / Wine storage / Mixed). Selection drives shelf depth and spacing recommendations in the construction knowledge package.
Hatch Type Options
The hatch is the access point from the barn floor above into the cold storage room. It is a Tier 1 pre-pour blocker — hatch structural opening must be placed on the foundation layer before the slab is formed.
Floor hatch (standard):
* Steel-framed opening in the barn slab with a hinged or removable steel cover, flush with the barn floor when closed
* Minimum opening size: 24" × 36" for fixed ladder access; 30" × 48" preferred for comfortable access with loaded crates; 36" × 60" for lowering large items
* Cover types: hinged (opens to one side — requires clear floor space for swing radius), removable grate (lift-out, no swing radius but must be stored when open), custom wood-framed flush insert
* Load rating: must be rated for expected live load. If farm equipment drives over this area, cover must be rated for the equipment’s axle load
Exterior bulkhead (alternative):
* Sloped steel bulkhead door set into the exterior foundation wall, stairs descending from the bulkhead to the cold storage room floor. Standard residential units available from Bilco and similar manufacturers.
* Advantages: no opening in the barn floor, easier to move large items. Disadvantages: exposes access to weather, requires exterior stair well excavation, may not be practical if barn wall is in a tight site location.
App field: Hatch type (Floor hatch / Exterior bulkhead). Floor hatch: opening dimensions and cover type required — both drive the edge beam detail and the foundation layer pre-pour blocker.
Ceiling Insulation Detail — Inverted Insulation Logic
The ceiling of the cold storage room is the underside of the barn slab above. Insulate the ceiling, not the floor or walls. This is the opposite of every other zone in the spec.
WHY INVERTED: the barn floor above is heated or above-freezing. Without ceiling insulation, heat conducts from the barn slab down into the cold storage room, warming it. The earth contact on the floor and walls is the cooling source — insulating the floor or walls would cut the room off from the ground temperature that keeps it cold. No vapor barrier on the floor either (Section 3.3) — controlled moisture from the earth is intentional.


Insulation spec: minimum R-20 rigid foam (two layers of 2" XPS, staggered joints) applied to the underside of the barn slab above the cold storage room, covering the full ceiling area. Applied after the barn slab cures, before the cold storage room is put into service. Mechanically fastened to the underside of the concrete slab with concrete anchors and foam-compatible adhesive.
App action: when Cold Storage zone type is confirmed, the surface record for the ceiling surface automatically sets insulation method = rigid board, R-value = R-20 minimum, vapor retarder = none (explicitly flagged). An amber advisory appears: “INVERTED INSULATION — insulate this ceiling surface (barn slab above). Do NOT insulate the cold storage floor or walls. This is intentional.”
Ventilation for Humidity Control
Cold storage rooms require controlled ventilation to manage humidity and prevent mold. Natural humidity from the earth floor benefits most produce, but without air exchange the room accumulates CO2 from produce respiration and can develop mold if condensation occurs.
Passive ventilation (adequate for most residential applications):
Two vent sleeves through the barn slab or foundation wall — one low (intake, near floor level) and one high (exhaust, near ceiling level). The temperature differential creates a natural stack effect. Sleeve diameter: 4" minimum, 6" preferred. Both vents must have insect screens and closeable dampers — close during extreme cold (below 20°F) to prevent freezing produce, open during moderate weather for air exchange.
Active ventilation (for larger rooms or when passive is insufficient):
A small inline fan (50–100 CFM, adequate for rooms up to 200 sq ft) on the exhaust vent, thermostat-controlled to run when room temperature rises above the target range.
App field: Ventilation type (Passive only / Active with fan). Passive: two vent sleeve locations added to pre-pour drawing or framing rough-in list. Active: fan Equipment Record added at Concept status, electrical circuit record created.
Produce Storage Guidelines by Food Type
Planning reference for shelf and bin layout. Does not drive structural or code requirements.
* Root vegetables (potatoes, carrots, beets, parsnips, turnips): 32°F–40°F, 90–95% RH. Potatoes must be kept in darkness — light causes greening. Keep separate from apples (ethylene sensitivity).
* Apples and pears: 30°F–35°F, 90–95% RH. Ethylene producers — store away from other produce.
* Winter squash and pumpkins: 50°F–55°F, 50–70% RH. Warmer and drier than root vegetables.
* Onions and garlic (cured): 32°F–40°F, 65–70% RH. Lower humidity than most root vegetables — keep away from high-humidity produce or store in mesh bags.
* Hard cheese: 50°F–55°F, 80–85% RH.
* Wine: 55°F–58°F, 60–70% RH. Stored horizontally to keep corks moist.
* Cured meats: 50°F–58°F, 70–80% RH.
Practical layout: place highest-humidity produce (root vegetables, apples) near the earth floor where natural humidity is highest. Place lower-humidity items (squash, onions, wine) on upper shelves. Separate ethylene producers from ethylene-sensitive produce.
App action: Intended use field selection generates a produce storage reference card in the construction knowledge package for the Cold Storage zone — lists temperature and humidity targets for selected produce types and suggests shelf/bin placement. Appears as a printable document alongside the build sequence card.
17. Build Sequence & Order of Operations
SCENARIO NOTE: the build sequence below applies to the Active Build Scenario only — the Scenario that has been promoted via the Scenario promotion workflow (Section 3.1b). While multiple Scenarios are in Draft or Active status and no promotion has occurred, Section 17 is in planning mode: the steps are visible and useful for understanding sequence and dependencies, but no step can be checked off as complete and no STOP gate is enforced. The build sequence becomes a live executable checklist only after one Scenario is promoted to Active Build. This prevents the build sequence from being ambiguous about which set of buildings it refers to when multiple options are still under consideration.Order of operations is non-negotiable. Three steps have hard STOP flags — no work proceeds past them without completion.
App behavior: each step in this table is a checkable milestone in the timeline tool (Section 14, Week 4). STOP-flagged steps are hard gates — attempting to mark a later step complete while a STOP predecessor is unchecked triggers a red blocker. Additional app actions on completion: Step 7 completion triggers the Document 4 availability check (all pre-pour elements must be placed on the layout canvas before generation is unlocked). Step 9 completion moves all under-slab budget line items to &#x201C;pour locked&#x201D; status (amounts frozen, no further edits). Steps 8 and 12 each record an inspection date and inspector name field — these fields are required before the step can be marked complete.

18. Technical Stack & Hosting
Item
	Decision
	Hosting
	Netlify — free tier. Static site. Deploy by dragging folder or GitHub. Shareable URL.
	Frontend
	Single HTML file with vanilla JavaScript. No framework for Phase 1.
	Storage
	Browser localStorage — auto-saves on every change. Per device. Family syncs via exported project JSON files. Storage limit handling: on every save, check estimated quota usage. If usage exceeds 80%, display a yellow banner: &#x201C;Storage is nearly full — export your project JSON now to avoid data loss.&#x201D; If a save fails (quota exceeded), display a red error with a single-click export button. The app must never silently swallow a failed save. Corrupt save recovery: on app load, attempt to parse localStorage. If parsing fails, show a recovery screen with options: (1) download the raw localStorage string as a .txt file for manual recovery, (2) clear localStorage and start fresh. Do not attempt silent recovery — always ask the user.
	JSON schema version
	Every exported project JSON includes a top-level &#x201C;schemaVersion&#x201D; field (e.g. &#x201C;schemaVersion&#x201D;: &#x201C;1.0&#x201D;). On import, the app reads schemaVersion before processing any other field. If schemaVersion is missing or mismatched, display a warning: &#x201C;This file was exported from a different version of the app. Some fields may be missing or ignored.&#x201D; The import proceeds but highlights any fields that could not be mapped. Phase 2 adds a migration function that automatically upgrades older exports to the current schema. Dashboard JSON export (Flask app at E:\anthony_dashboard) also includes schemaVersion and is versioned independently from the project JSON.
	AI integration
	Claude.ai companion — app generates structured prompts, user pastes into Claude.ai, pastes response back. No API key required for Phase 1.
	Multi-user Phase 1
	Share Netlify URL. Each person uses own device with own localStorage. Sync via export/import.
	Multi-user Phase 2
	Supabase free tier — user accounts, role-based access, builder read-only links.
	Dashboard export
	JSON file download compatible with Anthony and Ruth Dashboard Flask app at E:\anthony_dashboard. Milestone dates, project name, property name, builder name, planned and actual dates.
	PDF export
	Browser print-to-PDF using CSS print styles. No server required. Print CSS scope: @media print hides all navigation, sidebar, buttons, canvas toolbars, and interactive UI elements. Only the document content area renders. Target page size: US Letter (8.5&#x201D; × 11&#x201D;), portrait for text documents, landscape permitted for the pre-pour coordination drawing. Print header (every page): document title (e.g. &#x201C;Builder Introduction Brief&#x201D;) and generation date. Print footer (every page): project name and page number (e.g. &#x201C;Homestead — Page 2 of 5&#x201D;). Canvas handling: Fabric.js canvas elements do not render in browser print-to-PDF by default. For the pre-pour coordination document, the app generates an SVG export of the canvas at print time (using fabric.Canvas.toSVG()) and injects it into the print view as an inline SVG. The canvas element itself is hidden in print CSS; the injected SVG is shown. This must be triggered before window.print() is called.
	PDF storage
	Google Drive — app stores sharing links, not files. Recommended folder: Homestead Project / Bids / Contracts / Change Orders / Invoices / Permits / Inspections / Plans.
	Layout & pre-pour drawing
	Fabric.js via CDN at a pinned version (e.g. fabric.min.js@5.3.0 — specify exact version in the CDN URL, never use @latest). Pinning prevents silent breakage when Fabric.js releases updates with breaking changes. Used exclusively for the coordinate-accurate layout plan canvas and pre-pour coordination document view. All other UI remains plain HTML/JS. No additional frameworks. Offline degradation: if the CDN request fails (no internet), the canvas area displays a non-blocking message: &#x201C;Drawing tools unavailable offline — all other features work normally.&#x201D; All non-canvas features (decision wizard, budget, document generation, build sequence) must remain fully functional without CDN access. The app must never throw an uncaught error if Fabric.js fails to load.
	

DRAWING LIBRARY CONSTRAINT: Fabric.js is the sole exception to the no-framework rule. It is imported via CDN and used only for the layout plan canvas and pre-pour document view. It was selected because the pre-pour coordination document is the highest-priority output and requires a true coordinate-accurate drawing — a text checklist does not catch spatial conflicts between trades. Raw SVG generation in vanilla JS was evaluated but rejected: generating a correct, labeled, coordinate-accurate, trade-color-coded drawing without a canvas library is nearly as complex as using one, but with no interactivity and brittle code. All other UI components remain vanilla JS.


19. Open Questions
App behavior: open questions are tracked as a named list with per-question status (Open / In Progress / Resolved). Resolved questions are retained with a resolution note — they are never deleted, so the decision history is preserved. The cascade engine (Section 9) surfaces the highest-priority Open question as a Next Action when no decision-wizard step is pending. Resolving a question that maps to a Section 16 module (Water Service Details, Greywater, Backup Power Planning) automatically triggers that module&#x2019;s completion check. Questions with a &#x201C;Resolved by&#x201D; entry in the table below have a clear resolution path already identified; those without are genuinely open.
Open Question
	Context
	Priority
	Resolved by / Status
	Do they own the land yet?
	Affects whether permit process can begin. Cannot file for building permit without ownership or recorded purchase agreement.
	High
	Open — confirm with parents before any permit filing.
	How many outbuilding concepts total?
	Needs to be reframed with Scenario model in place. Two sub-questions: (1) How many distinct configurations (Scenarios) do you want to compare? E.g. one Scenario = settled on a direction; two or three Scenarios = comparing options. This determines how many Scenario records to create at setup. (2) How many buildings per configuration? A Scenario can be a single building or several. Different Scenarios can have different building counts. Determines how many Building records to create under each Scenario. Resolve at project setup — additional Scenarios and Buildings can always be added later, but knowing the rough count up front shapes the initial workspace. ADDITIONAL SUB-QUESTION: does any building contain a vehicle service pit or cold storage room? This must be answered before any foundation design work begins — see Priority 0 in Section 9.3 and foundation sequences in Section 8.1. HIGH priority.
	High
	Open — parents should enumerate all planned structures at first planning session.
	Is living quarters attached or separate?
	Affects foundation transition detail, fire separation requirements (IRC R302), and permit category (residential vs accessory structure).
	High
	Open — architectural decision, affects Section 3 building record setup.
	Barn or living quarters first in planning?
	User wants barn storage calculator first — confirm living quarters is Phase 1 priority or Phase 2.
	Medium
	Open — confirm planning priority order with parents.
	What farm equipment do they have or plan?
	Seeds the item library with equipment that drives electrical load calcs, storage dimensions, and door sizing.
	Medium
	Open — parents should start a written list. App prompts for this in the item library setup wizard.
	Propane tank location relative to buildings?
	Affects fireplace fuel routing, boiler placement, NFPA 58 setback calculations, and underground line routing in Site Plan layer.
	Medium
	Open — resolved by completing Propane trade package (Section 15.5) and placing tank on site plan.
	Well or rural water service?
	Metered rural water district service confirmed to existing barn. New buildings branch from this tap. Pipe sizing, tap location, and pressure zone all go in the same trench as underground electrical and septic.
	HIGH — must resolve before site work (Build Sequence Step 5)
	In Progress — service type confirmed. Resolve fully via Water Service Details module (Section 16). Move to Section 20.
	Greywater — call Miami County Health Dept
	Kansas does not broadly permit greywater reuse. A single call to Miami County Health Dept produces an Approved or Denied answer that immediately determines the slab rough-in spec. Approved and Denied both resolve the question.
	High — do first
	In Progress — call not yet made. Resolve via Greywater module (Section 16). Move to Section 20.
	Generator rough-in type at existing barn?
	Existing barn has a generator rough-in. The type (conduit only, conduit + wire, full transfer switch) affects the Backup Power Planning Module starting assets.
	Medium
	Resolved — input captured in Section 7.6 Backup Power Module Step 1 (existing infrastructure inventory). Complete the module to propagate.
	Electrical topology decision
	Main panel + sub-panels (one meter, inter-building feeders) vs independent meters (separate utility accounts per building). Inter-building conduit goes in the trench during site work regardless of choice.
	High
	In Progress — gated by Backup Power Planning Module (Section 16, row 1). Complete that module first, then this decision follows from Config A/B/C selection.
	How many brothers need access?
	Affects multi-user design scope for Phase 2 (Supabase accounts, role-based access, builder read-only links).
	Low
	Open — Phase 2 planning item. No action needed for Phase 1.
	Dashboard export: automatic or manual trigger?
	Affects Flask connector module complexity at E:\\anthony_dashboard. Automatic trigger requires a background sync mechanism; manual trigger is simpler for Phase 1.
	Low
	Open — Phase 1 uses manual export (JSON download). Automatic sync is a Phase 2 feature.
	House planning Phase 2 trigger?
	Foundation section deliberately deferred. Living quarters planning depth is Phase 1 priority; full house design tools are Phase 2.
	Low
	Open — Phase 2 scope. Triggered when living quarters surface records are complete and builder feedback received.
	Soil bearing capacity at this site — has a soil test been done?
	Miami County clay bearing capacity is highly variable — 1,000–1,500 psf for undisturbed clay but potentially far lower in disturbed or fill areas. Footing sizing in Section 8.1a uses tool defaults without a confirmed soil test. If bearing capacity is below 1,000 psf, screw pilings (Section 8.1a Footing Type 6) replace spread footings and significantly change the foundation cost and schedule. A soil test before foundation design begins prevents an expensive redesign. A geotechnical firm conducts the test — typically 1–3 borings at building locations, 2–4 ft below planned footing depth. Cost: approximately $800–$2,000 for a small rural site. The result (bearing capacity in psf) feeds directly into the Section 9.1 cascade row — Soil test result entered.
	HIGH
	Open — must be resolved before foundation design is Confirmed. Resolved by: soil test result entered in property record soil test field. If result < 1,000 psf: screw piling evaluation required before foundation type is locked (Section 8.1a, Section 9.1 cascade).
	

20. Next Steps — In Order
* Complete Backup Power Planning Module (Section 7.6) — run all five steps for this property. This gates the electrical topology decision and inter-building conduit sizing. Do before any site work.
* Call Miami County Health Dept about greywater approval — Approved or Denied both immediately resolve the slab rough-in spec. This is one phone call. Do first.
* Resolve water service details — complete the Water Service Details module (Section 16). Pipe sizing, tap location, and pressure zone must be decided before Build Sequence Step 5. The trench is open once.
* Confirm farm equipment list with parents — seeds the item library with electrical loads, storage dimensions, and door sizing requirements.
* Complete HVAC module in dedicated planning conversation — run after Backup Power (affects HVAC electrical load) and Greywater (affects ventilation strategy).
* Complete Solar / Renewable Energy module in dedicated planning conversation — run after HVAC module so BTU demand drives the load offset calculation.
* Begin building the app — spec is complete and gap-analyzed through all 21 sections. Start with Section 14 Week 1: property workspace, project organizer, surface record model, item library. Target output is the pre-pour coordination document — build backward from that.


Start with the pre-pour coordination document as the target output and work backward to the inputs needed. This gives a concrete, high-value deliverable that forces all the right decisions while providing the most critical owner-builder protection against expensive mistakes.

Complete Decision Log — Scenario Feature AdditionsScenario data model: Property → Scenario → Building hierarchy adopted. Scenario sits at Level 1.5 between Property and Building. Rationale: named tag approach cannot support multi-building Scenarios with independent budget rollup; snapshot approach creates maintenance burden when shared property data changes. Intermediate level is the minimal change that solves the problem cleanly.Site plan canvas split: property-level infrastructure (septic, propane, drainage, site utilities, Placeholder Buildings) shared across all Scenarios on the property canvas base layer. Each Scenario's buildings on their own independent building placement layer on top of the shared base. Cross-scenario building footprint conflicts flagged as advisory (not blockers) since overlapping footprints between Scenarios are often intentional.Builder Brief multi-scenario output: user chooses at generation time between Mode 1 (separate PDFs per Scenario) and Mode 2 (combined Options Brief with comparison table on page 1 followed by per-Scenario sections). Mode 2 recommended for builder meetings. Builder version of Options Brief strips all budget data per Section 2.1b filter rules.Budget scoping: all cascade rollups (propane load, septic fixture count, electrical topology, inter-building conduit) now scope to Scenario level first. Property-level costs (septic installation, electric service trench, site clearing, propane tank) remain at Property level in a "Property Costs — All Scenarios" section that does not belong to any Scenario.Default Scenario behavior: single Scenario is invisible in the UI. Scenario layer only surfaces when second Scenario is created. Ensures existing single-project users see no new complexity.Sections updated: 3 (data model title and new Section 3.1b), 3.2 (Building Scenario parent), 5.1 (Scenario Buildings layer), 9.2 (cascade scoping), 9.3 (queue scoping), 13.9 (budget rollup and comparison panel), 14 (Phase 1 feature list), 15.1 (Options Brief two modes), 15.2 (Document 2 Scenario scoping), 15.5 (trade package Scenario scoping), 16 (Scenario Comparison module row), 17 (build sequence Scenario note), 19 (outbuilding question reframed).Complete Decision Log — Zone Type Taxonomy AdditionsZone type taxonomy adopted: the free-text primary use field in Section 3.3 replaced with an enumerated zone type dropdown. Zone types defined: Equipment Storage/Hay Storage, Heated Workshop, Living Quarters, Kitchen, Bathroom, Mechanical Room, Cold Storage/Root Cellar, Vehicle Service Pit, Open Face/Equipment Bay, Grain Storage. Rationale: free-text primary use cannot drive cascade logic, pre-pour activations, or trade package filtering. An enumerated taxonomy allows the tool to activate exactly the right requirements for each zone type automatically.Vehicle Service Pit zone type: classified as a Tier 1 pre-pour blocker. Pit geometry must be decided before foundation design. Pour sequence: pit walls first, main slab second. Key requirements: engineered edge beam, oil/water separator (mandatory, petroleum cannot go to septic), IMC confined space ventilation (1.5 CFM/sqft), NEC 511 GFCI-everywhere, structural grating/cover opening. All activate automatically on zone type selection.Cold Storage/Root Cellar zone type: classified as a Tier 1 pre-pour blocker. Hatch location must be decided before slab is formed. Minimum depth 8 ft for Kansas ground temperature. Inverted insulation logic: insulate ceiling (barn slab above), not floor or walls. Vapor barrier exception on floor. No HVAC. Temperature from earth thermal mass.Living Quarters zone type: triggers IRC residential code path, AFCI on all circuits, egress window requirement on bedroom surfaces, smoke/CO detector circuits, ERV/HRV mechanical ventilation. This zone type is the trigger for full residential code requirements regardless of building type (barn or house).Kitchen zone type: triggers two 20A small appliance circuits (NEC 2020 210.52), range hood exterior exhaust sleeve, dishwasher circuit + air gap, ice maker stub, GFCI within 6 ft of sinks. All activate automatically.Priority 0 decision added to Section 9.3: below-grade room decision fires before any other zone decisions at building creation. Cannot be added after slab is poured without breaking concrete.Sections updated: 3.3 (zone type taxonomy), 5.1 (pit and cold storage layers), 5.2 (pre-pour items for pit, cold storage, kitchen), 7.1 (electrical zone type rules), 7.2 (plumbing zone type rules), 7.3 (HVAC zone type rules including ERV/HRV and confined space ventilation), 8.1 (foundation sequences for pit and cold storage), 9.1 (zone type cascade row), 9.3 (Priority 0 and Priority 1.5 decisions), 15.5 (trade packages zone-type-filtered), 16 (four new knowledge package modules), 19 (pit and cold storage pre-foundation open question).Complete Decision Log — Visual Grammar and Basement AdditionsSection 5.6 Visual Design Grammar added: specifies complete visual language for every trade layer (electrical, plumbing, HVAC/radiant, gas/propane, structural, pre-pour composite, site plan). (2) Every trade has a symbol palette of drop-and-place items, per-trade color coding, line style conventions, and an auto-generated diagram that renders in the Diagram View. (4) One-line electrical diagram auto-generates from system topology decisions (solar intent, battery, generator, sub-panels) — user never draws it.
