Complete Decision Log — v4.0 Additions
* Three-phase cost pipeline established: Phase 1 decision-support estimates (±25-35%), Phase 2 regional sourcing (±10-15%), Phase 3 quote comparison (±3-5%). Each phase uses a distinct Claude prompt with different inputs and structured outputs.
* Budget line item status model: Concept → Estimated → Quoted → Committed → Actual. Every line item moves through these statuses. Estimate is never deleted — preserved as benchmark for variance tracking. LABOR COST MODEL: every trade in the budget has both a materials line item and a labor line item. For owner-performed work, labor is set to $0 and marked &#x201C;Owner&#x201D;. For licensed or contracted trades, labor starts as a placeholder at Concept status and moves to Quoted when a subcontractor quote is received. The cascade engine flags any trade where: (a) labor is still $0 and the work is not marked Owner-performed, or (b) labor is marked Owner-performed but the trade is a licensed trade (electrical, plumbing, gas, HVAC) — licensed trades require a licensed contractor to inspect and final even when the owner does the rough work. The flag reads: &#x201C;Labor not accounted for — mark as Owner-performed or add quote placeholder.&#x201D; This prevents trades from falling silently through the budget. Owner-performed labor is tracked separately as a time estimate (hours) rather than a dollar amount — useful for scheduling and for deciding when a trade is worth hiring out as the project grows in scope.
* Variance tracking: estimate vs actual recorded for every line item. Calibration signal built over time — if a material category consistently comes in above estimate, that offset is applied to future estimates.
* Claude Prompt 1 (decision support): carries zone dimensions, construction type options, adjacent locked decisions, climate zone, and owner-builder intent. Returns recommendation with reasoning, cost range per option with confidence qualifier, what is included/excluded, and top watch items.
* Claude Prompt 2 (regional sourcing): carries complete materials takeoff quantities and specs, county and nearest towns, sourcing radius preference, lead time sensitivity. Returns suppliers by category with distance, what to ask for, current price range, lead time flags, and a generated call script.
* Call script generator: plain-language script the user reads when calling suppliers. Removes intimidation barrier for non-builder users. Tells exactly what words to say, what specs to give, what questions to ask, and what to record.
* Claude Prompt 3 (quote comparison): carries original estimate, takeoff quantities, and all quotes entered. Returns anomaly flags (quote >20% below others = risk, >30% above = investigate), spec verification, recommendation with reasoning, estimate variance diagnosis, and budget update.
* Regional supplier knowledge base seeded for Miami County Kansas: lumber (McCray Lumber Paola, Sutherlands Harrisonville, 84 Lumber Olathe), SIP panels (ship-to-site, 4-8 week lead time), engineered lumber (special order through local yards), ready-mix concrete (Paola, Louisburg, Osawatomie plants — always 3 quotes), metal roofing (farm supply, local steel), propane (Ferrellgas, AmeriGas, rural co-ops — buy vs rent at 5-year threshold).
* screw pilings / helical piers (specialty foundation contractor — not owner-installable): primary search radius KC metro, 40–60 miles from Paola. Known regional contractors: Magnum Piering (Kansas City metro, multiple office locations); Atlas Resistance Piers — find local authorized dealer at atlaspiers.com; Foundation Supportworks — regional dealer network covers southeast Kansas. Search terms for additional contractors: “helical pier installation Kansas City,” “screw pile contractor Paola KS,” “helical pile new construction southeast Kansas.” Get minimum 2 quotes — pricing varies significantly by shaft diameter, helix plate count, depth to bearing, and site access for the drive machine (skid steer or mini-excavator must reach every pile location). Ask each contractor for torque logs from a recent comparable project as a reference. Typical cost range for a small building foundation (8–12 piles): $3,000–$8,000 installed, which is often cost-competitive with conventional spread footings once excavation, forming, rebar, concrete, and cure time are fully priced. Pile contractor quote moves the foundation support line item from Concept to Quoted status in the budget. Phase 2 prompt includes screw pile contractors in the regional sourcing output when foundation support type = Screw Pilings.
FOOTING CONCRETE NOTE: ready-mix concrete is already seeded in Section 13.7 for Paola, Louisburg, and Osawatomie plants. Footing concrete is a separate pour from the slab — always request a separate quote for the footing yardage. Footing concrete spec: 3,000 psi minimum, standard aggregate, standard slump. If ICF basement walls are also being poured the same day, coordinate two separate mixes on one truck order where possible — ICF mix (4”–5” slump, 3/8” max aggregate) for wall pour, standard mix for footing pour. Confirm with batch plant that they can sequence two mixes on a single mobilization.
* Quote expiry tracking: 30 days = stale flag, 60 days = expired, requote required. Lumber and steel: recheck within 2 weeks of purchase. Ready-mix: quote day before pour.
* Budget summary view: shows Committed + Quoted + Estimated simultaneously with confidence bands. Total range narrows as items move from Estimated to Quoted. 15% contingency line shown explicitly for owner-builder projects.
* Sourcing radius model: primary within 30 miles (Paola, Louisburg, Harrisonville MO), secondary within 60 miles (Olathe, Overland Park, KC), ship-to-site for SIP panels, engineered lumber, specialty HVAC. Ready-mix is always local.


Complete Decision Log — v3.0 Additions (retained)
* Wall-to-roof structural matrix added: SIP, stud, ICF, and metal post-frame each have documented recommended roof structures, cautions, and Kansas-specific structural notes
* Roof shape compatibility by wall type: gable (best for SIP), shed/mono-pitch (simplest for lean-to), hip (avoid with SIP), gambrel (stud only), asymmetric (recommended for solar priority)
* Structural ridge beam established as the central SIP roof decision: structural ridge beam eliminates outward thrust and is the recommended approach for SIP walls
* Ridge beam cascade chain documented: SIP wall choice → structural ridge beam → ridge beam posts → foundation footings in pre-pour plan. Four-step cascade from wall choice to concrete decision.
* Ridge height calculation formula documented: wall panel height + (half span x pitch rise per foot) = ridge height. Must be calculated before ordering SIP wall panels — panel height is fixed at manufacture.
* Asymmetric roof documented for solar optimization: steeper south face (30-35 degrees), shallower north face, ridge offset toward north wall. South panels longer — specify at order.
* Roof pitch constraints documented: SIP with metal roofing minimum 3/12, solar optimal 6/12 to 8/12 for Kansas latitude, Kansas design snow load 20 psf, hurricane ties required at every rafter-to-plate connection
* Overhang depth established as a calculated dimension (not decorative): south overhang = window height x 0.5 for Kansas. Feeds HVAC solar gain calculation. Lives on roof plan layer.
* Roof plan added as a full layer in the layout architecture. Shows: ridge, posts, rafters, slope arrows, gutters, downspouts, overhang, solar array zone, and all penetrations (plumbing vent, lineset, flue, exhaust).
* Conflict detection on roof plan: automatic flags when penetration is inside solar zone, on a rafter, flue too close to array, downspout toward foundation, valley under array, or chimney cap below ridge
* Section view added as a third plan view alongside floor plan and roof plan. Shows complete load path from footing to ridge, all assembly layers, interior dimensions, thermal boundaries, and mechanical room cross-section.
* Section cut types documented: default N-S and E-W cuts, plus automatic cuts at bathroom, mechanical room, lean-to attachment, and thermal boundary locations
* Section view confirmed as companion to pre-pour document: same items shown in cross-section to confirm depths, slopes, and layer relationships before pour
* Structural elements visible on building plan: floor plan structural layer shows post bases, beam pockets, holdowns; roof plan structural layer shows ridge beam, posts, rafter spacing; section view shows complete load path with dimensions


Complete Decision Log — v2.0 Additions (retained)
* Surface record added as a first-class data model object at Level 4 of the four-level hierarchy
* Construction knowledge system: three-part package per surface (materials takeoff, build sequence card, watch list) context-assembled from decisions already made
* Layout layer architecture: 12 layers, each activating from specific decisions. Simple projects stay simple.
* Pre-pour coordination document established as the highest-priority tool output — Document 4 in generation flow
* All trades documented including licensed trades (electrical, plumbing, HVAC, gas) — owner intends to do work and have licensed professional check/final
* SIP-specific constraints fully documented: electrical routing (service chase or conduit), window bucks before erection, factory vs field routing decision before panel manufacture
* HVAC system selection matrix by zone type including SIP and cathedral ceiling constraints
* Solar fully expanded to three configurations: no solar / grid-tied / battery hybrid — each has different electrical panel, conduit, and permit implications
* Foundation decision tree by zone type: heated (radiant or standard) / unheated (gravel, slab, posts) / hybrid with engineered transition joint
* Miami County clay soil flags apply to every foundation type — vapor barrier and granular fill are non-negotiable
* Decision cascade engine: tracks locked vs open nodes, all downstream calculations update when node is locked
* Cross-scope cascades documented: shared walls update both zones, propane loads roll up to property tank sizing, electrical loads inform topology decision
* Propane and solar established as complementary systems — solar for electrical, propane for thermal
* CSST gas bonding coordination point between gas and electrical trades documented
* Pre-pour sequence: underground utilities → excavation → foam + vapor → all trades rough-in → inspection → pour. Radiant stays pressurized during pour.
22. Genericization — Architecture for Reuse Across Projects
This section is the authoritative statement of how the Homestead Planner should be structured when built as software intended to serve more than one project or property. It exists because the spec document was developed using a specific property as a working example — a farm in Miami County, Kansas — and project-specific assumptions are embedded throughout. This section tells a developer (or a future Claude session focused on a rebuild) how to separate those assumptions from the generic tool logic.
USE THIS SECTION AT THE START OF ANY CONVERSATION ABOUT: software architecture for the Homestead Planner, porting the tool to a new property or county, beginning a full rebuild, or onboarding a developer reading the spec for the first time. Paste it alongside the main spec document, not instead of it.


The three-layer architecture
Every piece of information in this spec belongs to one of three layers. A correctly built tool keeps these layers strictly separate.
Layer 1 — Tool engine (generic, in code)
The rules, cascade logic, calculations, and UI behavior. This layer contains no property-specific values. It operates on whatever data the property record provides. The tool engine is identical for every project. Examples of how hardcoded spec language translates to generic engine logic:
* Spec says “frost depth: 24 inches — Miami County requirement.” Engine reads: footing_minimum_depth = property.frost_depth_inches
* Spec says “natural gas not available — rural.” Engine reads: show fuel options where property.fuel_options includes option
* Spec says “clay content common in eastern Kansas — vapor barrier critical.” Engine reads: if property.soil_type in [expansive_clay, moderate_clay]: require vapor_barrier = true
* Spec says “Kansas wind zone — rated doors required.” Engine reads: if property.wind_speed_vult >= 115: activate high_wind_requirements
* Spec says “IECC Zone 4A minimums: walls R-20, ceiling R-49.” Engine reads: apply insulation minimums from IECC_table[property.climate_zone]
* Spec says “AFCI required in all living spaces (NEC 2020 210.12).” Engine reads: apply AFCI rules from NEC_table[property.electrical_code_edition]
Layer 2 — Property record (project-specific, in data)
The values that define a specific project. Every assumption currently hardcoded in the spec belongs here as a named field. The complete list of fields that must exist in the property record, drawn from assumptions currently embedded throughout the spec:
Location and jurisdiction:
* County and state
* Permit authority name and contact
* Applicable code editions — each independently selectable: building (IRC 2015 / 2018 / 2021 / 2024), electrical (NEC 2017 / 2020 / 2023), plumbing (UPC / IPC + edition), mechanical (IMC + edition), energy (IECC + edition)
* Local amendments — free-text per code edition
Climate and site:
* IECC climate zone (1A through 8 — drives insulation minimums, vapor retarder class, ventilation requirements)
* Frost depth in inches (entered manually or lookup from zone + county)
* Design heating temperature °F at 99% (ASHRAE — drives HVAC sizing, freeze protection specs)
* Design cooling temperature °F at 1% (ASHRAE — drives HVAC sizing, solar gain calculations)
* Wind exposure category (A / B / C / D per ASCE 7)
* Ultimate design wind speed mph, Vult (from IRC Figure R301.2(4) for the county — drives structural hardware requirements, door wind ratings, anchor bolt engineering flag)
* Soil type (well-draining sand/gravel / moderate clay / expansive clay / organic / unknown — drives vapor barrier requirement, granular fill requirement, footing sizing defaults, screw piling flag threshold)
* Site drainage condition (Elevated / Flat / Low-lying / Unknown — drives subsurface drainage recommendation)
* Peak sun hours per day (NREL PVWatts lookup for the location — drives solar production estimates)
* Average wind speed mph (NREL Wind Resource lookup — drives wind turbine viability)
* True north bearing offset / magnetic declination (degrees — drives compass correction for solar calculations)
Utilities available:
* Natural gas available (yes / no)
* Propane available (yes / no)
* Electric utility provider name
* Electric service distance from road in feet (entered on site)
* Water source (rural district / well / both)
* Water district name and contact (if applicable)
Existing infrastructure:
* Existing buildings on property (list — each with type, panel status, generator rough-in status)
* Existing generator rough-in (conduit only / wire pulled / transfer switch — per building)
* Existing water service entry point
Users and roles:
* Primary occupants (technical / non-technical — drives tooltip depth, wizard vs advanced mode defaults)
* User list with roles (Owner / Developer / Head Consultant / Collaborator / Builder / Architect)
Integrations:
* Dashboard export path or URL (currently E:\\anthony_dashboard in the spec — must be a configurable per-project field, not hardcoded in Section 18)
* Any other external tool integrations (project management, accounting, CAD) — configurable, not hardcoded
Layer 3 — Regional knowledge (location-derived, generated at runtime)
Supplier lists, local contractor names, regional pricing, permit fee ranges, local amendment details. This layer is never hardcoded in the tool engine. It is generated at runtime by Claude Prompt 2 (regional sourcing, Section 13.4) using the property location as input. The Miami County supplier list in Section 13.7 of the spec is the canonical example of what Prompt 2 should return for any US location — it is reference material for prompt design and output format, not a permanent database entry in the software.
Sections most affected by this substitution
In priority order for a developer review pass before building:
* Section 2 (The People and Property) — currently describes Miami County as fixed context. Should become a description of the property record schema, with Miami County values shown as example data in a table. HIGH substitution density.
* Section 8 (Foundation Planning) — frost depth, clay soil, and Kansas wind assumptions appear in nearly every paragraph. HIGH substitution density.
* Section 7 (Trade Knowledge, all trades) — propane-specific sizing, Kansas code references, Miami County inspector notes throughout. MEDIUM-HIGH substitution density.
* Section 12 (Systems and Code Knowledge) — hardcoded to IRC 2018 / NEC 2020 / IECC Zone 4A. Should be driven by property record code edition fields. MEDIUM substitution density.
* Section 13.7 (Regional Supplier Knowledge) — entirely Layer 3 content. In the built tool this section does not exist as a static list — it is the expected output format of Claude Prompt 2 for any location. Treat as a prompt design reference.
* Section 18 (Technical Stack) — Flask dashboard path is project-specific. Should be a configurable integration field. LOW substitution density otherwise.
* Sections 19 and 20 (Open Questions and Next Steps) — entirely project-specific. In the built tool these become the per-project decision log and task queue, populated from the property record, not static document content.


Known limitations of this architecture
The three-layer model is complete for the most common migration scenarios: different US county, different property, different users, different configuration. The following gaps exist for less common but real scenarios. A developer or future architect should be aware of them before starting a build.
Gap 1 — Different country (reference table portability):
The tool engine encodes US-specific reference tables: NEC wire ampacity (Table 310.16), UPC fixture units (Table 702.1), NFPA 54 gas pipe sizing, ASHRAE climate data, IECC insulation minimums, ASCE 7 wind speeds. For a Canadian, UK, Australian, or other non-US project, these tables are wrong or inapplicable. The property record’s code edition fields partially address this (you can select a different code edition), but the underlying lookup tables are still US-only. A non-US build would require replacing the encoded reference tables with country-specific equivalents. The spec does not describe how those tables are structured or stored — that is a developer design decision.
Gap 2 — Different building types (zone type taxonomy extensibility):
The zone type taxonomy (Section 3.3) covers residential and agricultural building types: living quarters, workshop, equipment storage, barn, cold storage, vehicle pit, etc. A commercial, industrial, or institutional project (retail, office, warehouse, school) would require zone types not in the current taxonomy, different occupancy classifications, different code paths (IBC instead of IRC), and different inspection sequences. The spec’s cascade engine is designed to be extensible — new zone types can be added — but the current taxonomy does not cover these building types and the spec provides no guidance on how to extend it for commercial use. Treat the current taxonomy as a starting point for residential/agricultural only.
Gap 3 — Platform integration flexibility:
The spec describes one integration point: a JSON export to a Flask dashboard at a configurable path. If the tool is extended to integrate with project management software (Asana, Procore, Buildertrend), accounting software (QuickBooks), CAD platforms (AutoCAD, Revit), or BIM tools, the integration architecture is not described beyond “make the export path configurable.” The takeoff export formats (Section 5.5.5) are well-structured for downstream integration — the CSV and JSON schemas are stable and designed for import into external tools — but the integration layer itself is underdefined. A developer building integrations should design a plugin or connector architecture rather than hardcoding each integration.
Gap 4 — Claude Prompt 2 reliability at unfamiliar locations:
Layer 3 regional knowledge depends on Claude Prompt 2 returning accurate regional supplier data for any property location. For well-populated US metro areas this works well. For remote rural areas, international locations, or regions with thin training data coverage, Prompt 2 may return generic or inaccurate results. The spec does not describe a fallback mechanism for poor Prompt 2 output — no manual override workflow, no supplier database fallback, no confidence indicator on returned supplier data. A production build should include a way for users to flag Prompt 2 supplier results as inaccurate and enter manual data, and should display a confidence indicator based on the specificity of the location.
Gap 1 — Extension: Country Change (International Use)
The original Gap 1 note covers non-US reference tables at a summary level. This extension specifies what actually changes when the tool is used in a different country — it is more comprehensive than a simple table substitution.
Layer 1 — Tool engine changes required for international use:
Unit system:
The entire spec is written in US customary units (inches, feet, BTU, gallons, psi, degrees Fahrenheit). A metric-system country requires complete unit system substitution — not just display formatting, but all encoded thresholds, default values, and calculation constants. Frost depth of 24" becomes 610mm. R-20 wall insulation maps to RSI 3.5. A 1/4" per foot drain slope becomes approximately 20mm per meter. The unit system must be a property record field (US customary / metric) that feeds every calculation and display string. This is not cosmetic — it is a fundamental data type change affecting every numeric field in the spec.
Building code framework:
The spec encodes US building codes (IRC, NEC, UPC, IMC, IECC) and their specific table lookups. Other countries use different frameworks: Canada uses NBC/NFCC, the UK uses Building Regulations with Approved Documents, Australia uses the NCC/BCA, and the EU uses Eurocodes. The encoded reference tables (NEC wire ampacity, UPC fixture units, NFPA gas pipe sizing, ASHRAE climate data, IECC insulation minimums) all have country-specific equivalents that must be substituted. This requires replacing the encoded tables entirely, not configuring around them.
Climate zone framework:
The spec uses IECC climate zones 1A through 8. Canada, Europe, and Australia each use their own climate zone designations and insulation requirement tables. The climate zone field in the property record must accommodate multiple zone frameworks, and the insulation minimums, vapor retarder rules, and HVAC sizing assumptions must map to the correct framework for the project’s country.
Electrical system:
The spec assumes 120/240V, 60Hz, NEMA outlets, AWG wire sizing. Most other countries use 220–240V, 50Hz, IEC outlets, and metric wire cross-sectional areas (mm²). The entire electrical section (7.1, 7.1a) and the electrical layer visual grammar would require revision for a non-North American electrical system.
Wind and seismic:
The spec references ASCE 7 for wind speed design. Other countries use ISO 4354 (wind), Eurocode 1 (wind), or national standards. Seismic design — not currently addressed in the spec even for the US — would need to be added for countries with significant seismic risk (Japan, New Zealand, Chile, western US, Turkey, etc.).
Layer 2 — Additional property record fields required for international use:
* Country field (drives unit system, code framework, climate zone framework, electrical system, and regional knowledge prompt language)
* Unit system (US customary / metric — auto-set from country, owner-overridable)
* Electrical system (120/240V 60Hz NEMA / 220–240V 50Hz IEC / other)
* Building code framework (IRC+NEC+UPC+IMC+IECC / NBC+CSA / UK Building Regulations / NCC Australia / Eurocode / other)
* Climate zone framework (IECC / NCC Australia / EN ISO 15927 / National standard)
* Seismic design category (None / Low / Moderate / High / Very High — drives structural engineering requirements flag)
Layer 3 — Regional knowledge:
Claude Prompt 2 (regional sourcing) must be prompted in the appropriate language for the country and instructed to use country-appropriate supplier categories, product standards, and pricing units. The Miami County supplier list format (Section 13.7) is a US-centric template. Supplier structures differ internationally: timber merchants vs lumber yards, builders’ merchants vs hardware stores, trade-only vs retail distribution channels. The Prompt 2 output format specification (Section 13.4) would need country-appropriate supplier category labels and pricing unit conventions.
Gap 5 — Alternative and Non-Conventional Construction Types
The current spec covers six construction types: 2×4 stud, 2×6 stud, SIP panel, ICF, metal purlin, and CMU. The incompatibility matrix (Section 5.7.1), cascade engine (Section 9.1), thermal envelope calculations, and materials takeoff engine are all calibrated around these six types. Adding alternative construction types — straw bale, rammed earth, earthship/tire wall, cob, adobe, hempcrete, mass timber, structural brick masonry, and stone masonry — is not a matter of adding rows to the construction type dropdown. Each alternative type changes the physics of the tool in ways that require fundamental additions to the spec before code can be written.
What makes alternative types architecturally different from the six current types:
Thermal mass vs R-value:
The current tool measures thermal performance as R-value (resistance to heat flow). Straw bale, rammed earth, cob, adobe, and stone masonry derive much of their thermal performance from thermal mass — the ability to absorb heat during the day and release it at night. Thermal mass performance cannot be expressed as R-value alone. A rammed earth wall at R-3 per inch performs very differently in a dry climate with large diurnal temperature swings (where thermal mass is highly effective) than in a cloudy humid climate. The HVAC load calculation (Section 7.3) does not currently account for thermal mass — it uses surface R-values only. Any type with significant thermal mass requires a different load calculation methodology.
Non-standard code pathways:
Many alternative construction types are not covered by the IRC or are covered only in alternative methods appendices (e.g., IRC Appendix R for light straw-clay, some state-level straw bale codes). The permit pathway is often “alternative materials and methods” approval under IRC Section R104.11, which requires local building department engagement at design stage. The tool would need a flag per construction type indicating whether a standard permit path exists in the project’s jurisdiction.
Non-standard takeoff units:
The current takeoff engine knows stud count (pieces), SIP panels (pieces by dimension), ICF forms (LF of wall × height), and sheet metal (SF). Alternative types require new units: straw bale = bales (a 3-string bale is approximately 16"×24"×48"); rammed earth = cubic yards of compacted earth mix; cob = cubic feet of cob material; adobe = block count (standard US adobe block: 10"×4"×14"); hempcrete = cubic feet of mix; stone masonry = cubic feet of wall or tons of stone. None of these are in the current pack quantity engine (Section 5.5.8).
Moisture management:
The vapor retarder class assignment logic (Section 5.7, Zone 4A rules) is calibrated for wood-framed and concrete-based assemblies. Many alternative types have fundamentally different moisture management approaches: straw bale walls must be able to dry to both sides (vapor-open assemblies); earthen walls (cob, adobe, rammed earth) are hygroscopic — they actively absorb and release moisture — and must never have vapor-impermeable finishes applied. The Class I vapor retarder block (Section 5.7.1) would apply to these types, but the reasoning and compatible alternatives would be completely different.
The key principle for any alternative construction type addition: the incompatibility matrix (Section 5.7.1) exists because construction failures are predictable. For the six current types, those failure modes are documented in the spec. For any new type, the developer must research and document the specific failure modes before writing the incompatibility matrix entries. Adding a construction type without its incompatibility matrix entries is dangerous — it allows the tool to guide an owner-builder into a failing assembly without warning.


What a developer must specify for each new construction type before writing code:
* Structural classification: load-bearing / non-load-bearing infill / hybrid. Determines which wall-to-roof combinations are valid.
* Thermal performance model: R-value based / thermal mass adjusted / hygrothermal (moisture-buffering) / combination. Determines which HVAC load calculation methodology applies.
* Moisture management approach: vapor-open / vapor-controlled / hygroscopic. Determines vapor retarder class assignment and compatible finishes.
* Takeoff unit: what the material is sold as and how quantities are calculated from wall geometry.
* Code pathway: standard IRC / alternative methods / state-specific code / no applicable code (requires engineer of record).
* Incompatibility matrix entries: what roof types, finishes, HVAC systems, and electrical routing methods are incompatible, and why.
* Wall assembly library entries: correct assembly from structural substrate to exterior finish.
* Climate zone constraints: which IECC climate zones this type is appropriate for.


Specific notes on the most likely future types:
Straw bale:
Best documented alternative type. Two subtypes: load-bearing (Nebraska style) and post-and-beam infill. R-value approximately R-1.5 per inch for a standard 3-string bale (18" wall = R-27), but hygrothermal behavior is complex. Must be kept dry — roof overhangs and raised foundation are critical. Plastered with lime or clay plaster on both faces — no vapor-impermeable paint or stucco. Romex wiring inside straw bale is impractical and should be blocked; surface-mounted conduit or a service chase are the correct electrical routing methods. IRC Appendix R and several state codes provide a code pathway.
Rammed earth:
Very high thermal mass, low R-value (approximately R-0.4 per inch — a 12" rammed earth wall is R-5, but thermal mass performance is much better in appropriate climates). Only appropriate in climates with large diurnal temperature swings and low humidity — western US dry regions, Australia’s interior, Mediterranean. Not well-suited to Zone 4A Kansas where high humidity and relatively small diurnal swings limit thermal mass effectiveness. The tool should flag rammed earth in Zone 4A with an advisory: “Rammed earth thermal mass provides limited benefit in Zone 4A mixed-humid climate — evaluate carefully with a building performance consultant.”
Earthship (tire walls):
A specific building system developed by architect Michael Reynolds using car tires packed with rammed earth as the primary load-bearing wall, with a distinctive U-shaped plan integrating passive solar, thermal mass, water harvesting, and food production. The earthship system is so holistic — it integrates site orientation, roof design, glazing, water, and food systems — that it would require its own zone type taxonomy rather than just a new wall construction type. It is less an alternative construction material and more an alternative building system. The tool’s modular approach could accommodate it, but it would require a dedicated design session to specify how the earthship system maps onto the data model.
Hempcrete (hemp-lime):
Hemp shiv (woody core of the hemp stalk) mixed with lime binder, cast around a structural frame (wood or steel — hempcrete is not structural on its own). R-value approximately R-2 per inch with significant thermal mass and moisture-buffering. Breathable assembly — must not have vapor-impermeable finishes. Growing code acceptance in the US under alternative materials provisions. Takeoff unit: cubic feet of hempcrete mix, or bags of hemp shiv + bags of lime binder separately.
Mass timber (CLT, glulam, heavy timber):
Well-established and code-supported system (IBC Chapter 23 for mass timber, IRC for light frame). CLT (Cross-Laminated Timber) panels behave similarly to SIP panels in the data model but with different structural, thermal, and fire resistance properties. Adding mass timber follows the same pattern as SIP panels — a structural panel system with defined performance characteristics and specific electrical routing requirements — but it differs in thermal mass behavior and fire resistance characteristics.
Structural brick masonry:
Important distinction: brick veneer over a stud or ICF backup wall is already covered in the Surface Record exterior finish options (Section 3.4) and the wall assembly library (Section 5.7). What is NOT covered is full structural brick masonry — load-bearing double-wythe or multi-wythe brick construction. Structural brick has its own code path (TMS 402/602, the Masonry Structures code), different thermal properties from veneer, its own lintel requirements over openings, and significantly increased foundation demands. A double-wythe brick wall weighs approximately 80–100 lbs per sq ft — roughly 4–5× a wood-framed wall — which changes footing sizing calculations substantially. The cascade engine’s footing auto-generation logic (Section 8.1a) would need to account for masonry wall dead loads, not just roof and floor live loads.
Stone masonry:
Three distinct systems with different behavior:
* Mortared stone masonry: laid in mortar like brick. Code path similar to CMU and structural brick (TMS 402/602). Very high thermal mass (R-0.08 per inch — a 12" stone wall is approximately R-1), low R-value, extremely heavy (150–170 lbs per cubic foot). Foundation demands are the highest of any wall type — continuous perimeter footing sizing must account for the full wall dead load. Appropriate only in climates where thermal mass is highly effective and exterior insulation is added to the assembly. Regional sourcing varies dramatically — stone is a locally-sourced material in most cases, and Prompt 2 regional sourcing would need to query local quarries and stone yards.
* Dry-stacked stone: no mortar, relies on gravity and interlocking. Traditional but not code-recognized for habitable structures in most US jurisdictions without an engineer of record. Not recommended for the tool to guide an owner-builder through without flagging that this requires alternative methods approval and likely a structural engineer.
* Stone veneer over backup wall: cosmetic application over stud or ICF backup, similar to brick veneer. Already partially covered in the Surface Record exterior finish options. The structural backup wall carries all loads; the stone veneer is a finish layer only. Takeoff unit: square feet of stone veneer at specified thickness.
Masonry construction type family — shared gaps:
Structural brick and mortared stone masonry both belong to the same structural family as CMU. All three share a common gap in the current spec: CMU is the only masonry type in the construction type taxonomy, and the footing sizing logic, thermal mass handling, and moisture management rules would all need extension to properly cover structural brick and stone. When adding any of these types, consider adding them as subtypes within an expanded “Masonry” construction type category (CMU / Structural brick / Mortared stone) rather than as entirely separate entries — they share enough behavior that a shared code path with type-specific overrides is more maintainable than three independent implementations.
When to use this section
Paste this section alongside the main spec document at the start of any conversation about: software architecture for the Homestead Planner, porting the tool to a new property or county, beginning a full rebuild, or onboarding a developer reading the spec for the first time.
The substitution rule for the entire spec document is: wherever the spec contains a hardcoded value that belongs in the property record (frost depth, soil type, fuel availability, climate zone, code edition, wind speed, existing infrastructure), replace it in code with a reference to the corresponding property record field. The rule stays the same. The input becomes a variable. The spec’s content is correct — it describes the right behavior. This section tells you how to make that behavior respond to any project’s data rather than one project’s assumptions.
